# The Progymnasmata of the Bet Midrash: A Rabbinic Handbook of Rhetorical Training

*Reconstructed from Tannaitic and Amoraic Sources*

---

### Editor's Preface

The document that follows is a reconstruction. No ancient rabbi sat down and composed a treatise titled "The Progymnasmata of the Bet Midrash." Yet the materials from which such a treatise might have been assembled are scattered throughout the rabbinic corpus -- in Mishnah and Tosefta, in the tannaitic midrashim, in Avot de-Rabbi Natan, and in the baraitot preserved within the Babylonian and Palestinian Talmuds. What we present here is a thought experiment grounded in philological reality: if a second- or third-century rabbi had undertaken to organize the rhetorical training of his students into a systematic manual -- drawing on the pedagogical traditions already operative in the bet midrash -- what might that manual have looked like? The conceit is not as fanciful as it may first appear. As Saul Lieberman demonstrated in *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine* (1950) and *Greek in Jewish Palestine* (1942), the rabbis of Roman Palestine were not sealed off from the Greco-Roman rhetorical culture that surrounded them. Henry Fischel's pioneering work on the chreia in rabbinic literature (1968, 1973), Richard Hidary's comprehensive *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric* (2018), and Marion Moeser's comparative study of the anecdote (2002) have shown in detail that rabbinic literary forms often parallel -- and sometimes directly engage with -- the exercises of the Greco-Roman rhetorical schools.[^1]

The voice of this reconstruction is that of a senior rabbi addressing his advanced students. He speaks in the idiom of the bet midrash: direct, authoritative, anchored in scriptural citation and the chain of tradition. Where he discusses techniques of argument, arrangement, and style, he does so not as a Greek rhetorician would -- abstractly and systematically -- but as a rabbi would: through example, through precedent, through the invocation of named masters and their characteristic modes of teaching. The reader will notice that the categories and sequence of topics sometimes echo those found in the Greek progymnasmata handbooks of Theon, Hermogenes, Aphthonius, and Nicolaus. This is deliberate. The reconstruction is organized so as to make visible the structural parallels between rabbinic pedagogy and Greco-Roman rhetorical education, without forcing the rabbinic material into foreign categories.

A word about the question of historical contact. The extent to which the rabbis of Roman Palestine were directly influenced by Greco-Roman rhetorical education remains a matter of scholarly debate. At one extreme, scholars like Lieberman and Daube argued for significant direct influence; at the other, scholars like Neusner have been more cautious about positing direct borrowing. Our reconstruction does not depend on resolving this debate. Whether the rabbis learned their techniques from Greek rhetoricians, whether both traditions drew independently on common Near Eastern roots, or whether the parallels are the result of convergent evolution under similar social conditions -- in any case, the structural parallels are real and illuminating. The purpose of this reconstruction is not to argue for Greek *influence* on rabbinic pedagogy but to use the Greek categories as a *lens* through which the internal logic of rabbinic rhetorical training becomes visible. The Greek progymnasmata provide a vocabulary and a framework; the rabbinic sources provide the substance.

Throughout, footnotes provide the scholarly apparatus: references to primary sources, identifications of Greco-Roman parallels, and citations of modern scholarship. The rabbinic texts quoted are drawn from standard editions; the reader is referred to critical editions (the Kaufmann manuscript for Mishnah, the Schechter edition for Avot de-Rabbi Natan, the Horovitz-Rabin edition for the Mekhilta, etc.) for philological precision. Hebrew and Aramaic texts are provided in their original language alongside English translation. For the complete catalog of chreia parallels that undergirds Part II, Section C of this manual, the reader should consult the companion volume, *Tannaitic Parallels to Theon's Chreia Taxonomy: A Comprehensive Catalog*.[^2]

---

## PART I: FOUNDATIONS

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין קמח אין תורה, אם אין תורה אין קמח</span>
>
> *If there is no flour, there is no Torah; if there is no Torah, there is no flour.* (Avot 3:17)

And I say to you: if there is no speech, there is no Torah; if there is no Torah, there is no speech. For Torah exists in the world only through the mouths of those who teach it, and those who teach it can do so only if they have mastered the art of speech.

Consider: the Holy One, blessed be He, created the world through speech. "And God *said*: Let there be light, and there was light." The very act of creation is an act of utterance. And when He wished to reveal His will to Israel, He did so through speech: "And God *spoke* all these words, saying." And when He wished to transmit His Torah from generation to generation, He did so not through writing alone but through the living voice of teacher to student: "And you shall teach them diligently to your children, and you shall *speak* of them." From creation through revelation through transmission, the medium is speech. The student who neglects the art of speech has therefore neglected the very mechanism by which the world was made, the Torah was given, and the tradition is sustained.

### I.A. What Is Rhetoric?

My son, before you take your seat among the students of the sages, you must understand what it is that we do here. When a sage rises to expound Torah before the congregation, when an advocate speaks on behalf of the accused before the bet din, when a teacher sits and instructs his disciples in the chambers of the study house -- all of these are engaged in a single craft. We call it the *hokhmah of ordered speech*: the wisdom of finding the right word, setting it in its proper place, and delivering it so that it enters the ear and reaches the heart.[^3]

> <span dir="rtl">חכמת הלשון הסדורה</span>

This is not mere cleverness with words. The nations of the world have their own teachers of this art, and they define it variously. One of their sages defined it as "the faculty of observing in any given case the available means of persuasion."[^4] Another called it "the science of speaking well."[^5] But for us, the matter is deeper. The art of speech in the bet midrash is an aspect of Torah itself. When the Holy One, blessed be He, gave the Torah at Sinai, He gave it in speech -- and every sage who rises to teach Torah rightly is, in some measure, extending the speech of Sinai into the world.

Indeed, the Mishnah itself is sometimes called *Torah she-be'al peh* -- "Torah that is on the mouth." Not Torah that is in the mind, not Torah that is in the book, but Torah that is *on the mouth* -- Torah that exists in the act of speaking it, of transmitting it from teacher to student through the living voice. This means that the quality of speech is not incidental to Torah but essential to it. A Torah poorly spoken is a Torah poorly transmitted. A sage who knows the law but cannot communicate it clearly has failed in his most basic obligation. The art of speech is therefore not a luxury for the talented few but a necessity for every teacher, every judge, every preacher, and every student who aspires to join their ranks.

The student should know: this art has always been recognized among our sages, even if they did not always name it with a single name. Consider what is told of Rabbi Meir:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי אחא בר חנינא: גלוי וידוע לפני מי שאמר והיה העולם שאין בדורו של רבי מאיר כמותו, ומפני מה לא קבעו הלכה כמותו? שלא יכלו חבריו לעמוד על סוף דעתו, שהוא אומר על טמא טהור ומראה לו פנים, על טהור טמא ומראה לו פנים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Aha bar Hanina said: It is revealed and known before the One who spoke and the world came into being that there was none in Rabbi Meir's generation equal to him. And why did they not fix the halakhah according to his view? Because his colleagues could not fathom the full depth of his reasoning, for he would declare the impure to be pure and show justification for it, and the pure to be impure and show justification for it.*[^6]

Here is a man who could argue one hundred and fifty reasons that an impure thing is pure, and one hundred and fifty reasons that a pure thing is impure -- and each argument with its own face of plausibility. This is not deception; it is mastery of the art. For how can a judge render true judgment if he cannot see the case from every side? How can a teacher instruct his students if he cannot anticipate every objection? Rabbi Meir's power was the power of *invention* -- the ability to discover arguments where lesser minds see none.

And note well: the Talmud adds that precisely *because* of this overwhelming ability, the halakhah was not fixed according to his view. His very mastery was a source of danger -- for his colleagues could not always tell which of his arguments he himself believed and which he offered merely as demonstrations of dialectical skill. This teaches the student an important lesson: rhetorical ability without restraint can be self-defeating. The sage who can argue everything persuasively risks being believed about nothing. The art of rhetoric includes knowing when *not* to deploy one's full powers -- when simplicity, directness, and a single clear argument will serve better than a dazzling display of dialectical virtuosity.

And Rabbi Meir was also called by another name: he was called the author of *meshalim* -- parables. It is said:

> <span dir="rtl">משמת רבי מאיר, בטלו מושלי משלים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased.*

Here invention and the mashal converge: the master of argument was also the master of parable. For the mashal is itself a tool of invention -- a way of discovering and presenting arguments that the abstract intellect alone cannot reach. We will discuss the mashal at length when we come to the preliminary exercises.

And consider, too, what happened when the question of public discourse arose in the house of study. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבי יהושע שהיה מדבר דברים בפני רבן גמליאל, והיו העם עומדים עד שאמרו לו: עד מתי אתה מצער אותנו?</span>
>
> *It happened that Rabbi Yehoshua was speaking before Rabban Gamaliel, and the people stood until they said: How long will you continue to distress him?*[^7]

And in the aftermath of that incident, the doors of the bet midrash were opened wide, and on that day many benches were added -- some say four hundred, some say seven hundred -- and no student who sought to enter was turned away.[^8] What was at stake in the removal of Rabban Gamaliel from his position? It was not merely a dispute about authority. It was a dispute about how discourse itself should be conducted in the house of study -- whether speech should be controlled and restricted, or open and expansive. The sages chose openness, and on that day much Torah was taught that had previously been suppressed.

There is yet a third example the student should ponder. It is taught regarding the sage Resh Lakish -- Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish -- that before he entered the bet midrash, he had been a gladiator, a man of physical power. When he encountered Rabbi Yohanan at the Jordan River and was drawn to Torah study, he brought with him the combative energy of the arena. And what happened? That combative energy was transformed -- not eliminated but redirected -- into the intellectual arena of the bet midrash. Resh Lakish became the sharpest challenger, the most formidable disputant, the one whose objections forced Rabbi Yohanan to think more deeply than he ever had before. When Resh Lakish died, Rabbi Yohanan could find no one to replace him -- not because no one knew as much Torah, but because no one could *challenge* him the way Resh Lakish had. They brought him Rabbi Elazar ben Pedat, who agreed with everything he said, and Rabbi Yohanan wept: "When I would state a law, Resh Lakish would raise twenty-four objections, and I would give twenty-four answers, and the halakhah would be clarified. But you say, 'A baraita supports you' -- do I not already know that I am right?"

This teaches us: the art of rhetoric is not merely the art of winning arguments. It is the art of sharpening arguments -- of testing propositions through challenge and counter-challenge until the truth emerges, polished and proven. The sage who is never challenged is impoverished; the sage who is challenged by a worthy opponent is enriched. This is why the bet midrash values *machloqet le-shem shamayim* -- dispute for the sake of Heaven -- above passive agreement.

And there is another dimension that the student must understand. The art of ordered speech in the bet midrash is also the art of *listening*. The sage does not merely speak; he listens -- to the text, to the question, to the objection, to the voice of his opponent. The ability to hear what another is truly saying, to identify the core of his argument, and to respond to *that* rather than to a caricature of it -- this is the mark of rhetorical mastery. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">אינו נכנס לתוך דברי חברו</span>
>
> *He does not interrupt his fellow's words.*

This is not merely a rule of courtesy; it is a rule of rhetoric. The sage who interrupts has not yet heard his opponent's full argument. He is responding to a partial understanding, and his response will therefore be partial. The sage who waits, who listens to the end, who absorbs the full force of the opposing position before responding -- that sage's response will be complete, precise, and devastating. Patience is a rhetorical virtue.

There is one more quality of the art of speech that the student must understand before we proceed: its relationship to *truth*. The nations of the world sometimes speak of rhetoric as if it were the art of making the weaker argument appear the stronger -- as if skill in speech were a tool for distortion. And indeed, this danger exists. The sage who masters the art of argument can, if he chooses, lead his listeners astray with persuasive but false reasoning. The Mishnah itself warns against this when it tells us that Rabbi Meir's ability to declare the impure pure and show justification for it made his colleagues unable to determine his true position.

But the proper understanding of rhetoric in the bet midrash is different. For us, the art of speech is in the service of truth, not opposed to it. The sage's goal is not to *create* truth through persuasion but to *reveal* truth that already exists in the Torah and to *transmit* that truth in the most clear, compelling, and memorable form possible. The art of rhetoric is therefore an art of fidelity -- fidelity to the text, fidelity to the tradition, and fidelity to the listener's capacity to understand. The sage who uses eloquence to obscure rather than illuminate has violated the fundamental covenant of the bet midrash: that all speech in this house is speech in the service of Heaven.

So let the student understand: the art we teach here is not ornament. It is the very mechanism by which Torah is transmitted, debated, clarified, and applied. Without mastery of ordered speech, there can be no bet midrash.[^9]

---

### I.B. The Three Genres of Discourse

Know, my son, that all speech in the house of Israel falls into three kinds. Every sage who opens his mouth does so for one of three purposes, and the student who cannot distinguish among them will stumble in his training. These three are: judgment (*din*), counsel (*eitzah*), and praise (*shevach*).[^10]

#### The First Genre: Judgment (Din)

The first kind of discourse is the speech of the bet din -- the house of judgment. When two parties come before the judges with a dispute, each one presents his case, and the judges deliberate. This is the discourse of prosecution and defense, of claim and counter-claim. The Mishnah teaches:

> <span dir="rtl">דיני ממונות פותחין בין לזכות בין לחובה. דיני נפשות פותחין לזכות ואין פותחין לחובה</span>
>
> *In civil cases, they open [deliberations] whether for acquittal or for conviction. In capital cases, they open for acquittal but do not open for conviction.*[^11]

Here is a principle of arrangement that governs all judicial speech: in matters of life and death, the discourse is tilted toward defense. The youngest judges speak first, so that they are not swayed by the opinions of their elders.[^12] Every student of the Sanhedrin must learn to argue both sides, but the structure itself -- the *seder* of the proceeding -- is designed to favor mercy.

The student who would master judicial discourse must learn the arts of examination (*bedikah* and *haqirah*) -- the probing of witnesses, the testing of testimony for consistency, the detection of contradiction. The Mishnah devotes an entire chapter to the admonitions given to witnesses in capital cases:

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד מאיימין על העדים? היו מכניסין אותן ומאיימין עליהן... שמא תאמרו מאומד, ומשמועה, עד מפי עד, מפי אדם נאמן... שמא אי אתם יודעין שסופנו לבודקכם בדריישה ובחקירה</span>
>
> *How do they admonish the witnesses? They bring them in and admonish them... perhaps you will say something based on conjecture, or hearsay, a witness quoting another witness, or quoting a trustworthy person... perhaps you do not know that ultimately we will examine you with inquiry and interrogation.*[^13]

This is the rhetoric of the courtroom: precise, structured, governed by rules of procedure that are themselves acts of persuasion -- for the admonition to the witnesses is designed to impress upon them the gravity of their testimony, and thereby to elicit truth.[^14]

And mark well the continuation of this admonition, for it is itself a rhetorical masterpiece -- an argument designed to make the witness feel the cosmic weight of his words:

> <span dir="rtl">לפיכך נברא אדם יחידי, ללמדך שכל המאבד נפש אחת מישראל מעלה עליו הכתוב כאילו איבד עולם מלא, וכל המקיים נפש אחת מישראל מעלה עליו הכתוב כאילו קיים עולם מלא</span>
>
> *Therefore Adam was created alone, to teach you that whoever destroys a single soul from Israel, Scripture charges it to him as though he had destroyed an entire world; and whoever preserves a single soul from Israel, Scripture credits it to him as though he had preserved an entire world.*

The judges are not merely stating a theological principle. They are deploying it as a rhetorical weapon -- a device to make the witness tremble before false testimony. The entire speech, from the opening admonition to the cosmic conclusion, is a carefully constructed argument from consequence: if you lie, you destroy a world. This is judicial rhetoric at its highest pitch.

The student should also observe the structure of advocacy in the bet din. In capital cases, the Mishnah teaches that a student may argue for acquittal but not for conviction. And if a judge who argued for conviction later wishes to change his position to acquittal, he may do so; but a judge who argued for acquittal may not reverse himself and argue for conviction. These procedural rules are not merely legal technicalities; they are rhetorical constraints -- they structure the discourse of the courtroom to produce a specific outcome: the preservation of life wherever possible.

Notice how deeply this principle extends. The very *arrangement* of speakers in the bet din is a rhetorical act: in capital cases, the youngest judges speak first, so that they are not intimidated or influenced by the opinions of their seniors. In civil cases, the eldest may speak first. This difference in speaking order reflects a difference in rhetorical strategy: in civil cases, the weight of experience and authority is an asset; in capital cases, it is a potential danger, for a junior judge who hears his master vote for conviction may be reluctant to dissent. The bet din's rules of procedure are, at every level, rules of rhetoric -- they govern who speaks, in what order, with what constraints, and toward what end.

The student should also mark the distinction between *bediqot* and *haqirot* -- between general examinations and specific cross-examinations of witnesses. The general examinations establish the framework of the testimony: what year, what month, what day, what hour, what place. The specific cross-examinations probe the details: what was the color of the garment, what was the color of the soil, was the fig ripe or unripe. A witness who contradicts himself on the general questions invalidates his testimony entirely; a witness who contradicts himself on the specific questions merely weakens it. This hierarchy of questioning is itself a principle of rhetorical analysis: the student learns to distinguish the essential framework of an argument from its supporting details, and to focus his challenges where they will do the most damage.

#### The Second Genre: Counsel (Eitzah) and Instruction (Hora'ah)

The second kind of discourse is the speech of counsel and halakhic instruction. When a sage rises before the assembly to declare what Israel should do -- whether to enact a decree, to permit or forbid, to set policy for the community -- he engages in deliberative discourse. He does not judge the past; he directs the future.

The great example is the deliberation that followed the removal of Rabban Gamaliel:

> <span dir="rtl">אותו היום סלקוהו לשומר הפתח ונתנה להם רשות לתלמידים ליכנס. שהיה רבן גמליאל מכריז ואומר: כל תלמיד שאין תוכו כברו לא יכנס לבית המדרש. ההוא יומא אתוספו כמה ספסלי</span>
>
> *On that day, they removed the doorkeeper and gave the students permission to enter. For Rabban Gamaliel used to proclaim: Any student whose interior is not like his exterior may not enter the study house. On that day, many benches were added.*[^15]

Rabban Gamaliel's original policy was itself deliberative rhetoric: he argued that restricting admission to the bet midrash would preserve the purity of learning. His opponents argued the contrary -- that opening the doors would increase Torah. Both were engaged in counsel: what course of action best serves the community? And the sages, when they debated whether to restore Rabban Gamaliel or to retain Rabbi Elazar ben Azariah, were likewise engaged in deliberation.[^16]

The halakhic discourse of the bet midrash is, at its core, deliberative. When the sages debated the eighteen decrees at the house of Hananiah ben Hizkiyyah ben Garon, they were not judging a case; they were setting policy for all of Israel.[^17] When Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai requested of Vespasian, "Give me Yavneh and its sages," he was making a deliberative argument -- persuading a ruler to adopt a course of action that would preserve the future of Torah.[^18]

The student who would master deliberative discourse must learn the art of presenting halakhic arguments -- of marshaling proofs from Scripture, from precedent, from logical inference -- and of anticipating the objections of his opponents. He must learn the structures of the *machloqet* (dispute), for the machloqet is itself a form of deliberation: two positions are presented, each with its reasoning, and the community must choose between them.

The structure of the machloqet deserves careful study, for it is the primary vehicle of deliberative rhetoric in the bet midrash. A machloqet is not merely a disagreement; it is a *structured* disagreement, governed by formal conventions. First, the positions are stated clearly and symmetrically: "Beit Shammai say X; Beit Hillel say Y." Then the reasoning of each side is presented, often through a challenge-and-response pattern: "What is the reasoning of Beit Shammai? Because Scripture says... And Beit Hillel? They would say to you..." Finally, a resolution is reached -- either through a vote, through the invocation of a decisive precedent, or through the declaration that the halakhah follows one side.

The student should notice that the Mishnah frequently preserves both sides of a machloqet even after the law has been decided. Why retain the losing position? Because the losing position may become relevant in a future case where conditions are different. And because the act of deliberation itself -- the weighing of alternatives, the testing of arguments -- is a form of Torah study that has value independent of its practical outcome. As it is taught: "Both these and these are the words of the living God." The machloqet is not a failure of consensus but a triumph of deliberation.

Consider the great debate at Yavneh over whether Ammon and Moab are obligated in the tithe of the poor in the sabbatical year. The sages debated the matter and voted; Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Eliezer took opposing positions, each marshaling scriptural arguments, logical inferences, and appeals to existing precedent. The entire discussion has the character of a deliberative assembly -- not unlike the assemblies of the Greeks, where speakers rise to urge different courses of action and the body votes. The difference is that in the bet midrash, the courses of action are halakhic: what should all of Israel do?

And consider, too, the deliberative rhetoric embedded in the Passover Haggadah, which is itself a persuasive discourse. The sage at the Seder table does not merely narrate the Exodus; he argues for its ongoing significance, addresses the questions of the four sons, and guides the assembly toward a particular emotional and theological conclusion. When Rabban Gamaliel declares, "Whoever does not say these three things on Passover has not fulfilled his obligation -- Pesah, Matzah, and Maror" -- he is engaged in deliberative rhetoric: he is persuading the community that this is the essential content of the Seder discourse.

#### The Third Genre: Praise and Blame (Shevach u-Genai)

The third kind of discourse is the speech of ceremony: the eulogy (*hesped*), the festival sermon (*derashah*), and the praise of sages. This is the discourse of occasion -- the speech that marks births and deaths, festivals and fasts, triumphs and catastrophes.

The sages gave great attention to the art of eulogy. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">מספידין ומתעננין בכל מקום שיש בו גדולי ישראל</span>
>
> *They deliver eulogies and engage in mourning rites wherever there are great ones of Israel.*

And the Talmud preserves remarkable examples of eulogistic rhetoric. When Rav died, his eulogizers said:

> <span dir="rtl">יום שמת רב, אמרו: בטלו כל מיני שבח</span>

And when the great sages of the generation of the Mishnah passed, each was eulogized with a formula that captured his distinctive quality:

> <span dir="rtl">משמת רבי מאיר, בטלו מושלי משלים. משמת בן עזאי, בטלו השקדנים. משמת בן זומא, בטלו הדרשנים. משמת רבי עקיבא, בטל כבוד התורה. משמת רבי חנינא בן דוסא, בטלו אנשי מעשה. משמת רבי יוסי קטנתא, פסקו חסידים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased. When Ben Azzai died, the diligent students ceased. When Ben Zoma died, the expositors ceased. When Rabbi Akiva died, the glory of Torah ceased. When Rabbi Hanina ben Dosa died, the men of deeds ceased. When Rabbi Yose the Small died, the pious ones ceased.*[^19]

Each of these formulae is a miniature epideictic oration: it identifies the sage with a single supreme quality, and it declares that his death marks the end of that quality in the world. This is the art of praise reduced to its most concentrated form.

The Talmud also preserves examples of elaborate eulogies that demonstrate the full range of epideictic technique. When the great sages of the land of Israel died, their eulogizers composed poetic formulae that combined praise, lament, and cosmic imagery. Consider the eulogies recorded for the death of various rabbis in Mo'ed Qatan:

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרבי אבהו, אזדלפו עמודי דקיסרי מיא</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Abbahu died, the pillars of Caesarea dripped water.*

And:

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרב הונא, תליסר נהרוותא דמשחא ודובשא איעכור</span>

The eulogizers employed hyperbole, cosmic imagery, and poetic parallelism -- the very techniques of heightened language that characterize epideictic speech. When they said that the pillars wept or that rivers of oil and honey were dammed up, they were not reporting facts; they were using the rhetoric of amplification to express the magnitude of the community's loss.

The art of *blame* (*genai*) is the mirror image of praise, and the sages practiced it too. When the Mishnah declares that "Korah and all his company have no share in the world to come," it is performing an act of epideictic blame -- a posthumous condemnation that serves as a warning to future generations. The prophetic rebukes (*tokhahot*) preserved in Scripture and echoed in rabbinic preaching are likewise epideictic: they blame the nation for its failings in order to motivate repentance.

The festival sermon (*derashah*) is likewise epideictic. When a sage stands before the congregation on Shabbat or on a festival and opens with a *petihta* -- a proem that begins from a remote verse and winds its way to the Torah portion of the day -- he is engaged in ceremonial discourse. He praises God, he praises Torah, he praises Israel. He may also blame: the festival derashah can contain rebuke (*tokhahah*), warning, and exhortation. But the frame is ceremonial, the occasion is public, and the purpose is to elevate the hearts of the listeners.[^20]

Consider the structure of the *petihta* more closely, for it is a masterwork of epideictic arrangement. The preacher begins with a verse from the Psalms or Proverbs -- a verse that seems to have no connection to the Torah reading of the day. Step by step, through a chain of interpretive links, he draws the listener from the remote verse toward the parashah. The entire journey is one of discovery and surprise: only at the final moment does the audience see how the beginning connects to the end. This technique builds suspense, sustains attention, and creates in the listener a feeling of intellectual and spiritual satisfaction -- the sense that all of Torah is interconnected, that every verse illuminates every other. This is rhetoric in the service of theology.

The conventions of the eulogy deserve particular attention, for the *hesped* is one of the most demanding forms of public discourse. The eulogizer must accomplish several things simultaneously: he must honor the dead, comfort the living, instruct the community, and express the communal grief in language that is elevated without being excessive. The Talmud debates the proper boundaries of eulogy: is it permitted to exaggerate the virtues of the deceased? The answer is nuanced -- one may *amplify* existing virtues (saying of a generous person that he was exceedingly generous) but one may not *invent* virtues that the deceased did not possess. This distinction between amplification and fabrication is itself a fundamental principle of epideictic rhetoric: the orator works with the materials that reality provides, but he shapes and heightens them through the art of language.

The student should also observe that the *hesped* has a required emotional trajectory. It begins with praise, moves through lament, and concludes with consolation. This tripartite structure -- praise, lament, consolation -- mirrors the structure of the book of Lamentations itself, which opens with a description of Jerusalem's former glory, descends into the depths of mourning, and concludes with a prayer for restoration. The eulogizer who follows this structure takes his audience on an emotional journey that is both cathartic and instructive.

The student who would master epideictic discourse must learn these conventions of the eulogy, the structures of the festival sermon, and the art of praise and blame as applied to character -- for the praise of sages is also, implicitly, a lesson in what virtues the student should cultivate and what vices he should avoid.[^21]

---

### I.C. The Five Faculties of the Orator

My son, the sage who would speak well must possess five faculties, each of which can be developed through training. They are: invention, arrangement, style, memory, and delivery. I will teach you each one in turn.[^22]

#### The First Faculty: Invention (Hamtza'ah)

Invention is the ability to find arguments. When the student is confronted with a question -- a legal problem, a textual difficulty, a challenge from an opponent -- he must know where to look for an answer. In the bet midrash, the question is always: *"From where do you derive this?"*

> <span dir="rtl">מנא הני מילי? מניין?</span>

This question -- *minayin?* -- is the engine of rabbinic invention. It demands that every claim be traced to its source, every ruling connected to its scriptural foundation. And the tools of invention are the hermeneutical rules (*middot*) by which Torah is expounded. The greatest of these are the thirteen middot of Rabbi Ishmael, which are recited daily as part of the morning liturgy:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי ישמעאל אומר: בשלוש עשרה מידות התורה נדרשת. מקל וחומר, ומגזירה שוה, מבנין אב מכתוב אחד ומבנין אב משני כתובים, מכלל ופרט ומפרט וכלל, כלל ופרט וכלל...</span>
>
> *Rabbi Ishmael says: The Torah is expounded by thirteen rules. From a minor premise to a major one [qal va-homer], from a verbal analogy [gezerah shavah], from a general principle derived from one verse and from a general principle derived from two verses, from a general statement and a particular statement and from a particular statement and a general statement, from a general statement followed by a particular statement followed by a general statement...*[^23]

These middot are not merely logical rules; they are *topoi* -- places where arguments can be found. The *qal va-homer* (a fortiori argument) is a topos of proportion: if the lesser case is so, how much more the greater? The *gezerah shavah* (verbal analogy) is a topos of similarity: where the same word appears in two contexts, the law of one context may illuminate the other. The *binyan av* (general principle from a particular case) is a topos of induction: from one clear case, a general rule is derived.

Let the student consider how each tool works in practice. The *qal va-homer* is perhaps the most natural of all argumentative forms -- even children use it instinctively. "If Father would not permit me to do X in a minor case, how much less would he permit me in a major case!" The sages formalized this natural reasoning and gave it rules: a *qal va-homer* argument must never impose more than the source case warrants (*dayo la-ba min ha-din lihyot ka-nidon*). This rule is itself a constraint on rhetorical excess -- it prevents the sage from using the a fortiori argument to draw conclusions that go beyond the evidence.

The *gezerah shavah* is more restrictive: it requires a tradition (*qabbalah*) linking two passages through a shared word. No sage may invent a gezerah shavah on his own authority. This restriction teaches an important lesson about the limits of invention: some argumentative tools are too powerful to be deployed without external authorization. The student should learn from this that not every argument one *can* make is an argument one *should* make.

The *binyan av* (constructing a prototype) is perhaps the most intellectually demanding of the middot. From a single clear case in Scripture, the sage derives a general principle that applies to all similar cases. This is the rabbinic form of induction -- moving from the particular to the universal. Consider: Scripture explicitly commands rest on the Sabbath for "your male slave and your female slave." From this single case, the sage constructs a *binyan av*: if even slaves, who have limited obligations, must rest, then surely free persons must rest; and if rest applies to human labor, the sage inquires whether it extends to the labor of animals, and finds confirmation in "your ox and your donkey." The *binyan av* builds outward from its prototype, extending the principle as far as the logic will carry it -- but no further. The student should practice this form of reasoning: take a single explicit rule and ask, what principle does it exemplify? To what other cases does that principle apply? Where does the analogy break down? For the *binyan av* is not unlimited: it applies only where the cases share the relevant characteristic (*tzad ha-shaveh*), and the student must learn to identify both the shared characteristic and the point of divergence.

The sage who masters these tools can find arguments in places where the untrained eye sees nothing. As it was said of Rabbi Akiva:

> <span dir="rtl">דברים שלא נתגלו למשה נתגלו לרבי עקיבא</span>
>
> *Things that were not revealed to Moses were revealed to Rabbi Akiva.*[^24]

And this was because Rabbi Akiva's method of interpretation -- his principle of *ribbuyim u-mi'utim* (inclusions and exclusions), which found meaning in every particle and every ornamental stroke of the Torah's letters -- gave him tools of invention that others lacked.[^25]

Let the student practice invention with a concrete example. Take the question: "From where do we derive that one who saves a single life is as if he saved an entire world?" The untrained student will cite only Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:5 and stop. But the student who has mastered the tools of invention will proceed further. He will ask: does Scripture itself hint at this principle? He will note that when Cain killed Abel, God said, "The *bloods* of your brother cry out" -- *bloods* in the plural, teaching that Abel's descendants were killed with him. He will invoke the *qal va-homer*: if one who destroys is held accountable for all future generations, how much more is one who preserves credited with all future generations. He will seek a *gezerah shavah* -- a verbal analogy linking the creation of Adam "alone" to other places where singularity implies totality. Each tool opens a new avenue of argument. This is what invention means in the bet midrash: not the creation of arguments from nothing, but the discovery of arguments that lie dormant in the text, waiting for the skillful interpreter to draw them out. The sage is like a miner who strikes the rock and finds gold beneath -- the gold was always there; the miner's skill lies in knowing where to strike. And just as a skilled miner works methodically, testing different veins and following the signs that indicate where ore lies hidden, so the sage works methodically through the middot, testing each hermeneutical tool against the text until one of them yields the argument he seeks. The student who has not mastered the tools of invention is like a miner who strikes randomly at the rock face; he may occasionally find something, but he will miss far more than he discovers.

#### The Second Faculty: Arrangement (Seder)

Arrangement is the ability to order a discourse so that each part is in its proper place. The student must learn: it is not enough to find good arguments; one must present them in the right sequence. A discourse poorly arranged is like a building with its foundation on the roof.

The paramount example of arrangement in rabbinic discourse is the *petihta* -- the proem of the midrashic sermon. The petihta begins with a verse from the Writings (*Ketuvim*) or the Prophets (*Nevi'im*) -- never from the Torah portion itself -- and through a chain of interpretive steps, it arrives at the opening verse of the day's parashah. The entire structure is one of *surprise*: the listener does not know where the preacher is going until the very end, when the remote verse and the near verse are suddenly shown to be connected. This is arrangement at its most artful.[^26]

In halakhic discourse, arrangement follows different principles. The Mishnah itself is a masterwork of arrangement: its six orders, its sixty-three tractates, its chapters and individual rulings are disposed according to principles of descending numerical order, thematic association, and mnemonic convenience. When Rabbi Judah the Prince compiled the Mishnah, he was not merely collecting laws; he was arranging them for maximum pedagogical effect.[^27]

The student should observe how the sages arrange their arguments in a legal discussion. Typically, the strongest argument is placed first or last -- never in the middle, where it may be overlooked. When multiple proofs are offered, they are arranged from strongest to weakest, or from scriptural proof to logical inference to precedent. This is not accident; it is art.

Consider, too, the arrangement of the Mishnah's chapters within a tractate. Tractate Berakhot, for example, opens with the laws of the Shema -- "From what time may one recite the evening Shema?" -- before proceeding to the Amidah, then to blessings over food, and finally to blessings over various phenomena. The movement is from the most common and obligatory (the twice-daily Shema) to the occasional and circumstantial (blessings upon seeing lightning or hearing thunder). This arrangement follows the principle of frequency: what is done most often is taught first. Other tractates follow the principle of descending magnitude: Bava Qamma begins with the most damaging torts and proceeds to lesser ones.

But the opening word of the entire Mishnah deserves special attention: *me'eimatai* -- "From what time?" The Mishnah does not begin with a statement but with a question. This is itself a principle of arrangement: begin with a question, and the listener is immediately engaged. He wants to hear the answer. If the Mishnah had begun with a declarative statement -- "One recites the evening Shema from such-and-such a time" -- the listener would receive the information passively. By beginning with a question, the Mishnah draws the listener into the argument as a participant. The student should remember this principle in his own discourse: a question at the beginning is worth more than a statement, because it activates the listener's mind.

The student should also learn the arrangement of a halakhic argument (*sugya*) as it unfolds in the Talmud: first the Mishnah states the law; then a question is raised (*maitibi*, "they objected"); then a resolution is offered; then further objections and resolutions follow, sometimes branching into tangential discussions before returning to the main theme. This structure -- thesis, objection, resolution, refinement -- is itself a form of arrangement, and the student who internalizes it will know how to organize his own legal arguments.

But the sugya has an additional feature that the student should study: the *tangential excursus* (*aggadeta de-agav*). In the midst of a halakhic discussion, the Talmud often digresses into an aggadic narrative, a biographical anecdote, or a theological reflection that seems unrelated to the legal question at hand. The untrained student may think this is a failure of arrangement -- a wandering from the point. But the skilled student will recognize that the digression is almost always thematically connected to the main discussion at a deeper level. The narrative may illustrate the human stakes of the legal question; the theological reflection may provide the conceptual framework within which the legal answer makes sense. The sugya's arrangement is not linear but *organic*: it allows the discussion to breathe, to touch upon the human dimensions of the law, and to return to the legal question enriched by the detour.

The student who learns to arrange his own arguments this way -- stating the law, acknowledging the objection, resolving it, and then pausing to reflect on the deeper significance of the question before offering his final ruling -- will produce discourse that is not only correct but compelling. For the listener who hears only the bare ruling receives the answer; the listener who hears the ruling embedded in its human and theological context receives the *understanding*.

#### The Third Faculty: Style (Lashon)

Style is the choice of language -- the decision to say a thing in one way rather than another. The sages were keenly aware of the difference between elegant expression and crude expression, between concise language and verbose language. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם ישנה אדם לתלמידו דרך קצרה</span>
>
> *A person should always teach his student in concise language.*[^28]

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם ילמד אדם את תלמידו דרך קצרה... ואמר רבי הונא: למה נקרא שמו של רבי מאיר? שהוא מאיר עיני חכמים בהלכה</span>
>
> *A person should always teach his student in concise language... And Rabbi Huna said: Why was he called Rabbi Meir? Because he would illuminate [me'ir] the eyes of the sages in halakhah.*

Conciseness is a virtue in the bet midrash, but it is not the only virtue. There are times when elaboration is required -- when a teaching is so difficult or so important that it must be stated from multiple angles, illustrated with multiple examples, and reinforced with multiple proofs. The art of style lies in knowing when to be brief and when to be expansive.

The sages also taught the importance of choosing clean and dignified language. The Torah itself, they noted, goes out of its way to avoid crude expression:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם יספר אדם בלשון נקיה, שהרי בנבלה הכתוב מקרא שמנה אותיות: ומן הבהמה אשר איננה טהורה. ובבכור הקדשים הכתוב מקרא עשר אותיות</span>
>
> *A person should always speak in clean language. For regarding the unclean animal, Scripture uses eight extra letters [to say "which is not pure" rather than "impure"]. And regarding the firstborn of sacred animals, Scripture uses ten extra letters [for similar euphemistic phrasing].*[^29]

If the Torah itself, which values every letter, is willing to add extra letters to avoid an ugly word, how much more should the sage choose his language with care.

The student should also learn the distinction between *lashon ba'alei batim* (the language of householders -- ordinary, unadorned speech) and *lashon chakhamim* (the language of sages -- precise, technical, and often elegant). In the bet midrash, the language of sages is preferred: terms are used with precision, formulations follow established patterns, and the choice of words reflects the care of a craftsman selecting his tools. Yet in public preaching, the language of householders has its place -- for the preacher must be understood by all, and there is no virtue in precision that is unintelligible to the audience.

The sages also recognized the power of *lashon melitzah* -- elevated, poetic language. The prophets spoke in melitzah, and the liturgical poets (*paytanim*) continued this tradition. There are moments in the bet midrash when plain speech is insufficient -- when the occasion demands a higher register, a more resonant mode of expression. The eulogy is such a moment; the festival derashah is another. The student should develop the ability to move between registers -- to speak plainly when clarity demands it and eloquently when the occasion warrants it.

And the student should know the three levels of style recognized by the tradition: the plain (*pashut*), the middle (*beinoni*), and the grand (*nisgav*). A halakhic ruling is stated plainly. An aggadic narrative is told in the middle style. A prophetic oracle or a liturgical poem is expressed in the grand style. Each level has its appropriate occasions, and the sage who uses the grand style for a minor ruling or the plain style for a cosmic declaration has misjudged his material.

The student should also learn the characteristic difference in style between halakhic and aggadic discourse. Halakhic style is precise, compressed, and formulaic: "If X, then Y; Rabbi A says Z." Every word carries legal weight; no word is wasted. Aggadic style is expansive, figurative, and emotionally evocative: it uses parables, hyperbole, dialogue, and vivid imagery to move the heart as well as the mind. The sage who speaks only in halakhic style will be respected but will not move his audience; the sage who speaks only in aggadic style will entertain but will not command authority. The complete orator masters both registers and knows when to employ each one.

There is a teaching that illustrates this point:

> <span dir="rtl">כד הוה דריש רבי אבהו באגדתא, הוו אתו כולי עלמא. כד הוה דריש רבי חייא בר אבא בשמעתתא, הוו אזלי כולי עלמא לגבי רבי אבהו</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Abbahu would expound in aggadah, all the people would come. When Rabbi Hiyya bar Abba would expound in halakhah, everyone would go to Rabbi Abbahu.*

The crowds preferred the aggadic preacher -- the master of vivid, engaging, emotionally resonant speech. But Rabbi Hiyya bar Abba's halakhic discourses carried the weight of law. The ideal, which few achieve, is to combine both: to teach halakhah with the vividness of aggadah, and to tell aggadah with the precision of halakhah.

#### The Fourth Faculty: Memory (Zikaron)

Memory is the most fundamental faculty of the scholar. Without memory, there can be no Torah. In a culture where the Oral Torah was, by definition, oral -- transmitted from mouth to ear, from teacher to student, without the aid of writing -- memory was not merely useful; it was the very medium of transmission.

The professional memorizer (*tanna*) who stood before the sage and recited the teachings was a living archive. The sage would instruct him to "recite" (*teni*), and the tanna would produce the relevant tradition from his vast store of memorized material. The sage would then expound upon what the tanna had recited.[^30]

And it is taught regarding the method of memorization:

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד סדר משנה? משה למד מפי הגבורה. נכנס אהרן, ושנה לו משה פרקו. נסתלק אהרן, וישב לשמאל משה. נכנסו בניו, ושנה להם משה פרקם. נסתלקו בניו... נכנסו זקנים... נכנסו כל העם... נמצא ביד אהרן ארבעה, ביד בניו שלושה, ביד הזקנים שניים, ביד כל ישראל אחד</span>
>
> *What was the order of teaching? Moses learned from the mouth of the Almighty. Aaron entered, and Moses taught him his lesson. Aaron withdrew and sat to the left of Moses. His sons entered, and Moses taught them their lesson. His sons withdrew... The elders entered... All the people entered... Thus Aaron had [heard it] four times, his sons three times, the elders twice, and all of Israel once.*[^31]

This system of fourfold repetition is itself a rhetorical technique: repetition is the mother of memory. And the student should know that the organization of material into numerical lists, mnemonic devices, and patterned sequences -- all of which pervade the Mishnah and baraitot -- serves the faculty of memory. When the sage teaches "on three things the world stands," or "there are four types of students," or "seven qualities characterize a sage," he is using number as a hook for memory.[^32]

The sages also employed *simanim* (mnemonic signs) -- brief words or phrases that encode the sequence of topics in a discussion. In the Talmud, one frequently encounters a *siman* at the head of a series of teachings: a string of unrelated words whose initial letters or key sounds correspond to the sequence of topics that follow. These simanim are the rabbinic equivalent of the *loci* and *imagines* -- the places and images -- that the Greek and Roman teachers of memory recommended. The principle is the same: attach the abstract content to a concrete, memorable form, and the content will be retained.

And the student should know that the very act of *shanah* -- of oral repetition -- is itself a mnemonic technique. The word *mishnah* comes from the root *sh-n-h*, to repeat. To learn is to repeat, and to repeat is to learn. Rabbi Hiyya taught his students in groups, assigning each group a different tractate, and then had them teach one another -- so that each student's memory was reinforced by hearing the material from multiple voices. This system of reciprocal teaching is a sophisticated mnemonic pedagogy that ancient rhetoricians would have recognized.

There is yet another dimension to the faculty of memory that the student must appreciate: the distinction between *rote memory* and *structural memory*. The tanna who memorizes a tractate word for word possesses rote memory -- and this is invaluable. But the sage who understands *why* the teachings are arranged in their particular order possesses structural memory -- and this is even more powerful. For if a sage with structural memory forgets a particular teaching, he can reconstruct it from its context: he knows what comes before and after, he knows the principle that governs the sequence, and he can reason his way back to the forgotten material. The sage with only rote memory has no such recourse; if a link in the chain breaks, all that follows is lost.

This is why the student must not only memorize but *understand the logic of arrangement*. When you learn a chapter of Mishnah, do not merely memorize its rulings in sequence. Ask yourself: why does this ruling precede that one? What principle connects them? Is the order chronological, numerical, thematic, or mnemonic? The student who can answer these questions has turned the arrangement itself into a mnemonic device -- and his memory will be more durable than that of the student who memorizes without understanding.

#### The Fifth Faculty: Delivery (Ne'imah)

Delivery is the art of the voice and the manner. A teaching poorly delivered, even if its content is excellent, will fail to reach its audience. The sages were attentive to the quality of vocal performance, particularly in the public reading of Scripture. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי שפטיה אמר רבי יוחנן: כל הקורא בלא נעימה ושונה בלא זמרה, עליו הכתוב אומר: וגם אני נתתי להם חוקים לא טובים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Shefatyah said in the name of Rabbi Yohanan: Concerning one who reads [Scripture] without melody and studies [Mishnah] without chanting, Scripture says: "Moreover I gave them statutes that were not good" (Ezekiel 20:25).*[^33]

The reading of Torah must be done with *ta'amei ha-miqra* -- the cantillation accents that convey the meaning, the rhythm, and the emotional coloring of the text. The study of Mishnah, too, has its own melody. These are not mere aesthetic preferences; they are pedagogical tools. The melody aids memory, it conveys emphasis, it signals the structure of the argument. A sage who drones in a monotone will lose his audience; a sage who chants with the proper melody will hold them.[^34]

Beyond the voice, delivery includes *bearing* -- the physical presence of the speaker. The sages spoke of the qualities that befit a teacher: dignity, composure, attentiveness to the audience. The sage who fidgets, who averts his eyes, who mumbles into his beard -- these failings of delivery undermine even the most learned discourse.

The student must also learn the art of *timing* -- knowing when to pause, when to raise the voice, when to lower it. The greatest sermons of the sages were delivered not merely with correct words but with the right rhythm, the right cadence, the right silences between the words.

And the student should know the power of *silence* as a tool of delivery. There are moments when the most effective speech is no speech at all. When Aaron was told of the death of his two sons, the Torah records:

> <span dir="rtl">וידום אהרן</span>
>
> *And Aaron was silent.*

That silence speaks more loudly than any words could. The sage who knows when to be silent -- after delivering a devastating argument, after posing a question he wishes the audience to ponder, after hearing a teaching that leaves him in awe -- that sage has mastered the highest art of delivery. Silence is not the absence of rhetoric; it is rhetoric at its most concentrated. The Talmud records that when the great sages finished a particularly profound teaching, the study house would fall silent -- and that silence was itself a form of honor and comprehension. The student should practice silence as deliberately as he practices speech.

There is also the matter of the *meturgeman* -- the official translator and amplifier who stood beside the sage during public lectures. The sage would speak quietly, often in brief phrases or in Hebrew that the common people could not fully understand, and the meturgeman would translate, amplify, and project the sage's words to the larger audience. This division of roles is itself a principle of delivery: the sage preserves his dignity and precision, while the meturgeman provides the force and accessibility. The student who understands this system understands that delivery is not a single art but a collaborative one -- and that the wise speaker knows when to speak softly and let another carry his words to the multitude.

It is also taught that the sage should modulate his speech according to his audience. Before scholars, one may speak in compressed, technical language. Before the common people, one must speak plainly and vividly, using parables and stories to convey the same teachings that are stated abstractly in the academy. This adaptation of delivery to audience is one of the most important skills the student can learn, and it cannot be taught through rules alone -- it must be practiced, observed, and refined through experience.

And consider the difference between delivery in the academy and delivery in the synagogue. In the academy, the sage speaks to a small group of students who are seated close to him, who share his vocabulary and his frame of reference, and who can interrupt with questions. His delivery is conversational, precise, and interactive. In the synagogue, the sage addresses a large and diverse assembly that includes scholars and laborers, men and women, adults and children. His delivery must be projected, vivid, and uninterrupted -- he must hold the attention of the entire assembly through the force of his voice, the clarity of his illustrations, and the emotional arc of his discourse. The same sage may teach the same teaching in both settings, but the *delivery* will be entirely different -- and the student who cannot make this adaptation will fail in one setting or the other.

The sage Bar Kappara was known for his ability to combine halakhic instruction with entertaining delivery, weaving jests and wordplay into his teaching so that his students would remain attentive. Some sages criticized this practice, arguing that it diminished the dignity of Torah. Others defended it, noting that a drowsy audience learns nothing. The tension between dignity and accessibility in delivery is one that every public speaker must navigate, and the right balance depends on the audience, the occasion, and the gravity of the subject matter.[^35]

---

### I.D. The Student and the Master

The bet midrash is a school -- but not in the way the nations of the world understand a school. Among the Greeks, a student pays a teacher and receives instruction in exchange. In the bet midrash, the relationship between master and disciple is more than commercial; it is covenantal. The student attaches himself to a sage as an apprentice attaches himself to a craftsman -- not merely to learn facts but to absorb a way of being, a way of speaking, a way of thinking about Torah.

The ancient teaching says:

> <span dir="rtl">משה קיבל תורה מסיני, ומסרה ליהושע, ויהושע לזקנים, וזקנים לנביאים, ונביאים מסרוה לאנשי כנסת הגדולה</span>
>
> *Moses received Torah from Sinai and transmitted it to Joshua, and Joshua to the elders, and the elders to the prophets, and the prophets transmitted it to the men of the Great Assembly.*[^36]

This chain of transmission (*shalshelet ha-qabbalah*) is not merely a historical record; it is a rhetorical lineage. Each link in the chain received not only the content of Torah but the *manner* of its transmission -- the art of teaching, the techniques of argument, the style of discourse that characterized his particular master. When we say that Hillel received from Shemayah and Avtalyon, or that Rabbi Akiva received from Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua, we mean not only that he learned their teachings but that he apprenticed in their art of speech.

The men of the Great Assembly themselves bequeathed three principles that are, in essence, principles of rhetorical pedagogy:

> <span dir="rtl">הם אמרו שלושה דברים: הוו מתונים בדין, והעמידו תלמידים הרבה, ועשו סייג לתורה</span>
>
> *They said three things: Be deliberate in judgment, raise up many disciples, and make a fence around the Torah.*[^37]

"Be deliberate in judgment" -- this is an instruction about arrangement and delivery: do not rush to verdict, but consider all arguments carefully. "Raise up many disciples" -- this is an instruction about the obligation to teach: the art of speech must be transmitted, generation after generation. "Make a fence around the Torah" -- this is an instruction about invention: anticipate the dangers, construct protective rulings, build arguments that safeguard the core of the law.

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">עשה לך רב, וקנה לך חבר</span>
>
> *Make for yourself a teacher, and acquire for yourself a companion.*[^38]

And elsewhere:

> <span dir="rtl">הוי מתאבק בעפר רגליהם, והוי שותה בצמא את דבריהם</span>
>
> *Sit in the dust at their feet and drink their words with thirst.*

The imagery is physical and intimate: the student is so close to the master that he is covered in the dust raised by the master's footsteps. This is not a metaphor for casual instruction; it is a metaphor for total immersion. The student does not merely attend lectures; he *lives* with his teacher, absorbing not only the content of his teaching but the rhythm of his speech, the movement of his thought, the quality of his character.

The teacher provides the model; the companion provides the sparring partner. Both are necessary for rhetorical training. The student learns from watching and listening to the master (*shimush talmidei chakhamim*); he develops his own skill by debating and practicing with his peers. The ideal sage -- the *talmid chakham* -- is not merely learned; he is the master of speech in all its dimensions: ethical, persuasive, precise.

It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">גדולה שימושה של תורה יותר מלימודה</span>
>
> *Greater is the service of Torah [scholars] than its study.*[^39]

Why is service -- attending upon a sage, observing his habits, watching how he conducts himself -- greater than formal study? Because formal study teaches the *content* of Torah, but service teaches the *art* of Torah: how the sage applies his learning to real situations, how he speaks to different people in different circumstances, how he carries himself, how he reasons through difficulties. This is precisely the kind of learning that cannot be reduced to rules; it must be absorbed through observation and imitation. When the student sees his master handle a difficult questioner with patience, or resolve a complex dispute with a single well-chosen analogy, or comfort a mourner with a word that is neither too much nor too little -- in those moments, the student learns more about the art of speech than any manual could teach.

The companions (*haverim*) play a different but equally essential role. With a companion, the student can practice what he has observed in the master. The two companions debate, challenge each other, test arguments, and refine their reasoning through the friction of disagreement. As iron sharpens iron, so one companion sharpens another. The Talmud preserves many examples of study-partners whose debates produced insights that neither could have reached alone. The relationship between Rabbi Yohanan and Resh Lakish, between Abbaye and Rava, between Rav and Shmuel -- these great partnerships are not merely biographical facts but models of the rhetorical training that occurs between equals who challenge each other with full force and full respect.

The method by which students learned in the bet midrash was itself a system of rhetorical training. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד סדר המשנה? רב אומר פרק לפני התלמיד, וחוזר ושונה לו, עד שלמדה. ואחר כך שואלו ומפרשה לו. ר' שמואל בר נחמני אמר: תנא תני קמיה דרב: תנא שנה וחוזר ושונה, מתרגם ושואל ומפרש</span>
>
> *What was the order of study? The master would state a chapter before the student, and repeat and review it with him, until he learned it. Then he would question him and explain it to him.*

And regarding the degree of patient repetition required:

> <span dir="rtl">תנו רבנן: כיצד סדר משנה? ... חוזר ושונה ארבעה פעמים</span>
>
> *Our rabbis taught: What was the order of Mishnah? ... He would repeat and review four times.*[^40]

This fourfold repetition is not rote memorization alone; each repetition is an opportunity for the student to test his understanding, to ask questions, to hear the teacher's phrasing and intonation, to absorb the *style* as well as the *content*. The student who sits at the feet of a sage for years is undergoing the most intensive rhetorical training imaginable -- not through formal exercises in rhetoric, but through total immersion in the practice of skilled speech.

The Greeks, too, understood the importance of the relationship between master and pupil in rhetorical education. Their ideal orator was described as "a good man skilled in speaking."[^41] Our ideal is not so different -- only we add: a righteous man, a man of Torah, skilled in the speech of the bet midrash. For us, rhetoric without righteousness is an abomination, and righteousness without the ability to communicate it is a failure of obligation.

And yet the student should know that our tradition places a specific emphasis on *character* as a prerequisite for rhetorical training that goes beyond even the Greek insistence on the orator's moral qualities. The sage must not merely *be* good; he must *embody* Torah in every dimension of his life. His speech, his silence, his manner of walking, his manner of eating, his dealings in the marketplace -- all of these are forms of communication, and all of them must be consistent with his teaching. The sage who teaches humility but conducts himself arrogantly, the sage who preaches compassion but deals harshly with his students, the sage who advocates truth but tolerates falsehood in his own affairs -- such a sage is not merely a hypocrite; he is a failed rhetorician, for his actions contradict his words, and the audience always trusts actions over words. The Talmud reports that certain sages were criticized not for the content of their teachings but for the inconsistency between their teachings and their conduct:

> <span dir="rtl">נאה דורש ואין נאה מקיים</span>
>
> *He expounds beautifully but does not practice beautifully.*

This is the severest criticism the bet midrash can offer: that the sage's rhetoric is flawless but his life fails to confirm it. The student must therefore understand that the ultimate exercise in rhetoric is not the composition of a perfect speech but the living of a life that makes every speech credible.

The Mishnah enumerates the qualities that distinguish a wise student from a foolish one, and these qualities are, at bottom, qualities of rhetorical competence:

> <span dir="rtl">שבעה דברים בגולם ושבעה בחכם. חכם אינו מדבר בפני מי שהוא גדול ממנו בחכמה ובמנין, ואינו נכנס לתוך דברי חברו, ואינו נבהל להשיב, שואל כענין ומשיב כהלכה, ואומר על ראשון ראשון ועל אחרון אחרון, ועל מה שלא שמע אומר: לא שמעתי, ומודה על האמת</span>
>
> *Seven qualities characterize a boor and seven a sage. A sage does not speak before one who is greater in wisdom and in years; he does not interrupt his fellow's words; he is not hasty to respond; he asks according to the subject and answers according to the law; he speaks on the first point first and the last point last; concerning what he has not heard he says "I have not heard"; and he acknowledges the truth.*[^42]

Mark each quality carefully: not speaking out of turn is a rule of delivery. Not interrupting is a rule of arrangement -- respect for the order of discourse. Not being hasty to respond is a rule of invention -- taking time to find the right argument. Asking according to the subject is a rule of relevance. Answering according to the law is a rule of accuracy. Speaking on the first point first and the last point last is a rule of arrangement. Admitting ignorance is a rule of intellectual honesty. And acknowledging the truth when one's opponent is right -- this is the supreme rhetorical virtue: the willingness to be persuaded by a superior argument.

And as the sage taught on his deathbed:

> <span dir="rtl">כשחלה רבי אליעזר, נכנסו תלמידיו לבקרו. אמרו לו: רבנו, למדנו אורחות חיים. אמר להם: הזהרו בכבוד חבריכם, ומנעו בניכם מן ההגיון, והושיבום בין ברכי תלמידי חכמים. וכשאתם מתפללים, דעו לפני מי אתם עומדים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Eliezer fell ill, his students entered to visit him. They said: Our master, teach us the paths of life. He said to them: Be careful of your colleagues' honor, keep your children from superficial reasoning, seat them between the knees of Torah scholars, and when you pray, know before Whom you stand.*

"Seat them between the knees of Torah scholars" -- this is the essential image of rabbinic education. The child sits literally at the feet of the master, absorbing Torah through proximity, through listening, through the physical intimacy of the educational relationship. And "know before Whom you stand" -- this is the ultimate lesson of rhetorical training: awareness of audience. Whether standing before a human court or before the heavenly court, the speaker must know his audience and adapt his speech accordingly.

The student should also know that the bet midrash recognized different levels of rhetorical proficiency among its students. Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai praised his five chief disciples, each for a different quality:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי אליעזר בן הורקנוס - בור סוד שאינו מאבד טיפה. רבי יהושע בן חנניה - אשרי יולדתו. רבי יוסי הכהן - חסיד. רבי שמעון בן נתנאל - ירא חטא. רבי אלעזר בן ערך - מעיין המתגבר</span>
>
> *Rabbi Eliezer ben Hyrcanus -- a plastered cistern that does not lose a drop. Rabbi Yehoshua ben Hananiah -- happy is she who bore him. Rabbi Yose the Priest -- pious. Rabbi Shimon ben Netanel -- fears sin. Rabbi Elazar ben Arakh -- an ever-flowing spring.*

The cistern that does not lose a drop is the master of memory. The ever-flowing spring is the master of invention. Both are essential; both are praised. And when asked which of the five he valued most, Rabban Yohanan replied: "I prefer the words of Elazar ben Arakh, for his words include all of yours" -- choosing invention over retention, the creative capacity over the archival one. Yet elsewhere, the sages say that Rabbi Eliezer ben Hyrcanus was the greater in practical terms, "for if all the sages of Israel were on one side of the scale and Eliezer ben Hyrcanus on the other, he would outweigh them all." The tension between these two evaluations -- the innovator and the conservator -- is itself a lesson in the complexity of rhetorical excellence.

---

## PART II: THE PRELIMINARY EXERCISES (PROGYMNASMATA)

The student who has grasped the foundations is ready to begin the exercises themselves. These are the preliminary exercises (*hargilot qodmot*) by which the apprentice develops his skill. Just as the craftsman's apprentice begins with simple tasks -- shaping a handle, fitting a joint -- before he is trusted with the complete work, so the student of discourse begins with simple forms before he attempts the full oration.

The nations of the world number these exercises at twelve or fourteen, and they arrange them in a fixed sequence from the simplest to the most complex. We need not follow their sequence exactly, for our traditions have their own order and their own logic. But the student should know that the principle of graduated difficulty is sound: one begins with what is small and builds toward what is large. As our sages teach:

> <span dir="rtl">אין אדם למד תורה אלא ממקום שלבו חפץ</span>
>
> *A person does not learn Torah except from the place where his heart desires.*

And yet the teacher must guide that desire toward the proper sequence. You would not hand a child a tractate of Kodashim before he has mastered Berakhot. Neither would you ask a student to compose a full forensic argument before he has mastered the fable and the narrative.

We begin with three: the fable (*mashal*), the narrative (*ma'aseh*), and the chreia (*ma'amar chakham*). These are the building blocks of all higher discourse, and the student who masters them will find the rest comes more easily. These three are ordered from the simplest to the most complex: the fable requires only imagination and analogy; the narrative requires observation, memory, and the ability to structure a report; the chreia requires all of these plus the ability to compress meaning into a single, unforgettable point.[^43]

A word about the method of training. Each exercise should be practiced in three stages. First, *recognition*: the student learns to identify the form when he encounters it in the texts he studies. When he hears a mashal, he should say to himself, "This is a mashal; its nimshal is such-and-such." When he encounters a ma'aseh, he should identify its six elements. When he reads a chreia, he should classify its type and form of expression. Second, *analysis*: the student learns to evaluate the form critically. Is this mashal effective? Does the correspondence between mashal and nimshal hold at every point? Is this narrative vivid? Does it omit any essential element? Is this chreia truly pointed, or is it merely a platitude dressed in a sage's name? Third, *composition*: the student learns to produce the form himself. He composes his own meshalim, constructs his own narratives, formulates his own chreiai. Only at the third stage does the student move from passive knowledge to active skill.

The teacher should assign exercises at all three stages, beginning with recognition and proceeding to composition only when the student has demonstrated mastery of analysis. A student who composes before he can analyze will produce crude work; a student who analyzes before he can recognize will miss the forms that surround him in the texts he studies every day.

---

### II.A. Fable (Mashal)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, the *mashal* -- the parable, the fable, the illustrative tale -- is the first and most accessible of the exercises. Even a child can understand a mashal, and even the greatest sage relies upon it. King Solomon himself, the wisest of all men, is described as a master of *meshalim*:

> <span dir="rtl">וידבר שלושת אלפים משל</span>
>
> *And he spoke three thousand parables.*[^44]

What is a mashal? It is a brief tale -- whether of animals, of kings, of householders, or of ordinary people -- that is told not for its own sake but for the sake of the teaching it illuminates. The mashal makes the abstract concrete. It takes a principle that the mind struggles to grasp and clothes it in a story that the ear receives with pleasure and the memory retains with ease.

The sages said:

> <span dir="rtl">אל יהי משל קל בעיניך, שעל ידי המשל אדם עומד על דברי תורה. משל למלך שאבדה לו מרגלית... על ידי פתילה בפרוטה מצאה. כך אל יהי משל קל בעיניך, שעל ידי המשל אדם עומד על דברי תורה</span>
>
> *Let not the mashal be trivial in your eyes, for through the mashal a person comes to understand words of Torah. A parable: A king lost a pearl... By means of a wick worth a penny, he found it. So let not the mashal be trivial in your eyes, for through the mashal a person comes to understand words of Torah.*[^45]

Note the elegance: the sage uses a mashal to teach the value of the mashal itself. The pearl is the deep truth of Torah; the wick is the humble parable that illuminates it. Without the cheap wick, the precious pearl remains lost in the dark.[^46]

#### Types of Meshalim

The student should know the main types of meshalim employed by the sages:

**The Animal Fable.** This is the oldest type, with roots in Scripture itself. Animals speak and act as humans, and their behavior illustrates a moral lesson. The greatest master of animal fables among the sages was Rabbi Meir:

> <span dir="rtl">משמת רבי מאיר, בטלו מושלי משלים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased.*

And it is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">שלוש מאות משלות שועלים היו לו לרבי מאיר</span>
>
> *Rabbi Meir had three hundred fox fables.*[^47]

Three hundred! And we possess only a handful of them. What has been lost is incalculable. But from what remains, we can see the art: the fox is cunning, the lion is powerful, the wolf is rapacious -- each animal embodies a human quality, and the interaction among them teaches a lesson about the human world.

**The King Parable (Mashal le-Melekh).** This is the most characteristic type of rabbinic mashal. The king in these parables typically represents God, the Holy One, blessed be He; the prince or the servants represent Israel or the individual soul; the palace represents the world or the Temple; the banquet represents the world to come. The king parable is the rabbinic preacher's primary tool for making theology vivid and accessible.

**The Householder Parable.** Related to the king parable but set in the domestic sphere. A householder hires workers, plants a vineyard, builds a house, marries off a child. These parables draw on everyday life to teach everyday ethics. The householder mashal is particularly effective when the teaching concerns the relationship between effort and reward, or between expectation and outcome. Consider:

> <span dir="rtl">משל למלך בשר ודם ששכר פועלים הרבה, ופועל אחד היה ממולח בלאכתו יותר מדאי. מה עשה? נטלו והיה מטייל עמו שורות ארוכות וקצרות</span>
>
> *A parable: A king hired many workers, and one worker was exceptionally skilled at his craft. What did the king do? He took him and walked with him along the long and short rows.*

The nimshal: God "walks" with the righteous -- granting them extra time and attention -- because they are skilled in the craft of Torah. The householder setting makes the analogy immediately accessible: every listener has seen a master craftsman receive preferential treatment from an employer.

**The Two-Path Parable.** A traveler comes to a fork in the road: one path is smooth at the beginning but thorny at the end; the other is thorny at the beginning but smooth at the end. This type of mashal, found in Sifrei Numbers, teaches the principle that present difficulty leads to future reward, while present ease leads to future suffering. It is one of the most commonly deployed parables in the aggadic midrashim, and the student should know it well.

**The Banquet Parable.** A king invites guests to a banquet but does not specify the time. The wise guests dress themselves in their finest garments and wait by the palace gate. The foolish guests go about their daily business, saying, "A banquet requires preparation -- there will be signs when it is near." Suddenly the king summons all guests. The wise enter in their finery and are seated; the foolish come in their work clothes and are turned away:

> <span dir="rtl">משל למלך שזימן לסעודה ולא קבע זמן</span>
>
> *A parable: A king who invited to a banquet but did not set a time.*

The nimshal: death comes without warning, and one must always be prepared. This type of mashal -- the banquet or feast parable -- is deployed whenever the teaching concerns preparedness, the uncertainty of the future, or the relationship between this world and the world to come. The image of the banquet is so potent because it combines pleasure with exclusion: those who are prepared enjoy the feast, while those who are not are shut out.
