The student should recognize this type when he encounters it, for it appears in many forms throughout the aggadic midrashim and is also found in the parables attributed to Jesus of Nazareth, suggesting a common stock of parabolic imagery shared across the traditions of first-century Palestine.

#### Examples

**Example 1: Jotham's Fable of the Trees** (Judges 9:8-15). This is the oldest fable in our tradition, and it serves as scriptural warrant for the entire genre:

> <span dir="rtl">הָלוֹךְ הָלְכוּ הָעֵצִים לִמְשֹׁחַ עֲלֵיהֶם מֶלֶךְ וַיֹּאמְרוּ לַזַּיִת מָלְכָה עָלֵינוּ. וַיֹּאמֶר לָהֶם הַזַּיִת הֶחֳדַלְתִּי אֶת דִּשְׁנִי... וַיֹּאמְרוּ הָעֵצִים לָאָטָד לְכָה אַתָּה מְלָךְ עָלֵינוּ</span>
>
> *The trees went forth to anoint a king over themselves. They said to the olive tree: Reign over us. The olive tree said to them: Shall I leave my richness?... The trees said to the thornbush: Come, you reign over us.*[^48]

Jotham's point: the worthy refuse power; only the worthless thornbush accepts it. The student should observe how the fable makes a political argument that would be dangerous to state directly -- Jotham is criticizing the kingship of Abimelech -- by clothing it in the innocent form of a tale about trees. This is the fable's essential function: to say what cannot safely be said outright.

**Example 2: A Fox Fable of Rabbi Meir.** Among the few preserved fox fables is the parable of the fox and the fishes, attributed to Rabbi Akiva but characteristic of the genre that Rabbi Meir perfected:

> <span dir="rtl">משל לשועל שהיה מהלך על גב הנהר, וראה דגים שהיו מתקבצים ממקום למקום. אמר להם: מפני מה אתם בורחים? אמרו לו: מפני רשתות שמביאין עלינו בני אדם. אמר להם: רצונכם שתעלו ליבשה ונדור אני ואתם כשם שדרו אבותי עם אבותיכם? אמרו לו: אתה הוא שאומרים עליך פיקח שבחיות? לא פיקח אתה אלא טיפש אתה! ומה במקום חיותנו אנו מתיירין, במקום מיתתנו על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *A parable: A fox was walking along the riverbank and saw fish swimming from place to place. He said to them: From what are you fleeing? They said: From the nets that people cast upon us. He said: Would you like to come up onto dry land, and you and I will dwell together as my ancestors dwelt with your ancestors? They said: Are you the one they call the cleverest of animals? You are not clever but foolish! If we are afraid in the place of our life [water], how much more so in the place of our death [dry land]!*[^49]

Rabbi Akiva applied this mashal to those who would abandon Torah study under Roman persecution: Torah is our water; to leave it is to leave our very element of life. The fable makes the argument with a vividness and emotional force that no abstract proposition could match.

**Example 3: A King Parable from the Mekhilta.** The tannaitic midrashim are rich with king parables. Consider this one from the Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael:

> <span dir="rtl">משל למלך בשר ודם שנכנס למדינה. אמרו לו עבדיו: גזור עליהם גזירות. אמר להם: לא. כשיקבלו את מלכותי אגזור עליהם גזירות; שאם לא יקבלו את מלכותי, היאך יקבלו את גזירותי?</span>
>
> *A parable: A king of flesh and blood entered a province. His servants said: Issue decrees upon them. He said: No. When they have accepted my sovereignty, I will issue decrees upon them. For if they do not accept my sovereignty, how will they accept my decrees?*[^50]

The *nimshal* (application): God first declared "I am the Lord your God" (the acceptance of sovereignty) before commanding "You shall have no other gods" (the decrees). The sequence of the Decalogue is itself an argument about political and theological legitimacy, and the king parable makes this argument concrete.

The king parable is so pervasive in rabbinic literature that the student must learn its conventions thoroughly. The phrase *mashal le-melekh basar va-dam* ("a parable of a king of flesh and blood") is one of the most common formulae in the midrashim. The "king of flesh and blood" always represents God -- but the qualifier "of flesh and blood" signals that the analogy is imperfect, that no human king can fully represent the divine. This qualifier is itself a rhetorical safeguard: it tells the listener, "I know this analogy has limits; I am using it to illuminate, not to equate."

The student should pay careful attention to the *opening formula* of each mashal, for the formula itself carries rhetorical information. "A mashal: to what is the matter comparable?" (*mashal: le-mah ha-davar domeh?*) -- this formula signals that the preacher is about to make an abstract teaching concrete. It invites the listener to shift from the mode of abstract reasoning to the mode of imaginative engagement. The question "to what is it comparable?" is an act of intellectual hospitality: it tells the listener, "I am going to meet you where you are, in the world of concrete experience, and from there I will lead you to the teaching." Other formulae -- "They told a mashal" (*mashlu mashal*), "Another mashal" (*mashal aher*), or simply "A mashal" (*mashal*) -- each has its own rhetorical register and its own implications for the relationship between preacher and audience.

Within king parables, certain stock figures recur: the prince (Israel, or the soul), the servants (the angels, or the nations), the queen (the Torah, or the Shekhinah, or the community of Israel), the banquet (the world to come, or the Sabbath), the rebellion (sin, or the worship of other gods). The student who masters these conventions can both interpret existing king parables and compose new ones. The stock figures are not limitations but tools: they provide a shared vocabulary that the preacher and his audience both understand.

**Example 4: The Twin Brothers Parable from Sifrei.** From Sifrei Deuteronomy:

> <span dir="rtl">משל לאחד שנכנס למדינה ואמרו לו: הזהר מן הדיין פלוני... מפני מה? מפני שהוא מכה ומלקה ואינו נושא פנים. כך אמר הקדוש ברוך הוא לישראל: בני, הזהרו מן המצוות הללו שכתבתי לכם</span>
>
> *A parable: One who entered a province was told: Beware of such-and-such a judge... Why? Because he strikes and flogs and shows no favoritism. So the Holy One, blessed be He, said to Israel: My children, be careful with these commandments that I have written for you.*[^51]

The mashal transforms the abstract principle of divine justice into a concrete scenario of a newcomer navigating a legal system -- a situation every listener could understand.

#### The Relationship Between Mashal and Nimshal

The student must understand the essential structure of every mashal: it consists of two parts, the *mashal* (the parable itself) and the *nimshal* (the application). These two must correspond point for point. Every element in the story should have a counterpart in the teaching it illustrates. A mashal in which the story wanders beyond its application is poorly constructed; a mashal in which the application does not account for every element of the story is incomplete.

The sages expressed this relationship with characteristic precision:

> <span dir="rtl">למה נמשלו דברי תורה למים? מה מים מניחין מקום גבוה ויורדין למקום נמוך, אף דברי תורה אין מתקיימין אלא במי שדעתו שפלה</span>
>
> *Why are words of Torah compared to water? Just as water leaves the high place and flows to the low place, so words of Torah endure only in one whose mind is humble.*

Here the mashal (water flowing downhill) and the nimshal (Torah resting with the humble) correspond exactly. The single point of comparison -- the movement from high to low -- carries the entire teaching. No element is wasted; no element is unexplained. This is the model the student should emulate.

But the student should also know that the greatest meshalim are those in which the mashal generates meaning beyond the explicit nimshal -- where the story is so rich that it continues to teach new lessons each time it is revisited. The mashal of the fox and the fish, for example, teaches not only that Torah is our element of life but also that apparent safety (dry land) can be fatal, that enemies may disguise themselves as friends, and that the wisdom of the masses (the fish recognize the trap) may exceed the cleverness of the powerful (the fox). A great mashal, like a great verse of Torah, contains more than its surface meaning.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice composing meshalim. Take a teaching -- for example, "Do not look at the vessel but at what is in it" (Avot 4:20) -- and compose a mashal to illuminate it. What kind of vessel? What is inside? Who is deceived by appearances? The student should draft the mashal, present it to his teacher, and revise it until the correspondence between mashal and nimshal is exact and the story is vivid enough to remember.

The teacher should also assign the reverse exercise: given a mashal, what is its nimshal? Given a story, what is its application? This trains the student's mind to move between the concrete and the abstract, between image and concept -- which is the fundamental movement of rabbinic thought.

A further exercise: take a mashal from the tradition and *critique* it. Where does the analogy break down? Every mashal has a *maqom she-nishbar* -- a breaking point where the parable and the teaching diverge. In the king parable, for example, the king is "of flesh and blood" -- finite, mortal, imperfect -- while God is infinite and perfect. The qualifier "flesh and blood" itself marks the breaking point. The student who can identify where the mashal fails is the student who truly understands both the mashal and the teaching it serves. For the sage who cannot see the limits of his own analogy will draw false conclusions from it, extending the parable beyond its intended range. The mashal is a lamp, not a sun: it illuminates a particular area, but it also casts shadows. The wise student knows both the light and the shadows.

A more advanced exercise: take a single teaching and compose three different meshalim to illustrate it -- one using animals, one using a king, and one using a householder. Which mashal is most effective? Why? The answer will teach the student that the choice of imagery must match the audience and the occasion. A king parable is appropriate for a discourse on divine sovereignty; an animal fable for a discourse on cunning and folly; a householder parable for a discourse on everyday ethics. The master of meshalim knows which type to deploy for maximum effect.[^52]

---

### II.B. Narrative (Ma'aseh / Sippur)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, the second exercise is the narrative -- the *ma'aseh*. If the mashal is a tale told for the sake of a teaching, the ma'aseh is a report of things that happened -- or that might plausibly have happened -- for the sake of a precedent. Where the mashal is fictional, the ma'aseh claims to be factual. Where the mashal teaches a principle, the ma'aseh establishes a practice.

A narrative, in our usage, is a report of something done or said by a named person in a specific circumstance. It may be brief -- "a ma'aseh concerning Rabbi so-and-so" -- or it may be extended into a full story with dialogue, complications, and resolution. But at its core, it is always a report of what happened, and it always carries legal or ethical authority.[^53]

The standard formula for introducing a narrative in tannaitic literature is:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ב...</span>
>
> *A ma'aseh concerning...*

Or:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה שהיה...</span>
>
> *A ma'aseh that occurred...*

When the student hears this formula, he should pay attention: a precedent is about to be established. The ma'aseh is not told for entertainment; it is told as evidence.

Indeed, the ma'aseh has a unique evidentiary status in rabbinic law. When a sage cites a halakhic ruling in the abstract, his opponents may challenge it with counter-arguments: "But perhaps the law is otherwise in a different case." But when a sage cites a ma'aseh -- "Rabbi so-and-so was confronted with such a case and ruled thus" -- the narrative carries an additional force: it demonstrates not merely what the law *should* be but what a competent authority *decided* it to be in practice. Theory can be debated endlessly; practice, once established, has the weight of accomplished fact. This is why the Talmud often resolves a dispute by citing a ma'aseh: the narrative of what was actually done settles what the abstract argument could not.

The student should therefore understand: to master the art of narrative in the bet midrash is not merely to tell good stories. It is to command the most powerful form of legal evidence available to the advocate. The sage who can say, "I have a ma'aseh" (*yesh li ma'aseh*) possesses an argument that no amount of abstract reasoning can easily overcome.[^54]

#### Types of Narratives

The student should learn to distinguish three types of narrative employed in the bet midrash:

**Historical Narrative (Ma'aseh she-hayah).** This is a report of events that actually occurred, told for their own importance or as context for understanding a sage's teaching. The destruction of the Temple, the persecutions under Hadrian, the establishment of the academy at Yavneh -- these are historical narratives that shape the framework within which all rabbinic discourse takes place.

**Legal Precedent Narrative (Ma'aseh le-Halakhah).** This is the most important type for the student of law. A ma'aseh is cited to establish that a particular sage ruled in a particular way on a particular occasion, and this ruling becomes precedent for future cases. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">אין למדין מן ההלכות, ואין למדין מן המעשה, אלא אם כן אמרו לו: הלכה למעשה</span>

In legal discourse, the ma'aseh functions as what the Greeks would call a *paradeigma* -- an example that carries probative force.

**Biographical Narrative of Sages (Ma'aseh be-Rabbi...).** This type recounts episodes from the lives of the sages -- their habits, their character traits, their responses to challenges -- and thereby holds them up as models for emulation. These narratives serve the epideictic function: they praise the sage's virtues and implicitly instruct the student in the qualities he should cultivate.

The student should be aware that these three types are not always sharply distinguished. A single narrative may serve multiple functions: the story of Hillel and the converts (Shabbat 31a) is at once a biographical narrative (it reveals Hillel's character), a legal precedent narrative (it establishes principles for conversion), and a historical narrative (it preserves a tradition about the tensions between the schools of Hillel and Shammai). The best narratives operate on multiple levels simultaneously, and the student should learn to recognize each level.

There is also a fourth type, less commonly discussed but very important: the **etiological narrative** (*ma'aseh le-ta'am*), which explains the origin or reason for a practice, an institution, or a custom. When the Mishnah tells us that certain prayers or blessings were instituted "because of" a particular event, it is providing an etiological narrative. These narratives have a special rhetorical force: they make current practice feel inevitable and deeply rooted, because they connect it to a foundational moment.

Consider, for example, the narrative that explains why the eighteen benedictions of the Amidah are arranged in their particular sequence. The Talmud asks:

> <span dir="rtl">מנין שאומרים גבורות גשמים בתחיית המתים?</span>
>
> *From where do we derive that we mention the power of rain in the blessing of the resurrection of the dead?*

The answer is an etiological narrative linking the current liturgical practice to a theological principle: God's power to bring rain and God's power to resurrect the dead are comparable acts of vivification, and the liturgical juxtaposition teaches the worshiper to see in every rainfall an echo of the ultimate resurrection. The practice (mentioning rain in the second blessing) is given a cosmic rationale through narrative.

Similarly, the narrative of the institution of the Qaddish, the narrative of the origins of specific fast days, and the accounts of how particular blessings came to be formulated -- all of these are etiological narratives that serve a dual function: they explain current practice and they invest it with the authority of a foundational moment. The student who masters the etiological narrative can make any practice seem ancient, necessary, and divinely ordained -- which is why this type of narrative is particularly important for the preacher and the liturgist.

#### Examples

**Example 1: Hillel and the Converts (Shabbat 31a).** This famous narrative cluster establishes Hillel's character as the model of patience and pedagogical skill:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה בנכרי אחד שבא לפני שמאי ואמר לו: גיירני על מנת שתלמדני כל התורה כולה כשאני עומד על רגל אחת. דחפו באמת הבניין שבידו. בא לפני הלל. גייריה. אמר לו: דעלך סני לחברך לא תעביד - זו היא כל התורה כולה, ואידך פירושה הוא, זיל גמור</span>
>
> *A ma'aseh: A certain gentile came before Shammai and said to him: Convert me on the condition that you teach me the entire Torah while I stand on one foot. He drove him away with the builder's cubit in his hand. He came before Hillel. He converted him. He said to him: That which is hateful to you, do not do to your neighbor -- this is the entire Torah; the rest is commentary. Go and learn.*[^55]

The student should observe the narrative structure: two parallel scenes (Shammai then Hillel), contrasting responses (rejection then acceptance), and a climactic saying that resolves the tension. This is not merely a report; it is a carefully shaped story whose structure itself carries meaning.

**Example 2: Rabban Gamaliel and the Elders on a Ship.** This narrative appears in multiple versions across the tannaitic corpus:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבן גמליאל ורבי אלעזר בן עזריה ורבי יהושע ורבי עקיבא שהיו באים בספינה</span>
>
> *A ma'aseh: Rabban Gamaliel, Rabbi Elazar ben Azariah, Rabbi Yehoshua, and Rabbi Akiva were traveling on a ship.*[^56]

What follows varies by version: in one, they discuss halakhic matters prompted by the circumstances of travel; in another, the ship is caught in a storm; in another, they sight the city of Rome and weep while Rabbi Akiva laughs. In the version where they see Rome, the narrative achieves particular power:

> <span dir="rtl">שוב פעם אחת היו עולין לירושלים, כיון שהגיעו להר הצופים קרעו בגדיהם. כיון שהגיעו להר הבית, ראו שועל שיצא מבית קדשי הקדשים. התחילו הן בוכין ורבי עקיבא משחק... אמר להם: מקום שנאמר בו "והזר הקרב יומת" - ועכשיו שועלים מהלכין בו? לכך אני שוחק... עכשיו שנתקיימה נבואת אוריה, בידוע שנבואת זכריה עתידה להתקיים</span>
>
> *Once again they were ascending to Jerusalem. When they reached Mount Scopus, they tore their garments. When they reached the Temple Mount, they saw a fox emerging from the Holy of Holies. They began to weep, but Rabbi Akiva laughed... He said to them: The place of which it was said "Any stranger who approaches shall die" -- and now foxes walk about in it? That is precisely why I laugh... Now that the prophecy of Uriah [of destruction] has been fulfilled, it is certain that the prophecy of Zechariah [of rebuilding] will be fulfilled.*

The multiplication of versions itself teaches the student an important principle: narrative is not fixed. The same core event can be retold with different emphases for different pedagogical purposes. The student who compares versions learns to see how arrangement and selection shape meaning. In one version, the lesson is halakhic; in another, eschatological; in a third, a demonstration of a sage's unique character. The narrative framework is the same -- sages journeying together -- but the content that fills it varies with the purpose of the retelling.

**Example 3: A Ma'aseh from Tosefta Establishing Legal Precedent.** The Tosefta frequently introduces legal precedents with the ma'aseh formula:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבן גמליאל שהיה יושב על גג ביתו בירושלים ובא טבי עבדו ואמר לו: הרי עבדך טבי לא רצה לישן בסוכה. אמר: שומעים אתם מה טבי עבדי אומר? יודע טבי עבדי שעבדים פטורים מן הסוכה</span>
>
> *A ma'aseh: Rabban Gamaliel was sitting on the roof of his house in Jerusalem, and his slave Tavi came and said to him: Your slave Tavi does not wish to sleep in the sukkah. He said: Do you hear what my slave Tavi says? My slave Tavi knows that slaves are exempt from the sukkah.*[^57]

This ma'aseh establishes a legal ruling (slaves are exempt from the obligation of sukkah) through the medium of narrative. The precedent is more memorable than a bare rule precisely because it is embedded in a story with characters, dialogue, and a specific setting.

**Example 4: The Beginnings of Rabbi Akiva (Avot de-Rabbi Natan).** The biographical narrative of how Rabbi Akiva came to study Torah at the age of forty is one of the most beloved stories in rabbinic literature:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבי עקיבא שהיה רועה צאן של בן כלבא שבוע. ראתה רחל בתו שהוא צנוע ומעולה. אמרה לו: אם אקדש לך, תלך לבית המדרש? אמר לה: הן. נתקדשה לו בצנעא. שמע אביה והוציאה מביתו</span>
>
> *A ma'aseh: Rabbi Akiva was a shepherd for Ben Kalba Savua. His daughter Rachel saw that he was modest and excellent. She said to him: If I become betrothed to you, will you go to the study house? He said: Yes. She was betrothed to him secretly. Her father heard and expelled her from his house.*[^58]

The narrative continues through years of poverty and study, culminating in Akiva's return as a great sage with thousands of students, at which point his father-in-law seeks reconciliation. This is biographical narrative at its most powerful: it serves as both precedent (it is never too late to begin Torah study) and as praise of the sage (Akiva's devotion and Rachel's self-sacrifice are exemplary).

#### Virtues of Narrative

The student should learn the four virtues that make a narrative effective: **clarity** (*be'ur*), **brevity** (*qitzur*), **plausibility** (*efsharut*), and **vividness** (*hiyyut*).

Clarity means that the listener can follow the events without confusion. The ma'aseh formula -- "A ma'aseh concerning Rabbi X, who was in place Y, when Z happened" -- provides clarity by anchoring the narrative immediately in its who, where, and when. A narrative that leaves the listener wondering "who is speaking?" or "where are we?" has failed in clarity.

Brevity means that no element is wasted. The tannaitic narrative is characteristically terse: it gives only the essential facts and trusts the listener to supply the rest. Compare the narrative of Hillel and the convert: "He drove him away with the builder's cubit in his hand." In one sentence, we see Shammai's character, his anger, the physical setting of a construction site, and the violence of the rejection. Nothing is explained; everything is shown.

Plausibility means that the narrative is believable -- that even if it involves miraculous elements (as some rabbinic narratives do), the human motivations and reactions are true to life. When the convert asks to learn the entire Torah on one foot, we recognize the audacity of the request; when Shammai reacts with anger and Hillel with patience, we recognize two authentic human responses to an outrageous demand.

Vividness means that the narrative engages the listener's imagination -- that it creates pictures in the mind. The best rabbinic narratives achieve this through concrete detail: Shammai's builder's cubit, Hillel's snow-covered body on the skylight, Rabbi Akiva's flesh combed with iron combs. These details do not merely report; they imprint themselves on the memory.

The student should practice each virtue separately. Take a narrative you know and retell it with maximum clarity: can a listener who has never heard the story follow every event? Now retell it with maximum brevity: what is the shortest version that still makes the point? Now retell it with attention to plausibility: does every character behave in a way that rings true? And finally, retell it with maximum vividness: what concrete details can you add or emphasize to make the scene come alive? Through these exercises, the student develops control over each of the four narrative virtues and learns to deploy them as the occasion demands.

The student should also observe how the sages achieved vividness through *dialogue*. The most memorable rabbinic narratives do not merely describe actions; they record speech. Hillel does not merely convert the gentile; he speaks to him. Shammai does not merely reject him; he drives him away with a specific implement in his hand. Rabbi Akiva does not merely die; he speaks to his students while dying. The inclusion of direct speech transforms a report into a scene, and a scene is always more vivid than a summary.

#### The Six Elements of Narrative

The student should know that every well-constructed narrative contains six elements: **person** (who?), **action** (what was done?), **place** (where?), **time** (when?), **manner** (how?), and **cause** (why?).[^59]

Consider the narrative of Rabbi Akiva's martyrdom:

- **Person**: Rabbi Akiva
- **Action**: He recited the Shema while being tortured
- **Place**: The Roman execution ground
- **Time**: The hour of reciting the Shema
- **Manner**: They were combing his flesh with iron combs; he was prolonging the word *Ehad* ("One")
- **Cause**: To fulfill the commandment of loving God "with all your soul" -- even if He takes your soul

> <span dir="rtl">בשעה שהוציאו את רבי עקיבא להריגה, זמן קריאת שמע היה, והיו סורקין את בשרו במסרקות של ברזל, והיה מקבל עליו עול מלכות שמים</span>
>
> *At the time when they took Rabbi Akiva out to be executed, it was the time for reciting the Shema, and they were combing his flesh with iron combs, and he was accepting upon himself the yoke of the kingdom of Heaven.*[^60]

When all six elements are present, the narrative achieves its full power. When one or more is missing, the student should note the omission and ask why: sometimes an element is omitted because it is obvious from context; sometimes the omission itself is meaningful.

#### The Exercise

The student should compose a narrative of a sage's ruling, including all six elements. Choose a halakhic decision you have learned, and construct a ma'aseh around it: who was the sage? where was he? when did the case arise? what were the circumstances? how did he rule? why did he rule that way? Present the narrative to your teacher for critique. The goal is not merely to report the ruling but to make it memorable and authoritative through the art of narrative.

A more advanced exercise: take a single narrative and retell it in three ways. First, tell it briefly -- in two or three sentences, capturing only the essential point. Second, tell it at moderate length, including the key details and dialogue. Third, tell it at full length, with all the circumstantial detail, dialogue, emotional coloring, and theological reflection you can muster. Compare your three versions. Which is most effective? The answer depends on the purpose: a brief version for citing precedent in legal argument, a moderate version for teaching in the bet midrash, a full version for the public derashah.

The teacher should also assign the exercise of *comparing* narratives: take two versions of the same story (for example, the two versions of Hillel in the snow found in ARN A and ARN B, or the account of Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai's escape from Jerusalem as told in ARN and in the Babylonian Talmud) and note the differences. What details does each version include and omit? How does the characterization differ? What theological or pedagogical emphasis does each version serve? Through this exercise, the student learns that narrative is never neutral -- every retelling is an interpretation, and every interpretation is a form of argument.

There is yet another exercise the student should practice: *perspective-shifting*. Take a well-known narrative and retell it from the point of view of a different character. The narrative of Hillel and the convert, for example, is typically told from the perspective of the narrator -- an omniscient observer who follows the convert from Shammai to Hillel. But what if the student retells it from the convert's perspective? The experience of being driven away by Shammai's cubit becomes more vivid; the relief of Hillel's reception becomes more poignant; the convert's internal transformation from curiosity to commitment becomes the center of the story. Or retell it from Shammai's perspective: what moved him to anger? Was it merely impatience, or was it a principled conviction that Torah cannot be trivialized? The student who practices this exercise learns to see every narrative from multiple angles, and thereby learns a crucial skill for both judicial advocacy (where one must understand both parties' perspectives) and for homiletical preaching (where one must engage an audience that may identify with different characters in the story).

This exercise has deep roots in our tradition. The midrashic practice of composing speeches for biblical characters -- what did Abraham say when he raised the knife? what did Isaac think on the altar? what did Sarah feel when she heard the news? -- is precisely this exercise of perspective-shifting applied to Scripture. The student who practices it with rabbinic narratives is doing what the midrashists did with the Torah: entering the story from an unexpected angle and discovering meanings that the surface reading conceals.[^61]

---

### II.C. Chreia (Ma'amar Chakham)

#### Definition

My son, of all the exercises, this one stands at the center. The chreia -- we may call it *ma'amar chakham* (a sage's saying) or *divrei chakhamim* (words of the wise) -- is a concise and pointed saying or action attributed to a specific, named sage. It is brief. It is memorable. It is attributed. And it makes its point with sharpness.[^62]

What distinguishes the chreia from the mashal? The mashal is a fictional tale; the chreia is a real saying of a real person. What distinguishes it from the ma'aseh? The ma'aseh can be long and circumstantial; the chreia is always concise -- a single utterance, a single act, captured in a sentence or two.

The student should know: all of Pirkei Avot is a collection of chreiai. When the Mishnah says "Hillel says..." or "Rabbi Akiva used to say..." and then records a concise, pointed teaching, that is a chreia. The entire structure of Avot -- sage by sage, each with his signature utterance -- is the structure of a chreia collection. And it is one of the greatest such collections in all of ancient literature.[^63]

The chreia is the central exercise for a reason: it is the form in which the sage's authority is most directly transmitted. A mashal can be told by anyone; a ma'aseh can be narrated about anyone; but a chreia is inseparable from the sage who uttered it. When we say "Hillel says: If I am not for myself, who is for me?" the force of the teaching depends in part on the fact that *Hillel* said it -- Hillel, the paradigm of patience and humility. The same words attributed to a lesser figure would carry less weight. This is why attribution matters, and why the sages were scrupulous about it:

> <span dir="rtl">כל האומר דבר בשם אומרו מביא גאולה לעולם</span>
>
> *Whoever says a thing in the name of the one who said it brings redemption to the world.*

Attribution is not merely an ethical obligation; it is a rhetorical one. The chreia's power comes from the union of the saying with the sayer. Strip away the name, and you have a maxim -- still valuable, but lacking the personal authority that gives the chreia its edge.

This is why Pirkei Avot begins with the chain of transmission: it establishes a lineage of named authorities stretching from Moses at Sinai to the rabbis of the Mishnah. Each sage in the chain is introduced by name, and each contributes his distinctive teaching. The structure of Avot is therefore the structure of a chreia collection: a sequence of attributed sayings, each bearing the stamp of its author's character and authority. The student who memorizes Avot has, in effect, memorized the entire chreia collection of the rabbinic tradition.

The student should also know the three qualities that distinguish a good chreia from a bad one: **aptness** (*hashva'ah*), **conciseness** (*qitzur*), and **pointedness** (*hidud*). A chreia must be *apt* -- that is, the saying must suit the character to whom it is attributed. A boorish remark does not make a chreia when attributed to Hillel; a gentle remark does not make a chreia when attributed to Shammai. The saying must fit the sage. A chreia must be *concise* -- brief enough to be remembered in a single hearing. If it requires a paragraph to state, it is not a chreia but a discourse. And a chreia must be *pointed* -- it must make its point with sharpness, with a sting, with a turn of thought that surprises or illuminates. A platitude is not a chreia, no matter how well attributed or how briefly stated. The chreia must provoke thought.

And the student should understand that the chreia occupies a unique position among the three preliminary exercises. The mashal is a tool of illustration -- it makes the abstract concrete. The ma'aseh is a tool of precedent -- it anchors the present in the past. But the chreia is a tool of *authority* -- it concentrates the sage's wisdom into its most potent and transmissible form. This is why, in the chain of rabbinic tradition, it is the chreiai that survive most readily: when a sage is remembered across the centuries, he is remembered for his signature sayings and his defining acts -- that is, for his chreiai. The mashal may be retold without its author; the ma'aseh may lose its details in transmission; but the chreia, with its inseparable bond between sage and saying, endures as long as the sage's name is spoken.

The student should also appreciate the economy of the chreia form. In a culture of oral transmission, where every word must be carried in human memory, brevity is not merely a stylistic preference but a survival strategy. The teachings that survive are the teachings that can be memorized -- and the chreia, precisely because it is short, pointed, and memorable, is the form best adapted to the conditions of oral culture. This is why Pirkei Avot, the most widely known collection of rabbinic wisdom, is a collection of chreiai: they are the teachings that lodged most firmly in the collective memory and were transmitted most faithfully across the generations.

I will now teach you the types of chreiai, the forms in which they are expressed, and the exercises by which they are practiced.

#### Types of Chreiai

##### Sayings-Chreiai

The most common type is the sayings-chreia -- a chreia that makes its point in words rather than actions. Within this type, there are two main species.

**The Statement Species.** A sage makes a declaration without being prompted by a question or a particular circumstance. He simply states a truth. The formula is "Rabbi X says..." (<span dir="rtl">רבי פלוני אומר</span>) or "He used to say..." (<span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר</span>).

*Unprompted statements* are the overwhelmingly dominant form in Pirkei Avot. Consider:

> <span dir="rtl">שמעון הצדיק היה משירי כנסת הגדולה. הוא היה אומר: על שלושה דברים העולם עומד - על התורה ועל העבודה ועל גמילות חסדים</span>
>
> *Simeon the Righteous was among the last of the Great Assembly. He used to say: On three things the world stands -- on Torah, on worship, and on deeds of lovingkindness.*[^64]

> <span dir="rtl">הלל אומר: אם אין אני לי, מי לי? וכשאני לעצמי, מה אני? ואם לא עכשיו, אימתי?</span>
>
> *Hillel says: If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am only for myself, what am I? And if not now, when?*[^65]

> <span dir="rtl">רבי טרפון אומר: היום קצר, והמלאכה מרובה, והפועלים עצלים, והשכר הרבה, ובעל הבית דוחק</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tarfon says: The day is short, the work is great, the workers are lazy, the reward is great, and the Master of the house is pressing.*[^66]

Each of these is pure statement chreia: no question was asked, no event prompted the utterance. The sage simply declares a truth, and the declaration is so perfectly formed that it lodges in the memory forever.

Note the formula *hu hayah omer* ("he used to say") that introduces many of these chreiai. This phrase indicates a habitual saying -- something the sage repeated on multiple occasions, a teaching so characteristic that it became his signature. The Greek chreia collections use a similar formula: *ephe* ("he used to say"). The parallel is striking, and the student should observe that the formula itself tells us something about the sage: this was not a casual remark but a deliberate, repeated teaching.

The student should also observe the difference between *omer* (present tense: "says") and *hayah omer* (past habitual: "used to say") and *amar* (past: "said"). Each formula carries a different weight. *Omer* (present) suggests a teaching that is timeless and still operative -- "Rabbi X says" implies that his teaching stands as law or wisdom for all time. *Hayah omer* (past habitual) suggests a teaching that characterized the sage during his lifetime -- it is retrospective and biographical. *Amar* (past) introduces a specific occasion -- "Rabbi X said [on such an occasion]" anchors the teaching in a narrative context. The student who attends to these formulaic distinctions will understand not only what the sage taught but how the tradition chose to frame his teaching.

Consider also these unprompted statements from beyond Pirkei Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי אליעזר אומר: הוי שקוד ללמוד תורה, ודע מה שתשיב לאפיקורוס</span>
>
> *Rabbi Eliezer says: Be diligent in the study of Torah, and know what to answer a heretic.*

Here is a chreia that explicitly addresses rhetorical preparedness: the sage must not only know Torah but must be able to *respond* -- to marshal arguments against an opponent. This is the art of debate in miniature.

And:

> <span dir="rtl">רבן גמליאל בנו של רבי יהודה הנשיא אומר: יפה תלמוד תורה עם דרך ארץ, שיגיעת שניהם משכחת עוון</span>
>
> *Rabban Gamaliel, son of Rabbi Yehudah ha-Nasi, says: Torah study combined with worldly occupation is excellent, for the exertion of both makes sin forgotten.*

This chreia has the structure of a maxim followed by its rationale -- a combined form that we will discuss further below.

*Circumstantial statements* arise from a specific situation. The sage observes something -- a scene, an event, a phenomenon -- and responds with a pointed remark. The formula often includes "he saw" (<span dir="rtl">ראה</span>) or "when he saw" (<span dir="rtl">כשראה</span>):

> <span dir="rtl">אף הוא ראה גולגולת אחת שצפה על פני המים. אמר לה: על דאטפת אטפוך, וסוף מטיפייך יטופון</span>
>
> *He [Hillel] also saw a skull floating on the water. He said to it: Because you drowned others, they drowned you; and in the end, those who drowned you will themselves be drowned.*[^67]

> <span dir="rtl">כשבא רבי שמעון בן יוחי מן המערה, ראה אדם אחד חורש וזורע. אמר: מניחין חיי עולם ועוסקין בחיי שעה?</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Shimon bar Yohai came out from the cave, he saw a man plowing and sowing. He said: They abandon eternal life and busy themselves with temporal life?*[^68]

The circumstantial statement is more dramatic than the unprompted statement because it has a setting and a trigger. The student should observe how the circumstance gives the saying its edge: Hillel's remark to the skull would be merely sententious without the skull; Shimon bar Yohai's exclamation would be merely pious without the years of cave-dwelling behind it.

There is also an entire category of circumstantial chreiai embedded in the laws of blessings. The Mishnah teaches:

> <span dir="rtl">על הזיקין ועל הזוועות ועל הברקים ועל הרעמים... אומר: ברוך שכוחו מלא עולם</span>
>
> *On seeing shooting stars, earthquakes, lightning, thunder... one says: Blessed is He whose power fills the world.*

This is the circumstantial chreia codified as law: "on seeing X, one says Y." The circumstance (a natural phenomenon) triggers the utterance (a blessing). The formula "on seeing" (*ha-ro'eh*) corresponds directly to the Greek circumstantial chreia opening "on seeing" (*idon*). The entire ninth chapter of Mishnah Berakhot is, in effect, a catalog of circumstantial chreiai, each specifying the appropriate verbal response to a particular visual, auditory, or experiential stimulus.

**The Response Species.** A sage is asked a question or confronted with a remark, and his answer constitutes the chreia.

*Response to a simple question:*

> <span dir="rtl">שוב מעשה בנכרי אחד שבא לפני שמאי. אמר לו: כמה תורות יש לכם? אמר לו: שתיים, תורה שבכתב ותורה שבעל פה</span>
>
> *Again, a gentile came before Shammai. He said to him: How many Torahs do you have? He said: Two -- the Written Torah and the Oral Torah.*[^69]

*Response to an inquiry (a question requiring a longer answer):*

> <span dir="rtl">בן זומא אומר: איזהו חכם? הלומד מכל אדם. איזהו גיבור? הכובש את יצרו. איזהו עשיר? השמח בחלקו. איזהו מכובד? המכבד את הבריות</span>
>
> *Ben Zoma says: Who is wise? One who learns from every person. Who is mighty? One who conquers his inclination. Who is rich? One who is happy with his portion. Who is honored? One who honors other people.*[^70]

This is the inquiry-response chreia at its most magnificent: four questions, four answers, each redefining a common term with a moral definition, each supported by a scriptural proof-text. The student should study this chreia carefully, for it is a model of economy, surprise, and completeness.

*Response to a remark:*

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו לו: עקביא, חזור בך בארבעה דברים שהיית אומר ונעשך אב בית דין לישראל. אמר להם: מוטב לי להיקרא שוטה כל ימי ולא להיעשות שעה אחת רשע לפני המקום</span>
>
> *They said to him: Akavya, retract the four things you used to say and we will make you head of the court of Israel. He said to them: Better for me to be called a fool all my days than to become wicked even for one hour before God.*[^71]

The remark is an offer -- retract your opinions for political advancement. The response is a refusal so principled that it has become a paradigm of intellectual integrity for all generations. The student should note the structure: the offer is attractive (who would not wish to be head of the court?), the sacrifice is real (being called a fool for one's entire life), and yet the sage chooses integrity over advancement. The chreia works because the stakes are high and the choice is clear.

Consider also this response to a remark:

> <span dir="rtl">כשהיה רבי עקיבא דורש, אמר רבי טרפון: עקיבא, כל הפורש ממך כפורש מן החיים</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Akiva was expounding, Rabbi Tarfon said: Akiva, whoever separates from you is as though he separates from life itself.*

This is not a question; it is not an inquiry. It is a spontaneous exclamation of admiration prompted by the experience of hearing Akiva's discourse. Rabbi Tarfon, himself a distinguished sage, is so overwhelmed by the quality of Akiva's exposition that he blurts out a remark that has the force of a blessing. The chreia captures a moment of rhetorical impact -- the audience's response to a masterful performance.

And on the deathbed of Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו לו תלמידיו: נר ישראל, עמוד הימיני, פטיש החזק, מפני מה אתה בוכה?</span>
>
> *His students said: Light of Israel, right pillar, mighty hammer -- why are you weeping?*

The students' remark -- three epithets of praise followed by a bewildered question -- prompts the sage's famous reply about the two paths before him. The remark itself is a work of epideictic rhetoric: the students are praising their master even as they question him. And his response, by contrast, is an expression of humility and fear that undercuts their praise entirely.

*Explanatory response (a response that includes its own rationale):*

> <span dir="rtl">שאלו את רבי יהושע: מפני מה נתנה תורה במדבר? אמר להם: מה מדבר הפקר לכל, אף תורה הפקר לכל</span>
>
> *They asked Rabbi Yehoshua: Why was the Torah given in the wilderness? He said to them: Just as the wilderness is ownerless for all, so too the Torah is ownerless for all.*[^72]

The response not only answers the question but provides the analogy that explains the answer. This is the fullest type of response chreia.

And consider this further example of an explanatory response, from the students of Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai:

> <span dir="rtl">שאלו תלמידיו את רבן יוחנן בן זכאי: מפני מה עבד עברי נרצע באוזן מכל אבריו? אמר להם: אוזן ששמעה על הר סיני "לא תגנוב" והלך וגנב - תירצע</span>
>
> *His students asked Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai: Why is the ear of the Hebrew slave pierced rather than any other limb? He said to them: The ear that heard at Mount Sinai "You shall not steal" and went and stole -- let it be pierced.*

The response does more than answer the question; it provides a midrashic reasoning that connects the specific law (ear-piercing) to the cosmic event (the revelation at Sinai). The listener receives not just a fact but a theology -- a sense that every detail of halakhah is connected to the foundational events of Israel's covenant.

**Double Chreiai.** The student should also know of the double chreia, in which two sages make opposing or complementary statements, each of which constitutes an independent chreia. The tannaitic *machloqet* (dispute) is the primary form of the double chreia in our literature:

> <span dir="rtl">בית שמאי אומרים: בערב כל אדם יטו ויקראו, ובבוקר יעמדו. ובית הלל אומרים: כל אדם קורין כדרכן</span>
>
> *Beit Shammai say: In the evening everyone should recline and recite [the Shema], and in the morning they should stand. Beit Hillel say: Everyone recites in their own way.*

Two schools, opposing positions, each with scriptural support. Each position is an independent chreia; together they form a double chreia that frames the debate and invites the student to consider both sides.

And on the meta-level, the sages reflected on the double chreia form itself:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים. ושאינה לשם שמים, אין סופה להתקיים. איזו היא מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת הלל ושמאי. ושאינה לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת קרח וכל עדתו</span>
>
> *Every dispute that is for the sake of Heaven will in the end endure. What is a dispute for the sake of Heaven? That of Hillel and Shammai. Not for the sake of Heaven? That of Korah and his company.*

This is a chreia *about* chreiai -- a teaching about the nature and value of the disputational form itself. The double chreia, when conducted for the sake of Heaven, is not a flaw or a failure of consensus; it is a permanent and productive feature of Torah discourse.

##### Action-Chreiai

Not every chreia is a saying. Sometimes the sage makes his point through action alone, without speech -- or with speech that is secondary to the act.

**Active action-chreiai** show the sage performing a deliberate act:

> <span dir="rtl">גער בו ודחפו באמת הבניין שבידו</span>
>
> *He [Shammai] rebuked him and drove him out with the builder's cubit in his hand.*[^73]

Shammai's action -- physically ejecting the convert with the measuring rod he happened to be holding -- makes a point about the proper attitude toward Torah that no mere words could convey with equal force. Note the detail of the builder's cubit (*ammat ha-binyan*): Shammai was a builder by trade, and the tool of his craft becomes the instrument of his rejection. The detail is not accidental; it anchors the action in a specific character with a specific identity.

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה בהלל הזקן שעלה לגג בית המדרש ונשתטח שם כדי לשמוע דברי תורה</span>
>
> *Hillel the Elder climbed onto the roof of the study house and lay flat there in order to hear words of Torah.*[^74]

Hillel's act of climbing the roof in the snow, nearly freezing to death rather than miss a lesson, makes a point about devotion to Torah that is more powerful than any verbal teaching. The snow falling on him, the darkness in the study house the next morning, the discovery of his frozen body in the skylight -- every physical detail reinforces the teaching. The student should observe that action-chreiai depend on vivid, concrete detail for their impact. Strip away the snow, and the story loses its power.

Consider also the active action-chreia of Rabban Gamaliel at the banquet in Jericho:

> <span dir="rtl">עמד רבן גמליאל ושימש</span>
>
> *Rabban Gamaliel stood and served [the food].*

The *nasi* -- the patriarch, the highest-ranking sage -- rises from his seat and serves food to the other guests with his own hands. No words are necessary. The act itself is the teaching: that greatness lies in service, not in being served. The students who witnessed it received a lesson in humility that no verbal instruction could have conveyed with equal force.

**Passive action-chreiai** show the sage experiencing something, and the experience itself carries the lesson:

> <span dir="rtl">בשעה שהוציאו את רבי עקיבא להריגה, זמן קריאת שמע היה, והיו סורקין את בשרו במסרקות של ברזל, והיה מקבל עליו עול מלכות שמים</span>
>
> *At the time when they took Rabbi Akiva out to be executed, it was the time for reciting the Shema, and they were combing his flesh with iron combs, and he was accepting upon himself the yoke of the kingdom of Heaven.*[^75]

What Rabbi Akiva *experienced* -- torture and execution -- becomes the supreme demonstration of the commandment to love God "with all your soul." The passive suffering is the teaching.

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי טרפון: אני הייתי בא בדרך, והטתי לקרות כדברי בית שמאי, וסיכנתי בעצמי מפני הליסטים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tarfon said: I was once traveling on the road and I reclined to recite [the Shema] as Beit Shammai say, and I endangered myself on account of robbers.*[^76]

Rabbi Tarfon's experience of danger while following Beit Shammai's ruling is itself the argument against that ruling. His colleagues respond: "You deserved to be harmed, for you violated the words of Beit Hillel." The passive experience proves the legal point.

The student should also consider the passive action-chreiai that teach the principle of measure for measure (*middah ke-neged middah*):

> <span dir="rtl">שמשון הלך אחר עיניו, לפיכך ניקרו פלשתים את עיניו. אבשלום נתגאה בשערו, לפיכך נתלה בשערו</span>
>
> *Samson followed his eyes, therefore the Philistines gouged out his eyes. Absalom took pride in his hair, therefore he was hanged by his hair.*

What these biblical characters *experienced* -- punishment through the very organ or quality by which they sinned -- is itself the teaching. No sage needs to explain the principle; the experience demonstrates it. The passive action-chreia is the form in which divine justice most clearly reveals itself: not through speech but through the eloquent correspondence between sin and consequence. The student should also note that the Mishnah itself (Sotah 1:7-9) devotes considerable attention to cataloging instances of measure-for-measure punishment, treating them as a systematic pattern rather than as isolated incidents. This is significant: the sages perceived in these passive action-chreiai not merely individual moral lessons but a general principle about the structure of divine governance. The catalogue of measure-for-measure punishments is, in rhetorical terms, a collection of passive action-chreiai organized to demonstrate a single overarching thesis -- that the universe is morally coherent, and that every action finds its appropriate response.

##### Mixed Chreiai

Mixed chreiai combine saying and action, but the action carries the primary force. The most famous example is Honi the Circle-Drawer:

> <span dir="rtl">עג עוגה ועמד בתוכה ואמר: ריבונו של עולם, בניך שמו פניהם עלי שאני כבן בית לפניך; נשבע אני בשמך הגדול שאיני זז מכאן עד שתרחם על בניך</span>
>
> *He drew a circle, stood in it, and said: Master of the Universe, Your children have turned to me because I am like a member of Your household. I swear by Your great name that I will not move from here until You have mercy on Your children.*[^77]

Both the action (drawing the circle, standing in it, refusing to move) and the speech (the oath, the petition) contribute to the chreia, but it is the action -- the brazen, almost defiant stance of a man who refuses to budge until God relents -- that makes this chreia unforgettable. And note how the story continues: rain began to drip. Honi said: "This is not what I asked for." Rain fell in torrents. Honi said: "This is not what I asked for." Finally, gentle rain fell. The three-stage response of the heavens corresponds to the three-stage demand of the sage. The action and the speech together create a narrative of escalating confrontation between man and God -- a confrontation that the man wins.

Shimon ben Shetach's response to Honi is itself instructive for the student of rhetoric: "Were you not Honi, I would excommunicate you. But what can I do? You act petulantly before God and He does your will, like a child who acts petulantly before his father, and the father does his will." This is a double-edged comment: it acknowledges Honi's unique status while also registering the impropriety of his method. It is the comment of a man who recognizes rhetorical power even when he disapproves of it.

And the most sublime mixed chreia in all of rabbinic literature is the death of Rabbi Akiva, where his dying words and his dying act converge:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו לו תלמידיו: רבנו, עד כאן? אמר להם: כל ימי הייתי מצטער על פסוק זה: בכל נפשך - אפילו הוא נוטל את נשמתך. אמרתי: מתי יבוא לידי ואקיימנו? ועכשיו שבא לידי לא אקיימנו? היה מאריך באחד עד שיצתה נשמתו באחד</span>
>
> *His students said: Our master, even to this extent? He said to them: All my life I was troubled by this verse: "with all your soul" -- even if He takes your soul. I said: When will I have the opportunity to fulfill it? Now that the opportunity has come, should I not fulfill it? He prolonged the word "One" until his soul departed on "One."*[^78]

Here the word and the act are indistinguishable. His speech *is* his dying, and his dying *is* his speech. This is the mixed chreia raised to a level that transcends rhetoric and becomes martyrdom.

The student should pause here and consider what this teaches about the ultimate purpose of rhetorical training. Rabbi Akiva did not learn to speak well merely in order to win debates. He learned to speak well in order to live -- and to die -- with his words and his deeds in perfect alignment. The mixed chreia of his death is the final examination of everything he learned in the bet midrash. All the exercises we teach -- invention, arrangement, style, memory, delivery; mashal, ma'aseh, chreia -- all of them converge in this single moment: a sage who has so thoroughly integrated word and deed that his last breath and his last word are one and the same thing.

This is the standard to which all rhetorical training aspires, even if few will ever achieve it. The sage's life is itself his greatest discourse, and the mixed chreia -- where saying and doing converge -- is the form that captures this truth most perfectly.[^79]

#### Forms of Expression

The student should know that chreiai may be expressed in many different forms -- that is, the *manner* in which the sage's saying is formulated varies. Just as the same melody can be played in different keys, and the same thought can be expressed in different words, so the same type of chreia can take on different forms of expression. The student who recognizes these forms will be able to analyze any chreia he encounters and compose new chreiai in whatever form best suits his purpose. I will now enumerate the twelve forms and give an example of each.[^80]

**1. In the manner of a maxim.** The chreia takes the form of a general moral principle. This is the most common form in Pirkei Avot and throughout tannaitic literature:

> <span dir="rtl">הלל אומר: במקום שאין אנשים, השתדל להיות איש</span>
>
> *Hillel says: In a place where there are no men, strive to be a man.*[^81]

This is a pure maxim: it states a general principle applicable to all people at all times. The attribution to Hillel makes it a chreia rather than an unattributed maxim -- but its form is that of a universal truth. Other examples include Shammai's "Say little and do much" (Avot 1:15), Ben He He's "According to the effort is the reward" (Avot 5:23), and the dozens of "Rabbi X says" teachings throughout Avot that take the form of general moral principles.

**2. In the manner of an explanation.** The chreia includes both a statement and its rationale:

> <span dir="rtl">חכמים, הזהרו בדבריכם, שמא תחובו חובת גלות ותגלו למקום מים הרעים, וישתו התלמידים הבאים אחריכם וימותו, ונמצא שם שמים מתחלל</span>
>
> *Sages, be careful with your words, lest you incur the penalty of exile and be exiled to a place of evil waters, and the students who follow you will drink and die, and the name of Heaven will be desecrated.*[^82]

The warning is stated, and then a chain of consequences explains why.

**3. With wit.** The chreia makes its point through a clever or humorous turn:

> <span dir="rtl">לא בשמים היא!</span>
>
> *"It is not in heaven!"*[^83]

Rabbi Yehoshua's deployment of Deuteronomy 30:12 to refute a heavenly voice -- using Scripture against Heaven itself -- is one of the most celebrated witticisms in rabbinic literature. The wit lies in the audacity: a verse about the accessibility of Torah is turned into a constitutional argument about the locus of legal authority. And the sequel is equally witty: when the Talmud reports that God smiled and said, "My children have defeated Me" (*nitzchuni banai*), the verb *nitzchuni* carries a double meaning: they have defeated Me, and they have made Me victorious. God *wants* to be defeated in argument by His children, because their victory means that the system He established -- a system in which human reason governs -- is working as intended.

Consider also Hillel's wit with the convert who demanded to learn Torah on one foot. The pedagogical trap -- teaching the alphabet and then reversing it -- is itself a witty maneuver: it forces the convert to recognize that he already trusts Hillel's authority for the Written Torah, and therefore has no grounds to reject his authority for the Oral Torah. The wit lies in using the student's own logic against him.

And the sage's quip at Pesahim 66b:

> <span dir="rtl">הניחו להם לישראל; אם אין נביאים הם, בני נביאים הם</span>
>
> *Leave Israel alone; if they are not prophets, they are the children of prophets!*

Hillel resolves a legal problem by appealing to popular practice with a witty formulation that elevates the common people to quasi-prophetic status.

**4. With a syllogism.** The chreia employs logical reasoning in an if-then structure:

> <span dir="rtl">כל שיראת חטאו קודמת לחכמתו, חכמתו מתקיימת. וכל שחכמתו קודמת ליראת חטאו, אין חכמתו מתקיימת</span>
>
> *One whose fear of sin precedes his wisdom -- his wisdom endures. One whose wisdom precedes his fear of sin -- his wisdom does not endure.*[^84]

If A precedes B, then C. If B precedes A, then not-C. A perfect syllogistic form. The student should note how many teachings in Avot follow this pattern. The four types of charity givers (Avot 5:13) systematically permute two variables -- willingness to give and willingness for others to give -- yielding four types: pious, wicked, and two intermediate. This fourfold syllogistic classification is a characteristic rabbinic form that has no exact parallel in the Greek progymnasmata but is closely related to the rhetorical technique of division (*divisio*).

**5. With an enthymeme.** The chreia leaves one premise unstated, requiring the listener to supply it:

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין אני לי מי לי? וכשאני לעצמי מה אני?</span>
>
> *If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am only for myself, what am I?*[^85]

The listener must supply the unspoken conclusions: "No one" and "Nothing." The power of the enthymeme is that it *engages* the listener -- it forces him to complete the argument in his own mind, and thereby to accept it more deeply than if the conclusion had been stated outright. Many of the most celebrated rabbinic sayings are enthymemes: they leave unsaid what the listener is meant to deduce, and this silence is more eloquent than speech.

Consider also Rabbi Yehoshua's deployment of Deuteronomy 30:12 against the heavenly voice:

> <span dir="rtl">לא בשמים היא!</span>
>
> *"It is not in heaven!"*

The unstated conclusion: if the Torah is not in heaven, then heavenly voices cannot overrule earthly legal authority. But this radical conclusion is left entirely for the listener to work out. The brevity is the brilliance.

**6. With an example.** The chreia illustrates its point by citing a precedent:

> <span dir="rtl">איזו היא מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת הלל ושמאי. ושאינה לשם שמים? זו מחלוקת קרח וכל עדתו</span>
>
> *Which is a dispute for the sake of Heaven? That of Hillel and Shammai. Not for the sake of Heaven? That of Korah and his company.*[^86]

Each abstract principle is anchored in a concrete historical example. The use of examples is one of the most powerful rhetorical techniques, because it grounds the abstract in the familiar. Every student knows Hillel and Shammai; every student knows Korah. By attaching the principle to the example, the sage makes the principle unforgettable.

Ben Zoma's four definitions (Avot 4:1) are also examples in this sense: each redefinition of a common term (wise, mighty, rich, honored) is supported by a scriptural example. "Who is wise? One who learns from every person -- *as it is said*: From all my teachers I have gained understanding (Psalm 119:99)." The scriptural citation functions as the example that proves the redefinition.

**7. With a wish.** The chreia takes the form of a blessing or aspiration:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר להם: יהי רצון שתהא מורא שמים עליכם כמורא בשר ודם</span>
>
> *He said to them: May it be God's will that your fear of Heaven be like your fear of flesh and blood.*[^87]

Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai's deathbed wish is remarkable for what it concedes: that human beings naturally fear other humans more than they fear God. The wish acknowledges this psychological reality and asks that the divine be granted at least the same weight as the human. The students respond with astonishment: "Only that much?" And the master replies: "Would that it were so! Know that when a person commits a transgression, he says, 'I hope no one sees me.'" The wish-chreia here opens into a brief dialogue that deepens its point. The wish is not naive idealism but hard-won realism about human nature.

Consider also the liturgical blessings that take the form of wish-chreiai: the priestly blessing ("May the Lord bless you and keep you"), the blessing over the new month, and the many formulaic wishes spoken by sages to their students upon parting. Each is a chreia in wish form -- a concise, attributed expression that makes its point through aspiration rather than assertion.

**8. In a symbolic manner.** The chreia makes its point through a symbolic gesture or image:

> <span dir="rtl">העולם הזה דומה לפרוזדור בפני העולם הבא. התקן עצמך בפרוזדור כדי שתיכנס לטרקלין</span>
>
> *This world is like a vestibule before the world to come. Prepare yourself in the vestibule so that you may enter the banquet hall.*[^88]

The spatial metaphor -- vestibule and hall -- makes an abstract theological claim into a concrete, navigable image. The student should note the difference between the symbolic and the figurative: the symbolic gesture or image *stands for* something else (the circle stands for sacred space; the vestibule stands for this world), while the figurative expression *describes* something in terms of something else. In practice, the two often overlap, but the distinction is worth learning.

Consider also the symbolic use of objects in rabbinic narrative. When Honi draws his circle and stands inside it, the circle is not merely a metaphor; it is a physical act that creates a symbolic boundary. The act of standing inside the circle *enacts* the sage's claim to a special relationship with God. This is symbolic rhetoric in the strongest sense: the symbol is not merely verbal but performative.

**9. In a figurative manner.** The chreia uses extended metaphor:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו: יש קנקן חדש מלא ישן, וישן שאפילו חדש אין בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the vessel but at what is in it: there may be a new vessel full of old [wine], and an old one that does not even have new [wine] in it.*[^89]

Vessel = person's appearance; wine = their wisdom. The figurative language transforms a platitude ("don't judge by appearances") into a vivid image.

Consider also the great figurative chain in which Torah is compared to water, to wine, to oil, to milk, and to honey -- each comparison illuminating a different quality. In Sifrei Deuteronomy, the Torah-as-water comparison is extended through many points of resemblance:

> <span dir="rtl">מה מים חיים לעולם, אף דברי תורה חיים לעולם. מה מים חינם לעולם, אף דברי תורה חינם לעולם. מה מים מניחין מקום גבוה ויורדין למקום נמוך, אף דברי תורה אין מתקיימין אלא במי שדעתו שפלה</span>
>
> *Just as water gives life to the world, so words of Torah give life. Just as water is free for the world, so words of Torah are free. Just as water leaves the high place and flows to the low place, so words of Torah endure only in one whose mind is humble.*

This extended figurative comparison is itself a rhetorical exercise: each point of resemblance is a separate argument, and the accumulation of points creates a powerful composite image. The student should practice composing such extended figurative chains: choose an image and draw out as many points of comparison as you can.

And Rabbi Elisha ben Avuyah's comparison of the young learner to ink on new paper and the old learner to ink on erased paper (Avot 4:20) is figurative in a different way: it uses a physical process (writing) as a metaphor for a cognitive one (learning). The concreteness of the image -- the student can visualize the crisp letters on clean paper and the blurred letters on smudged paper -- gives the teaching an immediate, almost physical impact.

**10. With double entendre.** The chreia carries two meanings simultaneously:

> <span dir="rtl">אם אני כאן, הכל כאן</span>
>
> *If I am here, everyone is here.*[^90]

Hillel's remark at the Water Drawing celebration can refer to himself (a boast about his own importance) or to God (the Shekhinah is here). The double meaning is noted already in the Talmud.

The double entendre is a particularly subtle form of expression because it requires the listener to hold two meanings in mind simultaneously. The ambiguity is not a flaw but a feature: it enriches the teaching by allowing it to work on multiple levels. Consider also the matzah of the Passover Seder:

> <span dir="rtl">מצה זו שאנו אוכלין, על שום מה?</span>
>
> *This matzah that we eat -- on account of what?*

The matzah is simultaneously *lehem oni* -- bread of poverty, recalling the affliction of slavery -- and *lehem she-onim alav devarim harbeh* -- bread over which many things are said, recalling the wealth of discourse at the Seder table. Poverty and abundance coexist in the same object, and the double meaning captures the paradox of the Exodus: the departure was so hasty that there was no time for the dough to rise (affliction), yet the very haste was the sign of liberation (freedom). The double entendre holds both meanings in productive tension.

**11. With a change of subject.** The speaker redirects the discussion:

> <span dir="rtl">כשביקש רבי מאיר להתפלל על שכניו הרעים שימותו, אמרה לו ברוריא אשתו: מאי דעתך? אומר "יתמו חטאים" - "חוטאים" כתיב? "חטאים" כתיב! התפלל עליהם שיעשו תשובה</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir sought to pray for his wicked neighbors to die, his wife Beruriah said to him: What is your reasoning? Because it is written "Let sins cease"? Does it say "sinners"? It says "sins"! Rather, pray for them that they should repent.*[^91]

Beruriah changes the subject from the sinners to the sins, redirecting the entire discussion from punishment to repentance. The rhetorical technique here is one of *redefinition*: Beruriah does not argue with Rabbi Meir's premise (that something bad must cease); she redefines the object. Not the sinners but the sins should cease. The change of subject is also a change of moral framework -- from retribution to rehabilitation. This is among the most powerful rhetorical moves available to the sage: do not argue within your opponent's frame; change the frame entirely.

Rabbi Yehoshua's "It is not in heaven!" at the oven of Akhnai is another example of change of subject: when the debate has escalated to miracles and heavenly voices, he redirects attention entirely from the theological question to the legal one. The Torah has already been given; earthly authority decides.

**12. In combined form.** The chreia employs two or more of the above forms simultaneously:

Ben Zoma's four questions (Avot 4:1) combine inquiry-response with maxim, with scriptural example, and with redefinition (a form of wit). Rabbi Tarfon's "The day is short..." (Avot 2:15-16) combines figurative language with explanation and maxim. These are among the richest chreiai in the literature precisely because they layer multiple forms of expression.

The entire narrative of Hillel and the three converts (Shabbat 31a) is a combined form on the grandest scale: it combines wit (the alphabet trick), example (the demonstration from experience), maxim (the Golden Rule), and enthymeme (the listener must draw the conclusion). The story of the oven of Akhnai (Bava Metzia 59b) combines change of subject, wit, enthymeme, and symbolic action.

The student should understand that the combined form is not merely an ornamental overlay -- it is a sign of depth. The simplest chreiai employ a single form of expression; the greatest chreiai layer multiple forms, creating a density of meaning that rewards repeated study. When a chreia makes its point simultaneously through wit, through example, and through enthymeme, it engages the listener's intellect, imagination, and moral sense all at once. This is why the greatest chreiai in our tradition -- Hillel's "If I am not for myself," Ben Zoma's four questions, Akiva's dying Shema -- are inexhaustible: each return to them reveals a new layer of meaning, expressed through a new form.

The student should practice identifying the forms of expression in every chreia he encounters. When he studies a new teaching in Avot or encounters a sage's saying in the Talmud, he should ask: what form does this chreia take? Is it maxim, explanation, wit, syllogism, enthymeme, example, wish, symbolic, figurative, double entendre, change of subject, or combined? If combined, which forms are layered? This analytical habit will sharpen the student's understanding of how chreiai work and will prepare him to compose chreiai of his own in whatever form the occasion requires.

For the mastery of forms is not merely an exercise in classification. It is a practical skill. The sage who knows all twelve forms can choose the one best suited to his audience and his purpose. Before scholars, a syllogistic chreia may be most effective; before the common people, a witty or figurative chreia will serve better. Before a mourner, a wish-chreia; before a disputant, an enthymeme. The forms are not ornaments but tools, and the skilled craftsman selects the right tool for each task.[^92]

#### Exercises with Chreiai

The student should practice the following eight exercises with each chreia he studies. These exercises develop flexibility, depth of understanding, and mastery of argumentation.[^93]

**1. Recitation (Shanah).** Report the chreia clearly, first in the sage's own words, then in your own words. The phenomenon of variant recitation is pervasive in rabbinic literature: Hillel's Golden Rule appears in Aramaic in Shabbat 31a, in Hebrew in Avot de-Rabbi Natan, and in a positive formulation (Rabbi Akiva's "Love your neighbor as yourself") in Sifra Qedoshim. The student should practice restating the teaching while preserving its essential meaning.

Consider also the teaching about the three pillars of the world, which appears in two recitations:

> <span dir="rtl">על שלושה דברים העולם עומד</span>
>
> *On three things the world stands* (Avot 1:2 -- Simeon the Righteous)

> <span dir="rtl">על שלושה דברים העולם קיים</span>
>
> *On three things the world endures* (Avot 1:18 -- Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel)

The verb changes (*omed* vs. *qayyam*), the lists change (Torah, worship, deeds of lovingkindness vs. justice, truth, peace), and the sage changes. Yet the structure is identical: "On three things the world X." This is variant recitation at the level of the tradition itself -- the same form deployed with different content by a later sage. The student should practice both kinds of recitation: exact reproduction and creative adaptation.[^94]

**2. Inflexion (Shinui).** Change the persons, numbers, or circumstances of the chreia. Consider Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai's two questions to his five disciples: "What is the right path?" and "What is the evil path?" (Avot 2:9-10). The same inquiry is posed twice, first in the positive, then in the negative, and each student's answer is inflected accordingly: good eye / evil eye, good companion / evil companion. This is systematic inflexion -- the same structure applied to opposite cases.

The fourfold permutations of Avot 5:14 demonstrate inflexion at its most systematic:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מדות בתלמידים: מהר לשמוע ומהר לאבד - יצא שכרו בהפסדו; קשה לשמוע וקשה לאבד - יצא הפסדו בשכרו; מהר לשמוע וקשה לאבד - חכם; קשה לשמוע ומהר לאבד - זה חלק רע</span>
>
> *Four types of students: Quick to learn and quick to forget -- the gain is offset by the loss. Slow to learn and slow to forget -- the loss is offset by the gain. Quick to learn and slow to forget -- wise. Slow to learn and quick to forget -- a bad lot.*

Two variables (speed of learning, speed of forgetting), each with two values (quick, slow), yield four permutations. This is logical inflexion applied to character types -- a complete mapping of all possible combinations. The student should take any chreia and restate it: in the second person instead of the third, as a negative instead of a positive, as applying to groups instead of individuals.[^95]

**3. Comment (He'arah).** Comment on the chreia, declaring it true, noble, or advantageous. When Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai evaluates his students' responses and says, "I prefer the words of Elazar ben Arakh, for his words include all of yours," he is performing the comment exercise: he declares one chreia superior to the others and gives his reason.[^96] When Rabbi Yehoshua exclaims, "Who will remove the dust from your eyes, Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai!" -- wishing the deceased master could hear a later teaching that confirmed his view -- he is performing the comment exercise across time.[^97]

The student should practice this exercise by taking a chreia he admires and composing three comments on it: one declaring it true (and explaining why), one declaring it noble (and showing how it elevates character), and one declaring it advantageous (and demonstrating its practical benefit). For example, take Hillel's saying, "In a place where there are no men, strive to be a man." The student might comment: it is *true*, because communities without leaders decay; it is *noble*, because it calls for courage in the face of collective failure; it is *advantageous*, because the one who steps forward in a crisis gains both merit and authority.

**4. Objection (Qushi).** Object to the chreia from the opposite point of view. Every dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel is an exercise in objection: one school's position is, by definition, an objection to the other's. When Rabbi Shimon ben Gamaliel responds to Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Tarfon's claim that they would never have executed anyone, saying "They too would increase bloodshed in Israel," he is performing the objection exercise with devastating conciseness.[^98]

The student should practice objecting to even the most revered teachings. Take Shammai's saying, "Say little and do much." One might object: is there not also value in speaking much, if the speech is Torah? Does not the Mishnah itself say, "One who increases Torah increases life"? The point of the exercise is not to undermine the teaching but to test its limits, to discover the conditions under which it applies and the conditions under which it does not. A teaching that cannot withstand objection is a weak teaching; a teaching that emerges strengthened from objection is a proven one.

**5. Expansion (Harhabah).** Enlarge upon the chreia by adding narrative, explanation, or additional arguments. The entire structure of Avot de-Rabbi Natan is an exercise in expansion: the concise sayings of Pirkei Avot are expanded into full chapters with narratives, scriptural proofs, and multiple illustrations. When the three-word teaching of Avot 1:2 ("On Torah, on worship, and on deeds of lovingkindness") becomes three full chapters in ARN, that is expansion in its purest form.

Consider how ARN expands Hillel's "Be of the disciples of Aaron" (Avot 1:12). The concise chreia is expanded with multiple narrative illustrations: how Aaron would make peace between quarreling friends, how he would approach each party separately and tell them that the other regretted the quarrel, how the two would then meet and embrace. The expansion does not change the teaching; it makes it vivid, concrete, and memorable. It moves from principle to practice, from abstraction to story.

The Tosefta, too, systematically expands Mishnaic teachings: it adds narratives, exceptions, alternative reasoning, and additional cases. The relationship between Mishnah and Tosefta is, in many places, precisely the relationship between a chreia and its expansion.[^99]

**6. Condensation (Qitzur).** Reduce the chreia to its most compact expression. The most celebrated example of condensation in rabbinic literature is the progressive reduction described by Rabbi Simlai:

> <span dir="rtl">דרש רבי שמלאי: שש מאות ושלוש עשרה מצוות נאמרו לו למשה... בא דוד והעמידן על אחת עשרה... בא ישעיהו והעמידן על שש... בא מיכה והעמידן על שלוש... חזר ישעיהו והעמידן על שתיים... בא חבקוק והעמידן על אחת: וצדיק באמונתו יחיה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Simlai expounded: 613 commandments were given to Moses... David came and reduced them to eleven... Isaiah reduced them to six... Micah to three... Isaiah again to two... Habakkuk to one: "The righteous shall live by his faith."*

This is condensation as theological method: the entire Torah progressively reduced to a single verse. And Hillel's Golden Rule is itself a condensation: "the entire Torah on one foot." And Ben He He's three Aramaic words -- *le-fum tza'ara agra*, "According to the effort is the reward" -- compress an entire theology of merit into a phrase no longer than a sigh.

The student should practice condensation by taking an extended teaching and reducing it to its core. What is the single sentence that captures the essence of the passage? What would remain if everything superfluous were stripped away? This exercise trains the student to distinguish the essential from the ornamental -- a skill that is invaluable in both legal argument and public preaching.[^100]

**7. Refutation (Teshuvah/Pirkah).** Refute the chreia on one or more grounds. The student should know the nine categories by which a chreia may be challenged:[^101]

- *Obscurity*: the teaching is unclear and requires explanation.
- *Pleonasm*: the teaching says more than necessary -- why the redundancy?
- *Ellipsis*: the teaching omits something essential -- what has been left unsaid?
