- *Impossibility*: the teaching states something that cannot be.
- *Implausibility*: the teaching, while not impossible, strains credulity.
- *Falsity*: the teaching contradicts known facts.
- *Unsuitability*: the teaching, while true, is inappropriate for the circumstances.
- *Uselessness*: the teaching serves no practical purpose.
- *Shamefulness*: the teaching leads to morally repugnant conclusions.

These nine categories pervade talmudic argumentation. Let me give you a concrete example of each:

*Obscurity*: The teaching of Rabbi Dosa ben Harkinas (Mishnah Eduyot 1:3) is challenged as unclear by later sages who cannot determine the exact scope of his ruling. They must seek clarification from his brother or from other witnesses to his teaching.

*Pleonasm*: Tannaitic interpreters regularly ask *lamah ne'emar?* -- "Why does the verse need to say this?" -- identifying apparent redundancies in Scripture or in halakhic formulations that must be justified. If a word is unnecessary, it must be teaching something additional; if a teaching repeats itself, the repetition must carry a distinct meaning.

*Ellipsis*: The midrashic technique of noting what Scripture "should have said but didn't" (*ha mah talmud lomar*) identifies gaps in a formulation that require filling in. When a mishnah states a rule without providing all the conditions under which it applies, the Talmud challenges it for ellipsis: *haserah mekhaserah, ve-hakhi qatani* -- "it is lacking, and this is what it should have taught."

*Impossibility*: When a teaching seems to describe an impossible scenario -- such as the claim that the generation of the wilderness has no share in the world to come (Mishnah Sanhedrin 10:3) -- it may be challenged on the grounds that God's mercy is without limit and repentance is always available.

*Implausibility*: Legal presumptions (*hazaqot*) in Bava Batra are challenged when they conflict with plausible counter-scenarios. If the presumption states that an occupant has owned a property for three years, the opponent may argue that the time frame is implausibly short or long for establishing true ownership.

*Falsity*: The genre of *stam* vs. tannaitic disagreement throughout the Talmud involves challenging earlier teachings as factually incorrect: "But have we not learned otherwise?"

*Unsuitability*: When Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Tarfon say, "Had we been in the Sanhedrin, no one would ever have been executed," Rabbi Shimon ben Gamaliel objects that their noble policy would *increase* bloodshed -- rendering it unsuitable despite its good intention.

*Uselessness*: The Sadducees challenge certain Pharisaic rulings (Mishnah Yadayim 4:6) as serving no practical purpose, demanding to know what benefit the community derives from them.

*Shamefulness*: Akavya ben Mahalalel refuses to retract his opinions for political gain, arguing that to do so would be *shameful* -- a betrayal of his intellectual integrity before God.

The student who internalizes these nine categories possesses a complete toolkit for critical analysis of any teaching, and he should practice applying each one to the chreiai he studies.[^102]

**8. Confirmation (Qiyyum/Asmakhta).** Provide arguments in support of the chreia, using as many proofs as possible. The entire structure of halakhic midrash -- Sifra, Sifrei, Mekhilta -- is confirmation: a legal principle is stated, then supported through close reading of Scripture, logical inference (*qal va-homer*, *gezerah shavah*), historical precedent, and appeal to tradition.

When Rabbi Akiva's teaching that Israel is beloved (Avot 3:14) is confirmed, the confirmation proceeds in stages: first by restatement with intensification ("an extra love was made known"), then by scriptural proof ("You are children of the Lord your God"), and implicitly by the chain of three parallel statements, each building on the last. The student sees a model of confirmation that layers multiple types of evidence.

The student should practice confirmation by taking a chreia and supporting it with as many different types of argument as he can muster: a proof from the Torah, a proof from the Prophets, a proof from the Writings, a logical inference, a supporting narrative, an appeal to the practice of known sages, and an argument from common experience. The more diverse the proofs, the stronger the confirmation. And the student should observe that the sages themselves practiced this method -- in the Mekhilta, a single principle is often confirmed through three or four independent scriptural proofs, as if to say: this truth is not supported by one verse alone but by the entire weight of Scripture.

The order of proofs in confirmation also matters. The strongest proof should come first or last -- never buried in the middle, where it may be overlooked. A proof from the Torah carries more weight than a proof from the Prophets, which carries more weight than a proof from the Writings. A proof from logic (*sevara*) is the weakest type of proof in halakhic discourse, though it is often the most persuasive in aggadic discourse. The student should learn to assess the strength of each type of evidence and arrange his confirmation accordingly.

And the student should know that confirmation and refutation are *complementary* exercises -- two faces of the same coin. The student who can confirm a teaching has mastered the art of building a case; the student who can refute a teaching has mastered the art of testing one. The sage who has practiced both can both construct and deconstruct any argument, and this double competence is the mark of true rhetorical mastery. In the Talmud, the finest sages are those who can argue a case from both sides -- who can confirm the position they hold and then immediately refute it, only to confirm it again with a deeper and more resilient argument. This dialectical movement -- confirmation, refutation, deeper confirmation -- is the heartbeat of talmudic reasoning.[^103]

#### The Chreia and the Related Forms

Before we conclude this section, the student should understand the relationship between the chreia and two closely related forms.

**The Maxim (Gnome).** A maxim differs from a chreia in that it is *unattributed* -- it is a general truth not connected to any specific sage. In Pirkei Avot, most teachings are chreiai (attributed to named sages), but some are maxims. Much of chapter 5 consists of unattributed maxims:

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה דברים נבראו בערב שבת בין השמשות</span>
>
> *Ten things were created at twilight on the eve of Shabbat.*

> <span dir="rtl">שבעה דברים בגולם ושבעה בחכם</span>
>
> *Seven qualities characterize a boor and seven a sage.*

No sage's name is attached. These are general truths, applicable to all, and they constitute maxims (*gnomae*) rather than chreiai. The student should learn to distinguish between the two: a maxim has the force of communal wisdom, while a chreia has the force of individual authority. Both are valuable; they serve different rhetorical purposes.

Similarly, the unattributed teachings of Mishnah Pe'ah 1:1 -- "These are things that have no prescribed measure: pe'ah, first fruits, the appearance offering, deeds of lovingkindness, and Torah study" -- are maxims. They carry the authority of the collective tradition rather than of any individual sage.

The distinction between chreia and maxim is important for the student because it affects how each is used in argument. When you cite a chreia, you invoke the authority of a named sage -- and your listener may accept or reject that authority. "But Rabbi Akiva holds otherwise!" is a valid response to a chreia. When you cite a maxim, you invoke the authority of the tradition itself -- and the maxim is harder to reject, precisely because no individual sage can be challenged. This is why the unattributed Mishnah (*stam mishnah*) carries more halakhic weight than an attributed opinion: it is treated as the consensus of the collective, not the view of any individual.

**The Reminiscence (Ma'aseh Arukh).** A reminiscence differs from a chreia in that it is *extended* -- it may run to many sentences or even many paragraphs, encompassing multiple scenes, multiple speakers, and narrative development. The stories we discussed under "Narrative" above -- Hillel in the snow, the deposition of Rabban Gamaliel, the martyrdom of Rabbi Akiva -- are reminiscences rather than chreiai when told in their full form. But each of these extended narratives *contains* a chreia at its core: a concise, pointed moment that captures the essence of the story. The skill of the student lies in being able to identify the chreia within the reminiscence and, conversely, to expand a chreia into a reminiscence when the occasion demands it.

Consider the full narrative of the four who entered the *Pardes* -- the mystical orchard:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבעה נכנסו בפרדס, ואלו הן: בן עזאי, ובן זומא, אחר, ורבי עקיבא. בן עזאי הציץ ומת. בן זומא הציץ ונפגע. אחר קיצץ בנטיעות. רבי עקיבא נכנס בשלום ויצא בשלום</span>
>
> *Four entered the Pardes, and they are: Ben Azzai, Ben Zoma, Aher [Elisha ben Avuyah], and Rabbi Akiva. Ben Azzai looked and died. Ben Zoma looked and was stricken. Aher cut the shoots. Rabbi Akiva entered in peace and departed in peace.*

In its brief Tosefta version, this is close to a chreia -- four characters, four outcomes. But in the expanded Babylonian Talmud version, it becomes a full reminiscence: Rabbi Akiva's warning to his companions before they enter, the scriptural verses cited to explain each fate, the narrative of Aher's subsequent career as a heretic, and the elaborate account of Rabbi Meir's attempts to bring his teacher back. The brief version captures the point; the long version unfolds its implications. The student should be able to work in both directions -- to condense a reminiscence into its chreia core and to expand a chreia into a full reminiscence.

#### Cross-Reference

For the complete catalog of chreia parallels organized by Theon's taxonomy -- including all examples of each type, each form of expression, and each exercise -- the student should consult the companion document, *Tannaitic Parallels to Theon's Chreia Taxonomy*. That catalog provides the raw material; this section has provided the pedagogical framework for understanding and practicing with it.

The student should use the companion catalog as a workbook. For each example in the catalog, practice the eight exercises: recite the chreia in your own words, inflect it into different forms, comment on it, object to it, expand it, condense it, refute it on at least three of the nine grounds, and confirm it with at least three different types of proof. If the student works through even a fraction of the catalog in this systematic way, he will emerge with a mastery of the chreia form that will serve him throughout his career as a sage, a teacher, and a judge.

The catalog also serves as a model for the student's own collection. The student should begin, from the earliest days of his training, to keep his own mental catalog of chreiai -- the memorable sayings and actions of his teachers, his colleagues, and the sages he encounters in the texts he studies. Over time, this personal collection will become his most valuable rhetorical resource: a treasury of pointed utterances that he can deploy in his own teaching, preaching, and argumentation. The sage who has a chreia ready for every occasion is the sage who commands attention; the sage who must search for words in the moment has already lost his audience.[^104]

---

#### Concluding Words on the First Three Exercises

My son, we have now completed the first three exercises: the fable, the narrative, and the chreia. Do not think that because they are called "preliminary" they are therefore simple or beneath you. These three exercises are the foundations upon which all higher discourse is built.

The mashal teaches you to think in images -- to translate the abstract into the concrete, to find the story that makes the truth visible. Without this ability, you will speak to the mind but not to the heart.

The ma'aseh teaches you to think in precedents -- to anchor your arguments in the authority of what has been done and decided by those who came before you. Without this ability, you will speak without authority, and your words will not endure.

The chreia teaches you to think in points -- to compress your teaching into its most concentrated, most memorable form. Without this ability, you will speak at length and be forgotten; with it, you will speak briefly and be remembered forever.

The three exercises also correspond to the three genres of discourse we discussed earlier. The mashal is the preacher's primary tool -- it belongs chiefly to epideictic discourse, to the sermon and the eulogy, where the goal is to move the hearts of the listeners. The ma'aseh is the judge's primary tool -- it belongs chiefly to judicial discourse, to the courtroom and the halakhic debate, where the goal is to establish what happened and what follows from it. The chreia is the counselor's primary tool -- it belongs chiefly to deliberative discourse, to the assembly and the policy debate, where the goal is to persuade toward a course of action by invoking the authority of a great sage. But all three are used in all three genres, and the master of rhetoric moves freely among them.

Moreover, the three exercises correspond to three modes of knowing. The mashal teaches through *imagination* -- it invites the listener to enter a fictional world and discover truth within it. The ma'aseh teaches through *experience* -- it anchors truth in what has actually occurred. The chreia teaches through *authority* -- it compresses truth into the word of a named, revered figure. The student who masters all three modes of knowing can reach any listener: the one who is moved by stories, the one who is persuaded by precedent, and the one who defers to the judgment of the wise.

In the sections that follow, we will proceed to the higher exercises: the refutation and confirmation of propositions (*thesis*), the formal encomium and invective, the comparison, the characterization (*ethopoeia*), and the practice oration. Each of these builds upon the three preliminary exercises we have covered here. The thesis exercise asks the student to argue for or against a general proposition -- a skill that requires the invention skills practiced in the chreia exercises, the arrangement skills practiced in the narrative exercises, and the figurative skills practiced in the mashal exercises. The encomium asks the student to compose a sustained speech of praise -- drawing on the epideictic techniques we discussed in Part I and the biographical narratives of Part II. The characterization asks the student to compose a speech in the voice of a specific character -- a skill that requires deep familiarity with the chreia tradition, since the student must know how each sage spoke and thought.

But the student who has mastered the fable, the narrative, and the chreia has laid the foundation upon which all else is built.

> <span dir="rtl">בן בג בג אומר: הפוך בה והפוך בה דכולא בה</span>
>
> *Ben Bag Bag says: Turn it over and turn it over, for everything is in it.*

Return to these exercises again and again. Practice them with different teachings, different audiences, different occasions. Let them become second nature.

Let me tell you how these three exercises work together in the actual practice of the sage. When a preacher rises to deliver the Shabbat derashah, he does not choose *either* mashal *or* ma'aseh *or* chreia. He weaves them together. He opens with a *petihta* -- that artful journey from a remote verse to the parashah -- and along the way he deploys a mashal to illuminate a difficult concept, a ma'aseh to establish precedent for the practice he is commending, and a chreia to drive his central point home with the authority of a named sage. The three exercises are not separate skills practiced in isolation; they are threads that the skilled preacher braids into a single discourse.

Similarly, in the bet din, the advocate weaves all three forms. He tells the story of what happened (ma'aseh), he draws an analogy to make the judges see his client's situation in a favorable light (mashal), and he cites the decisive ruling of a prior authority (chreia). The judge, too, uses all three: he narrates the facts as he finds them, he illustrates the applicable principle with a parable, and he anchors his decision in the words of the masters who preceded him.

And in the academy, the teacher uses all three in his daily instruction. He tells a story to engage the students' attention. He poses a parable to make an abstract principle concrete. He cites a chreia to give the lesson its capstone. Watch any master teacher at work, and you will see these three forms in constant interplay -- sometimes in sequence, sometimes layered atop one another, sometimes so seamlessly interwoven that the listener cannot tell where one ends and the next begins.

This is why we teach these exercises first: not because they are simple, but because they are foundational. Every higher form of discourse -- the thesis, the encomium, the forensic oration, the legislative proposal -- is built from these three elementary forms, combined and recombined in ever more complex arrangements.

And remember always: the purpose of these exercises is not to make you clever but to make you effective -- effective in the service of Torah, effective in the service of justice, effective in the service of your fellow human beings. The sage who uses his rhetorical skill to deceive, to manipulate, or to aggrandize himself has betrayed everything we teach here. The sage who uses his skill to illuminate Torah, to persuade toward righteousness, and to bring peace between human beings has fulfilled the deepest purpose of the art.

As the sage taught:

> <span dir="rtl">מרבה תורה מרבה חיים. מרבה ישיבה מרבה חכמה. מרבה עצה מרבה תבונה</span>
>
> *One who increases Torah increases life. One who increases sitting [in study] increases wisdom. One who increases counsel increases understanding.*

And as the sages of old concluded their teaching:

> <span dir="rtl">דרשו חכמים: תלמידי חכמים מרבים שלום בעולם, שנאמר: וכל בניך למודי ה', ורב שלום בניך - אל תקרי בניך אלא בוניך</span>
>
> *The sages expounded: Torah scholars increase peace in the world, as it is said: "All your children shall be taught of the Lord, and great shall be the peace of your children." Read not "your children" [banayikh] but "your builders" [bonayikh].*

May your Torah increase, may your wisdom increase, and may your counsel bring understanding to all who hear you. And may the peace that you build through your mastery of speech -- the peace between disputants, the peace between teacher and student, the peace between Israel and their Father in Heaven -- be your enduring legacy. For everything is in them.[^105]

[^1]: On the relationship between rabbinic and Greco-Roman rhetorical education, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine* (New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1950), esp. 47-82; Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature and Greco-Roman Philosophy* (Leiden: Brill, 1973); Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric: Sophistic Education and Oratory in the Talmud and Midrash* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2018), esp. 1-42; Moeser, *The Anecdote in Mark, the Classical World and the Rabbis* (Sheffield: Sheffield Academic Press, 2002). For the progymnasmata tradition, see the translations and commentary in Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric, Vol. I: The Progymnasmata* (Atlanta: Scholars Press, 1986) and Kennedy, *Progymnasmata: Greek Textbooks of Prose Composition and Rhetoric* (Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 2003).

[^2]: The companion catalog is organized according to Theon's categories as presented in Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, and covers types of chreiai, forms of expression, related forms, and exercises with chreiai, with tannaitic parallels for each category.

[^3]: The phrase *hokhmah of ordered speech* is our reconstruction; no single rabbinic term corresponds exactly to the Greek *rhetorike*. The closest rabbinic concept may be *melitzah* (eloquence) or *seder* (order) applied to discourse. On the absence of a single rabbinic term for rhetoric, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 15-18.

[^4]: Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 1.2 (1355b25-26): "the faculty of observing in any given case the available means of persuasion." Trans. Kennedy (New York: Oxford University Press, 2007).

[^5]: Quintilian, *Institutio Oratoria* 2.15.34: *bene dicendi scientia*. See also Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.5.6 for the definition of rhetoric as *ratio dicendi*.

[^6]: BT Eruvin 13b. The tradition about Rabbi Meir's 150 arguments is also reflected in the general characterization of his rhetorical prowess throughout the Talmud. Fischel (*Rabbinic Literature*, 88-92) discusses this passage in the context of Greco-Roman traditions about the sophist's ability to argue both sides of a case (*in utramque partem*), a central feature of rhetorical training. Cf. Cicero, *De Oratore* 3.21.80.

[^7]: BT Berakhot 27b. The incident that precipitated the deposition of Rabban Gamaliel as *nasi* is narrated in Berakhot 27b-28a. On this narrative as reflecting tensions about rhetorical authority in the academy, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 143-68.

[^8]: BT Berakhot 28a. The tradition preserves divergent numbers (400 or 700 benches) -- itself an illustration of the variant recitation that characterizes oral transmission.

[^9]: The identification of rhetoric with the mechanism of Torah transmission resonates with Daube's argument that rabbinic methods of interpretation were influenced by Hellenistic rhetorical practice: Daube, "Rabbinic Methods of Interpretation and Hellenistic Rhetoric," *Hebrew Union College Annual* 22 (1949): 239-264.

[^10]: The three genres of discourse were canonically formulated by Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 1.3 (1358a36-1358b8), who distinguished judicial (*dikanikon*), deliberative (*symbouleutikon*), and epideictic (*epideiktikon*). Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.4) follows the same threefold division. Our reconstruction maps these onto rabbinic categories: *din* (judgment), *eitzah/hora'ah* (counsel/instruction), and *hesped/derashah/shevach* (eulogy/sermon/praise). On the applicability of this tripartite scheme to rabbinic discourse, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 97-142.

[^11]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:1.

[^12]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:2: "They seat them [the judges] in a semicircle so that they can see one another. And two court scribes stand before them... And from where does the discussion begin? From the eldest." But in capital cases, 4:2 specifies that the discussion begins "from the side" (i.e., from the junior judges), to prevent the juniors from being swayed by the authority of the seniors.

[^13]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:5. The full admonition to witnesses in capital cases is one of the most rhetorically elaborate passages in the Mishnah -- it is, in effect, a persuasive speech designed to impress upon the witnesses the gravity of their testimony. See the discussion in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 108-114.

[^14]: Compare Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.7, on the examination and challenge of witnesses as a rhetorical technique.

[^15]: BT Berakhot 28a. On the significance of this passage for understanding access to rhetorical education in the academy, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories: Narrative Art, Composition, and Culture* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1999), 176-211.

[^16]: The deliberation over the leadership of the academy is itself a case study in deliberative rhetoric: the sages weigh considerations of honor, authority, lineage, and practical consequence. See BT Berakhot 27b-28a.

[^17]: BT Shabbat 13b, 17a. The eighteen decrees enacted at the house of Hananiah ben Hizkiyyah ben Garon are presented in the Talmud as the product of deliberative assembly.

[^18]: BT Gittin 56a-b; ARN A ch. 4. Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai's petition to Vespasian is cast as a deliberative speech in which the sage must persuade a hostile audience to grant a concession.

[^19]: Mishnah Sotah 9:15. This passage, with its formulaic "When X died, Y ceased," is a concentrated sequence of epideictic chreiai -- each one a miniature eulogy. On the literary form, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 45-51.

[^20]: On the *petihta* as a form of epideictic discourse, see Heinemann, "The Proem in the Aggadic Midrashim," in *Studies in Aggadah and Folk-Literature*, ed. Heinemann and Noy, Scripta Hierosolymitana 22 (1971): 100-122.

[^21]: The connection between praise of sages and moral instruction parallels the epideictic function described by Aristotle (*Rhetoric* 1.9) and especially by Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.7), who emphasizes that epideictic speech aims not merely to praise but to instruct the audience in virtue.

[^22]: The five faculties (*partes oratoriae*) are canonically enumerated in *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 1.3: *inventio*, *dispositio*, *elocutio*, *memoria*, *pronuntiatio*. Cicero (*De Inventione* 1.9) provides the same list. Our reconstruction maps these onto rabbinic equivalents: *hamtza'ah* (invention), *seder* (arrangement), *lashon/melitzah* (style), *zikaron* (memory), *ne'imah/hasha'ah* (delivery). For the argument that these faculties were operative in rabbinic pedagogy even if not explicitly theorized, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 43-96.

[^23]: The baraita of Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen middot is found at the opening of Sifra (the introduction, known as *Baraita de-Rabbi Ishmael*). It is recited daily in the liturgy before *Pesukei de-Zimra*. On the middot as tools of legal reasoning comparable to the Greek rhetorical *topoi*, see Daube, "Rabbinic Methods," 239-264; Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 47-68.

[^24]: BT Menachot 29b, in the famous narrative of Moses visiting Rabbi Akiva's classroom. On this passage as reflecting the rabbinic consciousness of interpretive innovation, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 95-98.

[^25]: On the methodological difference between Rabbi Ishmael (who held that "the Torah speaks in human language") and Rabbi Akiva (who derived meaning from every textual detail), see Sifrei Numbers 115; and the systematic treatment in Halbertal, *People of the Book: Canon, Meaning, and Authority* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1997), 25-53.

[^26]: On the *petihta* structure, see Heinemann, "The Proem in the Aggadic Midrashim," and the extensive treatment in Goldberg, "The Petiha as a Redactional Form," *Journal of Jewish Studies* 39 (1988): 169-192. The petihta's structure of distant beginning and convergent ending has been compared to the rhetorical technique of *insinuatio* (indirect approach) described in *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 1.9.

[^27]: On the redactional principles of the Mishnah, see Epstein, *Mevo'ot le-Sifrut ha-Tanna'im* (Jerusalem: Magnes, 1957), and Albeck's introductions to his edition of the Mishnah.

[^28]: BT Pesahim 3b. The principle of conciseness in teaching is also expressed at BT Hullin 63b. Compare Quintilian's emphasis on *brevitas* as a virtue of style (*Inst. Or.* 4.2.43-44).

[^29]: BT Pesahim 3a. The Torah's avoidance of the word *teme'ah* (impure) in favor of the longer phrase *asher einennah tehorah* (which is not pure) is cited as a model for clean language. Cf. the discussion in Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 38-39.

[^30]: On the *tanna* as professional reciter, see BT Sanhedrin 86a; Goodblatt, *Rabbinic Instruction in Sasanian Babylonia* (Leiden: Brill, 1975), 127-168.

[^31]: BT Eruvin 54b. This passage describes the fourfold repetition system of Mosaic teaching and is the locus classicus for rabbinic pedagogical method. Cf. *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.16-24, which prescribes an elaborate system of mnemonic places and images.

[^32]: On numerical lists as mnemonic devices in rabbinic literature, see Alexander, "The Rabbinic Lists of Forbidden Targumim," *Journal of Jewish Studies* 27 (1976): 177-191; and the broader discussion in Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth: Writing and Oral Tradition in Palestinian Judaism, 200 BCE-400 CE* (New York: Oxford University Press, 2001).

[^33]: BT Megillah 32a. Compare the emphasis on delivery (*pronuntiatio*) in *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.19-27 and Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.3.

[^34]: On cantillation as a pedagogical and hermeneutical tool, see Dotan, "The Relative Chronology of Hebrew Vocalization and Accentuation," *Proceedings of the American Academy for Jewish Research* 48 (1981): 87-99.

[^35]: Compare Quintilian's extensive discussion of the orator's physical bearing, gesture, and timing in *Inst. Or.* 11.3.1-184. The parallel between rabbinic and Greco-Roman attention to delivery is discussed in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 73-82.

[^36]: Mishnah Avot 1:1. This opening of Pirkei Avot establishes the chain of transmission as the foundational legitimating structure of the entire rabbinic enterprise. On the chain as rhetorical lineage, see Alexander, "The Oral Law: Tradition of the Fathers," in *The Cambridge History of Judaism*, vol. 3 (1999), 85-97.

[^37]: Mishnah Avot 1:1 (continued). The three directives of the Men of the Great Assembly.

[^38]: Mishnah Avot 1:6 (Yehoshua ben Perachyah).

[^39]: On *shimush talmidei chakhamim* (apprenticeship with sages) as a mode of education, see BT Berakhot 7b: "Greater is service of Torah [scholars] than study of Torah." The comparison with Greco-Roman rhetorical apprenticeship is developed in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 49-56.

[^40]: BT Eruvin 54b. See note 31 above. The system described here parallels the graduated instruction described in Quintilian's ideal rhetorical school (*Inst. Or.* 1.2-3 and 2.1-8).

[^41]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 12.1.1: *vir bonus dicendi peritus* -- "a good man skilled in speaking." This definition, attributed to Cato the Elder, is Quintilian's ideal of the complete orator. The rabbinic parallel -- the *talmid chakham* as one who embodies both learning and moral excellence -- is discussed in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 86-93.

[^43]: The progymnasmata handbooks list between twelve and fourteen preliminary exercises, beginning with fable and narrative and culminating in thesis and the proposal of a law. Our reconstruction covers the first three exercises, which are also the most directly paralleled in rabbinic literature. See Theon, *Progymnasmata*, in Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 61-112; Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata*, in Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 73-88; Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata*, in Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 89-127.

[^44]: 1 Kings 5:12 (4:32 in English Bibles). On Solomon as paradigmatic sage-orator, see the discussion in Stern, *Parables in Midrash: Narrative and Exegesis in Rabbinic Literature* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1991), 10-15.

[^45]: Shir ha-Shirim Rabbah 1:1:8 (and parallel in Midrash Mishlei 1:6). This meta-parable -- a parable about the value of parables -- is discussed in Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 15-18.

[^46]: On Theon's treatment of fable (*mythos*) as the first progymnasmata exercise, see Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 23-28. Theon defines the fable as "a fictitious story giving an image of truth" and identifies several types (Aesopic, Sybaritic, Cilician, Cyprian, Lydian, etc.). The rabbinic *mashal* corresponds most closely to the Aesopic type (animal fable) and to the Libyan type (involving human characters in fictional situations). Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 1) offers a similar classification.

[^47]: Sanhedrin 38b (some editions); cf. the related tradition in Sotah 9:15. On Rabbi Meir's fox fables, see Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 53-58, and Jacobs, "The Fables of Rabbi Meir," *Journal of Semitic Studies* 2 (1957): 147-152.

[^48]: Judges 9:8-15. This fable, spoken by Jotham son of Gideon to the men of Shechem, is the oldest complete fable in the Hebrew Bible and serves as scriptural warrant for the genre. On its rhetorical function, see Niditch, "The 'Sodomite' Theme in Judges 19-20: Family, Community, and Social Disintegration," *Catholic Biblical Quarterly* 44 (1982): 365-378; and Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 7-9.

[^49]: BT Berakhot 61b, in the narrative of Rabbi Akiva's decision to continue teaching Torah publicly despite the Roman prohibition. The fable is attributed to Rabbi Akiva, but its structure and motifs are characteristic of the Aesopic tradition. Fischel (*Rabbinic Literature*, 65-73) discusses the transmission of animal fables between Greco-Roman and rabbinic cultures. See also Theon's discussion of the fable's pedagogical function at *Progymnasmata* 1 (Hock and O'Neil, 24-26).

[^50]: Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael, Bahodesh 5 (on Exodus 20:2). The king parable (*mashal le-melekh basar ve-dam*) is the most characteristic rabbinic parable type and has no exact Greco-Roman equivalent, though it shares features with the Hellenistic *basileia* narrative. See Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 18-25, 188-204.

[^51]: Sifrei Deuteronomy 26. The parable functions as a warning about divine justice, using the familiar scenario of a newcomer learning the rules of a new jurisdiction.

[^52]: On the movement between *mashal* and *nimshal* as the central intellectual operation of midrashic thought, see Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 1-6, 93-107; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1990), 80-92.

[^53]: On Theon's treatment of narrative (*diegema*) as the second progymnasmata exercise, see Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 28-34. Theon defines narrative as "an account of things that happened or might have happened" and identifies four types: mythical, fictional, historical, and civic. The tannaitic *ma'aseh* corresponds most closely to the historical and civic types. Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 2) provides a similar classification.

[^54]: On the legal force of the *ma'aseh* formula, see Friedman, "The Historical Aggadah as a Simple Literary Form," in *Studies in Aggadah, Targum, and Jewish Liturgy in Memory of Joseph Heinemann*, ed. Petuchowski and Fleischer (Jerusalem: Magnes, 1981), 91-104.

[^55]: BT Shabbat 31a. This narrative cluster of three converts who approach first Shammai, then Hillel, is one of the most analyzed passages in rabbinic literature. On its structure as a chreia collection embedded within a narrative frame, see Fischel, "Studies in Cynicism and the Ancient Near East," 380-395; and Moeser, *The Anecdote in Mark*, 112-124.

[^56]: The "sages on a ship" narrative appears in Mishnah Makhshirin 5:9, BT Sanhedrin 32b, and elsewhere. See Hezser, *The Social Structure of the Rabbinic Movement in Roman Palestine* (Tubingen: Mohr Siebeck, 1997), 234-240, on travel narratives as a setting for sage discourse.

[^57]: Tosefta Sukkah 2:1. On the use of *ma'aseh* narratives in the Tosefta to establish legal precedent, see Hauptman, *Rereading the Mishnah: A New Approach to Ancient Jewish Texts* (Tubingen: Mohr Siebeck, 2005), 88-112.

[^58]: Avot de-Rabbi Natan, Version A, ch. 6 (Schechter ed.); cf. BT Ketubot 62b-63a, BT Nedarim 50a. On the biographical narratives of Rabbi Akiva's rise, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 213-238.

[^59]: The six elements of narrative are enumerated by Theon (*Progymnasmata* 2; Hock and O'Neil, 29-30): person (*prosopon*), action (*pragma*), place (*topos*), time (*chronos*), manner (*tropos*), cause (*aitia*). Hermogenes adds a seventh, "material." These correspond to the rabbinic practice of specifying the sage (person), his ruling or action (action), the locale (place), the circumstances (time and manner), and the reasoning (cause).

[^60]: BT Berakhot 61b. On the martyrdom narrative as a chreia and its rhetorical analysis, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 95-108; Boyarin, *Dying for God: Martyrdom and the Making of Christianity and Judaism* (Stanford: Stanford University Press, 1999), 93-126.

[^61]: Compare Theon's exercise instructions for narrative (*Progymnasmata* 2; Hock and O'Neil, 31-34), which include composing, expanding, and condensing narratives, as well as practicing narration from different perspectives.

[^62]: The definition follows Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 34-42): "A chreia is a concise statement or action which is attributed with aptness to some specified character or to something analogous to a character." On the chreia as the central exercise of the progymnasmata, see Hock and O'Neil, 1-22; and the comprehensive treatment in Mack and Robbins, *Patterns of Persuasion in the Gospels* (Sonoma: Polebridge Press, 1989), 1-46.

[^63]: On Pirkei Avot as a chreia collection, see Fischel, "Studies in Cynicism," 372-411; and Moeser, *The Anecdote in Mark*, 85-111. The formula *hu hayah omer* ("he used to say") closely parallels the Greek chreia opening *ephe* ("he used to say").

[^64]: Mishnah Avot 1:2.

[^65]: Mishnah Avot 1:14.

[^66]: Mishnah Avot 2:15.

[^67]: Mishnah Avot 2:7. On this as a classic circumstantial chreia, see Fischel, "Studies in Cynicism," 385-388, who compares it to Diogenes' *idon* (on seeing) formulae.

[^68]: Sifrei Deuteronomy 305; cf. BT Shabbat 33b. Rabbi Shimon bar Yohai's emergence from the cave and his reaction to ordinary life is a circumstantial chreia of the purest type.

[^69]: BT Shabbat 31a. The convert's question and Shammai's response constitute a simple question-and-answer chreia.

[^70]: Mishnah Avot 4:1. On the fourfold structure of this chreia and its parallels to Greco-Roman inquiry chreiai, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 78-84; Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 38-40.

[^71]: Mishnah Eduyot 5:6-7.

[^72]: Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael, Bahodesh 8; cf. Mekhilta Bahodesh 6.

[^73]: BT Shabbat 31a. Fischel ("Studies in Cynicism," 390-393) compares Shammai's physical response to the aggressive action-chreiai attributed to Diogenes and other Cynics.

[^74]: Avot de-Rabbi Natan, Version A, ch. 6 (Schechter ed.).

[^75]: BT Berakhot 61b.

[^76]: Mishnah Berakhot 1:3.

[^77]: Mishnah Ta'anit 3:8. On Honi's circle as both action and speech, see Green, "Palestinian Holy Men: Charismatic Leadership and Rabbinic Tradition," *Aufstieg und Niedergang der romischen Welt* II.19.2 (1979): 619-647.

[^78]: BT Berakhot 61b.

[^79]: On the convergence of word and act in martyrdom narratives as a form of the mixed chreia, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 95-108; and the literary analysis in Boyarin, *Dying for God*, 93-126.

[^80]: Theon enumerates twelve forms of expression for chreiai (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 42-48). Our examples are drawn from the complete catalog in *Tannaitic Parallels to Theon's Chreia Taxonomy*.

[^81]: Mishnah Avot 2:6.

[^82]: Mishnah Avot 1:11 (Avtalyon).

[^83]: BT Bava Metzia 59b (Rabbi Yehoshua, citing Deuteronomy 30:12).

[^84]: Mishnah Avot 3:9 (Rabbi Hanina ben Dosa).

[^85]: Mishnah Avot 1:14 (Hillel).

[^86]: Mishnah Avot 5:17.

[^87]: BT Berakhot 28b (Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai to his students on his deathbed).

[^88]: Mishnah Avot 4:16 (Rabbi Jacob).

[^89]: Mishnah Avot 4:20 (Rabbi/Yehudah ha-Nasi).

[^90]: Mishnah Sukkah 5:4 (Hillel the Elder at the Water Drawing celebration).

[^91]: BT Berakhot 10a.

[^92]: On the combined form as the most complex and richest type of chreia, see the discussion in Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 47-48. The rabbinic parallels are particularly rich in this category, as noted in the companion catalog.

[^93]: Theon describes eight exercises to be performed with chreiai (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 49-60): recitation, inflexion, comment, objection, expansion, condensation, refutation, and confirmation. For the argument that these exercises have systematic parallels in rabbinic pedagogy, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 100-118; Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 143-186; Moeser, *The Anecdote in Mark*, 125-142.

[^94]: On variant recitation in rabbinic literature as parallel to Theon's exercise of recitation, see Fischel, "Studies in Cynicism," 395-400.

[^95]: On inflexion in rabbinic literature, see the examples in the companion catalog, Part IV.2. The fourfold permutations of Avot 5:14-15 are a particularly systematic example of the inflexion exercise applied to chreia-like material.

[^96]: Mishnah Avot 2:9.

[^97]: Mishnah Kelim 3:8; BT Nazir 56b.

[^98]: Mishnah Makkot 1:10.

[^99]: On Avot de-Rabbi Natan as a systematic expansion of Pirkei Avot, see Kister, *Studies in Avot de-Rabbi Nathan: Text, Redaction, and Interpretation* (Jerusalem: Yad Izhak Ben Zvi, 1998); and the discussion in Saldarini, *Scholastic Rabbinism: A Literary Study of the Fathers According to Rabbi Nathan* (Chico, CA: Scholars Press, 1982).

[^100]: BT Makkot 23b-24a (Rabbi Simlai); BT Shabbat 31a (Hillel). On condensation as a rhetorical exercise and its rabbinic parallels, see the companion catalog, Part IV.6.

[^101]: Theon enumerates nine grounds for refutation (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 56-58): obscurity (*asapheia*), pleonasm (*pleonasmos*), ellipsis (*elleipsis*), impossibility (*adunaton*), implausibility (*apithanon*), falsity (*pseudos*), unsuitability (*aprepes*), uselessness (*anopheles*), and shamefulness (*aischron*).

[^102]: The nine categories of refutation map remarkably well onto talmudic modes of challenging a teaching. See Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 155-170, for a detailed analysis.

[^103]: On confirmation (*kataskeuē*) as the complementary exercise to refutation, see Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 58-60). The rabbinic parallel is the *asmakhta* -- the practice of confirming a ruling through scriptural support, logical argument, and appeal to precedent. See the companion catalog, Part IV.8.

[^104]: The companion document, *Tannaitic Parallels to Theon's Chreia Taxonomy: A Comprehensive Catalog*, provides the complete inventory organized according to Theon's categories: types of chreiai (sayings, action, mixed), forms of expression (twelve forms), related forms (maxim/gnome, reminiscence), and exercises (eight exercises), with tannaitic parallels for each category.

[^105]: An allusion to Avot 5:22, Ben Bag Bag's chreia: "Turn it over and turn it over, for everything is in it." The student is here reminded that even the preliminary exercises contain, in embryo, all the skills required for mature rabbinic discourse.

[^42]: Mishnah Avot 5:7. These seven qualities of the sage are, in effect, the seven virtues of the ideal participant in rabbinic discourse. Each quality describes a dimension of rhetorical competence: appropriate silence, respect for the order of discussion, deliberation before response, relevance, accuracy, sequential clarity, intellectual humility, and intellectual honesty. Compare the virtues of the ideal orator enumerated in Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 2.5.1-12 and 12.1-3.
### II.D. Maxim (Klal / Mishnat Chasidim)

#### Definition

My son, we turn now to the maxim -- which we call *klal*, or *mishnat chasidim*, or simply *divrei musar* -- the general principle. And I must teach you first how it differs from the chreia, for the two are close cousins, and the student who confuses them will confuse his audience as well.[^106]

A chreia, as you have learned, is a concise and pointed saying attributed to a specific, named sage. The power of the chreia lies in the union of the teaching with the teacher: "Hillel says..." -- and the name of Hillel lends the saying its authority, its context, its character. Strip away the name, and what remains? A maxim. The maxim states a universal truth without attribution. It speaks not in the voice of any particular sage but in the voice of wisdom itself -- the collective voice of the tradition, the anonymous voice that has no face and therefore cannot be challenged by naming another face against it.

> <span dir="rtl">כלל גדול בתורה</span>
>
> *A great principle in the Torah*

This is the formula of the maxim: not "Rabbi X says" but simply "this is a great principle" -- a truth that belongs to no one and therefore belongs to everyone. The nations of the world call this form *gnome* -- a general, sententious utterance expressing a universal truth about human life or moral duty. Their teacher Theon defines it as "a summary statement, in universal terms, dissuading from something or persuading toward something, or indicating what is the nature of each thing."[^107] But for us, the matter runs deeper than mere persuasion. The maxim, when it circulates without a name, becomes Torah itself -- indistinguishable from the voice of the tradition. And this is precisely its power.

Consider: the unattributed Mishnah (*stam mishnah*) carries greater halakhic weight than the attributed Mishnah. Why? Because when no name is attached, the teaching is treated as the consensus of all the sages -- it has passed through the crucible of collective agreement and emerged as the voice of the tradition itself. The same principle applies to the maxim. A chreia invites debate: "But Rabbi Akiva holds otherwise!" A maxim resists debate, because there is no individual authority to challenge. You cannot say "But Rabbi X disagrees" when no Rabbi X has been named. The maxim speaks with the authority of *klal Yisra'el* -- of all Israel, of all the sages, of the tradition entire.

The student should therefore know: the maxim is not a lesser form than the chreia. It is a *different* form, with different rhetorical properties. The chreia is a weapon of personal authority; the maxim is a weapon of collective authority. The sage who knows when to deploy each has mastered a fundamental distinction in the art of persuasion.[^108]

And understand this well: many of the most powerful teachings in our tradition began as chreiai and became maxims. When a sage's saying is so perfectly formed, so universally true, that it detaches from its author and circulates on its own -- when people quote it without remembering who said it -- it has undergone the transformation from chreia to maxim. This is not a loss but an elevation: the teaching has transcended the individual and become part of the common wisdom. Conversely, when the tradition wishes to give a maxim more force, it sometimes reattaches it to a sage -- transforming it into a chreia. The student who can move a teaching in both directions -- from chreia to maxim and from maxim to chreia -- has mastered the exercise we will practice at the end of this section.

#### Types of Maxims

The student should know the four types of maxims, for each serves a different purpose and requires different handling.[^109]

**The Declarative Maxim.** This type states a fact about the world -- what is, not what should be. It declares how things are, and the force of the declaration is that of observable truth.

> <span dir="rtl">הכל צפוי, והרשות נתונה</span>
>
> *All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given.*[^110]

This teaching is attributed in our Mishnah to Rabbi Akiva (Avot 3:15), and yet it functions as a maxim -- for it states a universal truth about the nature of divine knowledge and human freedom that transcends any individual sage. The attribution to Akiva gives it the force of a chreia; its content gives it the nature of a maxim. The student should observe this dual character: some teachings sit on the boundary between chreia and maxim, and their classification depends on how they are deployed. When you cite "Rabbi Akiva says: All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given," you invoke Akiva's authority. When you cite simply "All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given," you invoke the authority of truth itself.

And consider:

> <span dir="rtl">אין אדם חוטא אלא אם כן נכנס בו רוח שטות</span>
>
> *A person does not sin unless a spirit of folly enters him.*[^111]

This maxim, which circulates in the Talmud as a general principle, declares something about human nature: that sin is a form of temporary madness, that no person in full possession of his reason would willingly transgress. It is declarative -- it tells us what *is*, not what we should *do*. Yet it carries implicit moral force, for if sin is folly, then the wise person will guard against it.

And from the tradition of Proverbs, which the sages cite as authoritative maxims:

> <span dir="rtl">מוסר ה' בני אל תמאס ואל תקוץ בתוכחתו</span>
>
> *My son, do not despise the Lord's discipline, and do not loathe His rebuke.* (Proverbs 3:11)

The sages of the Talmud cite the proverbs of Solomon not as the words of Solomon but as the words of Scripture itself -- that is, as maxims of the highest authority, divine wisdom expressed in universal terms. When the Talmud opens an argument with *dikhtiv* ("as it is written") and quotes Proverbs, it is deploying a maxim -- not a chreia attributed to Solomon, but a universal truth embedded in the sacred text.

**The Hortatory Maxim.** This type urges the listener toward a course of action or away from one. It does not merely describe; it commands.

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the jar but at what is in it.* (Avot 4:20)[^112]

This maxim -- attributed in the Mishnah to Rabbi Meir, though some texts attribute it simply to "Rabbi" -- is hortatory: it tells the listener what to *do*. Do not judge by appearances. Look past the exterior to the interior. The form is imperative: "Do not look..." And because it is imperative, it has the force of a command, not merely an observation.

And consider:

> <span dir="rtl">הוי דן את כל האדם לכף זכות</span>
>
> *Judge every person in the scale of merit.* (Avot 1:6)

This too is hortatory -- a direct command to adopt a certain posture toward one's fellow human beings. The imperative form (*hevei*) marks it as a maxim of exhortation rather than mere declaration.

And from the wisdom tradition that the sages inherited and amplified:

> <span dir="rtl">בכל דרכיך דעהו, והוא יישר אורחותיך</span>
>
> *In all your ways know Him, and He will make your paths straight.* (Proverbs 3:6)

Rabbi bar bar Hannah said in the name of Rabbi Yohanan: this is a small verse upon which all the fundamentals of Torah depend.[^113] The maxim is so compressed, so universal, that it contains within itself the entire program of the religious life. This is the power of the hortatory maxim at its highest: a single sentence that, if followed, encompasses everything.

**The Compound Maxim.** This type combines a statement of fact with a reason, or a command with its justification. It has two parts: the maxim proper and its supporting clause.

> <span dir="rtl">ובטובה שהעולם נדון, והכל לפי רוב המעשה</span>
>
> *And the world is judged by goodness, yet all is according to the majority of deeds.* (Avot 3:15)

Here the maxim contains both a declarative element (the world is judged by goodness) and a qualifying element (yet the judgment follows the majority of one's deeds). The two clauses stand in productive tension: God's judgment is benevolent, *and yet* it takes account of the balance of one's actions. The compound maxim invites the listener to hold two truths simultaneously -- a characteristic mode of rabbinic thought.

And from the tradition of Ben Sira, whose maxims the rabbis preserved even as they debated his canonical status:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תשלח ידך בקערה עם אדם צר עין, כי אתה יודע שהוא מתחרט</span>
>
> *Do not reach into the dish with a stingy man, for you know that he begrudges it.*

The command ("do not reach") is accompanied by its reason ("for you know that he begrudges"). This is the compound maxim in its classic form: imperative plus rationale. The student who wishes to make a maxim persuasive should learn to compose in compound form, for the maxim that gives its reason is harder to dismiss than the maxim that simply commands.[^114]

**The Double Maxim (Thesis and Antithesis).** This type presents two opposing truths in a balanced structure -- a positive statement and its counterpart, or a thesis followed by its antithetical complement.

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין קמח אין תורה, אם אין תורה אין קמח</span>
>
> *If there is no flour, there is no Torah; if there is no Torah, there is no flour.* (Avot 3:17)

This is the double maxim par excellence: two conditional statements, each the mirror image of the other, together expressing a truth that neither alone could capture. Material sustenance requires spiritual purpose; spiritual pursuit requires material sustenance. The balanced structure -- the thesis and antithesis held in equipoise -- is itself the meaning. A single-sided statement ("Without flour there is no Torah") would be partial; the double form makes it complete.

And consider the magnificent sequence of double maxims in Avot 2:6:

> <span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר: אין בור ירא חטא, ולא עם הארץ חסיד, ולא הביישן למד, ולא הקפדן מלמד, ולא כל המרבה בסחורה מחכים</span>
>
> *He [Hillel] used to say: A boor cannot be sin-fearing, an ignoramus cannot be pious, a bashful person cannot learn, an impatient person cannot teach, and not all who engage excessively in commerce grow wise.*

Though attributed to Hillel (making it formally a chreia), this teaching functions as a sequence of declarative maxims -- each stating a universal truth about the relationship between character and achievement. Taken together, they form a portrait of the ideal student and teacher that transcends Hillel's individual authority.

And the great double maxim that governs all rabbinic discourse on dispute:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים. ושאינה לשם שמים, אין סופה להתקיים</span>
>
> *Every dispute that is for the sake of Heaven will in the end endure. And one that is not for the sake of Heaven will not in the end endure.* (Avot 5:17)

Thesis: dispute for the sake of Heaven endures. Antithesis: dispute not for the sake of Heaven does not endure. The double structure forces the listener to distinguish between two kinds of conflict -- and to recognize that the mere presence of disagreement tells us nothing about its value. Only its purpose reveals its worth.

#### The Relationship Between Chreia and Maxim

The student must understand this relationship thoroughly, for it illuminates the deeper workings of tradition.

A chreia becomes a maxim when the attribution is dropped. This happens naturally over time: a sage's teaching becomes so well known, so thoroughly absorbed into the common wisdom, that people quote it without remembering its source. The teaching has been *anonymized* by the tradition -- not through negligence but through a kind of elevation. It has ceased to be *this sage's opinion* and has become *what everyone knows*.

Conversely, a maxim becomes a chreia when the tradition reattaches it to a sage. This happens when the tradition wishes to give a floating maxim more rhetorical weight by anchoring it in personal authority. "Who is wise? One who learns from every person" circulates as both a chreia (attributed to Ben Zoma) and as a maxim (cited without attribution in various contexts). In its chreia form, it carries the authority of Ben Zoma's personal wisdom; in its maxim form, it carries the authority of universal truth. The sage who deploys it may choose either form depending on his rhetorical needs.[^115]

And there is a third transformation the student should know. Sometimes a maxim is *extracted* from within a longer teaching. The full teaching of Rabbi Akiva in Avot 3:15 reads:

> <span dir="rtl">הכל צפוי, והרשות נתונה, ובטובה העולם נדון, והכל לפי רוב המעשה</span>
>
> *All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given; and the world is judged by goodness, yet all is according to the majority of deeds.*

This is, in its full form, a chreia of Rabbi Akiva -- a compound teaching attributed to a named sage. But the opening clause -- "All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given" -- has detached from the rest and circulates independently as a maxim, perhaps the most famous philosophical maxim in all of rabbinic literature. The process of extraction -- pulling a single clause from a longer teaching and allowing it to circulate as an independent maxim -- is itself a rhetorical exercise that the student should practice.

#### Examples from the Tradition

Let the student study these maxims carefully, noting in each case the type, the source, and the rhetorical effect.

**Example 1: The Four Questions of Ben Zoma**

> <span dir="rtl">איזהו חכם? הלומד מכל אדם, שנאמר: מכל מלמדי השכלתי כי עדותיך שיחה לי. איזהו גיבור? הכובש את יצרו, שנאמר: טוב ארך אפיים מגיבור ומושל ברוחו מלוכד עיר. איזהו עשיר? השמח בחלקו, שנאמר: יגיע כפיך כי תאכל אשריך וטוב לך. איזהו מכובד? המכבד את הבריות, שנאמר: כי מכבדי אכבד ובוזי יקלו</span>
>
> *Who is wise? One who learns from every person, as it says: "From all my teachers I grew wise, for Your testimonies are my meditation" (Psalm 119:99). Who is mighty? One who conquers his inclination, as it says: "Better is one slow to anger than a mighty man, and one who rules his spirit than one who captures a city" (Proverbs 16:32). Who is rich? One who is happy with his portion, as it says: "When you eat the labor of your hands, happy are you and it is well with you" (Psalm 128:2). Who is honored? One who honors other people, as it says: "For those who honor Me I will honor, and those who despise Me will be lightly esteemed" (1 Samuel 2:30).* (Avot 4:1)

This teaching is formally a chreia -- attributed to Ben Zoma. But each of its four components functions as an independent maxim: "Who is wise? One who learns from every person" is a self-sufficient, universal truth that requires no attribution to carry conviction. The student should observe how each maxim redefines a common term: wisdom is not erudition but the willingness to learn; might is not physical strength but self-mastery; wealth is not abundance but contentment; honor is not being honored but honoring others. Each redefinition overturns conventional understanding and replaces it with a moral definition. This is the maxim at its most powerful: a single sentence that reorganizes the listener's entire way of seeing the world.[^116]

**Example 2: The Jar and Its Contents**

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו. יש קנקן חדש מלא ישן, וישן שאפילו חדש אין בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the jar but at what is in it. There is a new jar full of old [wine], and an old [jar] that does not even have new [wine] in it.* (Avot 4:20)

This maxim has two parts: the imperative ("do not look...") and the illustrative expansion ("there is a new jar..."). The expansion clarifies the imperative by providing concrete examples: a young scholar may be full of ancient wisdom, while an old scholar may be empty even of recent learning. The image of the jar and the wine is itself a miniature mashal embedded within a maxim -- proof that the forms we have studied are not separate containers but interpenetrating modes of expression. The student should note how the expansion rescues the maxim from mere platitude: "Don't judge by appearances" is a commonplace; "a new jar full of old wine" is vivid, specific, and memorable.

**Example 3: Proverbial Maxims Cited in the Talmud**

The sages frequently cite maxims from the Book of Proverbs as authoritative principles, deploying them in legal and aggadic contexts with the force of established wisdom. Consider how the Talmud uses the following:

> <span dir="rtl">בפרוע פרעות בישראל בהתנדב עם ברכו ה'</span>
>
> *When leaders lead in Israel, when the people volunteer, bless the Lord.* (Judges 5:2)

And:

> <span dir="rtl">חנוך לנער על פי דרכו, גם כי יזקין לא יסור ממנה</span>
>
> *Train a child according to his way; even when he grows old he will not depart from it.* (Proverbs 22:6)

This maxim from Proverbs is cited in various talmudic contexts to support the principle of individualized education -- that each student must be taught according to his own nature and capacity. It circulates as a maxim of universal educational wisdom, detached from any individual sage, bearing the authority of Scripture itself.[^117]

**Example 4: Anonymous Maxims of Avot Chapter 5**

The fifth chapter of Pirkei Avot is remarkable for its concentration of anonymous maxims -- teachings that carry no individual attribution:

> <span dir="rtl">בעשרה מאמרות נברא העולם. ומה תלמוד לומר? והלא במאמר אחד יכול להבראות? אלא להיפרע מן הרשעים שמאבדין את העולם שנברא בעשרה מאמרות, וליתן שכר טוב לצדיקים שמקיימין את העולם שנברא בעשרה מאמרות</span>
>
> *By ten utterances the world was created. And what does this teach us? Could it not have been created by one utterance? Rather, it is to exact punishment from the wicked who destroy the world that was created by ten utterances, and to give good reward to the righteous who sustain the world that was created by ten utterances.* (Avot 5:1)

No sage's name is attached. The teaching speaks in the voice of the tradition itself -- a maxim about the structure of creation that carries implicit moral force. The student should observe that even this seemingly theological declaration has a hortatory dimension: because the world was created with ten utterances (and therefore at great "expense"), the person who destroys it is more culpable and the person who sustains it more meritorious.

#### Exercises

The student should practice the following:

**First exercise: Transform chreia to maxim.** Take a chreia and strip it to its maxim. Begin with:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי טרפון אומר: היום קצר, והמלאכה מרובה, והפועלים עצלים, והשכר הרבה, ובעל הבית דוחק</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tarfon says: The day is short, the work is great, the workers are lazy, the reward is great, and the Master of the house is pressing.* (Avot 2:15)

Strip away "Rabbi Tarfon says." What remains? "The day is short, the work is great..." -- a maxim about the human condition that requires no authority beyond its own self-evident truth. Now observe: does the teaching gain or lose by the removal of the attribution? It loses the personal weight of Rabbi Tarfon's authority; it gains the universality of an anonymous truth. The student should compose a brief essay explaining which form is more appropriate for which rhetorical context.

**Second exercise: Transform maxim to chreia.** Take a maxim and compose a chreia around it. Begin with the anonymous maxim:

> <span dir="rtl">אין בור ירא חטא</span>
>
> *A boor cannot be sin-fearing.*

Now compose a narrative setting: a sage encounters a situation that prompts this remark. Who is the sage? What does he see or hear? What circumstance draws this maxim from his lips? The student should compose at least two different narrative frames for the same maxim, observing how the setting changes the maxim's meaning and force.

**Third exercise: Extract maxim from compound teaching.** Take a compound teaching and extract from it the maxim that can stand alone. Practice with:

> <span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר: לא עליך המלאכה לגמור, ולא אתה בן חורין ליבטל ממנה. אם למדת תורה הרבה נותנין לך שכר הרבה. ונאמן הוא בעל מלאכתך שישלם לך שכר פעולתך. ודע מתן שכרן של צדיקים לעתיד לבוא</span>
>
> *He [Rabbi Tarfon] used to say: It is not your obligation to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it. If you have learned much Torah, they give you great reward. And the Master of your work is trustworthy to pay you the reward of your labor. And know that the giving of reward to the righteous is in the world to come.* (Avot 2:16)

The extractable maxim is: "It is not your obligation to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it." This single sentence circulates independently as one of the most quoted maxims in all of Jewish tradition -- far more widely known than the sentences that follow it. The student should explain why this particular clause, and not the others, has achieved maxim status.

**Fourth exercise: Compose original maxims in the four types.** The student should compose one maxim of each type -- declarative, hortatory, compound, and double -- on a single theme (for example: the nature of friendship, the value of silence, or the obligation of communal responsibility). Each maxim should be concise enough to be memorized in a single hearing.[^118]

---

### II.E. Refutation (Teshuvah / Pirka)

#### Definition

My son, we come now to a harder exercise -- the exercise of refutation. This is not mere disagreement. A child can disagree. A fool can object. But to *refute* -- to challenge and dismantle a teaching systematically, using established categories of attack, marshaling evidence and argument with discipline -- that is the work of a trained mind.

In the bet midrash, we call this *teshuvah* -- an answer, a counter-argument, a response that undermines the position it addresses. We also call it *pirka* -- literally, a dismantling, a taking-apart. The Greek teachers call it *anaskeuē* -- the tearing down of a proposition -- and they teach it as a formal exercise, the fifth in the sequence of preliminary training.[^119]

The student should know: the exercise of refutation is not taught in order to make you a destroyer of teachings. It is taught to make you a builder of *stronger* teachings. For a teaching that has not been tested by refutation is a teaching that has not been proven. And a sage who cannot anticipate the objections to his own position is a sage who will be exposed by his opponents. The Talmud itself teaches this principle through the story of Rabbi Yohanan and Resh Lakish, which we recounted at the beginning of our studies: when Resh Lakish died, Rabbi Yohanan wept -- not because he had lost a friend who agreed with him, but because he had lost a friend who *challenged* him. The challenger is more valuable than the supporter, for the challenger forces you to strengthen your position or abandon it.

> <span dir="rtl">חרב אל הבדים ונואלו</span>
>
> *A sword upon the solitary ones, and they become foolish.* (Jeremiah 50:36)

And this verse the sages expound:

> <span dir="rtl">חרב על שונאיהם של תלמידי חכמים שיושבים בד בבד ועוסקים בתורה, ולא עוד אלא שמטפשים</span>
>
> *A sword upon the enemies of the Torah scholars [a euphemism for the scholars themselves] who sit alone, each by himself, and engage in Torah -- and moreover, they grow foolish.*[^120]

The sage who studies alone, without a partner to refute his arguments, becomes foolish -- not because he lacks knowledge, but because his knowledge is untested. His arguments have never been challenged and therefore have never been strengthened. He is like a sword that has never been sharpened: it has the form of a weapon but not the edge. The exercise of refutation is the whetstone upon which all argument is sharpened.

#### Categories of Refutation

The student should know the six grounds upon which a teaching may be challenged and dismantled. I will teach them to you in order, and for each one I will show you how the sages of the bet midrash employ it.[^121]

**First: From the Unclear (*mi-toch ha-safeq*).** If a teaching is ambiguous -- if it can be understood in more than one way, and the correct meaning is not determinable -- then it can be refuted on grounds of unclarity. The challenge takes the form: "What do you mean by this? If you mean X, then Y follows, which is problematic. If you mean Z, then W follows, which is equally problematic."

The Talmud's standard formula for this mode of refutation is:

> <span dir="rtl">היכי דמי? אילימא... אלא...</span>
>
> *What is the case? If you say [it means X]... then [difficulty]... Rather [it must mean Y]...*

This formula -- "What is the case? If you say..." -- is the refutation from unclarity in its purest talmudic form. The challenger demonstrates that the teaching, as stated, is ambiguous, and that each possible reading leads to a difficulty. The teaching is thereby undermined -- not because it is wrong, but because it is unclear.[^122]

**Second: From the Incredible (*mi-toch she-eino ra'ui le-he'amen*).** If a teaching makes a claim that strains belief -- if it asserts something that, while not strictly impossible, is so improbable as to be unworthy of credence -- then it can be refuted on grounds of incredibility.

The Talmud's formula for this challenge is often:

> <span dir="rtl">אפשר...?</span>
>
> *Is it possible that...?*

This devastating question -- "Is it possible?" -- is the refutation from the incredible. It does not claim that the teaching is logically impossible; it claims that it is so implausible as to require extraordinary proof. Consider the Talmud's challenge to certain aggadic narratives:

> <span dir="rtl">אפשר בימי יהושע לא נכבשה ובימי דוד נכבשה?</span>
>
> *Is it possible that in the days of Joshua it was not conquered but in the days of David it was conquered?*

The challenge assumes that what was not accomplished by the greater figure (Joshua) could not have been accomplished by the lesser (David, in this context). The teaching is refuted by exposing its implausibility -- not its impossibility, but its failure to cohere with what we know about the relative stature of the figures involved.

And consider the famous passage in BT Gittin 56b-57a, where the Talmud recounts extraordinary stories about the destruction of the Temple. At various points, the text pauses to ask: "Is it possible?" -- applying the refutation from the incredible to its own narratives. This self-reflective quality -- the tradition interrogating its own stories -- is characteristic of the rabbinic approach to refutation. Even the sacred narratives are not exempt from challenge.[^123]

**Third: From the Impossible (*mi-toch she-i efshar*).** If a teaching asserts something that is logically or physically impossible, it can be dismissed outright. This is the strongest form of refutation, for it claims not merely that the teaching is implausible or unclear but that it cannot be true under any interpretation.

The Talmud employs this form when it identifies a logical contradiction:

> <span dir="rtl">תרתי דסתרי אהדדי</span>
>
> *These two [statements] contradict each other.*

When two elements of a teaching are shown to be mutually exclusive -- when accepting one requires denying the other -- the teaching collapses under the weight of its own internal contradiction. This is the refutation from impossibility: the teaching cannot be true because it cannot even be coherent.

The formal *teyuvta* -- the "refutation" that conclusively defeats a position in the Talmud -- often operates on this ground:

> <span dir="rtl">תיובתא דרב... תיובתא</span>
>
> *It is a refutation of Rav... It is a [decisive] refutation.*

When the Talmud declares "teyuvta" -- repeating the word for emphasis -- it signals that the refutation is conclusive. The position has been shown to be impossible in light of a contradicting source, and no further defense is available. The doubled word is itself a rhetorical device: the repetition signals finality.[^124]

**Fourth: From the Inconsistent (*mi-toch ha-stirah*).** If a teaching contradicts another established teaching, or contradicts itself, it can be challenged on grounds of inconsistency. This differs from impossibility in that the contradiction is not internal to the teaching itself but arises from its relationship to other accepted principles.

The standard formula is:

> <span dir="rtl">ורמינהו</span>
>
> *And we raise a contradiction [from another source]!*

This single Aramaic word -- *u-reminhu* -- is one of the most common refutation formulas in the Talmud. It introduces a teaching from another source that appears to contradict the teaching under discussion. The challenged party must then reconcile the two sources -- distinguish between their circumstances, limit the scope of one, or acknowledge the contradiction and choose between them.[^125]

And there is another common formula:

> <span dir="rtl">מיתיבי</span>
>
> *We raise an objection [from a baraita]!*

The objection (*tiyuvta*) raised through *mitivi* is a formal refutation from inconsistency: a tannaitic source is cited that contradicts the amoraic ruling under discussion. The defendant must then harmonize the sources or concede.

**Fifth: From the Unfitting (*mi-toch she-eino na'eh*).** If a teaching, while technically possible and even consistent, is morally or aesthetically inappropriate -- if it attributes unworthy behavior to a revered figure, or leads to a conclusion that violates the spirit of the tradition -- it can be challenged on grounds of unfittingness.

The Talmud's formula:

> <span dir="rtl">חס ושלום! לא עשה כן</span>
>
> *God forbid! He did not do so.*

This formula -- "God forbid!" -- is the refutation from the unfitting in its most characteristic form. It refuses to accept a teaching because the teaching would impute something unworthy to a sage or to God. The objection is not logical but moral: "This cannot be right because it would be beneath the dignity of its subject."

Consider how the Talmud handles aggadic narratives that seem to attribute moral failings to biblical heroes. When a text suggests that a patriarch sinned, the sages often respond: "Whoever says that X sinned is in error" -- refusing the literal reading not on textual grounds but on grounds of unfittingness. King David, for example, is defended against the literal reading of the Bathsheba narrative: "Whoever says David sinned is in error, for it says: 'And David was successful in all his ways, and the Lord was with him.'"[^126] The refutation operates on the principle that a teaching which degrades a sacred figure must be resisted, even if its textual basis appears sound.

**Sixth: From the Inexpedient (*mi-toch she-eino mo'il*).** If a teaching, while true, leads to impractical or harmful consequences -- if its application would produce results that no reasonable person would accept -- it can be challenged on grounds of inexpediency.

The Talmud's formula:

> <span dir="rtl">אם כן, לא הנחת חיים לכל בריה</span>
>
> *If so, you have not left life for any creature!*

This formula -- "If so, you have left no room for living!" -- is the refutation from the inexpedient. It concedes that the teaching may be logically sound but argues that its practical consequences are intolerable. A ruling that, if followed, would make life impossible for the community must be rejected, regardless of its theoretical merit. And similarly:

> <span dir="rtl">אין גוזרין גזירה על הצבור אלא אם כן רוב צבור יכולין לעמוד בה</span>
>
> *We do not impose a decree upon the community unless the majority of the community can bear it.*[^127]

This principle is itself a standing refutation against any proposed ruling that is too burdensome: the very fact that the community cannot sustain it is grounds for its rejection. The inexpedient is the impermissible.

#### Examples from the Tradition

**Example 1: The Formal Objection (*Kushya*)**

The *kushya* -- the objection, the difficulty -- is the basic unit of refutation in the Talmud. Every *sugya* (talmudic discussion) is structured around a cycle of statement, objection, and resolution. The student should study the following example of how a teaching is challenged from multiple grounds:

> <span dir="rtl">תנן: הרואה מקום שנעשו בו ניסים לישראל אומר: ברוך שעשה ניסים לאבותינו במקום הזה</span>
>
> *We learned [in the Mishnah]: One who sees a place where miracles were performed for Israel says: Blessed is He who performed miracles for our ancestors in this place.*

The Talmud then asks: Which miracles? Only those recorded in Scripture, or also those that occurred after the biblical period? Only those that affected all of Israel, or also private miracles? Each question is a refutation from the unclear -- probing the ambiguity of the teaching to determine its precise scope.

**Example 2: "From Where Do You Derive This?" in the Mekhilta**

The Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael is a treasury of formal refutation. Its characteristic challenge is:

> <span dir="rtl">מניין אתה אומר?</span>
>
> *From where do you derive this?*

This is not a simple request for information; it is a formal refutation from insufficiency. The challenger demands that the proponent provide his scriptural basis -- and if the basis is found wanting, the teaching collapses. The formula "From where do you derive this?" puts the burden of proof on the speaker: you have made a claim, now support it or withdraw it.

Consider the Mekhilta's treatment of the laws of Passover (Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael, Pischa 1-18), where virtually every halakhic statement is challenged with "From where do you derive this?" and the response invariably begins with a scriptural proof. The entire structure is a cycle of assertion, challenge, and confirmation -- with the refutation exercise driving the process forward at every step.

**Example 3: "I Do Not Know What This Teaches Us"**

A distinctive rabbinic form of refutation is the formula:

> <span dir="rtl">איני יודע מה מלמדנו</span>
>
> *I do not know what this teaches us.*

This formula -- attributed to various sages -- is used to dismiss a proposed proof-text. The challenger does not dispute the text itself; he disputes the *relevance* of the text to the proposition it is meant to support. "I accept that this verse exists, but I do not see how it proves what you claim it proves." This is a refutation of the connection between evidence and conclusion -- a challenge to the logic of the proof rather than to the truth of the premise.[^128]

**Example 4: The Challenge to Aggadic Narratives**

In BT Gittin 56b-57a, the Talmud recounts a series of extraordinary narratives about the destruction of the Temple and its aftermath. At several points, the narrative strain becomes so great that the text itself pauses to question its own claims:

> <span dir="rtl">ומי יימר?</span>
>
> *And who says [this is true]?*

This self-interrogation -- the text challenging its own narrative -- is the refutation from the incredible applied reflexively. The Talmud acknowledges that some stories, even within the sacred tradition, strain credulity. The student should observe that refutation is not an attack on the tradition from outside; it is an activity the tradition performs on itself, internally, as a mode of self-purification.

#### Exercises

The student should practice the following:

**First exercise: Six-ground refutation.** Take the following teaching and refute it from each of the six grounds:

> <span dir="rtl">יפה שעה אחת בתשובה ומעשים טובים בעולם הזה מכל חיי העולם הבא</span>
>
> *One hour of repentance and good deeds in this world is better than all the life of the world to come.* (Avot 4:17)

From the unclear: What does "better" mean here? Better in what sense? From the incredible: Is it really possible that a single hour of mortal life surpasses all of eternal bliss? From the impossible: Does this not contradict the teaching that the world to come is the ultimate reward? From the inconsistent: Does this not contradict the second half of the same mishnah, which says "One hour of spiritual bliss in the world to come is better than all the life of this world"? From the unfitting: Would it not diminish the world to come to subordinate it to a mere hour? From the inexpedient: If this world is superior, why strive for the world to come at all?

The student should compose a refutation on each ground and then, as a further exercise, compose an answer to each refutation -- practicing both sides of the dialectic.

**Second exercise: Identify the category.** Read through a full *sugya* of the Talmud and identify each objection by its category. Is the objection from the unclear? From the inconsistent?
