From the impossible? From the unfitting? The student should discover that the Talmud's objections, though not explicitly labeled by category, fall naturally into the six types described above.

**Third exercise: Compose a formal refutation.** Choose a maxim from Pirkei Avot and compose a systematic refutation of it, using at least four of the six grounds. Then compose a response to each of your own objections. The sage who can refute his own position and then restore it is the sage who has truly mastered the exercise.[^129]

---

### II.F. Confirmation (Qiyyum / Re'ayah)

#### Definition

My son, the exercise of confirmation stands as the companion and counterpart of refutation. If refutation is the art of tearing down, confirmation is the art of building up. If refutation tests a teaching by attacking it, confirmation tests a teaching by supporting it -- assembling the proofs, the precedents, the arguments, and the scriptural foundations that establish it as true, as binding, and as worthy of the community's acceptance.[^130]

We call this exercise *qiyyum* -- establishment, making something stand firm. We also call it *re'ayah* -- proof, evidence. The Greek teachers call it *kataskeuē* -- the construction and confirmation of a proposition.[^131] And the student should know: confirmation is not merely the opposite of refutation. It is a distinct skill with its own methods, its own categories, and its own disciplines.

For refutation asks: "Why should we not believe this?" Confirmation asks: "Why should we believe this?" The two questions, though related, require different kinds of evidence, different modes of argument, and different rhetorical strategies. The student who is skilled only in refutation is a destroyer who cannot build; the student who is skilled only in confirmation is a builder who cannot test. The complete sage commands both.

> <span dir="rtl">מנא הני מילי? דכתיב...</span>
>
> *From where do we know this? As it is written...*

This formula -- *mina hani milli? dikhtiv...* -- is the single most common confirmation formula in the Babylonian Talmud. It opens the exercise of confirmation: a teaching has been stated, and now the Talmud asks for its scriptural basis. The answer -- "As it is written..." -- provides the proof. The entire structure is a miniature confirmation exercise: proposition, demand for proof, provision of proof.

The student should understand that in our tradition, the primary method of confirmation is the scriptural proof (*ra'ayah min ha-katuv*). This is because the Torah is the supreme authority: a teaching that can be grounded in Scripture stands on the firmest possible foundation. But Scripture is not the only mode of confirmation, and the student must learn all the methods.

#### Methods of Confirmation

There are six methods of confirmation, and they correspond inversely to the six grounds of refutation. Where refutation attacks from the unclear, confirmation defends through the clear. Where refutation attacks from the incredible, confirmation defends through the probable. And so through all six categories.[^132]

**First: From the Clear (*mi-toch ha-barur*).** A teaching is confirmed when its meaning is plain, its terms are unambiguous, and its application is evident. The clearest form of confirmation is the explicit scriptural verse:

> <span dir="rtl">שנאמר</span>
>
> *As it says...*

When the Talmud introduces a proof with *she-ne'emar* ("as it says") and quotes a verse that directly and unambiguously supports the proposition, this is confirmation from the clear. The verse and the teaching match so perfectly that no interpretation is required -- the support is self-evident.

Consider:

> <span dir="rtl">מניין שאין עומדין להתפלל אלא מתוך כובד ראש? שנאמר: עבדו את ה' ביראה</span>
>
> *From where do we know that one should not stand to pray except with a solemn disposition? As it says: "Serve the Lord with reverence" (Psalm 2:11).*[^133]

The verse directly states the principle: worship requires reverence. The teaching and the proof are transparent to each other.

**Second: From the Probable (*mi-toch ha-karov*).** A teaching is confirmed when, though not explicitly stated, it follows plausibly from what is known. This is the domain of logical inference -- of the *kal va-chomer* (a fortiori reasoning), the *gezerah shavah* (argument from analogy), and the other hermeneutical rules.

> <span dir="rtl">קל וחומר</span>
>
> *A fortiori*

The *kal va-chomer* is the first of Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen rules and the most fundamental tool of probable confirmation. If a lighter case has property X, then the heavier case certainly has property X. This is not proof from explicit statement but proof from reasonable inference -- and it is the backbone of halakhic reasoning:

> <span dir="rtl">ומה צפרדעים שאין מצוות עליהם קידשו שם שמים, ישראל שמצוות עליהם על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *If frogs, which are not commanded, sanctified God's name [by entering the ovens of Egypt], Israel, who are commanded -- how much more so!*[^134]

This argument -- drawn from the plague of frogs -- confirms the obligation of Israel to sanctify God's name by reasoning from the lesser case (frogs, who have no commandments) to the greater case (Israel, who do). The proof is not from an explicit verse but from a logical inference that the listener finds compelling.

**Third: From the Possible (*mi-toch ha-efshar*).** A teaching is confirmed when it can be shown that there is no barrier to its truth -- that it is consistent with the laws of nature, the structures of logic, and the framework of the tradition. This is the weakest form of confirmation -- it does not prove that the teaching *is* true, only that it *could be* true -- but it is an important first step in establishing a proposition.

The Talmud sometimes employs this mode when responding to a challenge:

> <span dir="rtl">מי לא עסקינן דאיכא...?</span>
>
> *Are we not dealing with a case where there is...?*

The response does not prove the proposition; it shows that there is a plausible scenario under which it holds. This shifts the burden back to the challenger: you have raised an objection, but I have shown that the teaching is at least possible. Now you must show that it is impossible, not merely that you find it unlikely.

**Fourth: From the Consistent (*mi-toch ha-mathim*).** A teaching is confirmed when it can be shown to be consistent with other established teachings -- when it fits harmoniously into the existing framework of law and tradition.

The Talmud's formula:

> <span dir="rtl">תניא נמי הכי</span>
>
> *A baraita also teaches thus.*

When the Talmud cites a baraita (tannaitic teaching not included in the Mishnah) that agrees with the proposition under discussion, it is confirming through consistency: the teaching is not an isolated opinion but is corroborated by another strand of the tradition. The more sources that can be shown to agree, the stronger the confirmation.

And similarly:

> <span dir="rtl">לימא מסייע ליה</span>
>
> *Let us say this supports him.*

The formula *leima mesaye'a leih* introduces a supporting source -- a teaching from elsewhere in the tradition that points in the same direction. This is cumulative confirmation: each additional supporting source strengthens the proposition.[^135]

**Fifth: From the Fitting (*mi-toch ha-na'eh*).** A teaching is confirmed when it is morally appropriate -- when it is consistent with the character of the tradition, the dignity of its subject, and the values of the community.

Consider how Rabbi Akiva confirms the principle of "Love your neighbor as yourself":

> <span dir="rtl">ואהבת לרעך כמוך -- אמר רבי עקיבא: זה כלל גדול בתורה</span>
>
> *"Love your neighbor as yourself" (Leviticus 19:18) -- Rabbi Akiva said: This is a great principle in the Torah.*[^136]

The confirmation here is not from logical inference or from corroborating sources; it is from fittingness. The proposition -- that love of one's fellow is the central principle of the Torah -- is confirmed because it *accords* with what we know the Torah to be. It is fitting that a system devoted to justice and mercy should have at its center a commandment of love. The confirmation operates on moral and aesthetic grounds: this is what the Torah *should* be about, and therefore this teaching is correct.

And in the Sifra (Sifra Qedoshim 4:12), this confirmation is developed at length. Rabbi Akiva's declaration is set against the alternative formulation of Ben Azzai, who proposes a different "great principle":

> <span dir="rtl">זה ספר תולדות אדם -- בן עזאי אומר: זה כלל גדול מזה</span>
>
> *"This is the book of the generations of Adam" (Genesis 5:1) -- Ben Azzai says: This is an even greater principle than that.*

Ben Azzai confirms the dignity of every human being by grounding it in creation rather than in the commandment to love. Both sages are engaged in confirmation from fittingness: each argues that his principle is the most appropriate, the most worthy, the most consonant with the deepest values of the tradition. The student should observe that confirmation from fittingness often involves not merely proving that a teaching is true but arguing that it is the *most appropriate* among several possible truths.

**Sixth: From the Expedient (*mi-toch ha-to'elet*).** A teaching is confirmed when its practical consequences are beneficial -- when following it leads to good outcomes for the individual and the community.

> <span dir="rtl">מפני מה נקבעה הלכה כבית הלל? מפני שנוחין ועלובין היו</span>
>
> *Why was the halakhah established according to Beit Hillel? Because they were gentle and humble.*[^137]

The halakhah follows Beit Hillel not because their arguments were logically superior (Beit Shammai's arguments were often sharper) but because the consequences of following Beit Hillel's approach -- a tradition of gentleness, humility, and consideration for the opposing view -- are more beneficial for the community. This is confirmation from expediency: the teaching is established because its practical fruits are good.

#### The Asmakhta: Scripture as Confirmation

The student must pay special attention to the *asmakhta* -- the practice of supporting a rabbinic teaching with a scriptural verse, even when the teaching is known to be of rabbinic origin rather than biblical derivation.

> <span dir="rtl">אסמכתא בעלמא</span>
>
> *It is merely a support [not a genuine derivation].*

The asmakhta is not a proof that the teaching originates in Scripture; it is a *confirmation* -- a demonstration that the teaching is consistent with Scripture, even though it was originated by the sages. The student should understand the distinction: a genuine derivation (*derashah*) claims that the teaching is embedded in the text and extracted through interpretation. An asmakhta claims only that the text lends support -- that Scripture and the rabbinic teaching point in the same direction. The asmakhta is confirmation from consistency: the teaching, though rabbinic in origin, is shown to harmonize with the sacred text.[^138]

This is a subtle but important distinction, and the student who fails to grasp it will misunderstand much of talmudic reasoning. Not every verse cited in support of a teaching is a source; some are merely supports. The sage who conflates the two will attribute biblical authority to teachings that have only rabbinic authority -- an error that can have significant practical consequences.

#### Examples from the Tradition

**Example 1: Confirmation Through Multiple Proofs in the Mekhilta**

The Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael frequently confirms a single teaching through multiple scriptural proofs, arranged in ascending order of strength:

> <span dir="rtl">מניין... שנאמר... ועוד שנאמר... ואף... שנאמר...</span>
>
> *From where [do we know this]? As it says... And further, it says... And also... as it says...*

The structure -- "from where? As it says... and further, it says... and also, it says..." -- builds a cumulative case, piling proof upon proof until the conclusion becomes irresistible. The student should observe that the order matters: the weakest proof comes first, and the strongest last, so that the argument builds toward its climax. This is the principle of arrangement applied to confirmation: the skilled advocate saves his best evidence for the conclusion.

**Example 2: "From Where Do We Know This? As It Is Written..."**

The standard confirmation sequence in the Bavli is:

> <span dir="rtl">מנא לן? דכתיב... ואימא... מסתברא...</span>
>
> *From where do we know this? As it is written... And perhaps [you might say it means something else]? It stands to reason [that our reading is correct because]...*

This three-part sequence -- proof, challenge, reconfirmation -- is the complete confirmation exercise in miniature. First, a verse is cited. Then an alternative reading is proposed (an embedded refutation). Then the original reading is restored with additional argument. The student should practice this three-part sequence until it becomes second nature, for it is the heartbeat of talmudic argumentation.

**Example 3: Rabbi Akiva's Derivation in Sifra Qedoshim**

Rabbi Akiva's declaration that "Love your neighbor as yourself" is a great principle of Torah (Sifra Qedoshim, parashah 4) is confirmed through an elaborate chain of reasoning:

The scriptural verse is cited (Leviticus 19:18). Then the scope of its application is explored: does "your neighbor" mean only your fellow Jew, or all humanity? Rabbi Akiva and Ben Azzai debate this question, each confirming his position through a different scriptural proof. Rabbi Akiva grounds his principle in the commandment of love; Ben Azzai grounds his in the creation narrative ("This is the book of the generations of Adam" -- Genesis 5:1), arguing that the common creation of all human beings establishes their common dignity. Both are practicing confirmation from Scripture, but each selects a different verse to support a different emphasis.

**Example 4: Confirmation Through Precedent**

The *ma'aseh* (narrative precedent) is itself a form of confirmation. When the Talmud cites a story about a sage who acted in a certain way, it confirms the teaching by demonstrating that a recognized authority accepted it in practice:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבי... שעשה כן</span>
>
> *There was a case with Rabbi... who acted in this manner.*

The narrative precedent confirms by showing that the teaching is not merely theoretical but has been implemented by an authoritative figure. The student should observe that this form of confirmation is particularly powerful in halakhic contexts, where the practice of the sages carries the force of law.

#### Exercises

The student should practice the following:

**First exercise: Four-fold confirmation.** Take a teaching and confirm it from Scripture, from reason, from precedent, and from authority. Begin with:

> <span dir="rtl">דרך ארץ קדמה לתורה</span>
>
> *Proper conduct preceded the Torah.* (Leviticus Rabbah 9:3)

Confirm from Scripture: find a verse that supports the priority of ethical behavior. Confirm from reason: construct a logical argument for why ethics must precede religious law. Confirm from precedent: cite a story of a sage who prioritized ethical conduct. Confirm from authority: cite an authoritative ruling that embodies this principle.

**Second exercise: Progressive confirmation.** Take a maxim and confirm it through a sequence of proofs, arranged from weakest to strongest, building toward a climax. Practice the technique of cumulative confirmation: each proof should strengthen the case beyond what the previous proof achieved.

**Third exercise: Confirmation against refutation.** Take the refutations you composed in the previous exercise (II.E) and answer each one with a specific confirmation. For every ground of attack, provide a corresponding ground of defense. The student who can both refute and confirm the same teaching has achieved the dialectical mastery that is the mark of the complete sage.[^139]

---

### II.G. Commonplace (Mussar Kelali)

#### Definition

My son, we come now to an exercise that stands at the threshold between the preliminary exercises and the higher forms of composition. This is the *mussar kelali* -- the general discourse on a virtue or a vice, addressed to no specific person but speaking of the quality itself in universal terms.[^140]

The Greek teachers call this exercise *koinos topos* -- the "commonplace" -- and they define it as a speech that amplifies something that is admitted by all to be good or evil, without reference to any particular person. The speech of the commonplace does not praise Hillel for his patience or blame Korach for his rebellion; it praises patience itself or condemns rebellion itself. It speaks in general terms, in universal categories, in principles that apply to all human beings at all times.[^141]

The student should understand how this exercise differs from what has come before. The chreia attributes a teaching to a named sage; the commonplace has no named subject. The encomium praises a specific person; the commonplace praises a quality. The narrative tells a particular story; the commonplace speaks of general patterns. The commonplace is, in a sense, the purest form of moral discourse: it addresses not what happened, not what was said, not who did or said it, but what *is* -- what is the nature of this virtue, what are the consequences of this vice, why should the listener cultivate one and avoid the other.

And the student should know: the bet midrash is full of this kind of speech. When the sages discourse at length on the evil of *lashon hara* (evil speech) without naming any particular speaker of evil; when they expatiate on the virtue of *tzedakah* (charity) without praising any particular donor; when they amplify the horror of *sinat chinam* (baseless hatred) without identifying any particular hater -- they are engaged in the exercise of the commonplace. Pirkei Avot itself contains extended passages of commonplace discourse, and the aggadic sections of the Talmud are rich in this form.

#### Types of Commonplace

There are two fundamental types: those directed against vices and those directed in praise of virtues.

**Against Vices.** The sage composes a speech amplifying the evil of a particular vice -- showing its destructive consequences, its corrosive effect on character, its damage to the community, and the divine punishment it invites.

**For Virtues.** The sage composes a speech amplifying the good of a particular virtue -- showing its beneficial consequences, its ennobling effect on character, its benefit to the community, and the divine reward it earns.

The student should observe that the speech against a vice and the speech for a virtue often mirror each other: the condemnation of miserliness is the mirror image of the praise of generosity; the denunciation of falsehood is the complement of the exaltation of truth. The skilled sage can compose both for any given pair of opposites.[^142]

#### The Structure of a Commonplace Speech

The student should know the typical arrangement of a commonplace discourse, for without structure, even the most passionate moral speech becomes a formless harangue.

**First: State the quality to be discussed.** Name the vice or virtue plainly: "We speak today of *lashon hara*, evil speech."

**Second: Define it.** What exactly is this quality? What are its boundaries? What distinguishes it from related but different qualities? The sages are precise in their definitions:

> <span dir="rtl">אי זהו לשון הרע? כל שנאמר שלא בפניו</span>
>
> *What constitutes evil speech? Anything said not in the person's presence.*

**Third: Show its consequences.** What happens to the person who practices this vice or neglects this virtue? What happens to the community? Amplify the consequences until the listener feels their full weight.

**Fourth: Provide scriptural proof.** Ground the discourse in the sacred text, showing that God Himself condemns this vice or praises this virtue.

**Fifth: Conclude with exhortation.** Urge the listener to flee from the vice or embrace the virtue. The conclusion should be urgent, direct, and memorable.[^143]

#### Examples from the Tradition

**Example 1: Against Evil Speech (Lashon Hara) -- BT Arakhin 15b**

The Talmud's discourse on evil speech is one of the most elaborate commonplace speeches in all of rabbinic literature:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי יוחנן משום רבי יוסי בן זמרא: כל המספר לשון הרע כאילו כפר בעיקר, שנאמר: אשר אמרו ללשוננו נגביר שפתינו אתנו מי אדון לנו</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan said in the name of Rabbi Yosei ben Zimra: Whoever speaks evil speech is as if he denied the fundamental [existence of God], as it says: "Who said: With our tongue we will prevail; our lips are with us; who is lord over us?" (Psalm 12:5)*[^144]

The amplification begins at the highest level: evil speech is equated with denial of God. The speaker of evil speech is not merely a gossip or a troublemaker; he is a denier of divine sovereignty. This is the technique of amplification through comparison with the most extreme category -- the *kofer ba-iqqar*, the denier of the fundamental.

And the amplification continues:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר ריש לקיש: כל המספר לשון הרע מגדיל עוונות כנגד שלוש עבירות: עבודה זרה, וגילוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים</span>
>
> *Resh Lakish said: Whoever speaks evil speech magnifies transgressions equivalent to the three [cardinal] sins: idolatry, sexual immorality, and murder.*

Now the amplification reaches its peak: evil speech is compared not merely to one cardinal sin but to all three simultaneously. The three sins for which a person must die rather than transgress -- the absolute upper limit of transgression -- are deployed as the measure of evil speech. The student should observe the rhetorical strategy: by comparing the vice to the most extreme reference points available, the sage makes the listener feel its full gravity.

And:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רב חסדא אמר מר עוקבא: כל המספר לשון הרע ראוי לסוקלו באבן</span>
>
> *Rav Hisda said in the name of Mar Ukva: Whoever speaks evil speech deserves to be stoned.*

The consequence is stated in the most severe terms: the punishment of stoning, reserved for the worst offenses. The amplification through consequence is complete: the evil speaker deserves the death penalty.

The student should study the entire passage in Arakhin 15b-16a, for it is a complete commonplace discourse: definition of the vice, amplification through comparison, scriptural proof, illustrative examples, and concluding exhortation.

**Example 2: Praise of Charity (Tzedakah) -- BT Bava Batra 9a-10a**

The Talmud's discourse on charity is the positive counterpart of the discourse on evil speech:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי אלעזר: גדולה צדקה מכל הקרבנות, שנאמר: עשה צדקה ומשפט נבחר לה' מזבח</span>
>
> *Rabbi Elazar said: Charity is greater than all the sacrifices, as it says: "To do charity and justice is preferred by the Lord to sacrifice" (Proverbs 21:3).*[^145]

The amplification begins with comparison: charity is greater than all the sacrifices. This is the technique of amplifying a virtue by placing it above a known good -- the sacrificial service, which is itself of immense value.

And the amplification continues:

> <span dir="rtl">ואמר רבי אלעזר: גדולה צדקה שמקרבת את הגאולה, שנאמר: שמרו משפט ועשו צדקה כי קרובה ישועתי לבוא</span>
>
> *And Rabbi Elazar said: Charity is great because it brings the redemption near, as it says: "Guard justice and do charity, for My salvation is close to coming" (Isaiah 56:1).*

Now the virtue is connected to the most cosmic consequence: the redemption of all Israel, the messianic age. The person who gives charity is not merely helping an individual in need; he is hastening the redemption of the world. This is amplification through cosmic consequence -- the highest form of praise for a virtue.

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי יהודה: גדולה צדקה שמקרבת את הגאולה</span>

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">ואמר רבי אלעזר: כל אדם שעושה צדקה ומשפט כאילו מילא כל העולם כולו חסד, שנאמר: אוהב צדקה ומשפט חסד ה' מלאה הארץ</span>
>
> *And Rabbi Elazar said: Every person who performs charity and justice is as if he filled the entire world with lovingkindness, as it says: "He loves charity and justice; the lovingkindness of the Lord fills the earth" (Psalm 33:5).*

The giver of charity fills the world with lovingkindness. The individual act becomes cosmic in its significance. The student should observe how each successive statement raises the stakes: from greater than sacrifices, to bringing redemption, to filling the world with lovingkindness. The structure is one of ascending amplification, each proof building on the one before.

**Example 3: Against Baseless Hatred (Sinat Chinam)**

> <span dir="rtl">מקדש שני, שהיו עוסקים בתורה ובמצוות וגמילות חסדים, מפני מה חרב? מפני שהיתה בו שנאת חנם. ללמדך ששקולה שנאת חנם כנגד שלוש עבירות: עבודה זרה, גילוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים</span>
>
> *The Second Temple, where they were engaged in Torah, commandments, and deeds of lovingkindness -- why was it destroyed? Because there was baseless hatred among them. This teaches you that baseless hatred is equivalent to the three sins: idolatry, sexual immorality, and murder.*[^146]

Here the commonplace discourse against baseless hatred reaches its most devastating amplification: this vice destroyed the Temple. The consequence is not theoretical but historical -- not what *could* happen but what *did* happen. The student should observe that the most powerful commonplace discourses ground their amplifications in historical reality: the abstract vice produced a concrete catastrophe.

**Example 4: Against Flattery (Chanufah) -- BT Sotah 41b**

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי שמעון בן לקיש: כל המחניף לחבירו סוף נופל בידו</span>
>
> *Resh Lakish said: Whoever flatters his fellow will eventually fall into his hands.*

And the discourse continues with the story of the assembly in which Agrippa the king read the Torah and wept when he reached the verse "You shall not place over yourself a foreigner" -- and the people called out to him, "You are our brother, you are our brother!" The Talmud states:

> <span dir="rtl">באותה שעה נתחייבו שונאיהם של ישראל כליה, שהחניפו לאגריפס</span>
>
> *At that moment, the enemies of Israel [a euphemism for Israel itself] were condemned to destruction, because they flattered Agrippa.*

Again, the commonplace discourse against flattery grounds its amplification in historical consequence: the flattery of Agrippa contributed to Israel's destruction. The abstract vice produced a concrete catastrophe. The student should observe the pattern: in the great commonplace discourses of the Talmud, the vice is never left in the abstract. It is always connected to specific, devastating consequences -- the destruction of the Temple, the fall of a nation, the death of the righteous.

**Example 5: Praise of Character Traits -- Avot 2:11**

> <span dir="rtl">אמר להם: צאו וראו איזוהי דרך רעה שיתרחק ממנה האדם. רבי אליעזר אומר: עין רעה. רבי יהושע אומר: חבר רע. רבי יוסי אומר: שכן רע. רבי שמעון אומר: הלווה ואינו משלם... רבי אלעזר אומר: לב רע. אמר להם: רואה אני את דברי אלעזר בן ערך מדבריכם שבכלל דבריו דבריכם</span>
>
> *He [Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai] said to them: Go out and see which is the evil way from which a person should distance himself. Rabbi Eliezer says: An evil eye. Rabbi Yehoshua says: A bad companion. Rabbi Yosei says: A bad neighbor. Rabbi Shimon says: One who borrows and does not repay... Rabbi Elazar says: An evil heart. He said to them: I prefer the words of Elazar ben Arakh to yours, for your words are included in his.* (Avot 2:11)

This passage contains five miniature commonplace speeches in embryonic form: each sage names a vice that he considers most dangerous, and Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai selects the most comprehensive -- the evil heart, which encompasses all the others. The student should take any one of these five vices and compose a full commonplace speech around it, following the five-part structure described above.

#### Exercises

The student should practice the following:

**First exercise: Amplification of a virtue.** Compose a speech amplifying the virtue of hospitality (*hakhnasat orchim*). Follow the five-part structure: state the virtue, define it, show its consequences, provide scriptural proof, and conclude with exhortation. Draw on the examples of Abraham's hospitality (Genesis 18), the reward promised to those who welcome guests, and the rabbinic teaching that hospitality to wayfarers is greater even than receiving the Divine Presence.

**Second exercise: Amplification of a vice.** Compose a speech condemning miserliness (*tzarut ayin*). Follow the same five-part structure. Draw on the contrast between the generous eye and the evil eye in Avot, the consequences of refusing to give charity, and the fate of those who hardened their hearts against the poor.

**Third exercise: Paired compositions.** Compose both a speech praising a virtue and a speech condemning its corresponding vice -- for example, truth and falsehood, or generosity and miserliness, or humility and arrogance. Observe how the two speeches mirror each other and how the arguments for one can be adapted into arguments against the other.

**Fourth exercise: Historical amplification.** Choose a vice condemned in the Talmud and compose a commonplace speech that grounds its amplification in a specific historical catastrophe -- as the Talmud grounds its condemnation of baseless hatred in the destruction of the Temple. The student should explain why the historical ground makes the speech more powerful than an abstract moral argument alone.[^147]

---

### II.H. Encomium (Shevach / Hesped)

#### Definition

My son, we come now to the exercise of praise -- the formal, structured speech that honors a specific person, place, thing, or time. We call it *shevach* (praise) or *hesped* (eulogy), and it is one of the most demanding forms of composition that the sage must master.[^148]

How does the encomium differ from the commonplace we just studied? The commonplace praises patience in general; the encomium praises the patience of *Hillel* in particular. The commonplace condemns cruelty in general; the encomium condemns the cruelty of *Pharaoh* in particular. The encomium is always specific: it names its subject, recounts the subject's history, details the subject's qualities, and arrives at a judgment of praise through the accumulation of specific evidence.[^149]

The Greek teachers -- Theon, Aphthonius, and the great Menander Rhetor -- have laid out the structure of the encomium with great precision, and the student should know it, for the rabbis of the bet midrash, even if they did not consult these treatises, followed a remarkably similar structure in their own eulogistic and panegyric compositions.[^150]

The structure is this: **origins** (*toledot*), **upbringing and education** (*giddulo ve-chinukho*), **deeds** (*ma'asav*), **fortune and divine favor** (*hatzlachato*), **comparison** (*dimyon*), and **death and legacy** (*mitato u-yerushat zikhro*). The student who masters this structure can compose an encomium for any subject -- a sage, a city, a holy day, a sacred institution.

#### Types of Encomium

The encomium is not limited to persons. The tradition recognizes at least four types:[^151]

**Praise of Sages.** This is the most common type in rabbinic literature -- the eulogy delivered at the death of a sage, the formulaic praise embedded in the chain of tradition, the biographical sketch that preserves a sage's distinctive qualities for posterity.

**Praise of Places.** Jerusalem, the Land of Israel, the Temple, the bet midrash itself -- all are subjects of encomiastic discourse in the rabbinic corpus.

**Praise of Times.** Shabbat, the festivals, the world to come -- each is praised in language that follows the pattern of the encomium.

**Praise of Virtues or Institutions.** The Torah itself, the institution of marriage, the covenant of circumcision -- these too receive encomiastic treatment.

#### The Hesped: The Primary Venue for Encomium

The student should know that the hesped -- the eulogy delivered for the dead -- is the primary venue for the encomium in rabbinic culture. The sages gave great attention to the art of eulogy, prescribing its structure, debating its limits, and preserving its most memorable examples.

The halakhah governs the hesped with precision:

> <span dir="rtl">כל שאינו נספד כהלכתו אין עצמותיו מתאספות</span>
>
> *Whoever is not eulogized properly -- his bones do not gather [in the resurrection].*[^152]

This teaching -- that improper eulogy has eschatological consequences -- underscores the seriousness with which the tradition takes the art of formal praise. The hesped is not a luxury; it is an obligation. And the obligation is not merely to speak *something* in praise of the dead but to speak *well* -- to compose an encomium that does justice to the deceased and satisfies the community's need for mourning and meaning.

The limits of the hesped are also prescribed:

> <span dir="rtl">מוסיפין על השבח אבל לא על הגנאי</span>
>
> *One may add to the praise but not to the blame.*

And:

> <span dir="rtl">אין מגדילין יותר מדאי, ואם היה אדם גדול מגדילין</span>
>
> *One should not exaggerate overly much, but if the person was great, one may magnify [his qualities].*

The distinction between amplification and fabrication is crucial: the eulogizer may heighten the qualities the deceased actually possessed, but he may not invent qualities the deceased lacked. This principle -- that epideictic speech must work with the materials reality provides -- is one of the deepest insights of both the rabbinic and the Greek rhetorical traditions.[^153]

#### Examples from the Tradition

**Example 1: Eulogy of the Great Sages -- Mo'ed Qatan 25a-b**

The Talmud in Mo'ed Qatan preserves a remarkable collection of eulogistic formulae composed for the deaths of major sages. Each formula captures the sage's distinctive quality in a concentrated, poetic utterance:

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרבי אבהו, איטפטפו עמודי דקיסרי מיא</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Abbahu died, the pillars of Caesarea dripped water [wept].*

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרב הונא, תליסר נהרוותא דמשחא ודובשא איעכור</span>
>
> *When Rav Huna died, thirteen rivers of oil and honey were obstructed.*

These eulogistic formulae employ cosmic imagery -- the pillars weep, the rivers of oil and honey are dammed up -- to express the magnitude of the community's loss. This is the technique of amplification through cosmic correspondence: the sage's death disrupts the natural order itself. The student should observe that these are not meant literally; they are exercises in epideictic hyperbole, designed to match the inner reality of grief with an outer image of cosmic significance.

And the classic formula that captures the pattern of the rabbinic encomium in its most compressed form:

> <span dir="rtl">אם בארזים נפלה שלהבת, מה יעשו אזובי הקיר?</span>
>
> *If a flame has fallen among the cedars, what shall the hyssop of the wall do?*

This formula -- the cedar and the hyssop -- is the encomium reduced to a single metaphor: if the greatest have perished, what hope have the lowly? The comparison (cedar/hyssop) and the implied exhortation (we must nevertheless persevere) are compressed into a single sentence.

And the formula attributed to those who eulogized Rabbi Akiva:

> <span dir="rtl">כי נח נפשיה דרבי עקיבא, אמרו עליו: אם בשלמי שבי ישועות כי אם, על הרשעים וחטאים על אחת כמה וכמה</span>

The eulogizers said: If for those who do God's will, such is their suffering -- for the wicked, how much more so! This is the *kal va-chomer* deployed in the context of eulogy: the righteous sage's death becomes an argument about divine justice.

**Example 2: Praise of Jerusalem -- BT Kiddushin 49b**

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה קבין יופי ירדו לעולם, תשעה נטלה ירושלים ואחד כל העולם כולו</span>
>
> *Ten measures of beauty descended to the world; nine were taken by Jerusalem and one by the rest of the world.*[^154]

This is the encomium of a place -- Jerusalem -- expressed through the technique of numerical proportion. The praise is accomplished not by describing Jerusalem's beauty in detail (though other texts do this) but by establishing its *proportion*: nine-tenths of all the world's beauty is concentrated in this single city. The student should observe the rhetorical economy: a single numerical statement accomplishes what paragraphs of descriptive prose might fail to achieve.

And the praise continues in other passages:

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה קבין חכמה ירדו לעולם, תשעה נטלה ארץ ישראל ואחד כל העולם כולו</span>
>
> *Ten measures of wisdom descended to the world; nine were taken by the Land of Israel and one by the rest of the world.*

The same numerical formula is applied to wisdom, creating a pattern of encomiastic amplification: not only beauty but wisdom is concentrated in this sacred place. The student should note how the repetition of the formula with different qualities builds a cumulative portrait of Jerusalem's and the Land of Israel's superlative character.

**Example 3: Praise of Shabbat -- BT Shabbat 119a**

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי חנינא: בואו ונצא לקראת כלה, לקראת שבת מלכתא. רבי ינאי לביש מאניה מעלי שבתא ואמר: בואי כלה, בואי כלה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Hanina said: Come, let us go out to greet the bride, to greet the Sabbath queen. Rabbi Yannai would don his garments on Sabbath eve and say: Come, O bride; come, O bride.*[^155]

This is the encomium of a time -- the Shabbat -- accomplished through personification. The Shabbat is not merely a day of rest; she is a bride, a queen, greeted with ceremony and reverence. The personification transforms the abstract (a division of time) into the concrete (a royal person), and in doing so, it transforms the listener's relationship to the Shabbat from obligation to love. The student should observe how the encomium of a time differs from the encomium of a person: the eulogizer must find a way to make the abstract concrete, and personification is the primary tool.

And consider the further amplification:

> <span dir="rtl">שני מלאכי השרת מלוין לו לאדם בערב שבת מבית הכנסת לביתו, אחד טוב ואחד רע. כשבא לביתו ומצא נר דלוק ושולחן ערוך ומיטה מוצעת, מלאך טוב אומר: יהי רצון שתהא כן לשבת אחרת, ומלאך רע עונה אמן בעל כורחו</span>
>
> *Two ministering angels accompany a person on Sabbath eve from the synagogue to his home, one good and one evil. When he arrives home and finds the lamp lit, the table set, and the bed made, the good angel says: "May it be God's will that it be so next Shabbat," and the evil angel answers "amen" against his will.*

This narrative is encomium through narrative: the praise of Shabbat is accomplished not through direct statement but through a story about angels who escort the Shabbat observer home. The good angel's blessing and the evil angel's reluctant "amen" together confirm the supreme value of Shabbat observance. The student should observe how the narrative encomium combines praise with instruction: by praising the well-prepared Shabbat, the narrative implicitly instructs the listener in how to prepare.

**Example 4: Eulogistic Formulae**

The tradition preserves standard formulae for eulogies, each expressing loss in a concentrated phrase:

> <span dir="rtl">ווי דאזיל ולא משתכח</span>
>
> *Woe for the lost and not forgotten.*

And:

> <span dir="rtl">ווי דחסיל עלמא ולא ידעי ביה</span>
>
> *Woe that the world is bereft and does not know it.*

These formulae are available to the eulogizer as building blocks -- prefabricated expressions of grief that can be inserted into a longer eulogy. The student should memorize them and learn to deploy them at the appropriate moment: the formula provides emotional punctuation, a shared expression that unites the community in its grief.

#### Exercises

The student should practice the following:

**First exercise: Full encomium of a sage.** Compose an encomium for your teacher following this order: his origins (where he came from, who his teachers were), his education (what he studied, how he grew in learning), his deeds (his rulings, his acts of piety, his service to the community), his influence (whom he taught, what institutions he built), and the legacy he leaves (what will endure after his death). Follow the six-part structure and include at least one scriptural proof, one illustrative narrative, and one comparison to a prior sage of blessed memory.

**Second exercise: Encomium of a place.** Compose a praise of the bet midrash -- your own house of study -- using the techniques of numerical proportion, personification, and amplification through comparison. Show why this place is uniquely worthy of praise.

**Third exercise: Encomium of a time.** Compose a praise of Shabbat or of a festival, using the technique of personification. Transform the abstract (a division of time) into the concrete (a beloved figure), and show why this time is the crown of the week or the year.

**Fourth exercise: Eulogy with restraint.** Compose a eulogy for a sage whose qualities were mixed -- who had great learning but also notable failings. Practice the art of amplifying the good without fabricating, and of acknowledging the real without destroying the honor of the dead. This is the most difficult exercise, for it requires the union of honesty and compassion that is the mark of the true sage.[^156]

---

### II.I. Vituperation (Genai / Qelalah)

#### Definition

My son, we come at last to the most dangerous exercise of all -- the formal speech of blame. We call it *genai* (disgrace) or *qelalah* (curse, condemnation), and it is the dark mirror of the encomium. Everything the encomium does in praise, the vituperation does in blame. Where the encomium recounts noble origins, the vituperation exposes base origins. Where the encomium celebrates wise education, the vituperation reveals foolish or wicked training. Where the encomium praises noble deeds, the vituperation catalogs wicked ones. Where the encomium honors a legacy, the vituperation exposes a disgrace that will endure as a warning to all future generations.[^157]

The Greek teachers call this exercise *psogos* -- blame, invective, censure -- and they teach it as the exact counterpart of the encomium, following the same structure in reverse. Theon instructs that the categories of vituperation are the same as those of encomium, "but with the opposite intention."[^158] And so they are: the same analytical framework that builds praise can be turned to the construction of blame.

But here I must pause and give the student a warning that the Greek teachers do not always give with sufficient force. The exercise of vituperation is *dangerous*. It is dangerous to the speaker, who may be consumed by the very hatred he articulates. It is dangerous to the listener, who may be corrupted by the pleasure of hearing others condemned. And it is dangerous to the community, which may be torn apart by the passions that blame inflames.

The sages of the bet midrash understood this danger, and they placed strict limits on the speech of blame:

> <span dir="rtl">כל המלבין פני חבירו ברבים כאילו שופך דמים</span>
>
> *Whoever shames his fellow in public is as if he shed blood.*[^159]

And:

> <span dir="rtl">כל היורד לגיהנם עולה, חוץ משלושה שיורדים ואינם עולים: הבא על אשת איש, והמלבין פני חבירו ברבים, והמכנה שם רע לחבירו</span>
>
> *All who descend to Gehinnom ascend [eventually], except for three who descend and do not ascend: one who commits adultery, one who shames his fellow in public, and one who gives his fellow a derogatory nickname.*[^160]

The student should ponder this: public shaming is ranked alongside the most severe transgressions. The sage who masters the art of blame and then deploys it recklessly has committed an act equivalent to bloodshed. Therefore, the exercise of vituperation must always be conducted under the strictest restraint. The student learns to compose blame *in order to understand its power and its limits* -- not in order to use it freely.

And there is a further prohibition that governs the speech of blame:

> <span dir="rtl">אין מזכירין שבחו של מת... ואין מספרין בגנותו</span>
>
> *One does not recount the praise of the dead [excessively]... and one does not speak of his disgrace.*

The prohibition against speaking ill of the dead sets a boundary around vituperation: even when blame is warranted, the death of the subject imposes restraint. The living may be rebuked, for rebuke may lead to repentance. But the dead cannot repent, and therefore blame serves no constructive purpose. The student should learn this principle deeply: vituperation is justified only when it serves a constructive end -- instruction, warning, prevention of future harm. Vituperation for its own sake, or for the pleasure of the speaker, is always forbidden.[^161]

#### The Structure of Vituperation

Despite these cautions, the tradition *does* preserve sharp and sustained speeches of blame -- directed not at private individuals but at paradigmatic villains whose wickedness serves as instruction for all generations. The structure mirrors that of the encomium, inverted:

**Base Origins.** The vituperated figure is shown to come from an unworthy background. His family, his ancestry, his beginnings are exposed as tainted.

**Bad Education.** His training is shown to have been misguided, corrupt, or perverse. The skills he acquired were turned to evil purposes.

**Wicked Deeds.** His actions are cataloged in detail, each one more shocking than the last, building toward a climax of moral outrage.

**Deserved Misfortune.** His downfall is presented as the just consequence of his wickedness -- measure for measure, the punishment fitting the crime.

**Warning.** The discourse concludes not with mourning (as in the hesped) but with a warning: let all who hear take instruction from this villain's fate.

#### Examples from the Tradition

**Example 1: Blame of Korach and His Congregation -- Numbers Rabbah 18**

The rabbis' treatment of Korach is a sustained vituperation that follows the classical structure with remarkable precision.

**Base Origins.** The midrash begins by examining Korach's genealogy:

> <span dir="rtl">ויקח קרח בן יצהר בן קהת בן לוי -- ולמה לא נאמר בן יעקב? שהיה יעקב מבקש רחמים על עצמו שלא יזכר שמו על מחלוקתם, שנאמר: בקהלם אל תחד כבודי</span>
>
> *"Korach son of Izhar son of Kohath son of Levi" -- and why does it not say "son of Jacob"? Because Jacob had prayed for mercy upon himself that his name not be mentioned in connection with their dispute, as it says: "In their assembly, let my honor not be united" (Genesis 49:6).*[^162]

The genealogy is used against the subject: the very fact that the lineage stops short of Jacob becomes evidence of the ancestor's horror at his descendant's wickedness. This is the vituperative inversion of the encomiastic use of genealogy: where the encomium traces noble lineage to its source, the vituperation shows that the lineage itself recoils from the villain.

**Bad Education and Wicked Counsel.** The midrash expands on Korach's rhetorical skill -- and turns it against him:

> <span dir="rtl">קרח שפיקח היה, מה ראה לשטות הזה? עינו הטעתו. ראה שלשלת גדולה עומדת ממנו -- שמואל ששקול כמשה ואהרן</span>
>
> *Korach, who was clever -- what did he see that led him to this folly? His eye deceived him. He saw a great chain [of descendants] arising from him -- Samuel, who was equal to Moses and Aaron.*

Korach was intelligent -- this is conceded. But his intelligence was corrupted by ambition. He saw, through prophetic vision, that his descendant Samuel would be a great leader, and he concluded that he himself deserved leadership now. The vituperation turns the subject's own qualities against him: his cleverness becomes the instrument of his folly, his vision becomes the source of his blindness.

And the midrash amplifies Korach's rhetorical seduction of the people:

> <span dir="rtl">מה עשה קרח? עמד וכנס מאתיים וחמישים ראשי סנהדראות... והלבישן טליתות שכולן תכלת. באו ועמדו לפני משה. אמרו לו: טלית שכולה תכלת מהו שתהא חייבת בציצית?</span>
>
> *What did Korach do? He stood and gathered two hundred and fifty heads of courts... and dressed them in cloaks entirely of blue wool. They came and stood before Moses. They said to him: A cloak that is entirely blue -- is it still required to have a fringe?*

The question is a trap -- a piece of sophistry designed to make Moses' law appear absurd. If a cloak is entirely blue, why does it need a blue thread in its fringe? The challenge is rhetorical, not legal: Korach is using the forms of legal argument to undermine legal authority. The midrash presents this as the paradigm of misused rhetorical skill -- the art of speech deployed not in the service of truth but in the service of rebellion.[^163]

**Example 2: Blame of the Generation of the Flood -- Genesis Rabbah 26-28**

The midrash on the generation of the Flood composes a sustained vituperation that proceeds through the classical categories:

> <span dir="rtl">וירא ה' כי רבה רעת האדם בארץ -- רבי ברכיה אמר: שהיו כותבים גמומסיות לזכר ולנקבה</span>
>
> *"And the Lord saw that the wickedness of man was great upon the earth" (Genesis 6:5) -- Rabbi Berekhiah said: They used to write marriage contracts for [unions between] male and male, and [between] female and female.*

The wicked deeds are cataloged with specificity: the generation of the Flood did not merely sin in general terms; they institutionalized their wickedness, formalizing it in legal documents. The vituperation amplifies the vice by showing that it was not merely practiced but systematized.

And the deserved misfortune:

> <span dir="rtl">הם חטאו ברותחין, ונידונו ברותחין</span>
>
> *They sinned with boiling [passion], and they were punished with boiling [waters].*

The measure-for-measure punishment confirms the justice of the vituperation: the punishment mirrors the sin, demonstrating that the blame is proportionate and the downfall is deserved.

**Example 3: Doeg and Ahitophel as Paradigms of Misused Skill -- BT Sanhedrin 106b**

> <span dir="rtl">דואג ואחיתופל שנו ארבע מאות שכלא דטומאה</span>
>
> *Doeg and Ahitophel studied four hundred types of impure reasoning.*[^164]

The Talmud presents Doeg and Ahitophel as the paradigmatic examples of brilliant minds turned to evil purposes. Both possessed extraordinary rhetorical and intellectual abilities -- the Talmud credits them with mastery of vast bodies of learning. But their learning was corrupted: Doeg informed against the priests of Nob (1 Samuel 22), leading to their massacre, and Ahitophel counseled Absalom's rebellion against David (2 Samuel 15-17).

The student should observe: the vituperation is sharpened precisely because the subjects possessed great gifts. A wicked fool is merely pitiable; a wicked genius is terrifying. The vituperation of Doeg and Ahitophel amplifies their evil by first establishing their brilliance -- and then showing how that brilliance was perverted. The structure is deliberate: acknowledge the ability, then demonstrate the corruption, and the contrast between the two amplifies the blame beyond what either element could achieve alone.

And the Talmud concludes with this devastating judgment:

> <span dir="rtl">ואמר רב יהודה אמר רב: אל ימנה אדם בביתו פרנס בנחת, שאלמלא נתמנה אחיתופל כראוי, לא בא לידי כך</span>

The counsel of the sages is that Ahitophel's wickedness was partly the consequence of his placement in an unsuitable role -- a warning that even great talent, when misdirected or improperly supervised, can become the instrument of destruction.

**Example 4: "Who Are the Am Ha-aretz?" -- BT Berakhot 47b**

> <span dir="rtl">תנו רבנן: איזהו עם הארץ? כל שאינו קורא קריאת שמע ערבית ושחרית</span>
>
> *Our rabbis taught: Who is an am ha-aretz [an ignorant person]? Whoever does not recite the Shema evening and morning.*

The Talmud then records a series of increasingly stringent definitions, each sage defining the *am ha-aretz* by a different criterion of religious negligence. This passage is a borderline vituperation -- it condemns a *type* of person (making it also a commonplace) while defining the specific behaviors that warrant the condemnation.

But the student should note the rabbinic counterbalance -- the voice of restraint that tempers the blame:

> <span dir="rtl">תנא: אין עם הארץ חסיד</span>
>
> *It was taught: An ignorant person cannot be pious.* (Avot 2:5)

This is not hatred of the ignorant but a statement of fact: without knowledge, piety is impossible. And the sages add the ethical limit:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תהי בז לכל אדם ואל תהי מפליג לכל דבר, שאין לך אדם שאין לו שעה ואין לך דבר שאין לו מקום</span>
>
> *Do not be contemptuous of any person and do not be dismissive of any thing, for there is no person who does not have his hour and no thing that does not have its place.* (Avot 4:3)

The vituperation of the *am ha-aretz* is bounded by this maxim: even the ignorant have their hour, and the sage who despises them has forgotten the limits of blame.

#### The Ethical Limits of Vituperation

The student must learn these limits as thoroughly as he learns the technique:

**First limit: Do not rejoice in another's downfall.**

> <span dir="rtl">בנפול אויבך אל תשמח ובכשלו אל יגל לבך</span>
>
> *Do not rejoice when your enemy falls, and let your heart not be glad when he stumbles.* (Proverbs 24:17, cited Avot 4:19)[^165]

This verse, cited in Pirkei Avot, establishes the fundamental ethical boundary of vituperation: even when blame is warranted, even when punishment is deserved, the sage must not take pleasure in it. The exercise of blame is performed with sorrow, not with glee. The sage who composes a vituperation with a smirk on his face has already failed the exercise, regardless of the technical quality of his composition.

**Second limit: Speak in order to instruct, not in order to wound.**

> <span dir="rtl">הוכח תוכיח את עמיתך ולא תשא עליו חטא</span>
>
> *You shall surely rebuke your fellow, and you shall not bear sin because of him.* (Leviticus 19:17)

The commandment to rebuke is accompanied by a warning: do not sin in the act of rebuking. The blame must serve a constructive purpose -- the correction of error, the prevention of future harm, the instruction of the community. Blame that serves only to wound, to humiliate, or to destroy is not rebuke but malice.

**Third limit: Blame the act, not the essence.**

> <span dir="rtl">שנאת חנם</span>
>
> *Baseless hatred*

The tradition distinguishes between hating a person's actions and hating the person himself. The sage may condemn wickedness without condemning the wicked to irredeemable damnation. Even Korach's descendants were not damned: "The sons of Korach did not die" (Numbers 26:11). The sage who composes a vituperation must leave room for the possibility of repentance -- must acknowledge, however implicitly, that even the worst villain might turn and live.

#### Exercises

The student should practice the following:

**First exercise: Controlled vituperation of a biblical villain.** Compose a formal vituperation of Pharaoh, following the five-part structure: base origins (his claim to divinity, his enslavement of the innocent), wicked education (his sorcerers and their deceptions), wicked deeds (the enslavement, the decree to drown the infants), deserved misfortune (the plagues, the drowning at the sea), and warning (let all tyrants learn from his fate). The composition should be vigorous in its condemnation but restrained in its tone -- blame without glee, instruction without malice.

**Second exercise: Identify the limits.** After composing your vituperation, go back through it and mark every point where you must exercise restraint. Where does the condemnation risk becoming personal hatred? Where does the amplification risk becoming fabrication? Where does the pleasure of eloquent blame risk corrupting the speaker? The student should annotate his own composition with these self-critical notes.

**Third exercise: Vituperation with counterbalance.** Compose a vituperation of Esau as he appears in rabbinic tradition -- but include, within the same composition, the tradition's acknowledgment of Esau's virtues (his honor of his father, his weeping when the blessing was taken). The student should practice the art of holding blame and acknowledgment in tension, composing a discourse that condemns the predominant wickedness while conceding the occasional virtue. This is the most mature form of the exercise: not pure blame, but balanced judgment that acknowledges the complexity of human character.

**Fourth exercise: Vituperation into commonplace.** Take your vituperation of a specific figure and extract from it a commonplace -- a general discourse on the vice that the figure exemplifies. Strip away the specific person and speak of the vice in universal terms. Observe how the discourse changes when it moves from the specific to the general: what is gained (universality, applicability to the listener's own life) and what is lost (the power of the specific example, the vividness of the individual case).[^166]

[^106]: The distinction between chreia and maxim (gnome) is explicitly drawn by all the major progymnasmata authors. Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 62-66) defines the maxim (*gnome*) as "a summary statement, in universal terms, dissuading from something or persuading toward something, or indicating what is the nature of each thing." He then contrasts it with the chreia: "The chreia differs from the gnome in that the chreia is always attributed to a character, while the gnome is stated without attribution." Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 4; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 78-79) follows the same distinction. See also Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 2.21 (1394a19-1395b20) on the gnome as a general statement about practical matters.

[^107]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 3 (Hock and O'Neil, 62). The definition emphasizes the universal (*katholou*) character of the maxim, in contrast to the particular attribution of the chreia.

[^108]: On the rhetorical distinction between personal authority (characteristic of the chreia) and collective authority (characteristic of the maxim/stam mishnah), see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 155-162; and the discussion in Halivni, *Mekorot u-Mesorot: Bava Metzia* (Jerusalem: Magnes, 2003), introduction, on the halakhic significance of attributed vs. unattributed traditions.

[^109]: Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 4; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 78-79) distinguishes declarative (*apophantike*), hortatory (*protreptike*), compound (*synthetike*), and double (*diplē*) types of maxim. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 8.5.3-8) provides a similar typology, noting that maxims may be simple or supported by a reason, and may state what is or urge what should be.

[^113]: BT Berakhot 63a. On the concept of a "small verse" that contains the whole Torah, cf. BT Makkot 23b-24a (Rabbi Simlai's derivation of the 613 commandments and their progressive condensation to a single principle).

Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 8.5.3) notes that maxims are more persuasive when accompanied by a reason (*ratio*), corresponding to the compound type.

[^115]: On the fluidity between attributed and unattributed traditions in rabbinic literature, see Alexander, "The Oral Law," in *The Cambridge History of Judaism*, vol. 3 (1999), 85-97; and Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 100-125.

[^116]: On Ben Zoma's fourfold question as a paradigm of the redefinition-maxim, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature and Greco-Roman Philosophy*, 78-84; and the comparison with Greco-Roman *paradoxical encomia* (praise of unexpected subjects) in Moeser, *The Anecdote in Mark*, 108-112.

[^117]: On the use of Proverbs in talmudic argument, see Fox, *Proverbs 1-9* (Anchor Bible; New York: Doubleday, 2000), 18-26, on the reception history of Solomonic wisdom in rabbinic tradition.

[^118]: The exercise of composing maxims in the four types parallels Theon's instruction that students should practice composing gnomae in each of the recognized types (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 66-68). Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 4) similarly prescribes exercises in which students elaborate and condense maxims.

[^119]: Theon treats refutation (*anaskeuē*) as an exercise to be applied to chreiai, fables, and narratives (*Progymnasmata* 3-4; Hock and O'Neil, 56-58). Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 5; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 103-105) treats it as an independent exercise, the fifth in the sequence, and defines it as "the overturning of a matter at hand." Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 5; Kennedy, 81-82) provides a similar treatment. On the relationship between the progymnasmata exercise of refutation and talmudic dialectics, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 155-186.

[^120]: BT Berakhot 63b. The verse from Jeremiah 50:36 is reread to apply to scholars who study in isolation (*bad badad*). The wordplay on *baddim* (solitary ones/liars) is characteristic of aggadic exegesis.

[^121]: The six categories here consolidate and adapt the nine categories of refutation enumerated by Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 56-58): obscurity, pleonasm, ellipsis, impossibility, implausibility, falsity, unsuitability, uselessness, and shamefulness. Our reconstruction consolidates these into six categories that correspond more naturally to the talmudic modes of objection: the unclear, the incredible, the impossible, the inconsistent, the unfitting, and the inexpedient. On the mapping of Theon's nine categories onto talmudic objection-types, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 155-170.

[^122]: The talmudic formula *heikhi dami? ilima... ela...* ("What is the case? If you say... then... rather...") is one of the most common structures of the Babylonian Talmud's dialectical argumentation. See Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument: A Study in Talmudic Reasoning and Methodology* (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1984), 35-52, on this formula and its variants.

[^123]: BT Gittin 56b-57a. On the Talmud's self-reflexive interrogation of its own aggadic narratives, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 238-256; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash*, 80-92.

[^124]: On the technical meaning of *teyuvta* (conclusive refutation) as distinct from *kushya* (difficulty), see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 57-62. The doubled *teyuvta* signals that the refutation is decisive and the defeated position is abandoned.

[^125]: On *u-reminhu* and other contradiction-raising formulae in the Talmud, see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 63-78; and Halivni, *Mekorot u-Mesorot*, introduction, 24-38.

[^126]: BT Shabbat 56a. The defense of biblical heroes against literal readings of their apparent sins is a recurring feature of rabbinic exegesis. See Kugel, *The Bible As It Was* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1997), 131-155, on the tradition of defending the patriarchs and other biblical figures.

[^127]: BT Bava Batra 60b and BT Avodah Zarah 36a. This principle -- that a decree must be bearable by the majority of the community -- functions as a standing refutation-from-inexpediency against overly stringent rulings. Cf. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 5; Kennedy, 104), who lists "the inexpedient" (*asympheron*) as a ground for refutation.

[^128]: The formula *eini yode'a mah melamdenu* ("I do not know what this teaches us") appears in various tannaitic contexts as a way of dismissing a proposed proof-text. See Epstein, *Mevo'ot le-Sifrut ha-Tanna'im*, 521-525, on this formula and its variants in the Mekhilta and Sifra.

[^129]: The exercise of composing refutations on multiple grounds parallels Theon's instruction that students should practice refuting chreiai, fables, and narratives on each of the enumerated grounds (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 56-58). The addition of the counter-exercise (composing answers to one's own refutations) reflects the talmudic principle that the complete sage must be able to argue both sides.

[^130]: On confirmation (*kataskeuē*) as the complementary exercise to refutation, see Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3-4; Hock and O'Neil, 58-60), who treats confirmation and refutation as a matched pair. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 6; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 105-107) devotes a separate chapter to confirmation. Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 6; Kennedy, 82-83) similarly pairs the two exercises.

[^131]: Aphthonius defines *kataskeuē* as "the confirmation of a matter at hand" (*Progymnasmata* 6; Kennedy, 105). The rabbinic equivalent *qiyyum* (establishment, making firm) is used in the Talmud to describe the process of confirming a ruling through scriptural support or logical argument.

[^132]: The six methods of confirmation here are the positive counterparts of the six grounds of refutation in II.E, following the principle articulated by Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 58-60) that confirmation and refutation employ the same categories with opposite valences.

[^133]: BT Berakhot 30b. This confirmation-from-Scripture is a paradigmatic example of the *mina lan? dikhtiv...* formula.

[^134]: BT Pesahim 53b (Tosafot citing Midrash); cf. BT Berakhot 61b. The *kal va-chomer* from the frogs is one of the most famous examples of a fortiori reasoning in rabbinic literature. On *kal va-chomer* as a form of probable confirmation, see Schwarz, *Der hermeneutische Syllogismus in der talmudischen Litteratur* (Vienna: Israel-Theol. Lehranstalt, 1901), 3-28.

[^135]: On the talmudic formulae *tanya nami hakhi* ("a baraita also teaches thus") and *leima mesaye'a leih* ("let us say this supports him") as modes of cumulative confirmation, see Jacobs, *The Talmudic Argument*, 79-96.

[^136]: Sifra Qedoshim, parashah 4 (on Leviticus 19:18). On the debate between Rabbi Akiva and Ben Azzai over the "great principle" of the Torah, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 115-120; and Fraade, *From Tradition to Commentary: Torah and Its Interpretation in the Midrash Sifre to Deuteronomy* (Albany: SUNY Press, 1991), 24-38.

[^137]: BT Eruvin 13b. The preference for Beit Hillel's rulings on grounds of their character (gentle, humble, and willing to cite the opposing view first) is a remarkable instance of confirmation from moral character rather than from logical superiority.

[^138]: On the distinction between *derashah* (genuine scriptural derivation) and *asmakhta* (scriptural support for a rabbinic ruling), see Halivni, *Peshat and Derash: Plain and Applied Meaning in Rabbinic Exegesis* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1991), 18-37; and the detailed treatment in Chernick, *Hermeneutical Studies in Talmudic and Midrashic Literatures* (Lod: Habermann Institute, 1984), 45-72.

[^139]: The paired exercise of refutation and confirmation parallels the pedagogical principle articulated by Theon (*Progymnasmata* 3; Hock and O'Neil, 58-60) that students should practice both exercises on the same material. The talmudic ideal of arguing both sides (*shakla ve-tarya*) embodies this same principle.

[^140]: The term *mussar kelali* ("general moral instruction") is our reconstruction; the rabbinic tradition does not use a single technical term for this exercise. The closest conceptual parallels are the aggadic discourses on general moral themes found in tractate Avot, in the aggadic sections of the Talmud, and in the midrashic homilies.

[^141]: Theon defines the commonplace (*koinos topos*) as "an amplification of admitted evils or goods" (*Progymnasmata* 6; Hock and O'Neil, 68-72). Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 7; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 108-110) provides a similar definition and emphasizes that the commonplace does not name specific individuals but addresses vices and virtues in general. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 2.4.22) places the commonplace among the fundamental exercises, noting its value for developing the student's ability to amplify.

[^142]: Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 7; Kennedy, 83-84) notes that commonplaces are typically "against" (vices) but can also be "for" (virtues). Aphthonius demonstrates both types in his model exercises.

[^143]: The five-part structure corresponds broadly to the arrangement prescribed by Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 7; Kennedy, 108-110), who recommends opening with a contrary, then proceeding through exposition, comparison, intention (*gnome*), digression, rejection of pity, and concluding exhortation.

[^144]: BT Arakhin 15b. The entire passage (15b-16a) is one of the most sustained commonplace discourses in the Talmud, comparable in scope and technique to the model commonplace speeches in the progymnasmata handbooks.

[^145]: BT Bava Batra 9a. The discourse on charity in Bava Batra 9a-10a constitutes a sustained positive commonplace, amplifying the virtue of tzedakah through a series of comparative and scriptural proofs.

[^146]: BT Yoma 9b. The destruction of the Second Temple attributed to baseless hatred is the paradigmatic example of historical amplification in rabbinic moral discourse.

[^147]: The exercise of historical amplification corresponds to Quintilian's recommendation (*Inst. Or.* 2.4.22-24) that students practice the commonplace by connecting vices and virtues to their historical consequences.

[^148]: Theon defines the encomium (*enkomion*) as "speech revealing the greatness of virtuous actions and other good qualities belonging to a particular person" (*Progymnasmata* 8; Hock and O'Neil, 72-80). Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 8; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 110-115) provides a detailed structure for the encomium. Menander Rhetor (*Peri Epideiktikon* I-II) offers the most elaborate treatment of epideictic genres, including encomia of persons, cities, lands, and occasions.

[^149]: On the rabbinic *hesped* (eulogy) as a form of encomium, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 97-115; and Galit Hasan-Rokem, *The Web of Life: Folklore and Midrash in Rabbinic Literature* (Stanford: Stanford University Press, 2000), 55-78, on the narrative and performative dimensions of rabbinic eulogies.

[^150]: On the structural parallels between the rabbinic hesped and the Greco-Roman encomium, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 103-115. Menander Rhetor's prescriptions for the funeral oration (*epitaphios logos*) in *Peri Epideiktikon* II.9 (Russell and Wilson eds., 170-178) provide the closest Greek parallel to the rabbinic hesped.

[^151]: Menander Rhetor's treatise encompasses encomia of persons, cities, lands, harbors, and occasions (festivals, seasons). The rabbinic parallels include eulogies of sages, praises of Jerusalem and the Land of Israel, and praises of Shabbat and the festivals.

[^152]: BT Mo'ed Qatan 25a, paraphrasing the concern that improper eulogy has consequences.

[^153]: The principle that the eulogizer may amplify existing virtues but may not fabricate nonexistent ones corresponds precisely to Quintilian's rule for epideictic speech (*Inst. Or.* 3.7.21-23): "It is permissible to amplify what is good, but not to invent what does not exist." On this principle in the context of rabbinic eulogies, see BT Berakhot 62a and BT Mo'ed Qatan 25a.

[^154]: BT Kiddushin 49b. The formula "ten measures descended to the world, X took nine and the rest of the world took one" is applied to various qualities (beauty, wisdom, suffering, etc.) and constitutes a distinctive rabbinic form of numerical encomium.

[^155]: BT Shabbat 119a. The personification of Shabbat as bride and queen is the foundation of the *Lekhah Dodi* liturgical poem by Solomon Alkabetz (16th century), which draws on these talmudic images.

[^156]: The exercise of the eulogy with restraint corresponds to Quintilian's discussion of the difficult epideictic cases -- cases where the subject's qualities are mixed and the orator must exercise judgment about what to amplify and what to pass over (*Inst. Or.* 3.7.15-18).

[^157]: Theon treats vituperation (*psogos*) as the exact mirror of the encomium (*Progymnasmata* 8; Hock and O'Neil, 80-82): "Vituperation is the opposite of encomium; for it reveals the baseness of actions and other bad qualities." Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 9; Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 115-118) follows the same pattern, providing a model vituperation of Philip of Macedon that inverts every category of the preceding model encomium.

[^158]: Theon, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Hock and O'Neil, 80): the categories of vituperation are "the same as those of the encomium, but with the opposite intention" (*enantiōs*).

[^159]: BT Berakhot 43b; cf. BT Bava Metzia 58b-59a. The equation of public shaming with bloodshed is based on the visible blanching of the shamed person's face (*mapik demei*, the draining of blood from the face).

[^160]: BT Bava Metzia 58b. The three categories of those who descend to Gehinnom and do not ascend represent the extreme boundary of rabbinic moral condemnation.

[^161]: On the prohibition against speaking ill of the dead (*lashon hara al ha-metim*), see BT Berakhot 19a and the discussion in Maimonides, *Mishneh Torah*, Hilkhot Evel 14:1.

[^162]: Numbers Rabbah 18:2 (on Numbers 16:1). The midrashic treatment of Korach's genealogy is a paradigmatic example of vituperative use of ancestry -- the inverse of the encomiastic use of genealogy. Compare Menander Rhetor's instructions for the *psogos* (*Peri Epideiktikon* II.12; Russell and Wilson, 182-184).

[^163]: Numbers Rabbah 18:3. The midrash's presentation of Korach as a skilled but corrupt rhetorician resonates with the Greek tradition's warnings about the sophist who uses rhetorical skill for destructive purposes. Cf. Plato, *Gorgias* 456a-461b; and the discussion in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 38-42.

[^164]: BT Sanhedrin 106b. On Doeg and Ahitophel as paradigms of misused intellectual ability in rabbinic tradition, see Fraenkel, *Darkhe ha-Aggadah ve-ha-Midrash* (Givatayim: Yad la-Talmud, 1991), 387-395.

[^165]: Proverbs 24:17, cited in Mishnah Avot 4:19. This verse establishes the fundamental ethical boundary of all blame-speech: even justified condemnation must not become a source of pleasure.

[^166]: The exercise of converting vituperation (of a specific figure) into commonplace (on the abstract vice) corresponds to the pedagogical sequence prescribed in the progymnasmata: the student moves from the particular to the general, from the concrete to the abstract, developing the ability to work in both registers. See Theon (*Progymnasmata* 8; Hock and O'Neil, 80-82) and Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 7-9; Kennedy, 108-118) on the relationship between commonplace, encomium, and vituperation.
### II.J. Comparison (Hekesh / Dimyon)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, we come now to the tenth exercise, and it is one that the sages of the bet midrash practiced with extraordinary frequency: the *comparison* -- in our language, *hekesh* or *dimyon*. What is a comparison? It is the setting of two subjects side by side so that the virtues or faults of each are illuminated by the presence of the other. The comparison is, in essence, a double encomium or a double vituperation: where the encomium praises one subject and the invective censures one subject, the comparison takes two subjects and examines them together, so that each becomes a mirror for the other.

The student should understand why this exercise is so important. The mind that can only evaluate a thing in isolation sees only half the picture. The mind that can place two things beside each other and discern where they correspond and where they diverge -- that mind has achieved a deeper understanding of both. The sage who compares Moses and Aaron, or Torah study and good deeds, or the First Temple and the Second Temple, does not merely display cleverness. He reveals dimensions of each subject that would remain invisible if each were considered alone.

The Greek teachers of rhetoric called this exercise *synkrisis* -- the placing together, the joining of two subjects for mutual illumination.[^167] Their greatest practitioner was the biographer Plutarch, who composed his famous *Parallel Lives* as a series of comparisons: a Greek statesman paired with a Roman statesman, so that the reader might discern the virtues common to both and the virtues unique to each.[^168] But the rabbis needed no instruction from Plutarch. The practice of comparison is embedded in the very logic of Torah interpretation. When the Torah places two subjects in proximity, the sage draws an inference -- this is the hermeneutical principle of *hekesh*, analogy by juxtaposition. And when the sage asks *mah matzinu* -- "what do we find?" -- he is performing a comparison: what do we find in this case that corresponds to what we find in that case? The comparison is not borrowed from the Greeks; it is native to the bet midrash. But the student who understands both traditions will see how pervasive and how powerful this exercise truly is.

> <span dir="rtl">היקש -- מה מצינו בזה, כך נמצא בזה; ואם לא כך, מה ביניהם?</span>
>
> *Hekesh -- what we find in this one, so we find in that one; and if not, what is the difference between them?*

#### Types of Comparison

The student should know the principal types of comparison employed in the bet midrash:

**Comparison of Persons.** This is the most common type: two sages, two biblical figures, two kings, two prophets are set side by side. The purpose is not always to declare one superior. Sometimes the purpose is precisely the opposite -- to show that each embodies a different virtue, and that both are necessary. The comparison of persons requires great delicacy, for the sage who compares must be fair to both subjects. He must not construct the comparison so that one side is a mere foil for the other. Both must be presented with full dignity, and the differences between them must be presented as genuine differences, not as deficiencies.

**Comparison of Things.** Torah and wisdom, prayer and sacrifice, the written Torah and the oral Torah, this world and the world to come -- the sages frequently compare abstract things, setting them beside each other to determine their relative weight and their proper relationship. These comparisons often take the form of the question: "Which is greater?" -- *eizeh gadol?* -- and the answer is sometimes surprising, for the sages do not always rank things in the expected order.

**Comparison of Actions.** Study and practice, speech and silence, giving charity publicly and giving charity secretly, judging favorably and judging unfavorably -- actions are compared to determine which is more praiseworthy, which is more effective, which is more pleasing before God. The comparison of actions is closely related to the thesis exercise that we will discuss in section II.M, for the thesis also asks general questions about the relative merit of different courses of conduct.

**Comparison of Times.** The First Temple era and the Second Temple era, the generation of the wilderness and the generation of the conquest, the age of prophecy and the age of the sages -- the rabbis frequently compare different historical periods, asking what characterized each, what virtues each possessed, and what failures brought each to its end. The comparison of times is a powerful tool for understanding the present, for the sage who compares his own era to a prior era can draw lessons about what must be preserved and what must be changed.

**Comparison by Numerical Lists.** A distinctive form of rabbinic comparison is the structured numerical list -- "there are four types of X" -- in which several categories of the same thing are set beside each other and evaluated.
