These lists are pervasive in Mishnah Avot and serve as a systematic form of comparison: by listing four types of students, or four types of charity-givers, or four types of temperaments, the sage creates a framework within which each type illuminates the others.

#### Examples

**Example 1: Hillel and Shammai Compared.**

The most famous comparison of persons in all of rabbinic literature is the ongoing comparison of Hillel and Shammai, and of their respective schools. The Talmud records:

> <span dir="rtl">שלוש שנים נחלקו בית שמאי ובית הלל. הללו אומרים: הלכה כמותנו, והללו אומרים: הלכה כמותנו. יצאה בת קול ואמרה: אלו ואלו דברי אלהים חיים הן, והלכה כבית הלל</span>
>
> *For three years Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel disputed. These said: The halakhah is according to us. And these said: The halakhah is according to us. A heavenly voice went forth and declared: Both these and these are the words of the living God -- but the halakhah is according to Beit Hillel.*[^169]

The student should contemplate this passage with great care, for it contains the deepest teaching about the nature of comparison. The heavenly voice does not say: "Beit Hillel are right and Beit Shammai are wrong." It says: "*Both* are the words of the living God." The comparison does not end in the annihilation of one side. Both positions retain their status as divine speech. Yet the halakhah must be decided, and it follows Beit Hillel. Why? The Talmud continues:

> <span dir="rtl">ומפני מה זכו בית הלל לקבוע הלכה כמותן? מפני שנוחין ועלובין היו, ושונין דבריהן ודברי בית שמאי. ולא עוד אלא שמקדימין דברי בית שמאי לדבריהם</span>
>
> *And why did Beit Hillel merit that the halakhah be fixed according to them? Because they were pleasant and forbearing, and they would study their own teachings and the teachings of Beit Shammai. And not only that, but they would present the teachings of Beit Shammai before their own.*[^170]

Here the comparison reveals its moral dimension. Beit Hillel prevailed not because their arguments were intrinsically stronger but because their *character* was more fitting for the transmission of Torah. They were humble; they studied the opposing view; they presented the opposing view before their own. The comparison of the two schools is simultaneously a comparison of two intellectual virtues: rigor (Shammai) and humility (Hillel). The tradition needs both, but humility takes precedence -- because the humble scholar can contain the rigorous scholar's views within his own teaching, while the rigorous scholar who lacks humility cannot.

The student should also observe how the comparison of Hillel and Shammai as individuals extends beyond halakhah into character. In the famous narrative of the three converts (BT Shabbat 31a), Shammai drives away the impatient questioner with a builder's cubit, while Hillel receives him with patience and converts him with a single sentence. The two sages are compared not merely in their legal positions but in their entire approach to human beings:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם יהא אדם ענוותן כהלל, ואל יהא קפדן כשמאי</span>
>
> *A person should always be gentle like Hillel and not exacting like Shammai.*[^171]

Yet even this comparison does not simply dismiss Shammai. Shammai's rigor has its place: his insistence on exactness preserves the integrity of the law. The tradition preserves both models precisely because both are needed -- gentleness for the teacher, rigor for the jurist. The comparison illuminates both.

**Example 2: Torah Study and Good Deeds Compared.**

One of the great debates in the bet midrash took the form of a comparison: which is greater, Torah study or the performance of good deeds? The Talmud records:

> <span dir="rtl">וכבר היה רבי טרפון וזקנים מסובין בעלייה בבית נתזה בלוד. נשאלה שאלה זו בפניהם: תלמוד גדול או מעשה גדול? נענה רבי טרפון ואמר: מעשה גדול. נענה רבי עקיבא ואמר: תלמוד גדול. נענו כולם ואמרו: תלמוד גדול, שהתלמוד מביא לידי מעשה</span>
>
> *It once happened that Rabbi Tarfon and the elders were reclining in the upper chamber of the house of Nit'zah in Lydda. This question was raised before them: Is study greater or is action greater? Rabbi Tarfon answered and said: Action is greater. Rabbi Akiva answered and said: Study is greater. They all answered and said: Study is greater, for study leads to action.*[^172]

The student should analyze this comparison with care. First, notice the setting: a formal symposium, with named sages reclining in a specific location. This is deliberative discourse at its highest level -- the comparison of two abstract goods before an assembly of the wise. Second, notice the structure: two positions are stated (Rabbi Tarfon for action, Rabbi Akiva for study), and then a resolution is offered that transcends both. Study is greater -- but not because action is unimportant. Study is greater *because it leads to action*. The comparison does not annihilate one of the two terms; it establishes a hierarchy that preserves both. Study is the root; action is the fruit. Without the root, there is no fruit. But a root without fruit is barren.

This pattern -- comparison leading not to the rejection of one term but to a hierarchy that preserves both -- is characteristic of rabbinic comparison. The sage who compares does not seek to destroy one of his subjects. He seeks to understand how they relate, which takes priority, and why. This is a fundamentally different mode of comparison from the agonistic comparisons of the Greek rhetorical tradition, where the synkrisis often culminates in a decisive judgment of superiority.[^173]

**Example 3: The First Temple and the Second Temple Compared.**

The Talmud records a comparison of historical eras that is breathtaking in its theological implications:

> <span dir="rtl">מקדש ראשון מפני מה חרב? מפני שלוש דברים שהיו בו: עבודה זרה, וגלוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים... אבל מקדש שני, שהיו עוסקין בתורה ובמצוות וגמילות חסדים, מפני מה חרב? מפני שהיתה בו שנאת חנם. ללמדך ששקולה שנאת חנם כנגד שלוש עבירות: עבודה זרה, גלוי עריות, ושפיכות דמים</span>
>
> *The First Temple -- why was it destroyed? Because of three things that were in it: idolatry, sexual immorality, and bloodshed... But the Second Temple, where they were engaged in Torah and mitzvot and acts of lovingkindness -- why was it destroyed? Because of groundless hatred. This teaches you that groundless hatred is equivalent to the three sins of idolatry, sexual immorality, and bloodshed.*[^174]

The student should observe the structure of this comparison. Two historical periods are set side by side, and the comparison reveals something that could not be seen by examining either period alone. The First Temple generation sinned openly and egregiously -- their sins are the three cardinal transgressions. The Second Temple generation was, by all outward measures, righteous -- they studied Torah, performed commandments, and practiced charity. Yet their hidden sin -- groundless hatred of one another -- was, in the divine calculus, equivalent to all three cardinal sins combined.

This comparison teaches the student something about the nature of comparison itself: sometimes the comparison reveals that appearances are deceiving. The generation that looked worse was, in one sense, more honest in its wickedness. The generation that looked better harbored a sin so subtle and so corrosive that it outweighed the overt transgressions of their predecessors. The comparison forces the student to reassess his assumptions -- to see that outward piety and inward malice can coexist, and that the latter is more destructive than the former.

The comparison also teaches a lesson about the *method* of comparison. The sage does not merely list the sins of each period; he *weighs* them against each other. He uses the comparison as a scale: if the destruction of the First Temple was caused by three great sins, and the destruction of the Second Temple by one sin, then that one sin must be equal in weight to all three combined. The comparison generates a theological proposition that could not be derived from either period considered alone.

**Example 4: The Numerical Lists of Mishnah Avot.**

The fifth chapter of Pirkei Avot is a treasury of structured comparisons in the form of numerical lists. Consider:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מדות בתלמידים: מהר לשמוע ומהר לאבד -- יצא שכרו בהפסדו. קשה לשמוע וקשה לאבד -- יצא הפסדו בשכרו. מהר לשמוע וקשה לאבד -- חכם. קשה לשמוע ומהר לאבד -- זה חלק רע</span>
>
> *Four types of students: Quick to learn and quick to forget -- his gain is offset by his loss. Slow to learn and slow to forget -- his loss is offset by his gain. Quick to learn and slow to forget -- this is a wise person. Slow to learn and quick to forget -- this is an evil portion.*[^175]

This is comparison at its most systematic. Two variables -- speed of acquisition and speed of loss -- are combined in all four possible permutations. The result is a complete typology: every student falls into one of these four categories. The comparison illuminates each type by setting it beside the others: the "wise person" (quick to learn, slow to forget) is understood most fully when contrasted with his opposite (slow to learn, quick to forget), and each mixed type (quick-quick, slow-slow) is understood as a balance of gain and loss.

The same chapter provides similar fourfold comparisons of charity-givers:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מדות בנותני צדקה: הרוצה שיתן ולא יתנו אחרים -- עינו רעה בשל אחרים. יתנו אחרים והוא לא יתן -- עינו רעה בשלו. יתן ויתנו אחרים -- חסיד. לא יתן ולא יתנו אחרים -- רשע</span>
>
> *Four types of charity-givers: One who wants to give but does not want others to give -- his eye is evil toward others. One who wants others to give but does not want to give himself -- his eye is evil toward his own. One who wants to give and wants others to give -- this is a pious person. One who does not want to give and does not want others to give -- this is a wicked person.*[^176]

Again, two variables (willingness to give personally, willingness for others to give) are combined in all four permutations. The comparison reveals not only the character of each type but the hidden motivations behind charitable behavior: the person who gives but does not want others to give is motivated not by generosity but by the desire for exclusive recognition. The comparison exposes what would remain hidden if each type were described in isolation.

These numerical lists are a distinctive contribution of rabbinic pedagogy to the art of comparison. Where the Greek synkrisis typically compares two subjects, the rabbinic numerical list compares three, four, seven, or even ten -- creating a complete taxonomy through systematic comparison.[^177]

**Example 5: "Who Is Greater -- Moses or Aaron?"**

The question of comparing Moses and Aaron is itself a subject of rabbinic reflection. The sages noted that Scripture sometimes names Moses before Aaron and sometimes Aaron before Moses:

> <span dir="rtl">אלה אהרן ומשה... אלה משה ואהרן. לומר לך: משה ואהרן שקולין, אהרן ומשה שקולין</span>
>
> *"These are Aaron and Moses"... "These are Moses and Aaron" -- to tell you: Moses and Aaron are equal; Aaron and Moses are equal.*[^178]

The varying order teaches that neither is subordinate to the other. Each excelled in his own domain -- Moses in prophecy and law, Aaron in priesthood and peacemaking. The tradition that Aaron was *ohev shalom ve-rodef shalom* -- "a lover of peace and a pursuer of peace" (Avot 1:12) -- places him alongside Moses not as an inferior but as a complement. The prophet needs the priest; the lawgiver needs the peacemaker. The comparison does not rank them hierarchically but reveals their complementary greatness.

This pattern -- comparison that reveals complementarity rather than hierarchy -- is deeply characteristic of rabbinic thought. The sage does not ask "who is better?" but "what does each contribute that the other does not?" This is the ethical heart of the comparison exercise: it teaches the student to see the good in multiple subjects, to resist the temptation of simplistic ranking, and to appreciate that the world requires many kinds of excellence.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice comparison by following this method:

First, select two subjects that invite comparison -- two sages, two virtues, two historical events, two mitzvot. The subjects must have enough in common that comparison is meaningful, but enough difference that comparison is illuminating. To compare Moses and a common laborer would be pointless; the gap is too great. To compare two nearly identical rulings would be tedious; the similarity is too close. The art lies in choosing subjects that are different enough to be interesting but similar enough to be instructive.

Second, describe each subject separately, noting its essential qualities. This is where the skills of the encomium come into play: the student must be able to present each subject in its full dignity before setting them beside each other.

Third, identify the specific points of comparison. Do not compare everything about the two subjects -- compare them along specific axes: their origins, their methods, their achievements, their legacy, their virtues, their limitations. Each axis of comparison should yield a specific insight.

Fourth, draw a conclusion -- but let the conclusion preserve both subjects. Do not compare in order to destroy. Compare in order to illuminate.

The teacher should assign exercises such as: "Compare the leadership of Moses with the leadership of Joshua -- not to declare one superior, but to illuminate the virtues of each." Or: "Compare the giving of charity with the study of Torah, arguing first for the primacy of one, then for the primacy of the other, and finally reaching a resolution that honors both." Or: "Compare two meshalim that teach the same lesson and determine which is more effective, and why."

The advanced student should attempt the comparison of times: "Compare the generation of the wilderness with your own generation. In what ways are we like them? In what ways are we different? What can we learn from the comparison?" This exercise requires not only rhetorical skill but historical imagination and moral honesty -- for the student who compares his own generation to an earlier one must be willing to see both the superiority and the inferiority of his own time.

> <span dir="rtl">אל תאמר: מה היה שהימים הראשונים היו טובים מאלה, כי לא מחכמה שאלת על זה</span>
>
> *Do not say: How was it that the former days were better than these? For it is not from wisdom that you ask this.* (Ecclesiastes 7:10)

The sage compares not to romanticize the past but to learn from it -- and to learn from the present as well.[^179]

---

### II.K. Speech-in-Character (Dibbur be-Shem / Ethopoeia)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, we come now to an exercise in which the rabbinic tradition excels beyond all others: the *speech-in-character* -- the composition of a speech that a specific person might have said in a given situation. The Greeks called this exercise *ethopoeia* -- the making of character through speech -- and their teachers of rhetoric assigned students to compose speeches such as: "What would Achilles say upon learning of the death of Patroclus?" or "What would Andromache say as she watched Hector go out to battle?"[^180]

But what the Greek students practiced as a classroom exercise, the sages of the bet midrash practiced as a sacred art. For the midrash is, in large measure, the art of giving voice to those whom Scripture left silent. What did Abraham say when he was commanded to bind his son Isaac? Scripture tells us only: "And Abraham rose early in the morning" (Genesis 22:3). But what was in his heart? What words did he speak -- to himself, to God, to the angels? The midrash undertakes to supply these words, and in doing so, it transforms a terse biblical narrative into a living drama of faith, doubt, and obedience.

What did Moses say at the burning bush beyond what Scripture records? What thoughts passed through his mind when God said, "I will send you to Pharaoh"? Scripture gives us Moses's objections -- "Who am I that I should go to Pharaoh?" (Exodus 3:11), "They will not believe me" (Exodus 4:1), "I am not a man of words" (Exodus 4:10) -- but the midrash detects in these spare phrases an entire inner drama that it elaborates with astonishing psychological depth.

The student should understand: when the sage puts words in the mouth of a biblical character, he is not engaging in mere fiction. He is performing an act of interpretive imagination that is disciplined by the constraints of the text. The words he invents must be *consistent* with what Scripture tells us about the character. They must arise naturally from the situation Scripture describes. They must illuminate something that the text implies but does not state. The sage who invents a speech for Abraham must know Abraham -- must have studied every verse in which Abraham appears, every action he takes, every word he speaks -- so thoroughly that the invented speech sounds like something Abraham would truly say. This is not fantasy; it is faithful imagination.

> <span dir="rtl">כל מה שתלמיד ותיק עתיד לחדש, כבר נאמר למשה מסיני</span>
>
> *Everything that a seasoned student will one day innovate was already spoken to Moses at Sinai.*[^181]

The sage who composes a speech for Abraham is not adding to Scripture; he is uncovering what was always there, latent in the text, waiting for the interpreter's ear to hear it.

#### Types of Speech-in-Character

The teachers of rhetoric distinguished three types of this exercise, and all three find parallels in the rabbinic tradition:[^182]

**Speeches for Known Persons (Eidolopoeia).** This is the composition of a speech for a specific, named individual whose character is known from Scripture or tradition. The sage composes a speech for Abraham, for Moses, for David, for Esther -- for any named figure whose words and deeds are recorded and whose character can be inferred from the record. This is the most common type in the midrash, and the most demanding, because the audience knows the character and will immediately detect any false note.

**Speeches for Types of Persons (Prosopopoeia).** This is the composition of a speech for a type rather than an individual -- what would a mother say upon losing her child, what would a convert say upon entering the covenant, what would a soldier say as he goes into battle. The Mishnah's discussion of the priest anointed for war, who addresses the troops before battle (Deuteronomy 20:1-4; Mishnah Sotah 8:1), is an example of this type: the priest's speech is prescribed not for a specific individual but for anyone who holds that office. Similarly, the confessional formula recited over the tithe (Deuteronomy 26:13-15) is a speech assigned to a type -- any Israelite farmer who brings his tithe.

**Speeches for Non-Human Entities.** This is the most daring type: the composition of a speech for God Himself, for the angels, for the Torah personified, for abstract qualities such as Justice and Mercy. The midrash is extraordinarily bold in this regard -- far bolder than the Greek rhetorical tradition, which generally confined its prosopopoeia to human speakers. The rabbis put words in the mouth of God, of the Torah, of the Shekhinah, of the attribute of Justice and the attribute of Mercy -- and they do so not as blasphemy but as theology, using invented speech to explore the inner life of the divine.

#### Examples

**Example 1: What Did the Angels Say When Israel Crossed the Sea?**

The Talmud records a scene of extraordinary theological daring. When the Egyptians were drowning in the Red Sea, the ministering angels wished to sing a song of praise. But God rebuked them:

> <span dir="rtl">בקשו מלאכי השרת לומר שירה לפני הקדוש ברוך הוא. אמר להם הקדוש ברוך הוא: מעשה ידי טובעים בים ואתם אומרים שירה?</span>
>
> *The ministering angels wished to sing a song before the Holy One, blessed be He. The Holy One, blessed be He, said to them: My creatures are drowning in the sea, and you would sing a song?*[^183]

This passage is a masterpiece of ethopoeia on multiple levels. First, the angels are given a characteristic action -- they wish to sing, because singing praise is what angels do. Their desire to sing is not malicious; it arises naturally from their nature. Second, God is given a speech that reveals the divine character: even at the moment of Israel's triumph, God grieves for the Egyptian dead, because they too are "My creatures" -- *ma'aseh yadai*, the work of My hands. The speech attributed to God contains an entire theology of divine compassion in a single sentence.

The student should observe: the sage who composed this passage did not find it in Scripture. The Torah says nothing about the angels' reaction at the Red Sea. But the sage inferred it -- from the general principle that angels sing (Isaiah 6:3), from the specific circumstance of the drowning, and from the theological conviction that God's mercy extends even to the wicked. The invented speech is faithful to the character of God as revealed throughout Scripture, and it illuminates a dimension of the Exodus narrative that the text itself leaves unexplored.

A parallel passage in BT Sanhedrin 39b attributes a similar sentiment to God but in a slightly different context, when the nations of the world sought to praise God after the fall of Egypt:

> <span dir="rtl">אין הקדוש ברוך הוא שמח במפלתן של רשעים</span>
>
> *The Holy One, blessed be He, does not rejoice in the downfall of the wicked.*[^184]

Here the ethopoeia is even more compressed: God's character is revealed not through a quoted speech but through a narrative description of His emotional state. The sage claims to know what God feels -- and what God feels is sorrow, even for His enemies. This is ethopoeia at its most theologically audacious.

**Example 2: Abraham Before Nimrod.**

Genesis Rabbah 38 contains an elaborate narrative in which the young Abraham, having smashed his father Terah's idols, is brought before King Nimrod for judgment. The midrash constructs a dialogue between Abraham and Nimrod that is entirely absent from the biblical text:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לו נמרוד: נעבוד לאש? אמר לו אברהם: נעבוד למים שמכבין את האש. אמר לו: נעבוד למים. אמר לו: נעבוד לענן שנושא את המים. אמר לו: נעבוד לענן. אמר לו: נעבוד לרוח שמפזר את הענן. אמר לו: נעבוד לרוח. אמר לו: נעבוד לאדם שסובל את הרוח. אמר לו: מילין את משתעי -- אני איני משתחוה אלא לאור. הריני משליכך בתוכו, ויבוא אלוה שאתה משתחוה לו ויצילך הימנו</span>
>
> *Nimrod said to him: Let us worship fire. Abraham said: Let us rather worship water, which extinguishes fire. He said: Let us worship water. Abraham said: Let us rather worship the cloud, which carries the water. He said: Let us worship the cloud. Abraham said: Let us rather worship the wind, which disperses the cloud. He said: Let us worship the wind. Abraham said: Let us rather worship the human being, who withstands the wind. Nimrod said: You are speaking mere words -- I bow only to fire. I will throw you into it, and let the God you worship come and save you from it.*[^185]

This is ethopoeia of the highest order. The sage has constructed a philosophical dialogue -- a chain of reasoning in which each proposed deity is surpassed by a greater natural force -- and placed it in the mouth of Abraham. The speech reveals Abraham's character: his logical mind, his fearless wit, his willingness to follow an argument wherever it leads, even to the mouth of the furnace. Nimrod's character is also revealed: his impatience with reason, his resort to brute force when argument fails him, his worship of the most destructive element rather than the most creative.

The student should notice that this dialogue has no basis whatsoever in the biblical text of Genesis. Abraham's confrontation with Nimrod is not mentioned in Scripture. Yet the midrash constructs it with such skill that it feels inevitable -- as if this is exactly what must have happened, given what we know about Abraham's character from the rest of the biblical narrative. This is the mark of successful ethopoeia: the invented speech feels not invented but discovered, as if it had always been there, hidden in the white spaces between the letters of the Torah.

**Example 3: Moses's Speech to God After the Golden Calf.**

The Talmud in Berakhot 32a constructs an extraordinary dialogue between Moses and God in the aftermath of the Golden Calf. God says to Moses, "Let Me alone, that My anger may burn against them, and I will consume them" (Exodus 32:10). The midrash asks: why did God say "let Me alone"? Is anyone holding Him back? And it answers: God was hinting to Moses that the matter depended on him -- that if Moses would pray, God would relent.

Moses then presents a series of arguments to God, each one more daring than the last:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר משה לפני הקדוש ברוך הוא: רבונו של עולם, כסא של שלוש רגלים אינו יכול לעמוד לפניך בשעת כעסך, כסא של רגל אחת על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *Moses said before the Holy One, blessed be He: Master of the Universe, a throne of three legs [the three patriarchs] cannot stand before You in Your hour of anger -- a throne of one leg [Moses alone], how much less!*[^186]

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לפניו: רבונו של עולם, אתה אמרת לי: לך רד -- רד מגדולתך. כלום נתתי לך גדולה אלא בשביל ישראל? ועכשיו ישראל חטאו, אני למה לי?</span>
>
> *He said before Him: Master of the Universe, You said to me "Go, descend" -- descend from your greatness. I was given greatness only for the sake of Israel. Now that Israel has sinned, what use am I?*

And still further, Moses argues:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לפניו: רבונו של עולם, זכור לאברהם ליצחק ולישראל עבדיך -- אם באש אתה שורפן, זכור לאברהם שנתן עצמו לישרף על קדושת שמך. אם בחרב אתה הורגן, זכור ליצחק שפשט צוארו על גבי המזבח. אם בגלות אתה מגלן, זכור ליעקב שגלה לחרן</span>
>
> *He said before Him: Master of the Universe, remember Abraham, Isaac, and Israel Your servants -- if You would burn them with fire, remember Abraham who offered himself to be burned for the sanctification of Your name. If You would slay them with the sword, remember Isaac who stretched out his neck upon the altar. If You would exile them, remember Jacob who went into exile to Haran.*[^187]

The student should observe the rhetorical brilliance of this invented dialogue. Moses deploys three distinct types of argument: first, a *qal va-homer* (a fortiori argument) -- if the three patriarchs together cannot stand before God's anger, how can Moses alone? Second, a *logical consequence* argument -- if Moses's greatness exists only for Israel's sake, then destroying Israel renders Moses's mission meaningless. Third, an *appeal to precedent* -- each form of punishment that God might inflict has already been endured voluntarily by one of the patriarchs, so punishing their descendants would dishonor the patriarchs' merit.

This is advocacy of the highest order -- a forensic speech in the heavenly court, with Moses as defense attorney and God as judge. The sage who composed this passage has imagined his way into Moses's mind and constructed arguments that are both rhetorically powerful and theologically profound. Each argument reveals something about Moses's character: his humility (he does not claim to be able to save Israel by his own merit), his loyalty (he ties his fate to Israel's), and his knowledge of tradition (he invokes the patriarchs with precision, matching each patriarch to the specific form of suffering they endured).

**Example 4: The Dialogue Between God and Torah Before Creation.**

Genesis Rabbah opens with one of the most remarkable examples of ethopoeia in all of rabbinic literature -- a dialogue between God and the Torah before the creation of the world:

> <span dir="rtl">התורה אומרת: אני הייתי כלי אומנותו של הקדוש ברוך הוא. בנוהג שבעולם, מלך בשר ודם בונה פלטין -- אינו בונה אותה מדעת עצמו אלא מדעת אומן, והאומן אינו בונה אותה מדעת עצמו אלא דיפתראות ופינקסאות יש לו... כך היה הקדוש ברוך הוא מביט בתורה ובורא את העולם</span>
>
> *The Torah says: I was the artisan's tool of the Holy One, blessed be He. In the way of the world, a king of flesh and blood who builds a palace does not build it from his own knowledge alone but from the knowledge of an architect, and the architect does not build from his own knowledge alone but has blueprints and notebooks... So the Holy One, blessed be He, looked into the Torah and created the world.*[^188]

Here the Torah itself is given a voice -- it speaks in the first person, describing its role in creation. This is prosopopoeia of the most daring kind: an abstract entity (the Torah) is personified and given speech. The Torah describes itself as God's blueprint, the architectural plan according to which the world was constructed. The metaphor of the architect and his blueprints transforms an abstract theological proposition (God created the world through Torah) into a vivid, concrete image.

The student should note that the passage opens with the words "The Torah says" -- *ha-Torah omeret*. This formula signals to the listener that what follows is ethopoeia: the sage is putting words in the mouth of a non-human entity. The listener understands the convention and does not take the speech as literal dictation from the Torah scroll. Rather, the listener understands that the sage is using the device of personification to explore a theological truth -- that Torah precedes and undergirds the created world.

**Example 5: Rachel's Speech Before God.**

In Lamentations Rabbah, Petihta 24, the midrash constructs one of the most emotionally powerful examples of ethopoeia in all of rabbinic literature. When Israel is sent into exile after the destruction of the Temple, the patriarchs and Moses intercede before God, each one recounting his own merits and pleading for mercy. Abraham invokes the binding of Isaac. Jacob invokes his years of labor. Moses invokes his faithful leadership. But none of them succeeds in moving God to compassion. Then Rachel speaks:

> <span dir="rtl">רבונו של עולם, גלוי לפניך שיעקב עבדך אהבני אהבה יתירה, ועבד בשבילי לאבא שבע שנים, וכשהשלימו אותם שבע שנים... יעץ אבי להחליפני לאחותי... ולא עוד אלא שמסרתי לה כל הסימנים שמסר לי יעקב... וכבשתי את רחמי וחסתי על אחותי שלא תצא לחרפה. ומה אני שאני בשר ודם עפר ואפר לא קינאתי לצרה שלי ולא הוצאתיה לידי בושה... ואתה מלך חי וקיים רחמן, מפני מה קינאת לעבודה זרה שאין בה ממש והגלית בני?</span>
>
> *Master of the Universe, it is revealed before You that Jacob Your servant loved me with an extraordinary love, and he labored for my father for seven years on my behalf, and when those seven years were completed... my father schemed to substitute my sister for me... And more than that: I gave her all the secret signs that Jacob had given me... And I suppressed my compassion and had pity on my sister so that she would not be put to shame. And if I, who am flesh and blood, dust and ashes, was not jealous of my rival and did not expose her to shame... then You, who are the living and eternal King, the Merciful One -- why were You jealous of idolatry, which has no substance, and exiled my children?*[^189]

The student should observe: this is the climax of the entire passage. Where the patriarchs and Moses argued from merit and precedent, Rachel argues from analogy -- a *qal va-homer* of devastating force. If a mortal woman could suppress her jealousy and protect her rival, surely the immortal God can suppress His jealousy of empty idols and protect His children. The argument succeeds where all others failed:

> <span dir="rtl">מיד נתגלגלו רחמיו של הקדוש ברוך הוא ואמר: בשבילך רחל אני מחזיר את ישראל למקומן</span>
>
> *Immediately the compassion of the Holy One, blessed be He, was stirred, and He said: For your sake, Rachel, I will return Israel to their place.*

The sage who composed Rachel's speech has achieved something extraordinary. He has given voice to a woman who has been largely silent in the biblical narrative, and he has made her the most effective advocate in the heavenly court -- more effective than Abraham, more effective than Moses. Her argument succeeds because it is grounded not in abstract merit but in lived experience: she knows what it means to swallow jealousy, because she has done it. The speech is faithful to Rachel's character as Scripture reveals it -- patient, selfless, willing to sacrifice her own happiness for her sister's dignity -- and it extends that character into a theological argument of breathtaking power.

#### The Ethical Dimension

The student should understand that this exercise carries a particular ethical responsibility. When you compose a speech in the character of a biblical figure or a sage, you must imagine that character *faithfully*. You must not project your own views onto the character. You must not make Abraham sound like you, or Moses argue as you would argue. You must study the character so thoroughly that you can set aside your own perspective and inhabit theirs.

This is an exercise in *empathy* as much as in rhetoric. The sage who can imagine what Abraham felt at the binding of Isaac has achieved a depth of understanding that mere intellectual analysis cannot reach. The sage who can give voice to Rachel's grief has entered into the experience of another human being so completely that the boundary between self and other has become permeable.

But the student must also know the limits of this exercise. The speeches we compose are *interpretive* -- they are our best attempts to understand what a character might have said, given what Scripture tells us. They are not prophecy. They are not revelation. They are the product of disciplined imagination, and they remain subject to the text that generated them. If a proposed speech contradicts what Scripture explicitly states about a character, then the speech must be revised or abandoned. The text has authority; our interpretations serve the text, not the reverse.

> <span dir="rtl">אל תעש דבריך עיקר ודברי תורה טפל</span>
>
> *Do not make your own words primary and the words of Torah secondary.*

#### The Exercise

The student should practice composing speeches in character by following this method:

First, select a biblical scene in which a character is present but silent -- or in which a character speaks briefly but the situation calls for more extensive speech. Good candidates include: Ruth addressing Naomi on the road from Moab (Ruth 1, where Ruth's famous speech is preserved but Naomi's response and Ruth's inner deliberation are not); Esther preparing to approach the king uninvited (Esther 4-5, where the decision is recorded but the inner deliberation is not); Joseph recognizing his brothers in Egypt (Genesis 42, where Joseph's actions are described but his private thoughts are not).

Second, study every verse in which this character appears. Note the character's distinctive traits, speech patterns, values, and relationships. If it is Abraham, note his hospitality, his obedience, his willingness to argue with God (Genesis 18), and his silence before Isaac on the way to Moriah. If it is Moses, note his humility, his anger, his insistence on justice, and his love for Israel.

Third, compose a speech that arises naturally from the situation and is consistent with the character's established traits. The speech should reveal something about the character that the text implies but does not state. It should feel, to the listener, like a discovery -- as if these words had always been hidden in the text, waiting to be found.

Fourth, test the speech against the text. Does anything in the speech contradict what Scripture explicitly says? Does the speech remain faithful to the character's known values and manner of speaking? Would a listener who knows the biblical text well accept this speech as plausible?

The teacher should assign: "Compose the speech that Ruth might have given to Naomi, using what Scripture implies but does not state. Consider: what arguments would Ruth have made? What fears would she have addressed? How would she have spoken of her dead husband, of her native land, of the God of Israel whom she was choosing to follow?" The student should present his composition to the class, and the class should evaluate it for fidelity to the character, rhetorical effectiveness, and interpretive insight.[^190]

---

### II.L. Description (Tziyyur / Ekphrasis)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, we come now to the twelfth exercise: *description* -- in our language, *tziyyur*, the painting of a scene with words. What is description? It is vivid verbal depiction that brings the subject before the eyes of the listener, so that he does not merely *hear* about the thing described but *sees* it, as if he were standing there himself.

The teachers of rhetoric called this quality *enargeia* -- vividness, the power of making the absent present through speech.[^191] And they are right to prize it, for the sage who can describe what he has seen -- or what tradition reports -- so vividly that his listener trembles, weeps, or rejoices as if present at the event has achieved the highest aim of the art. He has overcome the distance of time and space and brought his listener into the living presence of the thing described.

Make your student *see* what you describe, as if he were standing there. This is the principle. When you describe the Temple service on Yom Kippur, your listener should feel the crush of the crowd in the Temple courts. When you describe the giving of Torah at Sinai, your listener should hear the thunder and see the lightning. When you describe the destruction of Jerusalem, your listener should smell the smoke and hear the weeping.

> <span dir="rtl">ציור -- להביא את הדבר לפני עיני השומע, כאילו הוא עומד שם</span>
>
> *Tziyyur -- to bring the matter before the eyes of the listener, as if he were standing there.*

The student should understand why this exercise matters. The Torah is not an abstraction. It records events that happened in specific places, at specific times, to specific people. The sage who cannot make those events vivid to his listener has reduced the Torah to a collection of propositions, stripped of their lived reality. But the sage who can describe the binding of Isaac so that the listener sees the knife glinting in the sunlight, hears the wood crackling under Isaac's feet, feels the wind on the mountaintop -- that sage has restored the Torah to its full power as a record of encounter between God and humanity.

Moreover, description is an act of memory. The Temple has been destroyed. The sacrificial service has ceased. The pilgrimage festivals are no longer celebrated as they were. The sage who describes the Temple service in vivid detail preserves it in the only medium that remains: the spoken word. Description becomes, in the absence of the thing described, an act of sacred remembrance -- a way of keeping alive what would otherwise be lost to oblivion.

#### Types of Description

The student should know the principal types of description employed by the sages:[^192]

**Description of Persons.** The physical appearance, bearing, and distinctive features of a sage or biblical figure. The rabbis sometimes describe the beauty, the stature, the dress, or the physical peculiarities of their subjects. These descriptions serve not merely to satisfy curiosity but to convey something about the character's inner nature -- for the sages believed that a person's appearance could reflect his spiritual condition.

**Description of Places.** The Temple, Jerusalem, the bet midrash, a city under siege, a garden, a wilderness. Descriptions of places in rabbinic literature serve to ground abstract teachings in concrete settings. The description of the Temple is the most elaborate and sustained example, occupying entire chapters of the Mishnah and reaching extraordinary levels of architectural detail.

**Description of Times.** The dawn of Yom Kippur, the evening of Passover, the moment before Shabbat descends, the hour of the destruction. Descriptions of times serve to evoke the emotional and spiritual quality of particular moments -- the anticipation, the solemnity, the terror, the joy that attaches to sacred or catastrophic time.

**Description of Events.** The crossing of the Red Sea, the giving of Torah, the destruction of the Temple, the martyrdom of a sage. Event-descriptions are the most complex, because they combine all the other types: they involve persons acting in places at times, and the description must integrate all these elements into a coherent and vivid scene.

**Description of Objects and Artworks.** The vessels of the Temple, the priestly garments, the ark of the covenant, the menorah. The Mishnah and the tannaitic midrashim contain remarkably detailed descriptions of ritual objects, and these descriptions serve both practical and liturgical purposes -- they preserve the specifications of objects that can no longer be seen, and they evoke the splendor of the sacred service.

#### Examples

**Example 1: The Temple Service on Yom Kippur.**

The Mishnah's description of the Yom Kippur service in tractate Yoma is the most sustained and elaborate example of ekphrasis in all of rabbinic literature. Consider the description of the High Priest's preparations and appearance:

> <span dir="rtl">פרשו סדין של בוץ בינו לבין העם. פשט ירד וטבל, עלה ונסתפג. הביאו לו בגדי זהב, ולבש וקידש ידיו ורגליו</span>
>
> *They spread a linen sheet between him and the people. He undressed, went down and immersed, came up and dried himself. They brought him the golden garments, and he dressed and sanctified his hands and feet.*[^193]

And the description of the High Priest emerging from the Holy of Holies:

> <span dir="rtl">ובא לו אצל פר ואצל שעיר הנשרפים... ובא לו אצל שעיר המשתלח וסמך שתי ידיו עליו והתודה... כך היה אומר: אנא ה', חטאו עוו פשעו לפניך עמך בית ישראל. אנא ה', כפר נא לחטאים ולעוונות ולפשעים שחטאו ושעוו ושפשעו לפניך עמך בית ישראל. ככתוב בתורת משה עבדך: כי ביום הזה יכפר עליכם לטהר אתכם, מכל חטאתיכם לפני ה' תטהרו</span>
>
> *He came to the bull and to the goat that were to be burned... He came to the goat that was to be sent away and laid both his hands upon it and confessed... Thus did he say: Please, O Lord, Your people the house of Israel have sinned, have transgressed, have committed iniquity before You. Please, O Lord, atone for the sins and transgressions and iniquities that Your people the house of Israel have sinned and transgressed and committed before You. As it is written in the Torah of Moses Your servant: "For on this day He shall atone for you, to purify you; from all your sins before the Lord you shall be purified."*[^194]

And then the extraordinary moment:

> <span dir="rtl">וכשהיה מזכיר את השם, הכהנים והעם העומדים בעזרה, כשהיו שומעים שם המפורש שהוא יוצא מפי כהן גדול, היו כורעים ומשתחוים ונופלים על פניהם ואומרים: ברוך שם כבוד מלכותו לעולם ועד</span>
>
> *And when he pronounced the Name, the priests and the people standing in the Temple court, when they heard the Explicit Name issuing from the mouth of the High Priest, would kneel and prostrate themselves and fall on their faces and say: Blessed be the name of His glorious kingdom forever and ever.*[^195]

The student should observe the descriptive technique at work here. The Mishnah does not merely state that the High Priest confessed over the goat; it gives us the exact words of the confession, the physical gestures (laying both hands, kneeling, prostrating), the emotional response of the crowd (falling on their faces), and their verbal response (the blessing). The description moves from the visual (the High Priest's movements) to the auditory (the pronunciation of the Name) to the physical (kneeling and falling) to the verbal (the response). It engages multiple senses simultaneously, creating an experience rather than merely conveying information.

This is description in the service of sacred memory. The Temple has been destroyed; the Yom Kippur service can no longer be performed. But the Mishnah's description is so vivid, so precise, so richly detailed that the reader who studies it can, in some measure, be present at the service. The description preserves the experience even after the reality it describes has ceased to exist. This is the deepest function of ekphrasis in the bet midrash: it makes memory do the work of presence.

**Example 2: The Joy of the Water Drawing.**

The Mishnah in Sukkah 5:1-4 contains a famous declaration followed by an elaborate description:

> <span dir="rtl">מי שלא ראה שמחת בית השואבה, לא ראה שמחה מימיו</span>
>
> *He who has not seen the joy of the Water Drawing celebration has never seen joy in his life.*[^196]

This bold claim -- that no other joy compares -- is not left as an empty assertion. The Mishnah proceeds to describe the celebration in extraordinary detail:

> <span dir="rtl">חסידים ואנשי מעשה היו מרקדים לפניהם באבוקות של אור שבידיהם, ואומרים לפניהם דברי שירות ותושבחות. והלויים בכנורות ובנבלים ובמצלתים ובחצוצרות ובכלי שיר בלא מספר, על חמש עשרה מעלות היורדות מעזרת ישראל לעזרת נשים</span>
>
> *Pious men and men of good deeds would dance before them with burning torches in their hands, and they would recite before them words of song and praise. And the Levites with lyres and harps and cymbals and trumpets and countless musical instruments, upon the fifteen steps that descend from the Court of the Israelites to the Women's Court.*[^197]

The description continues with the specific actions of named sages:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו עליו על רבן שמעון בן גמליאל: כשהיה שמח שמחת בית השואבה, היה נוטל שמונה אבוקות של אור וזורק אחת ונוטל אחת, ואין נוגעות זו בזו</span>
>
> *They said of Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel: When he would rejoice at the Water Drawing celebration, he would take eight burning torches and throw one up and catch another, and none of them would touch each other.*

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו: כשהיה רבן שמעון בן גמליאל משתחוה, היה נועץ שני גודליו בארץ ושוחה ונושק את הרצפה וזוקף</span>
>
> *They said: When Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel would prostrate himself, he would plant both thumbs in the ground, bend down and kiss the floor, and straighten up again.*

The student should notice how the Mishnah builds its description. It begins with the general scene -- dancing, torches, music -- and then narrows to the specific: a named sage juggling eight torches, another performing a physical feat of prostration. The movement from general to specific is a classic descriptive technique: it first sets the scene and then populates it with memorable individuals. The result is a description that is both panoramic and intimate -- the reader sees both the vast crowd and the individual sage.

The description also engages the senses systematically: sight (the burning torches in the darkness), sound (the musical instruments), and movement (the dancing, the juggling, the prostration). The sage who composed this passage was not merely recording facts; he was painting a scene, using words to create an experience of joy so vivid that even a reader who never saw the Temple celebration can feel something of its power.

**Example 3: Rabbi Yohanan's Beauty.**

The Talmud in Bava Metzia 84a contains a startling description of the physical beauty of Rabbi Yohanan:

> <span dir="rtl">שופריה דרבי יוחנן מעין שופריה דיעקב אבינו. שופריה דיעקב אבינו מעין שופריה דאדם הראשון</span>
>
> *The beauty of Rabbi Yohanan was a reflection of the beauty of Jacob our father. The beauty of Jacob our father was a reflection of the beauty of Adam, the first man.*[^198]

And more specifically:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי יוחנן הוה רגיל דהוה יתיב אשערי דטבילה. אמר: כי סלקן בנות ישראל מטבילת מצוה, לפגעו בי, כי היכי דלהוי להו זרעא שפירי כוותי</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan would customarily sit at the gates of the immersion pool. He said: When the daughters of Israel come up from their immersion of mitzvah, let them encounter me, so that they may have children as handsome as I.*

The student should observe that this description serves a purpose beyond mere portraiture. Rabbi Yohanan's extraordinary beauty is set in the context of a theological claim: that human beauty descends from Adam through Jacob to the present, diminishing with each generation. The description of a person becomes a description of the human condition -- the progressive loss of primordial perfection. Furthermore, Rabbi Yohanan's decision to sit at the immersion pool transforms his physical beauty from a personal attribute into a communal resource -- he wishes to use his beauty in the service of Israel's future. The description of the body becomes an occasion for reflection on the purpose of physical gifts.

The passage also introduces the figure of Resh Lakish -- a man of extraordinary physical power who encounters Rabbi Yohanan at the Jordan River. The contrast between the two men -- one beautiful, the other strong -- and their subsequent partnership as study partners creates a comparison through description: beauty and strength, each incomplete without the other, united in the service of Torah.

**Example 4: The Destruction of the Temple.**

The Talmud in Gittin 55b-58a contains a sustained narrative of the destruction of Jerusalem and the Temple that is one of the most vivid and harrowing passages in all of rabbinic literature. The description moves through multiple scenes:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי יוחנן: מאי דכתיב "אשרי אדם מפחד תמיד"?... אקמצא ובר קמצא חרוב ירושלים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan said: What is the meaning of the verse "Happy is the man who fears always"?... Because of Kamtza and Bar Kamtza, Jerusalem was destroyed.*[^199]

The narrative proceeds with extraordinary descriptive power through the escalating sequence of events: the humiliation at the banquet, the failed attempt at reconciliation, the deliberate provocation of Rome, the siege, the famine. Consider the description of the famine:

> <span dir="rtl">מרתא בת בייתוס, עשירה שבירושלים היתה. שיגרה לשלוחה: לך הבא לי סלת. עד דאזל, איזדבן. אתא אמר לה: סלת ליכא, חיוורתא איכא. אמרה ליה: זיל אייתי. עד דאזל, איזדבן. אתא ואמר לה: חיוורתא ליכא, גושקרא איכא... נפקה לשוקא ואיתלי פרתא בכרעא ומתה</span>
>
> *Martha bat Boethus was the wealthiest woman in Jerusalem. She sent her servant: Go and bring me fine flour. By the time he went, it was sold. He came and said: There is no fine flour, but there is white flour. She said: Go and bring it. By the time he went, it was sold. He came and said: There is no white flour, but there is coarse flour... She went out to the marketplace and dung stuck to her foot and she died.*[^200]

The student should observe the technique. The sage does not simply state: "There was a famine in Jerusalem." He shows us the famine through the experience of one woman -- a wealthy woman accustomed to the finest foods, who is reduced, step by step, to coarser and coarser provisions until even the coarsest is unavailable. The descending sequence -- fine flour, white flour, coarse flour, barley flour -- each one sold out before the servant can return -- creates a rhythm of accumulating desperation. And the final image -- the great lady who goes out into the street and dies from stepping in dung -- is so specific, so physical, so undignified that it conveys the horror of the siege more powerfully than any abstract statement about suffering could.

This is ekphrasis in the service of mourning. The sage who composed this passage was not merely recording history; he was creating a verbal monument to the destruction, a description so vivid that future generations would feel the weight of the catastrophe and understand what was lost.

**Example 5: The Sotah Ritual.**

The Mishnah in Sotah 1:4-6 describes the ritual ordeal of the suspected adulteress with a vividness that makes the reader a witness to the procedure:

> <span dir="rtl">היה מביאה לשער המזרח שעל פתח שער נקנור... ואוחז בבגדיה -- אם נקרעו נקרעו, ואם נפרמו נפרמו, עד שהוא מגלה את לבה, וסותר את שערה</span>
>
> *He would bring her to the Eastern Gate, at the entrance of the Nicanor Gate... and he would seize her garments -- if they tore, they tore; if they ripped, they ripped -- until he exposed her chest, and he would loosen her hair.*[^201]

And the description continues:

> <span dir="rtl">היתה מתכסה בלבנים -- מלבישה שחורים. היו עליה כלי זהב ושרשראות... מעבירין אותן הימנה, כדי לנוולה</span>
>
> *If she was dressed in white, they would clothe her in black. If she wore gold ornaments and chains... they would remove them from her, in order to make her repulsive.*

The student should observe that this description is not celebratory but deliberately discomfiting. The Mishnah describes the ritual with the same precision it applies to any other legal procedure, but the effect is to make the reader witness an act of public humiliation. The tearing of garments, the loosening of hair, the removal of ornaments, the replacement of white with black -- each detail makes the scene more vivid and more disturbing. The Mishnah does not editorialize; it simply describes. But the description itself is the argument: by making the reader see the ritual in all its harsh specificity, the Mishnah forces the reader to confront what the ritual actually entails.

This is an important lesson for the student of description: sometimes the most powerful form of argument is not argument at all but description. The sage who describes the sotah ritual without commentary leaves the listener to draw his own conclusions -- and the conclusions are all the more powerful for being drawn independently. Description can be a form of advocacy that is more effective than explicit argumentation, because it engages the listener's moral imagination rather than merely his logical faculty.

#### The Quality of Enargeia

The student should understand that the supreme quality of description is *enargeia* -- the Greek word for vividness, for the quality that makes the absent present.[^202] The sage who possesses enargeia does not merely inform his listener about a scene; he places his listener *within* the scene. The listener does not merely know what happened; he experiences it.

How is enargeia achieved? Through the accumulation of specific, sensory details. The sage does not say "the Temple was beautiful"; he describes the golden vine that hung over the entrance, the smoke of the incense rising in a straight column, the sound of the High Priest's footsteps in the silence of the Holy of Holies. He does not say "the destruction was terrible"; he shows us Martha bat Boethus dying in the street. He does not say "the celebration was joyful"; he shows us Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel juggling eight torches in the torchlight.

The rule is: show, do not tell. Describe what the eye sees, the ear hears, the hand touches, the nose smells. Let the listener's own imagination complete the picture. The sage who says "it was sad" has told the listener what to feel; the sage who describes the details and lets the sadness arise naturally from the description has made the listener feel it for himself -- and what one feels for oneself is felt more deeply than what one is told to feel.

> <span dir="rtl">אין לך לומר "היה נורא" אלא תתאר את המראה ויפחד השומע מעצמו</span>
>
> *Do not say "it was terrible" -- describe the scene and let the listener be terrified on his own.*

#### The Exercise

The student should practice description by following this method:

First, select a scene from the tradition that calls for vivid depiction. Good candidates include: the giving of Torah at Sinai (Exodus 19-20), the binding of Isaac (Genesis 22), the crossing of the Red Sea (Exodus 14-15), the dedication of Solomon's Temple (1 Kings 8), the fall of Jerusalem (2 Kings 25), or any scene from the Mishnah's descriptions of Temple ritual.

Second, identify the sensory details that the text provides or implies. What would one see, hear, feel, smell at this scene? What is the quality of light? What sounds fill the air? What is the temperature? What are the textures?

Third, compose a description that integrates these details into a coherent scene. Begin with the broadest view -- the setting, the time of day, the general atmosphere -- and then narrow to specific details: a particular person, a particular gesture, a particular sound. Move from the general to the particular, as the Mishnah does in its description of the Water Drawing celebration.

Fourth, test the description for enargeia. Read it aloud. Does the listener see the scene? Does he feel present? If not, add more specific detail. If the description feels abstract or propositional, it has not yet achieved its purpose.

The teacher should assign: "Describe the scene of the giving of Torah at Sinai so that your listener trembles as if he stood there. Use the details provided by Scripture -- the thunder, the lightning, the thick cloud, the sound of the shofar growing louder and louder, the mountain smoking, the people trembling -- and compose a description that makes these details vivid and immediate. Remember: your listener was not at Sinai, but through your description, he should feel as if he was." The student should present his description, and the class should evaluate it for vividness, faithfulness to the text, and emotional power.[^203]

---

### II.M. Thesis (She'elah Kelalit)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, we come now to the thirteenth exercise, and it is one of the most demanding: the *thesis* -- the *she'elah kelalit*, the general question. What is a thesis? It is the arguing of a general, abstract proposition -- not a particular case involving particular persons and circumstances, but a universal question that applies to all persons and all circumstances.

The thesis asks not "Should Reuven divorce his wife?" but "Is it good for a man to marry?" The thesis asks not "Should Shimon go to the Land of Israel?" but "Is it better to live in the Land of Israel or in the Diaspora?" The thesis asks not "Was Rabbi Tarfon right in this particular case?" but "Is study greater than action?" The generality is the essence: the thesis removes all the particular circumstances that make a specific case unique and asks the question in its most universal form.

> <span dir="rtl">השאלה הכללית -- אינה שואלת על ראובן ושמעון, אלא על האדם מצד היותו אדם</span>
>
> *The general question -- it does not ask about Reuven and Shimon, but about the person as such.*

Why is this exercise so important? Because the sage who can argue only particular cases is limited to the cases that come before him. But the sage who can argue general propositions can illuminate any case that arises, because he has thought through the underlying principles. The thesis exercise trains the mind to ascend from the particular to the universal -- to see the general rule behind the specific instance.[^204]

The Greek teachers of rhetoric distinguished between the thesis (a general question) and the hypothesis (a particular question), and they regarded the thesis as the more philosophically demanding of the two.[^205] The rabbis made a similar distinction, though not always with explicit terminology. When the sages debated whether study or action is greater, they were arguing a thesis. When they debated whether Reuven's specific situation called for study or action, they were arguing a hypothesis. The thesis prepares the student for the hypothesis, because the student who has already argued the general proposition can more easily apply it to a specific case.

#### Types of Thesis

The student should know the two principal types of thesis:

**The Practical Thesis.** This concerns what one should *do*: Is it better to study or to act? Should one pray at fixed times or spontaneously? Should a sage teach Torah for payment? Should one participate in the government of the nations? These are practical theses -- they concern conduct, policy, and the ordering of human life. The practical thesis is closely related to deliberative discourse, for it asks the same kind of question that an assembly would ask: what course of action is best?

**The Theoretical Thesis.** This concerns what is *true*: Is the world eternal or created? Is suffering a punishment or a purification? Is divine providence general or particular? Does the soul survive death? These are theoretical theses -- they concern the nature of reality, of God, and of the human condition. The theoretical thesis is more philosophically demanding than the practical thesis, because it asks questions that may not admit of a definitive practical answer. Yet the sages did not shy away from theoretical theses; they debated them with vigor and often with surprising openness to multiple positions.

The student should also understand that many theses in the bet midrash combine the practical and the theoretical. The question "Is suffering a good thing?" is both theoretical (what is the nature of suffering?) and practical (how should one respond to suffering?). The question "Is study greater than action?" is both theoretical (what is the proper hierarchy of values?) and practical (how should a sage allocate his time?). The most powerful theses are those that operate on both levels simultaneously.

#### Examples

**Example 1: "Is Study Greater or Is Action Greater?"**

We have already encountered this debate in our discussion of comparison (section II.J), but here we consider it as a thesis -- a general question argued before an assembly of sages. The Talmud records:

> <span dir="rtl">וכבר היה רבי טרפון וזקנים מסובין בעלייה בבית נתזה בלוד. נשאלה שאלה זו בפניהם: תלמוד גדול או מעשה גדול? נענה רבי טרפון ואמר: מעשה גדול. נענה רבי עקיבא ואמר: תלמוד גדול. נענו כולם ואמרו: תלמוד גדול, שהתלמוד מביא לידי מעשה</span>
>
> *It once happened that Rabbi Tarfon and the elders were reclining in the upper chamber of the house of Nit'zah in Lydda. This question was raised before them: Is study greater or is action greater? Rabbi Tarfon answered and said: Action is greater. Rabbi Akiva answered and said: Study is greater. They all answered and said: Study is greater, for study leads to action.*[^206]

The student should analyze this as a thesis exercise. The question is general -- it asks about study and action *as such*, not about a specific student's situation. The setting is formal -- a symposium of elders in a specific location, a setting that lends gravity and authority to the deliberation. The arguments are representative: Rabbi Tarfon argues for action, because action produces tangible results in the world. Rabbi Akiva argues for study, because study generates the knowledge that makes right action possible. The resolution transcends both positions: study is greater *because it leads to action* -- which means that study without action is incomplete, and action without study is misdirected.

The student should notice the rhetorical structure of the resolution. It does not say "Rabbi Akiva wins." It says "they all answered" -- the resolution is collective, a consensus that emerges from the deliberation itself. And the resolution is formulated as a *reason*, not merely a verdict: study is greater *because* it leads to action. The "because" transforms the verdict into a teaching -- the listener learns not only which position prevailed but *why* it prevailed. This is characteristic of rabbinic thesis-argumentation: the resolution must be reasoned, not arbitrary.

**Example 2: "Should a Sage Teach Torah for Payment?"**

This thesis is debated across multiple sources, each one contributing a different argument:

The Mishnah in Avot 1:13 records Hillel's warning:

> <span dir="rtl">ודאשתמש בתגא -- חלף</span>
>
> *And one who makes use of the crown [of Torah for personal gain] -- shall perish.*[^207]

The Mishnah in Avot 4:5 records Rabbi Tzadok's teaching:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תעשם עטרה להתגדל בהן, ולא קרדום לחפור בהן</span>
>
> *Do not make them [words of Torah] a crown with which to aggrandize yourself, nor a spade with which to dig.*[^208]

And the Talmud in Nedarim 37a provides the counterargument -- that some form of compensation may be necessary to sustain those who dedicate their lives to teaching:

> <span dir="rtl">כדרך שלמדני חנם כך אני מלמד חנם... אמר רב: שכר שימור, שכר פיסוק טעמים</span>
>
> *"Just as I learned freely, so I teach freely"... Rav said: [What is permitted is] payment for supervision, payment for the teaching of cantillation marks.*[^209]

The student should observe how the thesis develops across these sources. The initial position is clear: Torah should be taught without payment, because Torah is not a commodity to be bought and sold. But the counterargument is equally compelling: if teachers cannot earn a livelihood, they will be forced to abandon teaching, and Torah will cease to be transmitted. The resolution -- that payment is permitted for ancillary services (supervision, vocalization) but not for the Torah itself -- is a nuanced middle position that honors both the ideal of gratuitous teaching and the practical necessity of supporting teachers.

This is a model of thesis-argumentation that the student should internalize. The thesis exercise does not seek a simple answer to a complex question. It seeks a nuanced resolution that accounts for the strongest arguments on both sides. The student who can only argue one side of a thesis has not yet mastered the exercise. The student who can argue both sides and then find a resolution that preserves the insights of each -- that student has achieved rhetorical maturity.

**Example 3: "Is Suffering a Good Thing?"**

The Talmud in Berakhot 5a-b contains one of the most searching theological discussions in all of rabbinic literature, structured as a thesis on the nature of suffering:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבא, אמר רב סחורה, אמר רב הונא: כל שהקדוש ברוך הוא חפץ בו מדכאו ביסורין, שנאמר: "וה' חפץ דכאו החלי." יכול אפילו לא קבלם מאהבה? תלמוד לומר: "אם תשים אשם נפשו" -- מה אשם לדעת, אף יסורין לדעת</span>
>
> *Rava said, Rav Sehora said, Rav Huna said: Whoever the Holy One, blessed be He, desires, He crushes with suffering, as it is said: "The Lord desired to crush him with sickness" (Isaiah 53:10). One might think: even if he does not accept them with love? The verse teaches: "If his soul offers itself as a guilt offering" -- just as a guilt offering requires conscious intent, so suffering requires conscious acceptance.*[^210]

The passage continues with the famous teaching about "sufferings of love" -- *yissurin shel ahavah* -- the idea that God sends suffering not as punishment but as an expression of love, to purify and elevate the righteous:

> <span dir="rtl">אם רואה אדם שיסורין באין עליו, יפשפש במעשיו. פשפש ולא מצא, יתלה בביטול תורה. ואם תלה ולא מצא, בידוע שיסורין של אהבה הם</span>
>
> *If a person sees that sufferings come upon him, let him examine his deeds. If he examines and finds nothing [wrong], let him attribute it to neglect of Torah study. And if he attributes it [to that] and finds nothing, it is certain that these are sufferings of love.*

But the thesis is not resolved so easily. The passage continues with a remarkable counterpoint -- sages who refuse to accept the theology of redemptive suffering:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי יוחנן חלש, על לגביה רבי חנינא. אמר ליה: חביבין עליך יסורין? אמר ליה: לא הן ולא שכרן. אמר ליה: הב לי ידך. יהב ליה ידיה ואוקמיה</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan fell ill. Rabbi Hanina came to visit him. He said to him: Are your sufferings dear to you? He said to him: Neither they nor their reward. He said to him: Give me your hand. He gave him his hand and he raised him up.*[^211]

The student should observe the extraordinary honesty of this passage. The thesis -- "Is suffering a good thing?" -- is argued on multiple levels. On the theological level, suffering can be understood as divine love, as purification, as an opportunity for merit. On the experiential level, Rabbi Yohanan -- himself a sage of the highest rank, who had lost ten sons to illness -- refuses the theology: "Neither they nor their reward." He wants neither the suffering nor whatever spiritual benefit it might confer. And his friend does not rebuke him for this refusal; he simply takes his hand and heals him.

The thesis is not fully resolved. The passage presents both the theology of redemptive suffering and its rejection by one who has actually suffered -- and it lets both positions stand. This is one of the most important lessons the thesis exercise can teach: some questions do not admit of definitive resolution. The honest sage argues both sides as forcefully as he can and then acknowledges that the question remains open. The thesis exercise trains the student not only to argue but to recognize the limits of argument -- to know when a question exceeds the capacity of human reason to resolve.

**Example 4: "Should One Pray at Fixed Times or Spontaneously?"**

The Mishnah in Berakhot 4:4 records a debate that is, in essence, a thesis on the nature of prayer:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי אליעזר אומר: העושה תפלתו קבע -- אין תפלתו תחנונים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Eliezer says: One who makes his prayer fixed -- his prayer is not supplication.*[^212]

And the Talmud in Berakhot 29b explores the question further:

> <span dir="rtl">מאי קבע? רבי יעקב בר אידי אמר רב: כל שתפלתו דומה עליו כמשאוי. ורבנן אמרי: כל שאינו אומרה בלשון תחנונים. רבה ורב יוסף דאמרי תרוייהו: כל שאינו יכול לחדש בה דבר</span>
>
> *What is "fixed"? Rabbi Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rav: Anyone whose prayer seems to him like a burden. And the rabbis say: Anyone who does not pray in the language of supplication. Rabbah and Rav Yosef both say: Anyone who cannot innovate something in his prayer.*[^213]

The thesis here concerns the fundamental nature of prayer. Is prayer primarily a legal obligation -- a mitzvah to be performed at set times, with set words, in a set manner? Or is prayer primarily an expression of the heart -- a spontaneous outpouring that cannot be reduced to formula? The tension between these two conceptions runs through the entire tractate Berakhot, and the sages offer multiple definitions of what makes prayer "fixed" (and therefore deficient): it is fixed if it feels like a burden, if it lacks the language of supplication, if the one who prays cannot add anything new to it.

The student should observe that the thesis is not resolved by choosing one position over the other. Rabbinic prayer is, in practice, both fixed and spontaneous: the *matbe'a shel tefillah* (the set form of prayer) provides the structure, while the *kavvanah* (intentionality) of the one who prays provides the spontaneous, personal element. The thesis exercise has led to a resolution that preserves both positions by integrating them into a single practice: pray with fixed words, but make them new each time through the depth of your intention.

This is a resolution of the type that the bet midrash favors: not the victory of one side over the other but the integration of both into a richer, more complex whole. The student who practices the thesis exercise should always seek this kind of resolution -- a synthesis that honors both sides of the debate by showing how they can coexist.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice the thesis exercise by following this method:

First, formulate the thesis as a clear, general question. The question should be debatable -- it should admit of genuine arguments on both sides. A thesis that can be resolved by a simple appeal to a verse or a ruling is too easy; it is a question of fact, not a question of principle. The thesis should concern a genuine tension between two goods, two values, two approaches that are each defensible in their own right.

Second, argue one side of the thesis with full force. Marshal every argument you can find: scriptural proofs, logical reasoning, precedents, analogies, consequences. Present the strongest possible case for this position. Do not hold back or weaken your arguments in anticipation of the other side. The student must learn to argue each side as if he believed it completely.

Third, argue the other side with equal force. This is the harder step, because the student's natural inclination is to favor one side. But the thesis exercise requires that both sides be argued with equal vigor and equal honesty. The student who presents a weak version of the opposing position has cheated -- he has not truly engaged with the difficulty of the question.

Fourth, seek a resolution. The resolution may take one of several forms: it may declare one side the winner with a reasoned explanation; it may find a synthesis that integrates both positions; or it may honestly acknowledge that the question remains open. All three forms of resolution are legitimate. What is not legitimate is to ignore the difficulty of the question or to pretend that one side has no merit.

The teacher should assign: "Argue both sides of this thesis: 'Is it better to live in the Land of Israel or in the Diaspora among students of Torah?' Consider the arguments for the Land of Israel: the mitzvah of settlement, the holiness of the land, the promise to the patriarchs. Consider the arguments for the Diaspora: the presence of great academies, the ability to spread Torah among the nations, the dangers of a land beset by enemies. Seek a resolution that honors both positions." The student should present his arguments to the class, and the class should evaluate the strength of each side and the wisdom of the resolution.[^214]

---

### II.N. Introduction of a Law (Takkanah / Gezerah)

#### Definition and Purpose

My son, we come now to the fourteenth and final preliminary exercise -- the most advanced, the most demanding, and the most directly relevant to the actual work of the bet midrash: the *introduction of a law*. In this exercise, the student argues for or against a proposed enactment -- a *takkanah* (positive enactment) or a *gezerah* (restrictive decree). Here you stand before the assembly and argue that a decree should or should not be enacted, that an existing law should or should not be modified, that a practice should or should not be established for all of Israel.

The Greek teachers of rhetoric called this exercise *eisphora nomou* -- the proposal of a law -- and they placed it last among the preliminary exercises, at the very summit of the sequence, because it requires all the skills practiced in the preceding thirteen exercises combined.[^215] And rightly so. Consider what the student must do when he argues for or against a proposed enactment:

He must *narrate* the circumstances that have given rise to the need for the enactment -- this requires the skills of narrative (exercise II.B). He must *praise* the values that the enactment will protect and *blame* the harms it will prevent -- this requires the skills of encomium and invective (exercises II.F and II.G). He must *compare* the proposed enactment with existing laws to show its consistency or its novelty -- this requires the skills of comparison (exercise II.J). He must *describe* the situation that will result if the enactment is adopted, and the situation that will result if it is not -- this requires the skills of description (exercise II.L). He must argue the *thesis* that the underlying principle of the enactment is sound -- this requires the skills of the thesis exercise (exercise II.M). He must compose *speeches in character* for those who will be affected by the enactment -- this requires the skills of ethopoeia (exercise II.K). He must cite *maxims* and *chreiai* from the sages to support his position -- this requires the skills of the chreia exercise (exercise II.C). In short: the introduction of a law is the capstone exercise because it integrates all the other exercises into a single, complex act of persuasion.

> <span dir="rtl">התקנה -- היא ראש כל התרגילים, שהיא צריכה את כולם: סיפור ושבח וגנאי, היקש וציור, שאלה ודיבור בשם. הכל בה</span>
>
> *The enactment -- it is the head of all the exercises, for it requires them all: narrative and praise and blame, comparison and description, thesis and speech-in-character. Everything is in it.*

The student should also understand: this exercise is not merely academic. The actual work of the bet midrash includes the enactment of *takkanot* and *gezerot* -- positive enactments and preventive decrees that shape the life of the community. The sage who masters this exercise is not merely practicing a rhetorical skill; he is preparing himself for the most consequential form of speech in the Jewish legal tradition: the speech that creates or modifies law.

#### Methods of Argument

The student who argues for or against a proposed enactment should deploy the following methods, which the sages employed in their own legislative deliberations:[^216]

**Argument from Justice (Tzedek).** The enactment should be *just* -- it should protect the rights of the weak, prevent the exploitation of the vulnerable, and ensure that the scales of justice are not tipped unfairly. The sage argues: "This enactment is necessary because without it, a class of persons will be deprived of their rights." Or, conversely: "This enactment should not be adopted because it will create an injustice that is worse than the harm it seeks to prevent."

**Argument from Legality (Din).** The enactment should be *consistent* with existing law -- it should not contradict established halakhah without compelling reason. The sage argues: "This enactment is supported by the following existing principles..." Or: "This enactment contradicts the following established ruling and should therefore be rejected unless the proponents can show that circumstances have changed sufficiently to warrant a departure from precedent."

**Argument from Possibility (Efshar).** The enactment should be *practicable* -- it should be something that the community can actually observe. The sage argues: "This enactment can be implemented because the community already observes a similar practice." Or: "This enactment should not be adopted because the community cannot bear it -- one does not impose a decree upon the public unless the majority can endure it."

This last argument has explicit rabbinic warrant:

> <span dir="rtl">אין גוזרין גזירה על הציבור אלא אם כן רוב הציבור יכולין לעמוד בה</span>
>
> *One does not impose a decree upon the public unless the majority of the public can endure it.*[^217]

**Argument from Consistency (Hashva'ah).** The enactment should be *consistent* with the general principles and spirit of the Torah, even if no specific verse directly addresses the matter. The sage argues: "This enactment is consistent with the Torah's concern for X" -- where X is a general principle such as human dignity, the sanctity of life, the protection of the poor, or the integrity of the family.

**Argument from Expedience (To'elet).** The enactment should produce *benefit* -- it should serve a recognizable good and prevent a recognizable harm. The sage argues: "This enactment will produce the following practical benefits..." Or: "The harms produced by this enactment outweigh its benefits."

**Argument from Propriety (Kavod / Derekh Eretz).** The enactment should be *proper* -- it should conform to standards of decency, dignity, and the honor due to God, to Torah, and to human beings. The sage argues: "This enactment is required by the principle of human dignity" -- *mipnei kavod ha-beriyot* -- which is so powerful that it can override even certain negative commandments.

#### Examples

**Example 1: Hillel's Prozbul.**

The most famous example of legislative argumentation in all of rabbinic literature is Hillel's institution of the *prozbul* -- a legal instrument that effectively circumvented the biblical cancellation of debts in the sabbatical year. The Mishnah records:
