> <span dir="rtl">פרוזבול אינו משמט. זה אחד מן הדברים שהתקין הלל הזקן. כשראה שנמנעו העם מלהלוות זה את זה, ועוברין על מה שכתוב בתורה: "השמר לך פן יהיה דבר עם לבבך בליעל" -- התקין הלל פרוזבול</span>
>
> *A prozbul is not cancelled [by the sabbatical year]. This is one of the things that Hillel the Elder enacted. When he saw that the people were refraining from lending to one another, and were transgressing what is written in the Torah: "Beware lest there be a base thought in your heart" [Deuteronomy 15:9] -- Hillel enacted the prozbul.*[^218]

The student should analyze the arguments implicit in this enactment. Hillel faced a classic legislative dilemma: a biblical law (the cancellation of debts) was producing a result opposite to its intended purpose. The Torah's purpose in cancelling debts was to protect the poor from perpetual indebtedness. But the *effect* of the law was that no one would lend to the poor as the sabbatical year approached, because lenders knew their loans would be cancelled. The poor were thus harmed by the very law designed to help them.

Hillel's argument runs as follows: The *purpose* of the Torah's law is to protect the poor. The *effect* of the law, under current social conditions, is to harm the poor. Therefore, an enactment that modifies the law's operation -- while preserving its underlying purpose -- is not a violation of Torah but a fulfillment of it. The prozbul does not abolish the sabbatical cancellation; it creates a legal mechanism (transferring the debt to the court) that allows lending to continue, thereby serving the Torah's original purpose of protecting the vulnerable.

This is argument from justice and from expedience simultaneously: the enactment is just because it serves the poor, and it is expedient because it solves a practical problem. But Hillel also needed an argument from legality -- how could a rabbinic enactment modify a biblical law? The Talmud records the legal basis: Hillel relied on the principle that the sabbatical cancellation of debts is, in the post-Temple era, of rabbinic rather than biblical force, and the rabbis have the authority to modify their own enactments when circumstances require it.[^219]

The student should observe the rhetorical brilliance of this legislative argument. Hillel does not argue *against* the Torah; he argues *for* the Torah's purpose, showing that the enactment serves the Torah's deepest intention even as it modifies the Torah's letter. This is the highest form of legislative advocacy: to show that the proposed change is not a departure from tradition but a deeper fidelity to it.

**Example 2: Rabban Gamaliel's Enactments Regarding Divorce.**

The Mishnah in Gittin 4:2 records several enactments made by Rabban Gamaliel the Elder to protect women in the divorce process:

> <span dir="rtl">בראשונה היה עושה בית דין במקום אחר ומבטלו. התקין רבן גמליאל הזקן שלא יהו עושין כן, מפני תיקון העולם</span>
>
> *Originally, [a husband] could convene a court in another place and cancel [the get, the bill of divorce, after it had already been dispatched]. Rabban Gamaliel the Elder enacted that they should not do so, for the betterment of the world.*[^220]

The problem: a husband could send a bill of divorce to his wife through a messenger, and then -- before the wife received it -- convene a court in another location and cancel it. The wife, not knowing of the cancellation, would believe herself divorced and might remarry. Her second marriage would then be invalid, and any children from it would be of impaired status. The husband's power to cancel the get from a distance thus placed the wife in an impossible situation: she could never be certain that the divorce was final.

Rabban Gamaliel's enactment removed this power -- the husband could no longer cancel a get after dispatching it. The argument is explicitly stated: *mipnei tikkun ha-olam* -- "for the betterment of the world." This is argument from justice (protecting women from deception and uncertainty), from expedience (preventing the birth of children of impaired status), and from propriety (it is undignified to leave a woman in a state of legal limbo).

The phrase *tikkun ha-olam* -- "betterment of the world" or "repair of the world" -- appears repeatedly in the fourth chapter of Gittin as the justification for various enactments. The student should note that this phrase is itself a rhetorical formula: it signals that the enactment is motivated by a comprehensive concern for social well-being, not by narrow technical considerations. When the sage invokes *tikkun ha-olam*, he is making an argument from the broadest possible principle: the duty to ensure that the world functions justly and decently.

**Example 3: The Canonicity of Ecclesiastes and Song of Songs.**

The Mishnah in Yadayim 3:5 records a debate that is, in essence, a legislative argument about whether two books should be included in the biblical canon -- whether they "defile the hands" (a technical term indicating sacred status):

> <span dir="rtl">כל כתבי הקודש מטמאין את הידים. שיר השירים וקהלת מטמאין את הידים. רבי יהודה אומר: שיר השירים מטמא את הידים וקהלת מחלוקת. רבי יוסי אומר: קהלת אינו מטמא את הידים ושיר השירים מחלוקת</span>
>
> *All the Holy Scriptures defile the hands. Song of Songs and Ecclesiastes defile the hands. Rabbi Yehudah says: Song of Songs defiles the hands, and Ecclesiastes is disputed. Rabbi Yose says: Ecclesiastes does not defile the hands, and Song of Songs is disputed.*[^221]

And then the decisive intervention:

> <span dir="rtl">אמר רבי עקיבא: חס ושלום! לא נחלק אדם מישראל על שיר השירים שלא תטמא את הידים, שאין כל העולם כולו כדאי כיום שניתן בו שיר השירים לישראל. שכל הכתובים קודש, ושיר השירים קודש קדשים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Akiva said: God forbid! No person in Israel ever disputed that Song of Songs defiles the hands, for the entire world is not worth the day on which Song of Songs was given to Israel. For all the writings are holy, and Song of Songs is the holy of holies.*[^222]

The student should analyze the rhetorical strategies deployed in this debate. The question is legislative: should these books be accorded canonical status? The arguments against inclusion are implicit: Ecclesiastes contains skeptical statements that seem to contradict other books of the Bible ("Vanity of vanities, all is vanity"); Song of Songs contains erotic poetry that seems out of place in sacred scripture. The arguments for inclusion deploy multiple methods: Rabbi Akiva argues from propriety (it would be unseemly -- "God forbid!" -- to exclude so sacred a text), from valuation (the day Song of Songs was given is worth more than the entire world), and from theological classification (it is not merely holy but "the holy of holies").

Rabbi Akiva's argument is notable for its rhetorical force. He does not merely state his position; he expresses horror at the alternative. The phrase *chas ve-shalom* -- "God forbid!" -- frames the opposing position as not merely wrong but unthinkable. And the classification of Song of Songs as "the holy of holies" -- the term reserved for the innermost sanctum of the Temple -- elevates the book to the highest possible status. This is legislative advocacy at its most passionate: the speaker deploys not only logical arguments but emotional force to move the assembly toward his position.

**Example 4: The Eighteen Decrees.**

The Mishnah in Shabbat 1:4 records a momentous day of legislation:

> <span dir="rtl">אלו מן ההלכות שאמרו בעליית חנניה בן חזקיה בן גרון, שעלו לבקרו. נמנו ורבו בית שמאי על בית הלל, ושמונה עשרה דבר גזרו בו ביום</span>
>
> *These are among the halakhot that were stated in the upper chamber of Hananiah ben Hizkiyyah ben Garon, when they went up to visit him. They counted [votes] and Beit Shammai outnumbered Beit Hillel, and eighteen things they decreed on that day.*[^223]

The Talmud records that this day was regarded as catastrophic by some sages -- "as difficult as the day on which the Golden Calf was made" -- because the majority of Beit Shammai overrode the minority of Beit Hillel through sheer numbers, and some said through intimidation. This tradition serves as a cautionary tale about legislative process: even when the outcome is formally legitimate (a majority vote), the process by which the majority was achieved matters. A law enacted through coercion, even if its content is sound, lacks the full legitimacy of a law enacted through genuine deliberation.

The student should observe that the rabbinic tradition itself contains a vigorous debate about the legitimacy of these eighteen decrees -- a debate *about* legislation that is itself a form of legislative argument. Those who defend the decrees argue from authority (they were enacted by a legitimate assembly) and from precedent (they have been observed for generations). Those who criticize the process argue from propriety (legislation should not be enacted through intimidation) and from justice (the minority's rights must be respected even when the majority prevails).

**Example 5: The Takkanot of Ezra.**

The Talmud in Bava Kamma 82a preserves a tradition about ten enactments attributed to Ezra the Scribe -- foundational legislation for the post-exilic community:

> <span dir="rtl">עשרה תקנות תיקן עזרא: שקורין בתורה בשבת במנחה, ושני וחמישי... ושיהו רוכלין מחזירין בעיירות... ושיהו מכבסין בחמישי בשבת... ושתהא אשה חופפת וטובלת</span>
>
> *Ten enactments were enacted by Ezra: that they read from the Torah on Shabbat afternoon, and on Monday and Thursday... and that peddlers should travel among the towns... and that laundry should be done on Thursday... and that a woman should comb her hair and immerse.*[^224]

The student should observe the range of these enactments: they concern public worship (Torah reading), commerce (access to peddlers for small communities), domestic life (laundry schedules), and personal purity (immersion practices). Each enactment implies an argument: the Torah should be read three times a week so that no three days pass without Torah; peddlers should travel among the towns so that cosmetics and other necessities are available even in small communities (this is explained as being for the sake of women's dignity -- *meshum chen*); laundry should be done on Thursday so that Shabbat garments are clean; women should comb and immerse so that they are not repelled by the immersion process and thereby neglect the mitzvah.

Each of these arguments deploys one or more of the methods we have discussed: justice (ensuring access to goods), expedience (practical scheduling), propriety (dignity of Shabbat observance and women's comfort), and consistency (regular Torah reading as an expression of the principle that Torah must be constantly studied). The student who studies Ezra's enactments will see a complete model of legislative argumentation: each enactment is justified, each justification appeals to a recognizable principle, and the ten enactments together constitute a comprehensive program for communal life.

#### The Rabbi's Warning

The student should know: this exercise requires all the previous skills combined. To argue for or against an enactment, you must be able to narrate the circumstances that make the enactment necessary, to praise the values it protects, to blame the harms it prevents, to compare it with existing laws, to describe the world as it will be if the enactment is adopted, to argue the thesis that its underlying principle is sound, and to speak in the voice of those whom it will affect. The introduction of a law is not a single skill but a *synthesis* of all skills -- the capstone that crowns the entire sequence of preliminary exercises.

Moreover, this exercise carries the greatest responsibility of all, for its outcome is not merely academic. A mashal that fails is merely a bad story. A description that fails is merely an unclear picture. But a law that fails -- a takkanah that is unjust, a gezerah that cannot be observed, an enactment that does more harm than good -- affects the lives of real people. The sage who argues for a law that damages the community bears a burden that no other exercise imposes.

> <span dir="rtl">כל מי שבידו למחות ואינו מוחה... כל העולם כולו נענש בעוונו</span>
>
> *Whoever has the power to protest and does not protest... the entire world is punished for his sin.*

And equally: whoever has the power to enact legislation and enacts it poorly -- the entire community suffers the consequences of his failure. This is why the introduction of a law is the final exercise: the student must first master all the other exercises before he is ready for this one, because this one stakes everything on the quality of the argument.

#### The Exercise

The student should practice the introduction of a law by following this method:

First, identify a situation that calls for legislative action. The situation should be realistic -- it should involve a genuine tension between competing values or a genuine problem that existing law does not adequately address.

Second, formulate the proposed enactment clearly and precisely. The enactment should be stated in a form that could actually be adopted: "That from this day forward, X shall be the practice" or "That the existing practice of Y shall be modified as follows."

Third, argue *for* the enactment, deploying as many of the six methods as are relevant: justice, legality, possibility, consistency, expedience, propriety. Each argument should be supported by scriptural or rabbinic sources where possible.

Fourth, argue *against* the enactment with equal force, showing what objections could be raised and how the enactment might fail or produce unintended consequences.

Fifth, reach a conclusion -- either for or against the enactment, or proposing a modified version that addresses the strongest objections.

The teacher should assign: "Compose arguments for and against this proposed enactment: 'That women should be permitted to testify in the bet din.' Consider the arguments for: the principle of human dignity, the evolving circumstances of society, the precedent of Deborah who judged Israel. Consider the arguments against: the existing halakhic tradition, the principle of not innovating where the tradition is settled, the concern that changing one element of testimony law will destabilize the entire system. Present both sides with full force and reach a reasoned conclusion." The student should present his arguments to the class, and the class should evaluate them for thoroughness, fairness, and persuasive power.[^225]

---

#### Concluding Words on the Fourteen Preliminary Exercises

My son, you have now traversed all fourteen preliminary exercises of the bet midrash. You have learned to compose fables and narratives, to elaborate and condense the teachings of the sages, to craft arguments for and against propositions, to praise the worthy and censure the wicked, to compare subjects for mutual illumination, to give voice to those whom Scripture left silent, to describe scenes so vividly that the listener feels present, to argue general theses with nuance and honesty, and to propose and defend legislative enactments before the assembly.

These fourteen exercises are not ends in themselves. They are *preliminary* -- they prepare you for the real work of the sage: the derashah before the congregation, the advocacy before the bet din, the instruction of students, the deliberation of law, and the composition of teachings that will endure for generations. Each exercise has trained a specific faculty of your mind and a specific capacity of your speech. Together, they have equipped you with the full range of skills that the sage requires.

But remember always: these skills are instruments, not ornaments. They exist to serve Torah, to serve justice, and to serve the community of Israel. The sage who uses his rhetorical mastery to aggrandize himself, to deceive his listeners, or to pervert justice has profaned the art. The sage who uses his mastery to illuminate Torah, to defend the innocent, to comfort the grieving, and to guide the community toward righteousness has fulfilled the deepest purpose of all that we have taught here.

> <span dir="rtl">הלל אומר: אל תאמר דבר שאי אפשר לשמוע, שסופו להשמע. ואל תאמר: לכשאפנה אשנה, שמא לא תפנה</span>
>
> *Hillel says: Do not say something that cannot be heard, for in the end it will be heard. And do not say: When I have leisure I will study, for perhaps you will never have leisure.* (Avot 2:4)

Now let us turn from the preliminary exercises to the practice of full discourse -- the construction of complete arguments, the composition of extended speeches, and the art of the sage who stands before the assembly and speaks in the name of Torah.[^226]

---

[^167]: On synkrisis (comparison) as a progymnasmata exercise, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 10 (in Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 62-65), who defines comparison as "a speech placing the better or the worse beside each other"; Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 106-112), who provides a model comparison of Achilles and Hector; and Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Kennedy, 82-83). Nicolaus the Sophist (*Progymnasmata* 10) provides the most extended theoretical discussion. On synkrisis as "a double encomium" or "a double invective," see Theon's definition as discussed in Hock and O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric*, 90-95.

[^168]: Plutarch's *Parallel Lives* (composed ca. 96-120 CE) represents the literary culmination of the synkrisis tradition. Each pair of biographies (one Greek, one Roman) concludes with a formal comparison (*synkrisis*) that evaluates the relative merits of the two subjects. On the relationship between Plutarch's method and the progymnasmata exercise, see Duff, *Plutarch's Lives: Exploring Virtue and Vice* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1999), 243-286. The chronological overlap with the tannaitic period is noteworthy: Plutarch was a near-contemporary of the sages discussed in this section.

[^169]: BT Eruvin 13b. This passage is one of the most frequently cited in all of rabbinic literature and has been the subject of extensive scholarly discussion. On its implications for rabbinic epistemology and the legitimacy of multiple legal opinions, see Halbertal, *People of the Book*, 54-72; and Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 198-215. The concept of "both these and these are the words of the living God" has no precise parallel in Greco-Roman rhetorical theory, where the synkrisis typically culminates in a judgment of superiority rather than a validation of both positions.

[^170]: BT Eruvin 13b (continuation). The justification for the halakhah following Beit Hillel -- their humility and their willingness to study and present the opposing view first -- introduces an ethical criterion into legal authority that is distinctive to the rabbinic tradition. Compare Quintilian's emphasis on the moral character of the orator (*Inst. Or.* 12.1.1), though Quintilian does not go so far as to make humility a criterion for the authority of legal opinions.

[^171]: BT Shabbat 31a. The comparison of Hillel and Shammai in this narrative cluster functions as what Moeser (*The Anecdote in Mark*, 115-120) calls a "contrastive chreia pair" -- two chreiai set in deliberate contrast to illuminate the character of both sages.

[^172]: BT Kiddushin 40b. This famous debate at Lydda is discussed in detail in Urbach, *The Sages: Their Concepts and Beliefs* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1979), 603-605; and in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 220-224, who notes its structural similarity to Greco-Roman philosophical debates on the relative primacy of *theoria* (contemplation) and *praxis* (action), as in Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics* 10.7-8.

[^173]: The characteristic Greco-Roman synkrisis, as described in the progymnasmata handbooks, typically culminates in a judgment of superiority: one subject is declared better than the other. See Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 106-112), whose model comparison of Achilles and Hector concludes with a clear verdict in favor of Achilles. The rabbinic tendency to preserve both sides of a comparison -- to declare "both are the words of the living God" -- represents a distinctive departure from this pattern.

[^174]: BT Yoma 9b. This comparison of the two Temple periods is analyzed in Urbach, *The Sages*, 545-548; and in Alon, *The Jews in Their Land in the Talmudic Age* (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1989), vol. 1, 58-75. The theological claim that *sinat hinnam* (groundless hatred) is equivalent to the three cardinal sins is one of the most consequential comparative propositions in rabbinic literature.

[^175]: Mishnah Avot 5:12. On the fourfold classification as a systematic form of comparison, see Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 78-84, who compares it to the Aristotelian practice of establishing categories through the combination of binary variables.

[^176]: Mishnah Avot 5:13. The analysis of charity-giving through the lens of self-regarding vs. other-regarding motivation parallels discussions of the proper motives for beneficence in Seneca, *De Beneficiis*, though there is no evidence of direct borrowing.

[^177]: On the numerical list as a distinctively rabbinic form of systematic comparison, see Alexander, "The Rabbinic Lists of Forbidden Targumim," *Journal of Jewish Studies* 27 (1976): 177-191; and the broader discussion of enumerative forms in Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 71-95.

[^178]: Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael, Pisha 1; cf. Numbers Rabbah 2:1. The principle that the alternation of names indicates equality is a hermeneutical rule that generates comparative propositions from textual phenomena.

[^179]: The warning from Ecclesiastes 7:10 against nostalgic comparisons is itself a meta-comparative statement -- a caution about the misuse of the comparison exercise. The sage compares in order to learn, not in order to lament.

[^180]: On ethopoeia (speech-in-character) as a progymnasmata exercise, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 8 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 47-49); Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 9 (Kennedy, 84-86); and Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 11 (Kennedy, 115-117). Quintilian discusses the exercise at *Inst. Or.* 3.8.49-54, emphasizing the importance of adapting one's speech to the character, circumstances, and emotional state of the person whose voice one assumes. Theon distinguishes *eidolopoeia* (composing speeches for known historical persons) from *prosopopoeia* (composing speeches for types of persons or for non-human entities). On the pervasiveness of ethopoeia in rabbinic midrash, see Kugel, "Two Introductions to Midrash," in *Midrash and Literature*, ed. Hartman and Budick (New Haven: Yale University Press, 1986), 77-103.

[^181]: JT Peah 2:4 (17a); cf. Leviticus Rabbah 22:1. This principle -- that all future interpretive innovations are already implicit in the Sinaitic revelation -- provides the theological warrant for the rabbinic practice of composing speeches and dialogues that are not found in Scripture. The sage who puts words in Abraham's mouth is not inventing but uncovering.

[^182]: The threefold classification (eidolopoeia, prosopopoeia, and speeches for non-human entities) follows Theon's and Hermogenes' taxonomy with modifications. Theon distinguishes *eidolopoeia* (impersonation of a known person) from *prosopopoeia* (personification of an abstract entity or type). Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 9) adds a third category, *ethopoeia* proper, for speeches expressing the character (*ethos*) of a known type of person. The rabbinic tradition is particularly rich in prosopopoeia -- speeches attributed to God, to the Torah, to abstract attributes -- a feature that distinguishes it from the more anthropocentric Greek rhetorical tradition.

[^183]: BT Megillah 10b; cf. BT Sanhedrin 39b. The attribution of this sentiment to God is one of the most theologically audacious examples of ethopoeia in rabbinic literature. On the literary and theological function of divine speech in midrash, see Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 188-204; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash*, 93-115.

[^184]: BT Sanhedrin 39b. Cf. the parallel in Midrash Mishlei 11, where the same principle is articulated in different terms. The claim to know God's emotional state is a form of prosopopoeia that goes beyond anything in the Greek rhetorical tradition, where the gods' speeches are composed in mythological contexts rather than as theological propositions.

[^185]: Genesis Rabbah 38:13 (Theodor-Albeck edition). This dialogue has been extensively analyzed as a philosophical debate in the Socratic mode. See Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 120-135, who compares Abraham's chain argument to the Socratic practice of *elenchus* (refutation through questioning). The narrative has no scriptural basis but became one of the most widely known midrashic narratives in Jewish tradition.

[^186]: BT Berakhot 32a. On Moses's advocacy before God as a form of forensic rhetoric, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 108-114; and Urbach, *The Sages*, 451-462, on the theological implications of human-divine dialogue in rabbinic thought.

[^187]: BT Berakhot 32a (continuation). The tripartite structure of Moses's final argument -- matching each form of potential punishment to a patriarch who endured it voluntarily -- is a sophisticated form of argument from precedent that combines ethopoeia (Moses's speech) with comparison (each patriarch matched to a punishment) and chreia (each patriarch's defining act recalled as a single, telling detail).

[^188]: Genesis Rabbah 1:1 (Theodor-Albeck edition). On the personification of Torah as God's artisan or blueprint, see Urbach, *The Sages*, 198-213; and Fishbane, *Biblical Myth and Rabbinic Mythmaking* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2003), 19-54. The passage draws on Proverbs 8:30, where Wisdom describes herself as *amon* -- variously translated as "artisan," "nursling," or "confidant" -- beside God during creation.

[^189]: Lamentations Rabbah, Petihta 24 (Buber edition). This passage has been the subject of extensive literary and theological analysis. See Stern, *Parables in Midrash*, 140-145; and Boyarin, *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash*, 121-133. Rachel's argument -- a *qal va-homer* from human to divine compassion -- is one of the most rhetorically effective speeches in all of rabbinic literature. On the prominence of female voices in Lamentations Rabbah, see Mandel, "The Loss of Center: Changing Attitudes Towards the Temple in Aggadic Literature," *Harvard Theological Review* 99 (2006): 17-35.

[^190]: Compare Theon's exercise instructions for ethopoeia (*Progymnasmata* 8; Kennedy, 47-49), which include composing speeches for known persons in specific circumstances, with attention to the character's age, status, emotional state, and the nature of the situation. Hermogenes (*Progymnasmata* 9; Kennedy, 84-86) emphasizes three temporal orientations for the exercise: what the character would say about the past, the present, and the future. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.8.49-54) stresses the importance of *decorum* -- the speech must be appropriate to the character, not merely clever.

[^191]: On *enargeia* (vividness) as the defining quality of ekphrasis, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 11 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 65-69), who defines ekphrasis as "a descriptive speech that brings the subject vividly (*enargos*) before the eyes"; Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, 117-120); and Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 10 (Kennedy, 86). Quintilian discusses *enargeia* (which he translates as *evidentia*) at *Inst. Or.* 8.3.61-71, defining it as "the quality of making the listener feel that he sees what is described." The concept is central to the rhetorical theory of description across the Greco-Roman tradition.

[^192]: Theon enumerates the following types of ekphrasis: descriptions of persons (*prosopa*), events (*pragmata*), places (*topoi*), and times (*chronoi*). Aphthonius adds descriptions of artworks and objects. Hermogenes provides a similar list. Our categorization follows Theon's with the addition of the category of objects and artworks, which is particularly relevant to rabbinic descriptions of the Temple and its furnishings.

[^193]: Mishnah Yoma 3:6. The detailed description of the High Priest's changes of garment -- from golden to white and back -- is among the most precisely choreographed passages in the Mishnah. On the Mishnah Yoma's descriptions as a form of ekphrasis, see Kulp, "The Origins of the Seder and Haggadah in the Context of the Early History of the Liturgy," *Currents in Biblical Research* 4 (2005): 109-134.

[^194]: Mishnah Yoma 6:2. The verbatim quotation of the confession -- including the pronunciation of the divine Name -- is itself a form of descriptive preservation: the Mishnah records not only the actions but the exact words of a ritual that can no longer be performed.

[^195]: Mishnah Yoma 6:2 (continuation). The description of the crowd's response -- kneeling, prostrating, falling on their faces -- moves from the auditory (hearing the Name) to the physical (the bodily response) to the verbal (the spoken blessing). This multi-sensory description exemplifies the principle of *enargeia*.

[^196]: Mishnah Sukkah 5:1. The superlative claim -- "has never seen joy in his life" -- functions as a rhetorical frame for the description that follows: having set the emotional register at the highest possible level, the Mishnah must then provide a description vivid enough to justify the claim.

[^197]: Mishnah Sukkah 5:2-4. On the Water Drawing celebration as a locus of vivid description in the Mishnah, see Rubenstein, *The History of Sukkot in the Second Temple and Rabbinic Periods* (Atlanta: Scholars Press, 1995), 125-165.

[^198]: BT Bava Metzia 84a. On the description of Rabbi Yohanan's beauty and its literary and theological functions, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 64-92. The chain of beauty -- Adam to Jacob to Rabbi Yohanan -- is itself a form of comparison through description: each generation represents a diminishment from the primordial perfection of Adam.

[^199]: BT Gittin 55b. The destruction narrative in Gittin 55b-58a has been extensively analyzed as a literary composition. See Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 139-175; and Cohen, "The Destruction: From Scripture to Midrash," *Prooftexts* 2 (1982): 18-39. The narrative's descriptive power -- its accumulation of vivid, specific, often grotesque details -- serves a mnemonic and liturgical function: it preserves the memory of the catastrophe in a form that can be retold and relived in each generation.

[^200]: BT Gittin 56a. The descending sequence of flour types is a rhetorical device -- a *climax* in reverse (anticlimax or *bathos*) -- that conveys the progressive desperation of the siege through the specificity of its details. Compare Quintilian's discussion of descriptive specificity as a tool of *enargeia* at *Inst. Or.* 8.3.66-70.

[^201]: Mishnah Sotah 1:5-6. On the sotah ritual as described in the Mishnah, see Hauptman, *Rereading the Mishnah*, 221-240; and Fonrobert, *Menstrual Purity: Rabbinic and Christian Reconstructions of Biblical Gender* (Stanford: Stanford University Press, 2000), 95-112.

[^202]: On the concept of *enargeia* and its centrality to the theory of ekphrasis, see Webb, *Ekphrasis, Imagination and Persuasion in Ancient Rhetorical Theory and Practice* (Farnham: Ashgate, 2009), esp. 87-130; and Zanker, "Enargeia in the Ancient Criticism of Poetry," *Rheinisches Museum* 124 (1981): 297-311. Quintilian's treatment at *Inst. Or.* 8.3.61-71 is the most extensive in the Latin tradition.

[^203]: Compare Theon's exercise instructions for ekphrasis (*Progymnasmata* 11; Kennedy, 65-69), which include composing descriptions of persons, places, events, and times, with attention to sensory detail and the quality of *enargeia*. Aphthonius (*Progymnasmata* 12; Kennedy, 117-120) provides model descriptions of the Alexandrian acropolis and a battle scene. The rabbinic counterpart -- describing the Sinai theophany or the Temple service -- serves the same pedagogical function: training the student in vivid, sensory description.

[^204]: On thesis (*thesis*) as a progymnasmata exercise, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 69-72); Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, 120-124); and Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 11 (Kennedy, 86-88). Quintilian discusses the thesis at *Inst. Or.* 2.4.24-25 and 3.5, defining it as a general question (*quaestio infinita*) as opposed to the hypothesis, a particular question (*quaestio finita*). Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.8, provides the classic distinction between thesis and hypothesis.

[^205]: On the distinction between thesis (general) and hypothesis (particular), see Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.8; Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.5.5-18; and the discussion in Kennedy, *The Art of Persuasion in Greece* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1963), 10-12. The thesis was considered more philosophically demanding because it required the student to argue about universal principles rather than particular circumstances. Aristotle (*Rhetoric* 1.2) makes a similar distinction between general and particular premises of enthymemes.

[^206]: BT Kiddushin 40b. See note 165 above. In the context of the thesis exercise, this passage exemplifies the ideal structure: a general question is posed, opposing positions are argued by named authorities, and a collective resolution is reached that preserves the insights of both sides.

[^207]: Mishnah Avot 1:13. The metaphor of Torah as a "crown" (*taga*) that must not be exploited for personal gain is itself a thesis-statement -- a general proposition about the proper relationship between sacred knowledge and material benefit. On the economic dimensions of Torah teaching in the tannaitic period, see Hezser, *The Social Structure of the Rabbinic Movement*, 334-378.

[^208]: Mishnah Avot 4:5. The double metaphor -- crown and spade -- extends the thesis by addressing two distinct forms of exploitation: using Torah for prestige (*atara*, crown) and using Torah for income (*kardom*, spade).

[^209]: BT Nedarim 37a. The distinction between teaching Torah itself (which must be gratuitous) and providing ancillary services (for which compensation is permitted) is a characteristic rabbinic resolution of a thesis: the general principle is upheld, but a practical exception is carved out that allows the system to function.

[^210]: BT Berakhot 5a. On the theology of suffering in rabbinic literature, see Urbach, *The Sages*, 444-461; and Kraemer, *Responses to Suffering in Classical Rabbinic Literature* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1995), esp. 73-125. The passage in Berakhot 5a-b is the locus classicus for the rabbinic doctrine of *yissurin shel ahavah* (sufferings of love).

[^211]: BT Berakhot 5b. The sequence of three visitation narratives -- Rabbi Hiyya bar Abba, Rabbi Yohanan, and Rabbi Eliezer -- each structured identically (the visitor asks "Are your sufferings dear to you?" and the sick sage replies "Neither they nor their reward") constitutes a powerful refutation-by-repetition of the theoretical thesis about redemptive suffering. Three times the thesis is tested against experience, and three times experience wins. On the literary structure of this passage, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 215-230.

[^212]: Mishnah Berakhot 4:4. Rabbi Eliezer's position that "fixed" prayer is not true supplication represents one side of a thesis on the nature of prayer: spontaneity as the essence of genuine communion with God.

[^213]: BT Berakhot 29b. The three definitions of "fixed" prayer -- prayer experienced as a burden, prayer without the language of supplication, prayer without innovation -- represent three different arguments within the same thesis, each one identifying a different aspect of the problem.

[^214]: The thesis "Is it better to live in the Land of Israel or in the Diaspora?" is not explicitly formulated in these terms in the rabbinic sources, but the underlying tension pervades the literature. See BT Ketubot 110b-111a for the principal sources on the obligation to live in the Land of Israel; and the counter-traditions about the value of the Babylonian academies in BT Sukkah 20a and BT Sanhedrin 24a. On the thesis as a progymnasmata exercise that trains the student to argue both sides of a general question, compare Theon, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, 69-72), and Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, 120-124).

[^215]: On the introduction of a law (*eisphora nomou* / *nomou eisphora*) as the capstone exercise of the progymnasmata, see Theon, *Progymnasmata* 13 (Kennedy, *Progymnasmata*, 72-76); Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 14 (Kennedy, 124-127), who calls it the most demanding exercise because it requires all the skills practiced in the preceding exercises; Hermogenes, *Progymnasmata* 12 (Kennedy, 88); and Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 2.4.33, who discusses it as the exercise that most directly prepares the student for actual forensic and deliberative oratory. The rabbinic parallel -- the enactment of *takkanot* and *gezerot* -- represents the most consequential form of discourse in the halakhic tradition.

[^216]: The six methods of argument enumerated here (justice, legality, possibility, consistency, expedience, propriety) correspond broadly to the *topoi* (commonplaces) for arguing about laws enumerated in the Greco-Roman rhetorical tradition. See Aphthonius, *Progymnasmata* 14 (Kennedy, 124-127), who lists legality (*nomimon*), justice (*dikaion*), expedience (*sympheron*), and possibility (*dunaton*) as the principal heads of argument for the proposal of a law. Hermogenes provides a similar list. Quintilian (*Inst. Or.* 3.8.22-35) discusses the topics for deliberative argumentation, emphasizing the honorable (*honestum*), the useful (*utile*), and the possible (*possibile*).

[^217]: BT Bava Kamma 79b; BT Avodah Zarah 36a; BT Horayot 3b. This principle -- that legislation must be practicable by the majority of the community -- is a rabbinic counterpart to the Greco-Roman rhetorical topic of "possibility" (*dunaton*). On this principle and its implications for rabbinic legislative theory, see Elon, *Jewish Law: History, Sources, Principles* (Philadelphia: Jewish Publication Society, 1994), vol. 2, 507-534.

[^218]: Mishnah Sheviit 10:3-4. On the prozbul as a paradigmatic rabbinic legislative enactment, see Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 421-425; and the extensive discussion in Urbach, *The Sages*, 570-578. The term *prozbul* is itself a Greek loanword (*prosbolē* = a presentation [to the court]), illustrating the interpenetration of Greco-Roman and rabbinic legal terminology.

[^219]: BT Gittin 36a-b. The legal basis for Hillel's authority to modify the sabbatical cancellation is debated in the Talmud. Some hold that the sabbatical cancellation of debts is of biblical force even in the post-Temple era, and that Hillel's enactment was valid only because it operated within the existing framework (transferring the debt to the court, which is not subject to cancellation). Others hold that the cancellation is of rabbinic force in the post-Temple era, giving the rabbis broader authority to modify it. See Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 422-425.

[^220]: Mishnah Gittin 4:2. On Rabban Gamaliel's enactments as a program of social legislation designed to protect women's interests in the divorce process, see Hauptman, *Rereading the Mishnah*, 161-185; and Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 533-540.

[^221]: Mishnah Yadayim 3:5. On the debate over the canonicity of Ecclesiastes and Song of Songs as a legislative deliberation, see Leiman, *The Canonization of Hebrew Scripture* (Hamden, CT: Archon Books, 1976), 86-102; and Lightstone, "The Rabbis' Bible: The Canon of the Hebrew Bible and the Early Rabbinic Guild," in *The Canon Debate*, ed. McDonald and Sanders (Peabody, MA: Hendrickson, 2002), 163-184.

[^222]: Mishnah Yadayim 3:5 (continuation). Rabbi Akiva's passionate defense of Song of Songs is a masterpiece of legislative rhetoric: the exclamation *chas ve-shalom* functions as a *praemunitio* (preemptive defense), the superlative "the entire world is not worth the day" functions as an *amplificatio* (magnification of the subject's importance), and the classification "holy of holies" functions as a *definitio* that places the text in the highest possible category. On the rhetorical force of Rabbi Akiva's argument, see Kugel, "The Song of Songs," in *The Song of Songs: A New Translation*, ed. Bloch and Bloch (New York: Random House, 1995), 4-10.

[^223]: Mishnah Shabbat 1:4; BT Shabbat 13b, 17a. On the eighteen decrees and the controversy surrounding their enactment, see Alon, *The Jews in Their Land*, vol. 1, 253-261; and Safrai, "The Decision According to the School of Hillel in Yavneh," in *Proceedings of the Seventh World Congress of Jewish Studies* (Jerusalem, 1981), 21-44. The comparison of this day to the day of the Golden Calf (JT Shabbat 1:4, 3c) is itself a form of invective directed at the legislative process rather than the content of the legislation.

[^224]: BT Bava Kamma 82a. On the ten enactments of Ezra as foundational rabbinic legislation, see Elon, *Jewish Law*, vol. 2, 413-421; and Urbach, *The Sages*, 556-563. The attribution of these enactments to Ezra -- a figure who stands at the transition between the biblical and rabbinic periods -- lends them a quasi-scriptural authority.

[^225]: Compare Aphthonius's model exercise for the proposal of a law (*Progymnasmata* 14; Kennedy, 124-127), which argues for a law that those who kill tyrants should be rewarded. Aphthonius structures the argument under the heads of legality, justice, expedience, and possibility -- the same categories that our reconstruction deploys for rabbinic legislative argumentation. The structural parallel is striking, regardless of whether one posits direct historical contact between the two traditions.

[^226]: The transition from the preliminary exercises (*progymnasmata*) to full discourse mirrors the structure of the Greco-Roman rhetorical curriculum, in which the progymnasmata served as preparatory training for the *meletai* (practice declamations) and ultimately for actual forensic and deliberative oratory. See Bonner, *Education in Ancient Rome* (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1977), 250-276; and Webb, *Ekphrasis, Imagination and Persuasion*, 39-55. In the rabbinic context, the transition is from the preliminary exercises to the full practice of halakhic argumentation, midrashic composition, and public preaching.
## PART III: STASIS AND INVENTION

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם ישאל אדם עצמו קודם שידבר: מה השאלה?</span>
>
> *A person should always ask himself before he speaks: What is the question?*

My son, you have now practiced the preliminary exercises -- the fable, the narrative, the chreia. You have learned to compose, to analyze, to refute, and to confirm. But these are exercises with materials that others have provided. The time has come to learn how the sage discovers his own arguments and constructs his own discourse from the ground up. Before the sage can speak, he must think. Before he can argue, he must understand what kind of argument the case demands. And before he can understand the argument, he must identify the question.

This is the teaching of *stasis* -- the standing-point, the place where the dispute comes to rest on its fundamental issue. The nations of the world call this *stasis* or *constitutio*, and their teachers have elaborated it with great subtlety.[^227] But the principle itself is simple, and every judge in Israel has practiced it since the days of Moses: before you can resolve a dispute, you must determine what kind of dispute it is.

---

### III.A. The Four Questions (Stasis Theory)

The student should know: every dispute, every case, every halakhic problem, every interpretive difficulty can be reduced to one of four fundamental questions. These four questions are the axes upon which all argument turns. The sage who cannot identify which question he faces is like a physician who prescribes medicine without diagnosing the illness -- he may stumble upon the right treatment, but more often he will compound the disease.

The four questions are these:

#### The First Question: Conjecture (Ha-im Hayah? -- Did It Happen?)

> <span dir="rtl">האם היה? האם נעשה הדבר?</span>
>
> *Did it happen? Was the thing done?*

This is the question of fact. Before we can judge an act, we must establish that the act occurred. Before we can classify a substance, we must confirm that it exists. Before we can assess the character of a deed, we must verify that the deed was performed.

In the bet din, the question of conjecture is resolved through testimony. The Mishnah devotes extraordinary attention to the rules governing witnesses, for the establishment of fact is the foundation upon which all judgment rests. Consider the opening of the laws of testimony in capital cases:

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד בודקין את העדים? היו מכניסין אותן ומאיימין עליהן... בודקין אותן בשבע חקירות: באיזו שבוע, באיזו שנה, באיזו חודש, בכמה בחודש, באיזה יום, באיזו שעה, באיזה מקום</span>
>
> *How do they examine the witnesses? They bring them in and admonish them... They examine them with seven inquiries: In which sabbatical cycle? In which year? In which month? On what date of the month? On what day? At what hour? In what place?*[^228]

These seven inquiries -- the *haqirot* -- are designed to establish the basic facts: when and where the event occurred. Without agreement on these fundamentals, no testimony can stand. If one witness says "On the second of the month" and another says "On the third," their testimony is invalidated -- not because either is necessarily lying, but because the factual foundation of the case has been undermined.[^229]

Beyond the seven fixed inquiries, the judges may conduct additional cross-examinations -- *bediqot* -- probing the specific details of the event:

> <span dir="rtl">מרבין בבדיקות... אפילו שאלו: מה צבע הבגד, מה צבע האדמה, הרי זה משובח</span>
>
> *They multiply examinations... Even if they asked: What color was the garment? What color was the soil? -- this is praiseworthy.*[^230]

The student should observe: the distinction between *haqirot* (fixed inquiries) and *bediqot* (additional examinations) corresponds to a fundamental principle of argumentation. The *haqirot* establish the framework of the claim -- its essential coordinates in time and space. The *bediqot* test the reliability of the witness's perception and memory. A contradiction in the *haqirot* destroys the testimony entirely; a contradiction in the *bediqot* merely weakens it. This hierarchy teaches the student to distinguish the essential structure of any factual claim from its supporting details, and to direct his challenges accordingly.[^231]

The question of conjecture also arises in non-judicial contexts. When the sage encounters a halakhic problem, he must first establish the facts of the case. A woman comes before the bet din and says, "My husband has died." Is this a question of conjecture? Yes -- for the court must determine whether the husband is indeed dead before it can permit the woman to remarry. The rules governing the testimony of a single witness to a husband's death, the reliability of hearsay evidence in such cases, and the evidentiary weight of circumstantial indicators -- all of these are applications of the stasis of conjecture to a practical halakhic problem.[^232]

And in midrashic interpretation, too, the question of conjecture appears. When the sage asks, "Did Abraham actually bind Isaac upon the altar, or was this a vision?" -- he is raising a question of fact about the biblical narrative. When two sages disagree about whether a particular event described in Scripture occurred literally or figuratively, they are debating at the stasis of conjecture. The student should learn to recognize this question wherever it appears.

#### The Second Question: Definition (Mah Hu? -- What Is It?)

> <span dir="rtl">מה הוא? כיצד מגדירים את הדבר?</span>
>
> *What is it? How do we define the thing?*

This is the question of classification. Once we have established that an event occurred or that a substance exists, we must determine what category it belongs to. Is this act "work" on Shabbat? Is this substance "food" for the purposes of the blessing? Is this transaction a "sale" or a "gift"? The answer determines which set of laws applies.

The supreme example of definition in rabbinic law is the classification of the thirty-nine categories of labor forbidden on Shabbat:

> <span dir="rtl">אבות מלאכות ארבעים חסר אחת: הזורע, והחורש, והקוצר, והמעמר, הדש, והזורה, הבורר, הטוחן, והמרקד, והלש, והאופה, הגוזז את הצמר, המלבנו, והמנפצו, והצובעו, והטווה, והמיסך, והעושה שני בתי נירין, והאורג שני חוטין, והפוצע שני חוטין, הקושר, והמתיר, והתופר שתי תפירות, והקורע על מנת לתפור שתי תפירות, הצד צבי, השוחטו, והמפשיטו, והמולחו, והמעבדו, והמוחקו, והמחתכו, הכותב שתי אותיות, והמוחק על מנת לכתוב שתי אותיות, הבונה, והסותר, המכבה, והמבעיר, המכה בפטיש, והמוציא מרשות לרשות</span>
>
> *The principal categories of work are forty less one: sowing, plowing, reaping, binding sheaves, threshing, winnowing, selecting, grinding, sifting, kneading, baking, shearing wool, bleaching it, combing it, dyeing it, spinning, setting up the warp, making two heddles, weaving two threads, separating two threads, tying, untying, sewing two stitches, tearing in order to sew two stitches, trapping a deer, slaughtering it, flaying it, salting it, curing its hide, scraping it, cutting it, writing two letters, erasing in order to write two letters, building, demolishing, extinguishing, kindling, striking with a hammer, and carrying from one domain to another.*[^233]

This list is itself a masterwork of definition. Each category (*av melakhah*) is a genus, and under each genus fall numerous species (*toladot*). The entire system is a taxonomy -- a classification of human productive activity into its essential types. When a novel question arises -- "Is opening an umbrella on Shabbat forbidden?" -- the sage must determine which, if any, of the thirty-nine categories it falls under. Is it "building"? Is it "making a tent"? This is the stasis of definition in action: the facts are not in dispute (someone opened an umbrella); the question is how to classify the act.[^234]

The disputes that fill the Mishnah are, more often than not, disputes about definition. When Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel disagree about whether a particular quantity of dough is subject to the law of hallah, they are debating a question of definition: what counts as "dough" for the purposes of this law? When the sages disagree about whether a certain structure qualifies as a "sukkah," they are likewise engaged in definition: what are the essential characteristics that a structure must possess to fall within the legal category?

Consider the famous dispute about the definition of "twilight" (*bein ha-shemashot*):

> <span dir="rtl">איזהו בין השמשות? משתשקע החמה כל זמן שפני מזרח מאדימים. הכסיף התחתון ולא הכסיף העליון - בין השמשות. הכסיף העליון והשוה לתחתון - זה לילה, דברי רבי יהודה. רבי נחמיה אומר: כדי הילוך חצי מיל משתשקע החמה. רבי יוסי אומר: בין השמשות כהרף עין</span>
>
> *What is twilight? From when the sun sets, as long as the face of the east is reddish. If the lower [horizon] has become pale but not the upper -- that is twilight. If the upper has become pale and is equal to the lower -- that is night. These are the words of Rabbi Yehudah. Rabbi Nehemiah says: The time it takes to walk half a mil from sunset. Rabbi Yose says: Twilight is like the blink of an eye.*[^235]

Three sages, three definitions. The facts are the same -- the sun has set, the sky is changing color. The question is purely definitional: at what precise moment does "day" end and "night" begin? The answer has enormous practical consequences -- for Shabbat observance, for the timing of prayers, for when one may or may not eat on a fast day. But the dispute itself is about classification, not fact.

The student who masters the stasis of definition will recognize that many apparently intractable halakhic disputes are, at bottom, disagreements about how to draw the boundaries of a legal category. And he will learn that the resolution of such disputes often lies not in finding new facts but in articulating a clearer definition -- one that accounts for all the cases and draws the boundary in a place that the community can accept.

#### The Third Question: Quality (Keitzad Hu? -- What Is Its Character?)

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד הוא? מה טיבו של המעשה?</span>
>
> *What is its character? What is the nature of the act?*

This is the question of degree, justification, and mitigation. Once we have established that an act occurred (conjecture) and that it falls within a particular legal category (definition), we must assess its moral and legal quality. Was it done intentionally or accidentally? Was there justification? Are there mitigating circumstances? What degree of culpability attaches to the actor?

The rabbinic tradition devotes immense attention to questions of quality. The fundamental distinction is between *shogeg* (inadvertent) and *meizid* (intentional):

> <span dir="rtl">העושה בשוגג - חייב חטאת. העושה במזיד - חייב כרת</span>
>
> *One who acts inadvertently is liable to a sin-offering. One who acts intentionally is liable to excision (karet).*

This distinction, which pervades the entire halakhic system, is a stasis of quality: the act is the same, the classification is the same, but the moral character of the act -- and therefore the legal consequence -- differs according to the actor's state of mind.[^236]

The Mishnah elaborates multiple gradations along this spectrum. Between pure accident and full intention lie several intermediate categories:

- *Ones* (compulsion): The act was performed under duress. The Talmud establishes that one who is compelled by force is exempt from punishment.
- *Mitasek* (unintentional action): The person did not intend to perform the act at all -- he reached for one thing and accidentally touched another.
- *Shogeg* (inadvertent): The person intended the physical action but did not know it was forbidden.
- *Meizid* (intentional): The person knew the act was forbidden and performed it deliberately.
- *Muzhir ve-lo miskaven* (warned but without specific intent): The person was warned by witnesses but claims he did not intend the specific result.

Each gradation carries a different legal consequence, and the sage who adjudicates a case must determine not only *what* was done but *with what quality of intention* it was done. This is stasis of quality in its purest form.

Consider the elaborate treatment of degrees of culpability in tractate Makkot:

> <span dir="rtl">אלו הן הגולין: ההורג נפש בשגגה. היה מעגל במעגלה ונפלה עליו והרגתו... הוא גולה. ואם משיחלץ הימנה נפלה עליו והרגתו - אינו גולה</span>
>
> *These are the ones who go into exile [to a city of refuge]: One who kills a person inadvertently. If he was rolling [a stone] with a roller and it fell upon him and killed him... he goes into exile. But if after it had already been dislodged it fell upon him and killed him -- he does not go into exile.*[^237]

The Mishnah distinguishes between a death that occurs in the course of the dangerous activity (exile) and a death that occurs after the activity has concluded (no exile). The factual question (did he kill?) is settled. The definitional question (is this homicide?) is settled. What remains is the question of quality: was there sufficient negligence to warrant the penalty of exile? The answer turns on the precise moment at which the fatal event occurred relative to the dangerous activity -- a question of moral quality, not of fact or definition.

The student should also recognize that the stasis of quality encompasses questions of justification and excuse. In the laws of Sanhedrin, the court must determine not only whether the accused committed the act but whether the act was justified:

> <span dir="rtl">הבא במחתרת נידון על שם סופו</span>
>
> *The one who breaks in [to steal] is judged on account of his [probable] end.*[^238]

This principle -- that a householder may kill a burglar in self-defense because the burglar presumably intends violence -- is a ruling on the *quality* of the householder's act. The killing is acknowledged; the question is whether it is justified. The Mishnah's answer invokes a presumption about the burglar's intentions to establish that the householder acted in legitimate defense. This is the rhetoric of justification: the advocate does not deny the act but argues that the circumstances transform its moral character.

#### The Fourth Question: Procedure (Lifnei Mi? -- Before Whom?)

> <span dir="rtl">לפני מי? באיזה בית דין? מי דן את הדין?</span>
>
> *Before whom? In which court? Who judges the case?*

This is the question of jurisdiction, venue, and procedure. Even after the facts are established, the act classified, and the quality assessed, the sage must determine: which court has authority to hear this case? What procedural rules apply?

The Mishnah's treatment of jurisdictional levels is itself a complete system:

> <span dir="rtl">דיני ממונות בשלושה. גזילות וחבלות בשלושה. נזק וחצי נזק, תשלומי כפל ותשלומי ארבעה וחמישה בשלושה... דיני נפשות בעשרים ושלושה... סנהדרי גדולה של שבעים ואחד</span>
>
> *Civil cases [are judged] by three. Robbery and assault by three. Damages, half-damages, double payment, four-fold and five-fold payment by three... Capital cases by twenty-three... The Great Sanhedrin of seventy-one.*[^239]

The progression from three to twenty-three to seventy-one is itself a rhetorical statement: the gravity of the case determines the size and authority of the court. A dispute over property requires only three judges; a case in which a life hangs in the balance requires twenty-three; a case involving the entire nation -- a declaration of war, the appointment of a high priest, the extension of the city boundaries -- requires the full Sanhedrin of seventy-one.

The student should understand that questions of procedure are not merely technical; they are deeply substantive. The procedural rules that govern capital cases -- that the youngest judges speak first, that the court may not convene on the eve of Shabbat, that a verdict of guilt may not be rendered on the same day as the trial -- are designed to make conviction difficult. These procedural constraints are themselves arguments for the value of human life. The sage who argues that a case should be heard by a court of twenty-three rather than a court of three is not making a procedural point; he is making a substantive argument about the gravity of the matter.

Consider this teaching:

> <span dir="rtl">דיני נפשות - פותחין לזכות ואין פותחין לחובה. דיני נפשות - על פי אחד מזכין ועל פי שנים מחייבין. דיני נפשות - מחזירין לזכות ואין מחזירין לחובה</span>
>
> *In capital cases -- they open for acquittal and do not open for conviction. In capital cases -- by the word of one [majority] they acquit, and by the word of two [majority] they convict. In capital cases -- they may reconsider for acquittal but not for conviction.*[^240]

Every procedural asymmetry favors the defendant. The very structure of the proceedings is a form of advocacy -- an argument built into the institutional design of the court. The student who grasps this principle understands something profound about the relationship between procedure and substance: the rules of the game are never neutral. They always tilt the outcome in one direction or another, and the sage who understands procedure can use it strategically.[^241]

And the student should know: the four questions do not always arise in sequence. In some cases, the dispute is purely about conjecture -- did the event happen? In others, the facts are agreed upon and the entire argument concerns definition -- what category does this act belong to? In still others, the definition is clear and the dispute concerns quality -- was the act justified? And in some cases, the argument is entirely procedural -- the parties agree on facts, definition, and quality but disagree about which court should hear the case.

The skilled advocate identifies the stasis of the dispute at the outset and directs all his arguments to that point. The unskilled advocate scatters his arguments across all four questions, wasting his energy on points that are not in dispute. When the question is "Did it happen?" it is useless to argue about justification. When the question is "What is it?" it is useless to argue about jurisdiction. The sage who can say, "The dispute before us is a question of definition, and here is the correct definition" -- that sage has already won half the argument, because he has shown the court where to look.

This is the teaching of stasis. Master it, and you will never be lost in an argument again.[^242]

---

### III.B. The Topics of Invention (Middot)

My son, now that you know how to identify the question, you must learn where to find the answer. The *middot* -- the hermeneutical rules -- are your tools of invention. They are the places where arguments are found, the workshops where proofs are forged. The sage who masters the middot can find an argument in any verse, a proof for any proposition, a connection where others see only silence.

The nations of the world call these tools *topoi* -- "places" or "topics" -- and their great teacher Aristotle devoted an entire work to cataloging them.[^243] Our tradition has its own catalog, and it is more compact, more precise, and more powerful than theirs -- for our middot are not merely logical rules but keys to the Torah itself, tools for unlocking the meaning that the Holy One, blessed be He, embedded in every word and letter of Scripture.

#### Rabbi Ishmael's Thirteen Rules

The foundation of all hermeneutical invention is the *Baraita de-Rabbi Ishmael*, recited every morning as part of the liturgy. These thirteen rules are the sage's primary instruments for deriving law from Scripture:[^244]

**1. Qal va-homer (a fortiori).** If the lesser case is so, how much more the greater.

> <span dir="rtl">קל וחומר</span>

If a Nazirite, who has not sinned, is forbidden to drink wine, how much more should a sinner who has been sentenced refrain from pleasure? If an ordinary priest may not serve while intoxicated, how much more the High Priest on the Day of Atonement? The qal va-homer is the most natural form of argument -- even children use it: "If Father does not allow me to do this small thing, surely he will not allow the greater." The sages formalized it and gave it a crucial constraint: *dayo la-ba min ha-din lihyot ka-nidon* -- "it is sufficient for the derived case to be equal to the source case." The conclusion of a qal va-homer may not exceed the premise from which it is derived. This constraint prevents the argument from spiraling upward without limit.[^245]

**2. Gezerah shavah (verbal analogy).** Where the same word or phrase appears in two different legal contexts, the law of one context may be applied to the other.

> <span dir="rtl">גזירה שוה</span>

The word *mo'ed* ("appointed time") appears in connection with both Pesah and the daily offering. From this verbal parallel, the sages derive that just as the daily offering overrides the Sabbath, so too does the Pesah offering. The gezerah shavah is immensely powerful, for it allows the sage to bridge two passages of Scripture that may have no thematic connection, linking them through a single shared word. Precisely because of its power, it carries a restriction: no sage may innovate a gezerah shavah on his own authority; he must have received the verbal link as a tradition from his teacher.[^246]

**3. Binyan av mi-katuv ehad (general principle from one verse).** From a single explicit case in Scripture, a general rule is derived that applies to all analogous cases.

> <span dir="rtl">בנין אב מכתוב אחד</span>

Scripture states that a Hebrew slave goes free in the seventh year. From this single case, the sage constructs a *binyan av* -- a paradigmatic principle -- about the limits of servitude. The principle is then applied to analogous situations: just as the Hebrew slave is released, so the debtor's obligation is released in the sabbatical year. The binyan av is the rabbinic form of induction: from the particular to the general.[^247]

**4. Binyan av mi-shnei ketuvim (general principle from two verses).** When the same principle is stated in two different contexts, its generality is confirmed and it may be extended to cases not explicitly mentioned.

> <span dir="rtl">בנין אב משני כתובים</span>

If both the law of the goring ox and the law of the uncovered pit teach that the owner is liable for foreseeable harm, the principle of liability for foreseeable harm is established as a general rule and applied to cases where neither specific law applies.

**5. Kelal u-ferat (general and specific).** When Scripture states a general rule followed by a specific case, the law applies only to the specific case.

> <span dir="rtl">כלל ופרט</span>

"You shall bring an offering from the animals" (general) -- "from the cattle and from the flock" (specific). The specific terms limit the general: only cattle and flock, not wild animals.

**6. Perat u-khelal (specific and general).** When Scripture states a specific case followed by a general rule, the law applies to all cases encompassed by the general rule.

> <span dir="rtl">פרט וכלל</span>

This is the reverse of the previous rule: the general category at the end expands the scope beyond the specific cases mentioned at the beginning.

**7. Kelal u-ferat u-khelal (general, specific, general).** When a general rule is followed by a specific case which is followed by another general rule, the law applies to cases that are analogous to the specific case.

> <span dir="rtl">כלל ופרט וכלל - אי אתה דן אלא כעין הפרט</span>
>
> *General, specific, general -- you may derive only cases similar to the specific.*

This rule is one of the most elegant: the specific case serves as a paradigm, and the general categories on either side indicate that the law extends beyond the specific case but only to cases that share its essential characteristics. The student should practice identifying the "essential characteristic" (*tzad ha-shaveh*) of the specific case, for this is the fulcrum of the entire argument.[^248]

**8. Anything that was included in a general rule and was singled out to teach about a specific matter -- it was not singled out to teach about itself alone but to teach about the entire general rule.**

> <span dir="rtl">כל דבר שהיה בכלל ויצא מן הכלל ללמד - לא ללמד על עצמו יצא אלא ללמד על הכלל כולו יצא</span>

When Scripture takes one case out of a general category and legislates about it separately, the special legislation applies not only to that case but to all cases in the original category.

**9. Anything that was included in a general rule and was singled out to argue about a matter similar to its general rule -- it was singled out to make the general rule more lenient and not more strict.**

> <span dir="rtl">כל דבר שהיה בכלל ויצא לטעון טוען אחר שהוא כעניינו - יצא להקל ולא להחמיר</span>

**10. Anything that was included in a general rule and was singled out to argue about a matter different from its general rule -- it was singled out to make [the general rule] both more lenient and more strict.**

> <span dir="rtl">כל דבר שהיה בכלל ויצא לטעון טוען אחר שלא כעניינו - יצא להקל ולהחמיר</span>

**11. Anything that was included in a general rule and was singled out for a new ruling -- you may not return it to its general rule unless Scripture explicitly returns it.**

> <span dir="rtl">כל דבר שהיה בכלל ויצא לידון בדבר חדש - אי אתה יכול להחזירו לכללו עד שיחזירנו הכתוב לכללו בפירוש</span>

**12. A matter learned from its context, and a matter learned from its concluding phrase.**

> <span dir="rtl">דבר הלמד מעניינו ודבר הלמד מסופו</span>

When the meaning of a verse is ambiguous, it may be clarified by its surrounding context or by the concluding phrase of the passage. This rule teaches the student to read not in isolation but in context -- to let the environment of a word illuminate its meaning.

**13. Two verses that contradict each other -- the contradiction stands until a third verse comes and resolves between them.**

> <span dir="rtl">שני כתובים המכחישים זה את זה - עד שיבוא הכתוב השלישי ויכריע ביניהם</span>

This final rule addresses apparent contradictions in Scripture. When two verses seem to say opposite things, neither is rejected; instead, the sage searches for a third verse that harmonizes the two by limiting the scope of each. This rule teaches a profound principle: contradictions are not failures of the text but invitations to deeper understanding. The sage who encounters a contradiction should not despair but should search for the resolution that shows both verses to be true in their proper spheres.[^249]

The student should know: these thirteen rules are not merely logical procedures. They are *topoi* in the fullest sense -- systematic places where the sage goes to find arguments. When confronted with a legal question, the sage works through the middot one by one: Can I construct a qal va-homer? Is there a gezerah shavah? Can I build a binyan av? Does the text employ a kelal u-ferat structure? Each middah opens a potential avenue of argument, and the sage who has mastered all thirteen has thirteen doors through which he may enter any problem.[^250]

#### Rabbi Eliezer ben Yose ha-Gelili's Thirty-Two Rules

The student should also know that for *aggadic* interpretation -- the non-legal homiletical exposition of Scripture -- there exists a separate and larger set of tools. Rabbi Eliezer ben Yose ha-Gelili compiled thirty-two rules for the exposition of aggadah, and these are preserved in the introduction to *Midrash ha-Gadol*.[^251] Where Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen rules are the tools of halakhic invention, Rabbi Eliezer's thirty-two rules are the tools of rhetorical invention for homily, sermon, and theological reflection.

Among these thirty-two, the student should pay special attention to the following:

**Mashal (Parable).** The rule that Scripture sometimes speaks in parables, and the sage must distinguish the literal from the figurative. When Scripture says "The trees went forth to anoint a king over themselves" (Judges 9:8), this is mashal -- the trees represent human actors, and the sage must identify the nimshal. This rule authorizes the entire midrashic practice of parabolic interpretation.

**Gematria (Numerical equivalence).** The rule that the numerical values of Hebrew letters carry interpretive significance. Each Hebrew letter has a numerical value, and when two words or phrases share the same total, a connection may be drawn between them. This is not mere play with numbers; it is a tool of invention -- a way of discovering connections that the surface of the text does not reveal.[^252]

**Notarikon (Acronymic reading).** The rule that a word may be read as if its letters were the initials of a phrase. The word is "opened up" to reveal a hidden message encoded in its consonants. For example, the sages read the word *nimretzet* (Jeremiah 23:10) as an acronym for the sins of adultery (*ni'uf*), murder (*retzihah*), and similar transgressions.[^253]

**Al Tiqrei (Revocalized reading).** The rule that permits the sage to read a word with alternative vowels, thereby generating a new meaning. Since the Torah scroll is written without vowels, the consonantal text is susceptible to multiple vocalizations, and the sage exploits this to find new teachings in familiar words. We will discuss this at greater length under the treatment of wordplay.[^254]

**Shnei Ketuvim ha-Makhchishim (Two contradictory verses).** This rule, which also appears among Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen, receives expanded treatment in the aggadic context: when two verses contradict, the contradiction may be exploited homiletically to teach a theological lesson about the complexity of the divine nature or the human condition.

The student should understand: Rabbi Ishmael's thirteen rules and Rabbi Eliezer's thirty-two rules together constitute the complete invention system of the bet midrash. The former provides tools for legal argument; the latter provides tools for homiletical and theological argument. The sage who masters both possesses the full range of interpretive instruments -- he can find halakhic proof and aggadic illumination, legal precedent and theological insight, in every verse of Scripture.[^255]

#### The Middot as Topoi

Now I will tell you the deeper truth, my son. These middot function in exactly the same way as what the teachers of the nations call "topics" -- systematic places where arguments are found. The great teacher of Athens compiled a treatise he called the *Topics*, in which he cataloged the general forms of argument that apply to any subject matter: arguments from definition, from genus and species, from similarity, from difference, from degree, from consequence, from authority.[^256] The Roman teacher Cicero adapted these topics for the orator, showing how each one could generate arguments in legal and political debate.[^257] And the comprehensive teacher Quintilian synthesized these approaches, organizing the topics into a system that the aspiring orator could consult when searching for proofs.[^258]

Our middot are topics of the same kind -- but with a crucial difference. The Greek topics are general: they apply to any subject matter in any context. Our middot are specific: they apply to the interpretation of Torah. This specificity is not a limitation but a strength, for it means that our tools of invention are calibrated to the particular characteristics of the text we interpret -- its linguistic patterns, its structural conventions, its theological presuppositions. The qal va-homer is not merely "argument from degree"; it is argument from degree as applied to the graduated system of obligations and prohibitions that structures the Torah. The gezerah shavah is not merely "argument from verbal similarity"; it is argument from the specific verbal repetitions that the divine Author embedded in the text as interpretive signals.

The student who understands this connection between the middot and the topoi will appreciate both systems more deeply. He will see that the rabbinic sages and the Greek rhetoricians were engaged in a fundamentally similar project -- the systematic cataloging of the places where arguments can be found -- even as they worked with different texts, in different languages, toward different ends.[^259]

---

### III.C. The Three Modes of Persuasion

My son, you now know how to identify the question (stasis) and where to find your arguments (the middot). But the sage must know one thing more: arguments alone do not persuade. The greatest proof, delivered by a sage of bad character, will be dismissed. The most logical deduction, presented without feeling, will leave the audience cold. And the most emotional appeal, unsupported by evidence, will be recognized as manipulation.

The complete orator persuades through three modes, and the sage of the bet midrash -- whether he knows the Greek names or not -- employs all three.

#### Character of the Speaker (Middot ha-Darshan / Ethos)

The first mode of persuasion is the character of the speaker. When a sage of known integrity rises to speak, his words carry weight before he has uttered a single argument. His reputation precedes him; his moral standing is itself a form of proof. Conversely, when a sage of questionable character speaks, even his best arguments are received with suspicion.

The great teacher of Athens said it plainly: "Character is almost the most authoritative form of persuasion."[^260] And our sages said it in their own way:

> <span dir="rtl">כל שרוח הבריות נוחה הימנו, רוח המקום נוחה הימנו</span>
>
> *One with whom the spirit of his fellow creatures is pleased -- the spirit of God is pleased with him.*[^261]

And yet there is a counter-teaching that the student must also know:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the vessel but at what is in it.*[^262]

This teaching seems to say that character is irrelevant -- that only the content of the argument matters. But the Mishnah itself qualifies this: "There may be a new vessel full of old wine, and an old vessel that does not even contain new wine." The point is not that character is irrelevant but that it can be deceptive -- that one should not judge by *appearance* alone. The substance of the argument must be weighed on its own merits. But when the substance is equal, the character of the speaker tips the balance.

Consider the supreme example of character as persuasion: Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai before Vespasian.

> <span dir="rtl">אמר לו: שלום עליך מלך, שלום עליך מלך. אמר לו: לקטלא בעית - דלא מלכא אנא, ולקטלא בעית - דהוה לך למיתי לגבי עד האידנא... בין כך ובין כך אתי פריסתקא מרומי. אמר ליה: קום, דמית ליה קיסר וחשיבי דרומי אותבוך ברישא</span>
>
> *He said to him: Peace unto you, O king! Peace unto you, O king! [Vespasian] said: You deserve death on two counts -- I am not a king, and you should have come to me before now... Meanwhile a messenger arrived from Rome. He said to him: Rise, for the Emperor has died and the notables of Rome have seated you at the head.*[^263]

The sage's prophetic greeting -- "Peace unto you, O king!" -- was not merely a lucky guess. It was the culmination of a lifetime of moral and intellectual authority. Vespasian was persuaded to grant Yohanan's requests not because of any argument the sage presented but because the sage's *character* -- his calm under siege, his prophetic insight, his willingness to seek peace rather than war -- made him a credible interlocutor. The request "Give me Yavneh and its sages" was persuasive because of who asked it.

And the student should know the concept of *tokho ke-varo* -- "his inside is like his outside":

> <span dir="rtl">כל תלמיד חכם שאין תוכו כברו אינו תלמיד חכם</span>
>
> *Any Torah scholar whose inside is not like his outside is not a [true] Torah scholar.*[^264]

This principle, invoked when the doors of the bet midrash were opened after the deposition of Rabban Gamaliel, establishes that character is not merely an aid to persuasion -- it is a *qualification* for speaking in the bet midrash at all. The sage whose public teaching contradicts his private conduct is disqualified. His arguments may be logically sound, but they lack the foundation of personal integrity that gives arguments their persuasive force. This is a more radical claim than anything the Greek rhetoricians made: not merely that good character enhances persuasion, but that without good character, the sage has no right to speak.[^265]

#### Emotion (Hit'orerut ha-Lev / Pathos)

The second mode of persuasion is the stirring of the listeners' hearts. The sage who addresses only the mind addresses only half the person. To move the whole person -- to inspire action, to change behavior, to implant a teaching so deeply that it shapes the student's life -- the sage must reach the heart.

The *derashah* -- the public sermon -- is the primary venue for emotional persuasion in the bet midrash. The preacher does not merely instruct; he performs. His voice rises and falls, his imagery evokes awe or tenderness or grief, his narrative builds toward a climax that leaves the audience transformed. The preacher who cannot move his audience has failed, regardless of the accuracy of his scholarship.
