Consider the supreme example of emotional rhetoric in rabbinic literature: the narrative of the sages at the ruins of the Temple.

> <span dir="rtl">שוב פעם אחת היו עולין לירושלים... כיון שהגיעו להר הבית, ראו שועל שיצא מבית קדשי הקדשים. התחילו הן בוכין, ורבי עקיבא משחק. אמרו לו: מפני מה אתה משחק? אמר להם: מפני מה אתם בוכין? אמרו לו: מקום שכתוב בו "והזר הקרב יומת" ועכשיו שועלים הלכו בו, ולא נבכה?</span>
>
> <span dir="rtl">אמר להם: לכך אני משחק... עכשיו שנתקיימה נבואתו של אוריה, בידוע שנבואתו של זכריה מתקיימת... אמרו לו: עקיבא, ניחמתנו! עקיבא, ניחמתנו!</span>
>
> *Once again they were ascending to Jerusalem... When they reached the Temple Mount, they saw a fox emerging from the Holy of Holies. They began to weep, but Rabbi Akiva laughed. They said to him: Why do you laugh? He said to them: Why do you weep? They said: The place of which it is written "Any stranger who approaches shall die" -- and now foxes walk about in it -- shall we not weep?*
>
> *He said to them: That is precisely why I laugh... Now that the prophecy of Uriah [of destruction] has been fulfilled, it is certain that the prophecy of Zechariah [of restoration] will be fulfilled... They said to him: Akiva, you have comforted us! Akiva, you have comforted us!*[^266]

The student should analyze the emotional trajectory of this narrative. It begins with weeping -- a natural, spontaneous response to devastation. Rabbi Akiva's laughter is shocking, even offensive; it creates tension and demands explanation. The explanation, when it comes, transforms the emotional register entirely: what seemed like despair is revealed as the precondition for hope. The prophecy of destruction, once fulfilled, becomes the guarantee of the prophecy of restoration. The weeping is not denied but *reframed* -- it is shown to contain, within itself, the seeds of comfort. And the repetition of the final line -- "Akiva, you have comforted us! Akiva, you have comforted us!" -- is itself a rhetorical device: the doubling expresses the intensity of the emotional transformation.[^267]

The sages also recognized the emotional power of the *hesped* -- the eulogy. The Talmud records conventions for eulogistic speech that are explicitly designed to produce emotional effects:

> <span dir="rtl">חמימו ליה... דהוא שורפא דארעא דישראל</span>
>
> *Heat your emotions for him... for he was the burning one of the land of Israel.*[^268]

The instruction is explicit: the eulogizer is to *heat* the emotions of the audience -- to bring them to the boiling point of grief. And this is not manipulation; it is a service. The community needs to grieve, and the eulogizer provides the language and the emotional structure through which grief can be expressed, shared, and ultimately transcended. The sage who delivers a eulogy that does not make the audience weep has failed in his duty:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מי שלא מוריד דמעות על אדם כשר -- אינו ראוי</span>
>
> *Anyone who does not shed tears for a worthy person -- it is not fitting.*

The student should observe: emotional persuasion in the bet midrash is not unconstrained. It is channeled through specific forms -- the derashah, the hesped, the festival sermon -- each with its own conventions and its own appropriate emotional register. A eulogy demands grief; a Shabbat sermon demands joy and elevation; a fast-day sermon demands repentance and awe. The sage who brings the wrong emotion to the wrong occasion has misjudged his audience and his duty.

#### Argument (Ra'ayah / Logos)

The third mode of persuasion is proof through reasoning -- the marshaling of evidence, the construction of logical chains, the deployment of the middot to establish a conclusion. This is the mode that dominates the bet midrash, for the house of study is, above all, a house of argument.

The student has already learned the tools of argument in the section on the middot. Here we emphasize only one point: argument in the bet midrash is not merely logical; it is *authoritative*. The sage does not merely demonstrate that his conclusion follows from his premises; he demonstrates that his premises are rooted in Scripture, in tradition, and in the rulings of acknowledged masters. A brilliant argument that rests on no textual foundation is like a tree without roots -- it may be beautiful, but it will not withstand the wind.

The hierarchy of proofs is as follows:

1. *Scriptural proof* (*ra'ayah min ha-katuv*): A verse of Torah that directly supports the proposition. This is the strongest form of proof.
2. *Logical deduction* (*sevara*): An argument that demonstrates the conclusion through reasoning alone. "It stands to reason" (*mistabra*) is the formula. This carries great weight but can be overridden by Scripture.
3. *Precedent* (*ma'aseh*): A narrative showing that a recognized authority ruled in accordance with the proposition. As we learned in the section on narrative, a ma'aseh has evidentiary force.
4. *Analogy* (*heqqesh*, *qal va-homer*, *gezerah shavah*): An argument that extends a known law to a new case through one of the hermeneutical rules.
5. *Consensus* (*halakhah ke-*): The established ruling of the community, which carries its own authority independent of the reasoning behind it.[^269]

The sage who can marshal proofs from all five levels -- Scripture, logic, precedent, analogy, and consensus -- has constructed an argument of overwhelming force. The sage who relies on only one level leaves his argument vulnerable to challenge.

And the student should know: the three modes of persuasion -- character, emotion, and argument -- are not alternatives. They are complements. The complete discourse employs all three. The sage stands before his audience as a person of known integrity (character); he speaks with passion and conviction, moving the hearts of his listeners (emotion); and he supports his words with proofs drawn from Scripture, logic, and precedent (argument). A discourse that lacks any one of these three is incomplete. The sage who has character and learning but no emotional force will be respected but not followed. The sage who has emotional power but no character will be suspected. The sage who has character and emotional power but no learning will inspire devotion but not understanding.[^270]

As the great teacher of Athens said: the orator must understand all three and deploy each where it is needed. The teacher in the bet midrash, though he may never have read Aristotle, practices this principle every day -- for the art of persuasion is not a Greek invention but a human one, and wherever human beings speak to move other human beings, these three modes will be at work.[^271]

---

## PART IV: ARRANGEMENT OF DISCOURSE

> <span dir="rtl">הכל הולך אחר הסדר</span>
>
> *Everything follows the order.*

My son, you now possess the tools of invention -- you know how to identify the question and where to find the arguments. But arguments without arrangement are like bricks without mortar: they may be sound individually, but they do not yet form a building. The sage must learn to set his arguments in order -- to place each part of his discourse where it will achieve the greatest effect.

The nations of the world teach that a well-ordered discourse has six parts: the opening, the narration of facts, the division of the topic, the proof, the refutation, and the conclusion.[^272] Our sages, who speak in their own idiom, recognized these same parts under their own names and practiced them with mastery -- not because they learned from the Greeks, but because the logic of effective speech is universal. A discourse that begins without capturing the listener's attention will fail. A discourse that presents proofs without first stating the facts will confuse. A discourse that concludes without leaving the listener moved and instructed has wasted its opportunity. These truths are as evident in the bet midrash as in the Roman forum.

---

### IV.A. The Parts of a Derashah

The student should learn the six parts of a well-ordered derashah -- a public discourse on Torah -- and should practice composing each part separately before attempting to combine them into a complete oration.

#### Petihta (Exordium / Proem): The Opening

The petihta is the crown of the rabbinic sermon. It is the opening -- but what an opening! The sage does not begin with his theme. He does not begin with the Torah portion of the day. He begins from afar -- from a distant verse, typically from the Writings or the Prophets -- and through a chain of interpretive connections, he winds his way toward the parashah, arriving at the opening verse of the Torah reading at the very last moment.

This technique is counter-intuitive. The nations of the world teach that the opening should state the theme directly and capture the audience's attention through a clear announcement of the subject.[^273] The petihta does the opposite: it conceals the destination and takes the listener on a journey. The attention is captured not by directness but by mystery -- the audience wonders, "Where is he going? What does this verse from Psalms have to do with this week's Torah portion?" And when the connection is finally revealed, the effect is one of discovery, delight, and intellectual satisfaction.

Consider a petihta from Leviticus Rabbah, opening the discourse on Parashat Vayiqra:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי תנחומא פתח: "שומע תפילה עדיך כל בשר יבואו" (תהילים סה:ג). מהו "שומע תפילה"? כל הבריות מתפללין... ואף הקדוש ברוך הוא שומע תפילתם. ומנין? שנאמר: "ויקרא אל משה" -- אפילו כשהוא קורא למשה, הוא שומע תפילת כל בשר</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tanhuma opened: "You who hear prayer, unto You all flesh shall come" (Psalm 65:3). What is the meaning of "You who hear prayer"? All creatures pray... And even the Holy One, blessed be He, hears their prayer. From where do we know this? As it is said: "And He called to Moses" -- even when He is calling to Moses, He hears the prayer of all flesh.*[^274]

The preacher begins with a verse from Psalms about God hearing prayer. Through a chain of interpretive steps, he connects this to the opening words of Leviticus -- "And He called to Moses." The connection is theological: the God who calls Moses to receive the laws of sacrifice is the same God who hears the prayers of all flesh. The journey from Psalm 65 to Leviticus 1:1 is traversed through a series of homiletical links, each one illuminating an aspect of the theme that a direct approach would have missed.

The student should practice composing petihta'ot. Take the opening verse of any Torah portion and find a verse in Psalms, Proverbs, or Ecclesiastes that can serve as a starting point. Then build a chain of three or four interpretive links that connects the distant verse to the near one. The chain should be surprising but not arbitrary -- each link should feel, in retrospect, inevitable. The test of a good petihta is this: when the audience hears the final connection, they should think, "Of course! How did I not see that from the beginning?"[^275]

#### Ma'aseh / Sippur (Narratio): The Narration of Facts

After the opening, the sage grounds his discourse in narrative -- in the events, the circumstances, the situation that gives rise to the teaching. In a halakhic discourse, the narratio may be the statement of the case: "A man came before the court and said..." In a festival sermon, the narratio is the biblical story associated with the occasion: the Exodus on Pesah, the giving of Torah on Shavuot, the wilderness wandering on Sukkot.

The narratio should be clear, brief, and relevant. It should provide exactly the information the audience needs to understand the argument that follows -- no more and no less. The student has already learned the virtues of narrative -- clarity, brevity, plausibility, vividness -- in the section on the preliminary exercises. Here he applies those virtues to the narratio of a complete discourse.

The sage should remember: the way he tells the story shapes the argument that follows. A narratio that emphasizes the suffering of the plaintiff prepares the audience for a verdict of compassion. A narratio that emphasizes the culpability of the defendant prepares the audience for a verdict of strict justice. The choice of which facts to include and which to omit, which details to emphasize and which to pass over, is itself an act of persuasion -- and the sage should make these choices deliberately, not by accident.

#### Haqdamah (Divisio / Partitio): The Division of the Topic

After the narration, the sage announces the main divisions of his discourse. This is the *haqdamah* -- the announcement of the plan. "There are three things we must consider..." "Two questions arise from this text..." "The sages disagreed on four points..."

The divisio structure is one of the most characteristic features of rabbinic discourse. The Mishnah itself is built upon it:

> <span dir="rtl">על שלושה דברים העולם עומד: על התורה ועל העבודה ועל גמילות חסדים</span>
>
> *Upon three things the world stands: upon Torah, upon worship, and upon acts of lovingkindness.*[^276]

> <span dir="rtl">ארבעה אבות נזיקין: השור, והבור, והמבעה, וההבער</span>
>
> *Four primary categories of damage: the ox, the pit, the grazer, and fire.*[^277]

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מידות באדם</span>
>
> *There are four character types among people.*[^278]

Each of these is a partitio -- a division of the topic into its component parts. The listener hears "three things" or "four types" and immediately knows the structure of what follows. This is an enormous aid to comprehension and memory: the audience can track the discourse because they know its plan. The student should use the partitio whenever his discourse addresses multiple points. Without it, the audience is lost; with it, they are oriented.

#### Hokhahah (Confirmatio): The Proof Section

The confirmatio is the heart of the discourse -- the section where the sage marshals his arguments and proves his proposition. Here he deploys all the tools of invention he has learned: scriptural proofs, logical deductions, precedents, analogies, and the hermeneutical middot.

In halakhic discourse, the confirmatio typically follows this pattern: first, a proof from Scripture (*ra'ayah min ha-katuv*); then, a logical argument showing why the scriptural proof is compelling (*sevara*); then, a precedent from the rulings of earlier authorities (*ma'aseh*); and finally, an appeal to established consensus (*halakhah*). Multiple proofs are preferred over a single proof, for each additional proof strengthens the argument and makes it more difficult to overturn.

In aggadic discourse, the confirmatio may proceed differently: a chain of verses, each one reinforcing the teaching from a different angle; a series of parables, each one illuminating a different facet of the theme; or a progression of arguments from the lesser to the greater, building toward a climax. The sage should arrange his proofs in ascending order of power -- beginning with the weakest and ending with the strongest -- so that the discourse builds momentum and the audience is swept along by the cumulative force of the argument.[^279]

#### Teshuvah (Refutatio): Answering Objections

The sage who presents only his own arguments without anticipating objections has left his discourse vulnerable. The refutatio is the section where the sage raises the strongest objections to his position -- and answers them.

In the tannaitic midrashim, the refutatio has a characteristic formula that the student should know well:

> <span dir="rtl">יכול... תלמוד לומר...</span>
>
> *One might think [that the law is such-and-such]... Scripture teaches [otherwise]...*[^280]

This formula -- *yakhol... talmud lomar* -- is the backbone of midrashic refutation. The sage first states the objection in its strongest form: "One might think..." (*yakhol*). He gives the objection its full weight, presenting it fairly and even sympathetically. Then he refutes it: "Scripture teaches..." (*talmud lomar*). The refutation comes not from the sage's own reasoning but from Scripture itself -- the ultimate authority.

The student should mark how the *yakhol* formula works rhetorically. By stating the objection explicitly, the sage shows that he has considered it. He demonstrates intellectual honesty -- he is not hiding from the difficulty but confronting it directly. And by answering the objection with Scripture, he demonstrates that his position is not merely his own opinion but the teaching of the Torah. The refutatio thus accomplishes two things simultaneously: it defeats the objection, and it enhances the sage's credibility.

The student should also learn to anticipate which objections his audience is likely to raise. In the bet midrash, where the students are trained to challenge every proposition, the sage who fails to address the obvious objection will be interrupted. Better to raise the objection himself and answer it on his own terms than to wait for a student to raise it and be forced to improvise.

#### Chatimah (Peroratio): The Closing

The chatimah is the conclusion of the discourse -- the final words that the sage leaves ringing in the ears of his audience. If the petihta captures attention, the chatimah captures memory. What the audience remembers of the discourse is often determined by how it ends.

In the aggadic midrashim, the chatimah follows a powerful convention: the discourse ends with a message of comfort (*nechemta*) or redemption (*ge'ulah*). No matter how difficult the theme, no matter how stern the rebuke or how complex the argument, the sage brings the audience home to hope. The final words of the derashah look forward to the restoration of Israel, the rebuilding of the Temple, the coming of the Messiah, or the ultimate triumph of justice and mercy.

This convention is not merely sentimental. It reflects a deep theological commitment: that the purpose of all Torah teaching is to sustain Israel in hope, and that the sage who leaves his audience in despair has betrayed his calling. The rebuke must be followed by comfort; the lament must be followed by promise; the darkness must be followed by light.

> <span dir="rtl">כל הנביאים כולן לא נתנבאו אלא לימות המשיח</span>
>
> *All the prophets prophesied only for the days of the Messiah.*[^281]

The student should practice composing chatimot -- conclusions that return from the theme of the discourse to a message of hope, that circle back from the specific teaching to the grand arc of Israel's destiny. The chatimah should echo the petihta: if the opening verse was from Psalms, the closing verse might be from the same psalm or from a related one, creating a frame that gives the entire discourse a sense of completeness and closure.

---

### IV.B. The Yelamdenu Form

The student should know that among the various forms of rabbinic discourse, one stands out for its completeness and elegance: the *Yelamdenu* form, found in the Tanhuma-Yelamdenu midrashim and in related collections.[^282]

The Yelamdenu form begins with a question from the congregation:

> <span dir="rtl">ילמדנו רבינו</span>
>
> *Let our master teach us...*

The question is always halakhic: "How many candles does one light on Hanukkah?" "Is one permitted to do such-and-such on the festival?" "What is the law regarding...?" The sage answers with a brief halakhic ruling, citing the relevant authorities. But then, instead of stopping at the legal answer, he transitions to aggadah -- to a homiletical exposition that connects the halakhic question to the Torah portion of the week.

The transition is accomplished through a connecting verse -- a scriptural passage that bridges the halakhic ruling and the parashah. From there, the sage expands into a full derashah, complete with petihta, proofs, parables, and emotional appeals. At the end, the discourse returns to the original halakhic question, showing that the aggadic exposition has illuminated the legal ruling from a new angle. The legal question is answered not only technically but theologically -- the listener understands not only *what* the law is but *why* it matters.

Consider the structure as a complete oration:

1. **Exordium**: The halakhic question from the audience -- "Yelamdenu rabbenu" -- which captures attention and establishes the topic.
2. **Narratio**: The brief halakhic ruling, which provides the factual foundation.
3. **Transitio**: The connecting verse, which bridges law and narrative.
4. **Confirmatio**: The aggadic exposition, complete with scriptural proofs and parables.
5. **Peroratio**: The return to the original question, showing that law and narrative, halakhah and aggadah, are unified in a single teaching.

This form is remarkable for its integration of legal and homiletical discourse -- the two great modes of rabbinic speech -- into a single, seamless structure. The sage who masters the Yelamdenu form can answer a technical legal question and, in the same discourse, move his audience to tears, inspire them to repentance, and leave them with a profound theological insight. It is the complete oration of the bet midrash.[^283]

The student should practice this form by taking a simple halakhic question -- "What is the minimum quantity of matzah one must eat at the Seder?" -- and constructing a full Yelamdenu discourse around it. Begin with the legal ruling. Find a connecting verse. Build an aggadic exposition that illuminates the spiritual significance of the law. Return to the original question with a deeper understanding. If the student can do this, he has mastered the art of arrangement.

---

### IV.C. The Seder Haggadah as Model Oration

My son, I have taught you the parts of a derashah and the Yelamdenu form. Now I will show you that you have been practicing rhetoric your entire life without knowing it -- for the Passover Haggadah, which you have recited every year since childhood, is itself a fully arranged rhetorical discourse. It contains every part we have discussed, in their proper order, and it deploys every mode of persuasion we have cataloged.[^284]

#### Exordium: Capturing Attention

The Haggadah opens with two dramatic statements that capture the attention of the audience:

> <span dir="rtl">הא לחמא עניא די אכלו אבהתנא בארעא דמצרים. כל דכפין ייתי ויכול, כל דצריך ייתי ויפסח. השתא הכא, לשנה הבאה בארעא דישראל. השתא עבדי, לשנה הבאה בני חורין</span>
>
> *This is the bread of affliction that our ancestors ate in the land of Egypt. Let all who are hungry come and eat; let all who are in need come and celebrate the Pesah. Now we are here; next year in the land of Israel. Now we are slaves; next year, free people.*[^285]

This exordium accomplishes several things simultaneously. It presents the physical prop -- the matzah -- that will anchor the entire discourse in sensory experience. It issues an invitation that establishes the communal nature of the occasion. And it states the theme in its starkest form: the movement from slavery to freedom, from exile to redemption, from affliction to celebration. The antithetical structure -- "Now... next year," "slaves... free" -- is a figure of speech (*antithesis*) that makes the theme unforgettable.

Then comes the *Mah Nishtanah* -- the Four Questions:

> <span dir="rtl">מה נשתנה הלילה הזה מכל הלילות?</span>
>
> *Why is this night different from all other nights?*

The Four Questions function as the *partitio* within the exordium: they divide the topic into four concrete inquiries that the remainder of the Haggadah will answer. They also serve a pedagogical function: by putting the questions in the mouth of the youngest child, the Haggadah ensures that every participant -- regardless of age or learning -- is drawn into the discourse as a questioner, not merely a listener.

#### Narratio: The Historical Account

The answer to the questions begins with a narrative:

> <span dir="rtl">עבדים היינו לפרעה במצרים, ויוציאנו ה' אלהינו משם ביד חזקה ובזרוע נטויה</span>
>
> *We were slaves to Pharaoh in Egypt, and the Lord our God brought us out from there with a mighty hand and an outstretched arm.*

This is the narratio -- brief, clear, comprehensive. It establishes the essential facts: who (we), what (slavery and liberation), where (Egypt), by whom (God), and how (with power). From this single sentence, the entire historical narrative unfolds.

#### Divisio: The Four Sons

The Haggadah then introduces a divisio -- a classification of the audience into four types:

> <span dir="rtl">כנגד ארבעה בנים דיברה תורה: אחד חכם, ואחד רשע, ואחד תם, ואחד שאינו יודע לשאול</span>
>
> *The Torah speaks of four sons: one wise, one wicked, one simple, and one who does not know how to ask.*

This fourfold division is a masterwork of rhetorical analysis. The four sons represent four types of listeners -- four orientations toward the tradition -- and the Haggadah prescribes a different pedagogical approach for each one. The wise son receives a detailed halakhic answer. The wicked son receives a sharp rebuke that excludes him from the community and then draws him back. The simple son receives a straightforward narrative. The child who does not know how to ask receives an opening -- the parent initiates the conversation. This is the art of *audience analysis* built directly into the structure of the discourse.[^286]

#### Confirmatio: The Midrashic Proof

The central section of the Haggadah is a verse-by-verse midrash on Deuteronomy 26:5-8 -- the declaration made by the farmer when bringing the first fruits to the Temple:

> <span dir="rtl">ארמי אובד אבי, וירד מצרימה, ויגר שם במתי מעט, ויהי שם לגוי גדול עצום ורב</span>
>
> *My father was a wandering Aramean, and he went down to Egypt, and sojourned there, few in number, and there became a great, mighty, and numerous nation.*

Each phrase of this biblical passage is expounded with scriptural proofs -- verses from Exodus, Deuteronomy, and the Prophets are adduced to confirm and amplify each element of the narrative. "He went down to Egypt" -- compelled by divine decree. "And sojourned there" -- not to settle permanently but as a temporary resident. "Few in number" -- with only seventy souls. "And there became a great nation" -- as Scripture says, "The children of Israel were fruitful and multiplied greatly."

This is the confirmatio at its most systematic: the farmer's declaration is the thesis, and every phrase is proved and amplified with scriptural evidence. The method is pure halakhic midrash applied to a narrative text -- the same middot that derive law from Scripture here derive meaning from history.

#### Amplificatio: Emotional Crescendo

After the confirmatio comes a section of emotional amplification: the enumeration of the ten plagues, the computation by Rabbi Yose ha-Gelili, Rabbi Eliezer, and Rabbi Akiva that multiplies the plagues to fifty, two hundred, and two hundred and fifty, and the singing of *Dayenu*:

> <span dir="rtl">כמה מעלות טובות למקום עלינו! אילו הוציאנו ממצרים ולא עשה בהם שפטים - דיינו. אילו עשה בהם שפטים ולא עשה באלהיהם - דיינו</span>
>
> *How many levels of goodness has God bestowed upon us! Had He brought us out of Egypt and not executed judgments upon them -- it would have sufficed us. Had He executed judgments upon them and not upon their gods -- it would have sufficed us.*

Dayenu is a masterpiece of the figure called *climax* (*gradatio*) -- an ascending chain in which each link surpasses the previous one. The repetition of "it would have sufficed us" after each divine act creates a rhythm that is both hypnotic and emotionally overwhelming. The audience is swept along by the cumulative force of gratitude, each "dayenu" intensifying the one before it.[^287]

The spilling of wine at the mention of each plague is a kinesthetic element of the amplificatio -- it involves the body as well as the mind, making the discourse a multi-sensory experience. The audience does not merely hear about the plagues; they enact a response to them.

#### Peroratio: The Call to Action

The Haggadah concludes with Hallel -- psalms of praise -- and with declarations that unite the personal experience of the Seder with the cosmic narrative of redemption:

> <span dir="rtl">בכל דור ודור חייב אדם לראות את עצמו כאילו הוא יצא ממצרים</span>
>
> *In every generation, a person is obligated to see himself as if he personally had come out of Egypt.*[^288]

This is the peroratio -- the conclusion that transforms the narrative from a historical report into a present obligation. The audience is not merely hearing about the Exodus; they are *experiencing* it. The historical narrative becomes a call to action: you must see *yourself* as a liberated slave, and you must live accordingly. The discourse ends not with information but with transformation.

The Hallel psalms that follow provide the emotional conclusion -- praise, gratitude, and the forward-looking hope of future redemption: "Next year in Jerusalem!" The entire Haggadah, from the bread of affliction to the final song, is a single, perfectly arranged rhetorical discourse that employs every technique we have studied: exordium, narratio, divisio, confirmatio, amplificatio, and peroratio, enriched with ethos (the authority of the rabbinic tradition), pathos (the emotional arc from slavery to freedom), and logos (the midrashic proofs from Scripture).[^289]

The student who has participated in the Seder has already experienced a complete oration. Now he should return to the Haggadah with new eyes -- seeing in its familiar words the structure and the art that he has been learning throughout this manual.

---

## PART V: STYLE, FIGURES, AND TROPES

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם ילמד אדם את תלמידו דרך קצרה</span>
>
> *A person should always teach his student in concise language.* (BT Pesahim 3b)

My son, you have learned to find your arguments and to arrange your discourse. Now you must learn to clothe your thoughts in language -- to choose the right word, the right phrase, the right figure of speech for every occasion. This is the art of *lashon* -- style -- and it is the art that separates the competent sage from the memorable one. Many sages know the law; few can state it in a form that enters the ear and lodges in the heart. Many preachers have sound arguments; few can express them with a beauty that makes the audience carry the words home and repeat them to their children. The sage who masters style does not merely communicate; he *inscribes* his teaching upon the minds of his listeners.

---

### V.A. The Three Levels of Style

The nations of the world recognize three levels of style: the grand, the middle, and the plain.[^290] Our tradition recognizes the same three, though we describe them in our own terms. The student should learn to identify each level and to use it appropriately.

#### Grand Style (Lashon Gavo'ah)

The grand style is the language of elevation, majesty, and awe. It is the style of the prophets, of the liturgical poets, and of those rare moments in rabbinic discourse when the sage rises above instruction to touch the sublime. The grand style employs elaborate imagery, soaring metaphors, and rhythmic cadences that lift the listener beyond the ordinary and into the presence of the divine.

The supreme example of grand style in the rabbinic tradition is the description of the High Priest emerging from the Holy of Holies on the Day of Atonement. Ben Sira sang of it:

> <span dir="rtl">מה נהדר כהן גדול בצאתו מבית קדשי הקדשים... ככוכב אור מבין ענני עב, וכירח מלא בימי מועד, וכשמש משרקת על היכל עליון</span>
>
> *How glorious was the High Priest when he came forth from the Holy of Holies... Like a star of light among the clouds, like the full moon on the days of festival, like the sun shining upon the Temple above.*[^291]

And the Mishnah echoes this grandeur when it describes the Day of Atonement service:

> <span dir="rtl">וכך היה מונה: אחת, אחת ואחת, אחת ושתים, אחת ושלוש, אחת וארבע, אחת וחמש, אחת ושש, אחת ושבע</span>
>
> *And thus he would count: One. One and one. One and two. One and three. One and four. One and five. One and six. One and seven.*[^292]

Even the counting of the sprinklings of blood is given a ritual cadence that elevates it above mere enumeration. The repetition of "one" (*ahat*) at the beginning of each phrase is not a mathematical necessity but a rhetorical one: it anchors each sprinkling in the primal act and creates a rhythm that is both hypnotic and sacred.

The grand style is appropriate for descriptions of the Temple, for apocalyptic visions, for eulogies of the greatest sages, and for those moments in the derashah when the preacher speaks of the world to come, the messianic age, or the ultimate purposes of God. It is not appropriate for halakhic rulings, for everyday instruction, or for any occasion where clarity must take precedence over elevation. The sage who employs the grand style for a trivial subject makes himself ridiculous; the sage who employs it for a sublime subject achieves the heights of eloquence.

#### Middle Style (Lashon Beinonit)

The middle style is the standard register of the bet midrash -- the language of teaching, of the derashah, and of learned discourse among colleagues. It is neither elevated nor plain but occupies the ground between: clear but not bare, elegant but not ornate, rhythmic but not incantatory.

The characteristic voice of Mishnaic Hebrew is the middle style:

> <span dir="rtl">שלושה שאכלו כאחד חייבין לזמן. אכל דמאי, ומעשר ראשון שנטלה תרומתו, ומעשר שני והקדש שנפדו, והשמש שאכל כזית, והכותי - מזמנין עליהן</span>
>
> *Three who ate together are obligated to recite the invitation [to the blessing]. One who ate doubtfully-tithed produce, or first tithe from which the priestly portion has been removed, or redeemed second tithe or consecrated food, or the servant who ate an olive's bulk, or a Samaritan -- they may be included in the invitation.*[^293]

This is the middle style at its best: precise, measured, comprehensive. Every word carries legal weight; no word is wasted. And yet there is an elegance to the structure -- the list is arranged with care, the conditions are stated symmetrically, the language flows with a quiet rhythm. The Mishnah does not *try* to be beautiful; it achieves beauty through the discipline of precision.

The middle style is what the student should aim for in most of his discourse. It is the style that conveys competence, authority, and respect for the material. The student who speaks in the grand style when the middle would suffice sounds pompous; the student who speaks in the plain style when the middle is called for sounds unprepared.

#### Plain Style (Lashon Peshutah)

The plain style is the language of maximum simplicity and directness. It is the style of the aphorism, the proverb, and the brief moral instruction. It strips away all ornament and leaves only the essential thought, naked and unadorned.

The greatest practitioners of the plain style in our tradition are the sages of Pirkei Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין אני לי, מי לי? וכשאני לעצמי, מה אני? ואם לא עכשיו, אימתי?</span>
>
> *If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am for myself alone, what am I? And if not now, when?*[^294]

Three questions. No metaphor, no narrative, no proof from Scripture. Just the questions themselves -- and they are sufficient. The power of Hillel's teaching lies in its utter simplicity: there is nothing to add and nothing to take away. Each question is a world, and the three together encompass the entire moral life: self-reliance, responsibility to others, and urgency.

The plain style is appropriate for maxims, for fundamental moral instruction, for moments when the truth is so clear that any ornamentation would obscure it. It is the style the sage uses when he wants his words to pass directly from his mouth to the student's heart without the mediation of argument or imagery.

The student should know: the plain style is the most difficult to achieve. Any student of moderate ability can produce a passable discourse in the middle style. A talented student can achieve moments of grandeur. But the plain style -- the style that says everything in a few words, with nothing wasted and nothing missing -- this requires the highest mastery. As the sages taught:

> <span dir="rtl">לעולם ישנה אדם לתלמידו דרך קצרה</span>
>
> *A person should always teach his student in concise language.*[^295]

Conciseness is not poverty of thought; it is the compression of rich thought into a small space. The sage who can say in one line what others need a page to express has achieved the apex of style. And the student should remember that Hillel, the greatest master of the plain style, was also capable of elaborate argument and extended discourse. His simplicity was not the simplicity of ignorance but the simplicity of mastery -- the simplicity that lies on the far side of complexity.

---

### V.B. Figures of Speech in Rabbinic Literature

My son, the figures of speech are the ornaments of discourse -- the patterns and devices by which the sage shapes his language to achieve effects that plain statement cannot. The student should know these figures not merely to recognize them in the texts he studies but to deploy them in his own discourse. Each figure has its proper use and its proper occasion; the sage who knows them all and uses each one judiciously has a richer palette than the sage who speaks only in plain statement.

#### Repetition (Anaphora and Epiphora)

> Greek: *anaphora* (repetition at the beginning of successive clauses), *epiphora* (repetition at the end)

Repetition is the most basic and most powerful of all figures. It creates emphasis, rhythm, and emotional intensity. The prophets used it constantly, and the rabbinic tradition continued the practice.

Consider the formulaic repetition in Mishnah Sotah:

> <span dir="rtl">משמת רבי מאיר, בטלו מושלי משלים. משמת בן עזאי, בטלו השקדנים. משמת בן זומא, בטלו הדרשנים. משמת רבי עקיבא, בטל כבוד התורה</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Meir died, the composers of parables ceased. When Ben Azzai died, the diligent students ceased. When Ben Zoma died, the expositors ceased. When Rabbi Akiva died, the glory of Torah ceased.*[^296]

The repeated formula "When X died, Y ceased" (*mi-she-met... batlu*) is anaphora: the same opening phrase is repeated at the beginning of each clause. The effect is cumulative: each death piles upon the last, and the listener feels the weight of an entire civilization's decline. The figure also creates a mnemonic structure -- the repeated pattern makes the list easy to remember.

Epiphora -- repetition at the end of clauses -- appears in the *Dayenu* of the Haggadah, where each clause ends with the same word:

> <span dir="rtl">אילו הוציאנו ממצרים - דיינו. אילו עשה בהם שפטים - דיינו. אילו עשה באלהיהם - דיינו</span>
>
> *Had He brought us out of Egypt -- it would have sufficed us. Had He executed judgments upon them -- it would have sufficed us. Had He done so upon their gods -- it would have sufficed us.*

The repeated "dayenu" at the end of each line is both a figure of speech and a theological statement: each divine act, taken alone, would have been sufficient. The repetition drives the point home with the force of a hammer striking an anvil.[^297]

#### Antithesis

> Greek: *antithesis* (the juxtaposition of contrasting ideas in balanced phrases)

Antithesis is the placing of opposite ideas in parallel structures, so that the contrast between them is sharpened and made memorable. The sages used antithesis constantly:

> <span dir="rtl">חכם עיניו בראשו, והכסיל בחשך הולך</span>
>
> *The wise man's eyes are in his head, but the fool walks in darkness.* (Ecclesiastes 2:14)

This verse, which the sages cited frequently in midrashic discourse, is a pure antithesis: wise / fool, eyes in his head / darkness, seeing / blind. The parallel structure forces the listener to compare and judge.

And in the Mishnah:

> <span dir="rtl">כל מחלוקת שהיא לשם שמים, סופה להתקיים. ושאינה לשם שמים, אין סופה להתקיים</span>
>
> *Every dispute that is for the sake of Heaven will endure. And one that is not for the sake of Heaven will not endure.*[^298]

The antithesis is perfect: "for the sake of Heaven" / "not for the sake of Heaven," "will endure" / "will not endure." The balanced structure makes the teaching feel inevitable -- as if the very form of the sentence mirrors the moral truth it expresses.

#### Rhetorical Question

> Greek: *erotesis* (a question asked for effect, not for information)

The rhetorical question is one of the most common figures in rabbinic discourse. It appears in two characteristic forms:

The first is the *efshar* formula -- "Is it possible that...?":

> <span dir="rtl">אפשר כל התורה כולה ניתנה ולא ניתנה פרשת סוטה?</span>
>
> *Is it possible that the entire Torah was given and the passage of the Sotah was not included?*

The expected answer is "No" -- the question is not a genuine inquiry but a challenge to an opposing position. By casting his argument as a question, the sage invites the listener to supply the obvious answer and thereby become a participant in the argument.

The second is the *le-mah ha-davar domeh* formula -- "To what may this be compared?":

> <span dir="rtl">למה הדבר דומה? למלך שנכנס למדינה</span>
>
> *To what may the matter be compared? To a king who entered a province.*

This question is the gateway to a mashal. It is not a request for information but an invitation to enter the world of parable. The question prepares the listener's mind for figurative thinking -- it signals that the sage is about to move from the abstract to the concrete, from the literal to the imaginative.[^299]

#### Climax (Gradatio)

> Greek: *klimax* / Latin: *gradatio* (an ascending chain in which each element builds upon the last)

Climax is an ascending chain of statements in which each element takes up where the previous one left off, building to a crescendo. The supreme example in rabbinic literature is the chain in Mishnah Sotah:

> <span dir="rtl">רבי פנחס בן יאיר אומר: זריזות מביאה לידי נקיות, ונקיות מביאה לידי טהרה, וטהרה מביאה לידי פרישות, ופרישות מביאה לידי קדושה, וקדושה מביאה לידי ענוה, וענוה מביאה לידי יראת חטא, ויראת חטא מביאה לידי חסידות, וחסידות מביאה לידי רוח הקודש, ורוח הקודש מביאה לידי תחיית המתים</span>
>
> *Rabbi Pinhas ben Yair says: Alacrity leads to cleanliness, and cleanliness leads to purity, and purity leads to abstinence, and abstinence leads to holiness, and holiness leads to humility, and humility leads to fear of sin, and fear of sin leads to piety, and piety leads to the Holy Spirit, and the Holy Spirit leads to the resurrection of the dead.*[^300]

This is gradatio in its most elaborate form: a chain of ten links, each one following from the last, ascending from the mundane virtue of alacrity to the cosmic event of resurrection. The structure is both logical (each virtue enables the next) and rhetorical (the ascending movement creates a sense of spiritual ascent). The listener is carried upward by the sheer momentum of the chain.

#### Parallelism

> The juxtaposition of grammatically similar clauses or phrases

Parallelism is the pervasive structural principle of Mishnaic style. The Mishnah constantly presents laws in pairs and series of balanced clauses:

> <span dir="rtl">כל שיש לו חכמה ואין לו יראה, דומה לגזבר שמסרו לו מפתחות הפנימיות ומפתחות החיצוניות לא מסרו לו. בהי עייל?</span>
>
> *Anyone who has wisdom but not fear [of God] is like a treasurer who was given the inner keys but not the outer keys. Through where shall he enter?*[^301]

And:

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין חכמה, אין יראה. אם אין יראה, אין חכמה. אם אין דעת, אין בינה. אם אין בינה, אין דעת</span>
>
> *If there is no wisdom, there is no fear. If there is no fear, there is no wisdom. If there is no knowledge, there is no understanding. If there is no understanding, there is no knowledge.*[^302]

The parallel structure creates balance, rhythm, and a sense of completeness. Each pair mirrors the other, and the reversal of terms in the second clause ("if no wisdom, no fear" / "if no fear, no wisdom") creates a chiastic effect that reinforces the interdependence of the virtues.

#### Chiasmus

> Greek: *chiasmos* (an ABBA pattern in which elements are reversed)

Chiasmus is a reversal pattern in which the first element corresponds to the last, and the second to the third, creating an ABBA structure. The sages used chiasmus both in their own compositions and in their interpretation of Scripture.

In Pirkei Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר: אם אין קמח אין תורה, אם אין תורה אין קמח</span>
>
> *He used to say: If there is no flour, there is no Torah; if there is no Torah, there is no flour.*[^303]

The structure is chiastic: flour-Torah / Torah-flour (AB / BA). The reversal of terms teaches that the relationship between material sustenance and spiritual learning is reciprocal: each depends upon the other. The chiastic form makes this reciprocity visible in the very structure of the sentence.

In midrashic interpretation, the sages frequently identified chiastic structures in Scripture and derived legal or theological lessons from the reversal of elements. The student should learn to recognize chiasmus in the texts he studies and to employ it in his own compositions, for it is one of the most elegant and memorable of all figures.

#### Asyndeton

> Greek: *asyndeton* (the omission of conjunctions between items in a series)

Asyndeton -- the listing of items without connecting words -- is the characteristic style of the Mishnah. Where ordinary speech would say "X and Y and Z," the Mishnah says simply "X, Y, Z":

> <span dir="rtl">הזורע, והחורש, והקוצר, והמעמר, הדש, והזורה, הבורר</span>
>
> *Sowing, plowing, reaping, binding sheaves, threshing, winnowing, selecting...*[^304]

The effect is one of compression and energy. The list moves rapidly from item to item, creating a sense of abundance and comprehensiveness. The absence of conjunctions gives the list a staccato rhythm that suggests urgency and completeness -- as if the categories are so many and so self-evident that they need no connecting tissue.

#### Polysyndeton

> Greek: *polysyndeton* (the deliberate multiplication of conjunctions)

Polysyndeton is the opposite of asyndeton: the repeated use of "and" (*ve-*) between items in a series. Where asyndeton compresses, polysyndeton expands; where asyndeton suggests rapidity, polysyndeton suggests weight and deliberation.

> <span dir="rtl">ברוך אתה ה' אלהינו מלך העולם, אשר קדשנו במצוותיו, וצוונו, ועשנו קדושים, ורצה בנו</span>

The repeated "and" (*ve-*) in liturgical formulae gives each element its own weight: each blessing, each divine act, is savored individually rather than swept up in a list. The student should observe the difference between liturgical polysyndeton (which creates solemnity) and Mishnaic asyndeton (which creates efficiency), and should choose between them according to the occasion.

#### Isocolon

> Greek: *isocolon* (phrases or clauses of equal length and parallel structure)

Isocolon -- the use of phrases that match each other in length and rhythm -- is one of the most common figures in Pirkei Avot:

> <span dir="rtl">דע מאין באת, ולאן אתה הולך, ולפני מי אתה עתיד ליתן דין וחשבון</span>
>
> *Know from where you came, and to where you are going, and before whom you will give an accounting.*[^305]

Three parallel phrases, each beginning with a question word and ending with a significant destination: your origin, your end, your judgment. The balanced length of the phrases creates a sense of inevitability, as if the three questions are the three pillars upon which all moral reflection rests.

The student should practice composing isocola -- balanced phrases that match each other in length and weight. The effect is one of authority and finality: the listener feels that the sage has weighed his words with care, that every syllable has been measured. This is the figure of the aphorist, and the student who masters it can compress profound teaching into a memorable form.[^306]

---

### V.C. Tropes in Rabbinic Literature

My son, beyond the figures of speech -- which concern the *arrangement* of words -- there are the tropes, which concern the *meaning* of words. A trope occurs when a word or phrase is used in a sense different from its literal meaning -- when the sage says one thing and means another, or when the language itself reaches beyond what it literally denotes to suggest something larger, deeper, or other than what it appears to say.

The student should learn the principal tropes, for they are the very lifeblood of aggadic discourse. Without tropes, the sage could speak only of surfaces; with them, he can speak of depths.

#### Metaphor (Mashal / Hashala'ah)

> Greek: *metaphora* (transfer of meaning from one domain to another)

Metaphor is the most fundamental of all tropes. It occurs when a word that properly belongs to one domain is applied to another -- when "fire" is used for Torah, or "water" for wisdom, or "light" for understanding.

The sages employed metaphor constantly and with great deliberation:

> <span dir="rtl">מה מים יורדין מגבוה לנמוך, אף דברי תורה אין מתקיימין אלא במי שדעתו שפלה עליו</span>
>
> *Just as water flows from a high place to a low place, so words of Torah endure only in one whose mind is lowly [humble].*[^307]

And:

> <span dir="rtl">מה מים מחיין את הנפש, כך דברי תורה מחיין את הנפש. מה מים מטהרין את האדם מטומאתו, כך דברי תורה מטהרין את הטמא מטומאתו</span>
>
> *Just as water gives life to the soul, so words of Torah give life to the soul. Just as water purifies a person from his impurity, so words of Torah purify the impure from his impurity.*

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">מה אש אינו מתקיים ביחידי, אף דברי תורה אין מתקיימין ביחידי</span>
>
> *Just as fire cannot be maintained by a solitary person, so words of Torah cannot be maintained by one who studies alone.*[^308]

In each of these, the physical properties of the natural element are transferred to Torah: water's descent, water's life-giving power, water's purifying capacity, fire's need for community. The sage does not say "Torah is *like* water" in a vague, decorative sense; he identifies specific properties of water that correspond to specific properties of Torah, and each correspondence yields a teaching. The metaphor is not ornament but argument -- it reveals truths about Torah that abstract statement alone could not convey.

The student should practice analyzing metaphors by asking three questions: What is the source domain? What is the target domain? Which properties of the source are being transferred to the target? The answers will reveal the structure of the metaphor and the teaching it carries.

#### Metonymy (Kinui)

> Greek: *metonymia* (substitution of one name for another to which it is related)

Metonymy is the substitution of one word for another to which it stands in a relationship of association -- cause for effect, container for contained, attribute for the thing itself. The most common metonymy in rabbinic literature is the use of "Heaven" (*shamayim*) for God:

> <span dir="rtl">הכל בידי שמים חוץ מיראת שמים</span>
>
> *Everything is in the hands of Heaven except the fear of Heaven.*[^309]

"Heaven" is used because the divine name is too sacred for casual utterance; the metonymy is both a figure of speech and an act of reverence. Similarly, "the Name" (*ha-Shem*) stands for God, "the Place" (*ha-Maqom*) stands for God, and "the Holy One, blessed be He" (*ha-Qadosh Barukh Hu*) replaces the Tetragrammaton in rabbinic discourse. Each of these metonymies carries its own theological nuance: "Heaven" emphasizes God's transcendence; "the Place" emphasizes His omnipresence; "the Holy One" emphasizes His sanctity.[^310]

#### Synecdoche

> Greek: *synekdoche* (the use of a part for the whole, or the whole for a part)

Synecdoche is the use of a part to represent the whole, or the whole to represent a part. In rabbinic literature:

> <span dir="rtl">כל נפש</span>
>
> *Every soul* -- meaning every person.

> <span dir="rtl">כל ישראל</span>
>
> *All Israel* -- meaning the community, though not every individual may be included.

> <span dir="rtl">כל ישראל יש להם חלק לעולם הבא</span>
>
> *All Israel have a share in the world to come.*[^311]

The word "all" (*kol*) is a synecdoche: it means the community as a whole, not necessarily every individual without exception (as the Mishnah itself goes on to list those who are excluded). The student should learn to recognize when "all" means "all without exception" and when it means "the generality" -- for much halakhic argument turns on precisely this distinction.

#### Hyperbole (Guzma)

> Greek: *hyperbole* (exaggeration for emphasis)

Hyperbole is deliberate exaggeration for the sake of emphasis. The remarkable fact is that the rabbis themselves recognized and discussed this trope. In a passage of extraordinary self-awareness, the Talmud states:

> <span dir="rtl">דברה תורה לשון הבאי</span>
>
> *The Torah speaks in exaggerated language.*[^312]

This principle -- that the Torah itself employs hyperbole -- is invoked to explain passages that describe cities "fortified to the heavens" (Deuteronomy 1:28) or a land "flowing with milk and honey." The sages recognized that these are not literal descriptions but rhetorical amplifications designed to impress upon the listener the magnitude of the reality described.

The Talmud further catalogues specific instances of recognized hyperbole:

> <span dir="rtl">שלושה דברים דברו חכמים בלשון הבאי: תפוח, ואפר, ושילוח</span>
>
> *Three things the sages spoke of in exaggerated language: the ash-heap [of the Temple altar], the vine, and the Shiloah spring.*

That the sages themselves identified and cataloged their own use of hyperbole demonstrates a sophisticated rhetorical self-consciousness. They were not naive about their own figures of speech; they knew when they were exaggerating and they did it deliberately, for specific rhetorical purposes. The student should learn from this: hyperbole is not a vice but a tool. The sage who never exaggerates lacks color; the sage who always exaggerates lacks credibility. The art lies in knowing when amplification serves the teaching and when it distorts it.[^313]

#### Irony

> Greek: *eironeia* (saying the opposite of what one means, or feigning ignorance)

Irony is the use of words to convey a meaning opposite to their literal sense, or the adoption of a stance of feigned innocence that allows the speaker to make a devastating point indirectly. The master of irony in Scripture is Elijah on Mount Carmel:

> <span dir="rtl">ויהי בצהרים ויהתל בהם אליהו ויאמר: קראו בקול גדול כי אלהים הוא, כי שיח לו, וכי שיג לו, וכי דרך לו, אולי ישן הוא ויקץ</span>
>
> *And at noon Elijah mocked them and said: Cry aloud, for he is a god! Either he is musing, or he has gone aside, or he is on a journey, or perhaps he is asleep and must be awakened!* (1 Kings 18:27)[^314]

Elijah's "suggestions" -- that Baal is meditating, traveling, or sleeping -- are devastatingly ironic. He pretends to take the prophets of Baal seriously, offering helpful advice about why their god has not responded. But the audience understands that each "suggestion" is a mockery: a god who sleeps or travels is no god at all. The irony is a weapon more effective than any direct attack, for it makes the prophets of Baal ridiculous.

The midrash expands on Elijah's irony, drawing out its implications and amplifying its bite. The rabbinic tradition recognized irony as a legitimate mode of prophetic discourse, and the sages themselves occasionally employed it -- though with restraint, for irony is a dangerous tool that can wound as easily as it enlightens.

#### Allegory (Remez / Mashal Arukh)

> Greek: *allegoria* (an extended metaphor in which every element of the narrative carries a second meaning)

Allegory is the sustained application of metaphor across an entire narrative, so that every element of the story represents something else. The supreme example in the biblical tradition is the allegorical reading of Song of Songs:

> <span dir="rtl">שיר השירים... רבי עקיבא אומר: חס ושלום! לא נחלק איש בישראל על שיר השירים שאינו מטמא את הידיים, שאין כל העולם כולו כדאי כיום שניתן בו שיר השירים לישראל. שכל הכתובים קודש ושיר השירים קודש קדשים</span>
>
> *Song of Songs... Rabbi Akiva says: God forbid! No one in Israel ever disputed that Song of Songs renders the hands impure [i.e., is sacred Scripture], for the entire world is not worthy of the day on which Song of Songs was given to Israel. For all the Writings are holy, but Song of Songs is the Holy of Holies.*[^315]

For Rabbi Akiva, Song of Songs is not a love poem between a man and a woman; it is an allegory of the love between God and Israel. The beloved is God; the lover is Israel; the garden is the Torah; the wedding is the covenant at Sinai. Every element of the poem has a corresponding element in the theological drama of Israel's relationship with the divine.

The extended parables of the midrash -- the elaborate king parables that stretch across multiple scenes, with a prince, a palace, servants, a banquet, a rebellion, and a reconciliation -- are also allegories. Each element of the narrative maps onto an element of the theological reality. The student should learn to read these parables on both levels simultaneously: enjoying the narrative surface while extracting the theological teaching that lies beneath.

#### Personification

> Greek: *prosopopoeia* (attribution of speech or human qualities to non-human entities)

Personification is the attribution of speech, thought, or human qualities to abstract concepts, natural forces, or inanimate objects. In rabbinic midrash, personification is remarkably common. Torah speaks. Wisdom calls out. Death enters and makes claims. The letters of the alphabet petition God for the privilege of beginning the creation.

> <span dir="rtl">בשעה שביקש הקדוש ברוך הוא לברוא את העולם, באו אותיות לפניו... באתה תי"ו ואמרה: רבונו של עולם, רצונך שתברא בי את העולם, שאני חותם של "אמת"</span>
>
> *When the Holy One, blessed be He, sought to create the world, the letters came before Him... The letter Tav came and said: Master of the world, may it be Your will to create the world through me, for I am the seal of "truth" [emet].*[^316]

In this famous midrash, each letter of the Hebrew alphabet is personified as a petitioner before the divine throne, each arguing why it should be the first letter of creation. The personification transforms an abstract question -- why does the Torah begin with the letter *bet*? -- into a vivid narrative drama. The student should observe how personification makes abstract theological questions emotionally engaging: the listener cares about which letter will be chosen because the letters have been given personality and desire.

The personification of Torah as a woman -- the bride, the beloved, the princess -- is another powerful example. When the sages say that Torah "cries out" to be studied, or "sits and mourns" when neglected, they are using personification to create an emotional bond between the student and his subject of study. The student does not merely learn Torah; he enters into a relationship with a living presence. This personification is not decorative; it is transformative -- it changes the student's attitude toward learning itself.[^317]

---

### V.D. Rabbinic Wordplay

My son, the final dimension of style that you must master is the art of wordplay -- the discovery of new meanings through the sounds, shapes, and numerical values of words. The nations of the world sometimes dismiss wordplay as frivolous. But in the bet midrash, wordplay is a serious instrument of interpretation. Through sound and number, hidden meanings emerge. The sage who can hear the echoes between words, who can read the numerical signatures hidden in the text, who can unlock the acronyms encoded in Scripture -- that sage possesses tools of invention that the literal-minded will never discover.

These are not mere games. They are instruments of interpretation -- ways of mining the Torah for meanings that lie beneath the surface of the text. And they rest on a theological premise: that the divine Author of the Torah chose every word, every letter, every sound with infinite care, and that the sage who attends to these details is attending to the mind of God.

#### Paronomasia: Al Tiqrei

> <span dir="rtl">אל תקרי... אלא...</span>
>
> *Do not read [it as]... but [as]...*

The *al tiqrei* ("do not read") formula is the characteristic device of rabbinic wordplay. Since the Torah scroll is written without vowels, the consonantal text can be vocalized in more than one way. The *al tiqrei* device exploits this ambiguity by proposing an alternative vocalization that yields a new meaning.

The most famous example:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תקרי "בניך" אלא "בוניך"</span>
>
> *Do not read "your children" [banayikh] but "your builders" [bonayikh].*[^318]

The verse (Isaiah 54:13) reads: "All your children [banayikh] shall be taught of the Lord, and great shall be the peace of your children." The sages revocalize *banayikh* (your children) as *bonayikh* (your builders), yielding: "All your builders shall be taught of the Lord." The teaching: Torah scholars are not merely the children of Israel; they are its builders. They construct the community through their learning.

Another celebrated example:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תקרי "חרות" אלא "חירות"</span>
>
> *Do not read "engraved" [harut] but "freedom" [herut].*[^319]

The verse (Exodus 32:16) describes the tablets of the law as having writing "engraved" (*harut*) upon them. The sages revocalize: the writing is not merely engraved but represents "freedom" (*herut*). The teaching: true freedom comes only through Torah. The person who accepts the law is not enslaved by it but liberated through it.

The student should observe that the *al tiqrei* device is always grounded in the consonantal text -- the alternative reading must be phonologically plausible, arising from a realistic revocalization of the same consonants (or consonants that are phonetically close). The sage does not change the text; he reads it differently. This distinction is crucial: the *al tiqrei* is not a correction of Scripture but a *second reading* -- an additional layer of meaning discovered within the existing text. Both readings are valid; the *al tiqrei* does not replace the original but supplements it.

The student should also know that the sages debated whether *al tiqrei* readings carry genuine legal authority or are merely homiletical devices. In some cases, an *al tiqrei* generates a halakhic ruling; in others, it generates only an aggadic insight. The student should learn to distinguish between these uses and should employ the *al tiqrei* with awareness of its appropriate scope.[^320]

#### Gematria

> <span dir="rtl">גימטריא</span>
>
> *Gematria: the calculation of numerical values of Hebrew words*

Gematria exploits the fact that every Hebrew letter has a numerical value (aleph = 1, bet = 2, ... yod = 10, kaf = 20, ... qof = 100, resh = 200, shin = 300, tav = 400). When two words or phrases share the same numerical total, a connection is drawn between them.

The classic example:

> <span dir="rtl">"ויריק את חניכיו ילידי ביתו שמונה עשר ושלוש מאות" -- שמו של אליעזר שלוש מאות ושמונה עשר</span>
>
> *"And he armed his trained men, born in his house, three hundred and eighteen" (Genesis 14:14) -- the numerical value of the name "Eliezer" is three hundred and eighteen.*[^321]

The number 318 in the verse matches the gematria of "Eliezer" -- Abraham's chief servant. The midrash derives from this that Abraham went to battle with Eliezer alone, and the "318 trained men" is a reference to this single servant whose name encodes the number. Whether one takes this literally or homiletically, the gematria functions as a tool of invention: it generates an interpretation that the surface reading of the text does not suggest.

The student should know that gematria is listed among the thirty-two rules of Rabbi Eliezer ben Yose ha-Gelili as a legitimate hermeneutical tool. It is used primarily in aggadic contexts, though occasional halakhic applications exist. The student should practice calculating gematria values and should develop the ability to recognize numerically significant words and phrases in the texts he studies. But he should also exercise restraint: not every numerical coincidence is meaningful, and the sage who finds gematria everywhere risks finding meaning nowhere.

#### Notarikon

> <span dir="rtl">נוטריקון</span>
>
> *Notarikon: reading a word as an acronym*

Notarikon is the practice of reading each letter of a word as the initial of a separate word, thereby "unpacking" a single word into a phrase or sentence. The word is treated as an acronym -- a compressed message that the sage expands.

The Talmud provides a well-known example:

> <span dir="rtl">"נמרצת" -- "נואפת, מורדת, רוצחת, צוררת, תועבה"</span>
>
> *"Nimretzet" [an adjective describing a curse, Jeremiah 23:10] -- [read as an acronym:] adulteress [no'efet], rebellious [moredet], murderess [rotzahat], oppressor [tzoreret], abomination [to'evah].*[^322]

Each consonant of the word *nimretzet* (nun, mem, resh, tzadi, tav) becomes the first letter of a word naming a sin. The single adjective is "unpacked" into a catalog of transgressions. The notarikon transforms a seemingly simple word into a compressed indictment.

The student should practice notarikon by taking significant words from the texts he studies and exploring whether their consonants can be read as initials of a meaningful phrase. Not every word will yield a notarikon, and the sage must exercise judgment about which readings are illuminating and which are forced. The test of a good notarikon is whether the expanded phrase sheds genuine light on the context in which the original word appears.

#### Atbash and Letter-Substitution Systems

Beyond gematria and notarikon, the sages employed letter-substitution ciphers as interpretive tools. The most well-known is *atbash*, in which the first letter of the alphabet is replaced by the last, the second by the second-to-last, and so on (aleph becomes tav, bet becomes shin, and so forth):

> <span dir="rtl">אתב"ש: א=ת, ב=ש, ג=ר, ד=ק</span>
>
> *Atbash: aleph = tav, bet = shin, gimel = resh, dalet = qof...*

The prophet Jeremiah used atbash to encode the name "Babel" (*bavel*: bet-bet-lamed) as "Sheshakh" (*sheshakh*: shin-shin-kaf):

> <span dir="rtl">"ותשת אחריהם מלך ששך" -- ששך בא"ת ב"ש: בבל</span>
>
> *"And the king of Sheshakh shall drink after them" (Jeremiah 25:26) -- "Sheshakh" in atbash is "Bavel" [Babylon].*[^323]

Other letter-substitution systems include *albam* (aleph = lamed, bet = mem), *atbah*, and others. These systems were used sparingly but deliberately, and the student should be aware of their existence as tools available for interpretation.

The rabbi now counsels: These are not mere games, my son. Through sound and number, through acronym and cipher, hidden meanings emerge from the text. The Torah is not a surface to be skimmed but a depth to be plumbed. Every letter is deliberate; every sound is significant; every numerical value is a potential key. The sage who attends to these details is not playing with words -- he is listening to the voice of the divine Author, who speaks not only through the plain meaning of the text but through the very fabric of its language.

And yet the student must learn restraint. Not every word conceals a notarikon. Not every gematria is significant. Not every *al tiqrei* yields a genuine teaching. The sage who applies these tools indiscriminately will produce readings that are clever but empty -- readings that delight the ear but do not nourish the mind. The proper use of wordplay is to illuminate what the plain meaning has already hinted at -- to confirm and enrich and deepen what careful reading has suggested. Wordplay that contradicts the plain meaning, wordplay that produces absurdities, wordplay that serves no teaching purpose -- this is not interpretation but self-indulgence, and the serious student will avoid it.[^324]

---

[^227]: The theory of stasis (*stasis* in Greek, *constitutio* in Latin) was systematized by Hermagoras of Temnos in the second century BCE, as reported by Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.8-10. Hermogenes of Tarsus later produced the most influential treatment in his *On Stases* (Peri Staseon), and Quintilian provides a comprehensive survey in *Institutio Oratoria* 3.6. On stasis theory and its four categories (conjecture, definition, quality, procedure), see Heath, "Hermogenes' *On Stases*," *Oxford Readings in Classical Rhetoric*, ed. Worthington (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2000), 151-172. The application of stasis theory to rabbinic legal reasoning has not been systematically explored in the secondary literature, though Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 97-142, discusses related structural parallels.

[^228]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 5:1. The seven *haqirot* (inquiries) are the fixed questions that every court must put to witnesses in capital cases. On the rhetorical function of the haqirot as establishing the factual foundation of testimony, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 108-114.

[^229]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 5:1-2. The invalidation of testimony through contradictions in the haqirot is discussed extensively in BT Sanhedrin 40a-42a.

[^230]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 5:2. The distinction between *haqirot* (fixed inquiries whose contradiction invalidates) and *bediqot* (supplementary examinations whose contradiction does not invalidate) is one of the most significant procedural principles in rabbinic law of evidence. Compare Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.7, on the hierarchy of evidentiary questions.

[^231]: The distinction between essential framework and supporting detail in the examination of witnesses parallels the distinction in classical rhetoric between the *status* of a case (its essential issue) and the subsidiary arguments that support or challenge the main contention. See Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.10-14; Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.6.80-104.

[^232]: On the laws governing testimony to a husband's death and the evidentiary relaxations permitted in such cases, see Mishnah Yevamot 16:1-7. The relaxation of normal evidentiary standards in cases of agunah (a woman whose husband's status is unknown) reflects a deliberative judgment about the balance between evidentiary rigor and the welfare of the individual -- a quintessentially rhetorical consideration.

[^233]: Mishnah Shabbat 7:2. The thirty-nine categories of labor (*avot melakhah*) are traditionally derived from the labors involved in the construction of the Tabernacle. On the taxonomic structure of this list and its function as a definitional framework, see Halbertal, *Interpretive Revolutions in the Making* (Jerusalem: Magnes, 1997), 33-48.

[^234]: The application of the thirty-nine categories to novel cases is one of the primary arenas for definitional argument in rabbinic law. Each new technology or activity must be classified under one of the existing categories or exempted from Sabbath prohibitions, generating ongoing debates about definition and analogy. Compare the stasis of definition (*status definitionis*) in Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.8; Hermogenes, *On Stases* 2.

[^235]: BT Shabbat 34b, citing Tosefta Berakhot 2:7. The dispute over the definition of twilight has extensive practical consequences for Sabbath observance and is one of the most thoroughly analyzed definitional disputes in the halakhic literature.

[^236]: The distinction between *shogeg* and *meizid* pervades the entire halakhic system and functions as a paradigmatic instance of the stasis of quality. Compare the distinction between *dolus* (intent) and *culpa* (negligence) in Roman law, discussed in the *Digest* 50.17.132, and the rhetorical treatment of intent in Cicero, *De Inventione* 2.23-25.

[^237]: Mishnah Makkot 2:1-2. The laws of the cities of refuge (Numbers 35:9-34, Deuteronomy 19:1-13) represent one of the most elaborate treatments of degrees of culpability in any ancient legal system.

[^238]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 8:6, elaborated in BT Sanhedrin 72a. The principle of self-defense against a burglar is a ruling on the quality of the householder's act: the killing is acknowledged but justified. Compare the rhetorical treatment of justification and mitigation in Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 7.4.

[^239]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 1:1-4. The graduated jurisdictional system reflects a principle that the gravity of the case must be matched by the authority of the court -- a principle that has both legal and rhetorical implications, since the size and prestige of the tribunal affect the perceived significance of the proceeding.

[^240]: Mishnah Sanhedrin 4:1. The procedural asymmetries favoring the defendant in capital cases are among the most distinctive features of rabbinic criminal procedure. Compare the rhetorical principle that the burden of proof lies with the prosecution, discussed in Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.13.

[^241]: The stasis of procedure (*translatio* in Latin rhetorical terminology) concerns questions about who has jurisdiction, whether proper procedures have been followed, and whether the case should be heard at all. See Cicero, *De Inventione* 1.10; Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.6.68-78. The rabbinic attention to procedural detail as a substantive safeguard of justice anticipates modern legal discussions about the relationship between procedure and substance.

[^242]: On the four stases as a complete analytical framework, see Hermogenes, *On Stases*, ed. Heath (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1995); and the comprehensive survey in Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.6. The argument that rabbinic legal analysis implicitly employs a stasis-like framework is developed in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 97-142, and is consistent with Daube's broader thesis about the influence of Hellenistic legal reasoning on the rabbis: Daube, "Rabbinic Methods of Interpretation and Hellenistic Rhetoric," *Hebrew Union College Annual* 22 (1949): 239-264.

[^243]: Aristotle, *Topics* (Topika), the earliest systematic treatment of the "places" where arguments can be found. On the relationship between the Aristotelian topics and rhetorical invention, see Kennedy, *A New History of Classical Rhetoric* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1994), 62-70.

[^244]: The Baraita de-Rabbi Ishmael is found at the opening of Sifra (also known as Torat Kohanim), the tannaitic midrash on Leviticus. It is recited daily in the morning liturgy before Pesukei de-Zimra. On the thirteen middot as hermeneutical tools comparable to the Greek topoi, see Daube, "Rabbinic Methods," 239-264; Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 47-68; Chernick, *Hermeneutical Studies in Talmudic and Midrashic Literature* (Lod: Haberman Institute, 1984).

[^245]: The qal va-homer (a fortiori) is the most frequently used of the thirteen middot and the one most readily comparable to Greco-Roman logical forms. The constraint of *dayo* (sufficiency) is debated in Mishnah Bava Qamma 2:5 and BT Bava Qamma 25a. Compare Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 2.23.4, on the argument from degree (*mallon kai hetton*); Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.10.87-93.

[^246]: On the gezerah shavah and its restriction to received traditions, see BT Pesahim 66a; Niddah 19b. The requirement of received tradition (*qabbalah*) distinguishes the gezerah shavah from free verbal association and imposes a restraint on the power of analogical argument. Compare the distinction in classical rhetoric between *loci communes* (common topics available to all) and *loci proprii* (special topics restricted to particular fields): Cicero, *Topica* 2.8; Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.10.20-32.

[^247]: On the binyan av as a form of induction, see Schwarz, *Der hermeneutische Syllogismus in der talmudischen Litteratur* (Vienna: Israel-Theologische Lehranstalt, 1901); Chernick, *Hermeneutical Studies*, 45-78. Compare Aristotle, *Topics* 1.12, on induction (*epagoge*) as a form of reasoning from particular to general.

[^248]: The rule of kelal u-ferat u-khelal ("general, specific, general") is one of the most debated hermeneutical principles. The identification of the "essential characteristic" (*tzad ha-shaveh*) that links the specific case to other cases is a form of definitional reasoning closely paralleling the rhetorical topos of genus and species. See Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.10.55-63.

[^249]: The thirteen middot taken as a whole constitute a systematic hermeneutical toolkit that is functionally equivalent to the system of rhetorical topoi. For the fullest modern treatment, see Chernick, *Hermeneutical Studies*; and the comparative discussion in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 43-96.

[^250]: On the middot as "places" of argument comparable to the Aristotelian topoi, see Daube, "Rabbinic Methods," 239-264. Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 58-68, argues that the formal systematization of the middot shows the influence of Hellenistic logical and rhetorical theory.

[^251]: The thirty-two rules of Rabbi Eliezer ben Yose ha-Gelili are listed in the introduction (*Petihta*) to Midrash ha-Gadol and in several other late compilations. On the text and its attribution, see Enelow, *The Mishnah of Rabbi Eliezer* (New York: Bloch, 1933); and the discussion in Strack and Stemberger, *Introduction to the Talmud and Midrash*, trans. Bockmuehl (Minneapolis: Fortress, 1996), 25-30.

[^252]: On gematria as a hermeneutical tool, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 68-72; and the broader treatment of number symbolism in rabbinic literature in Urbach, *The Sages: Their Concepts and Beliefs*, trans. Abrahams (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1987), 100-102.

[^253]: On notarikon and its relationship to Hellenistic acronymic interpretation, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 69-71. The technique has parallels in Greek grammatical and interpretive traditions.

[^254]: On *al tiqrei* as a hermeneutical device, see Lauterbach, "The Ancient Jewish Allegorists in Talmud and Midrash," *Jewish Quarterly Review* 1 (1910-11): 291-333; Heinemann, *Aggadah and Its Development* (Jerusalem: Keter, 1974), 96-112.

[^255]: The distinction between halakhic and aggadic hermeneutical tools corresponds, in general terms, to the distinction between dialectical and rhetorical topics in the Aristotelian tradition. The dialectical topics (*Topics*) serve strict logical argumentation; the rhetorical topics (*Rhetoric* 2.23) serve persuasive discourse. See Kennedy, *A New History of Classical Rhetoric*, 62-70.

[^256]: Aristotle, *Topics* (*Topika*). The eight books of the *Topics* catalog the forms of argument available in dialectical debate, organized around the four predicables: definition, genus, property, and accident.

[^257]: Cicero, *Topica*, a compact adaptation of Aristotelian topics for Roman forensic and deliberative oratory.

[^258]: Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 5.10, which synthesizes the Aristotelian and Ciceronian treatment of topics into a comprehensive system for the practicing orator.

[^259]: On the structural parallel between rabbinic middot and Greco-Roman topoi, see Daube, "Rabbinic Methods," 239-264; Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 47-68. The comparison does not require the claim of direct borrowing; it is sufficient to note that both traditions independently developed systematic approaches to finding arguments in texts. Whether the similarity reflects contact, common ancestry, or convergent evolution remains a matter of scholarly debate.

[^260]: Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 1.2 (1356a13): "Character [ethos] is almost, so to speak, the most authoritative form of persuasion." Trans. Kennedy.
