There are situations in which the message is more effective when it comes through an intermediary -- when the sage's reserve and the meturgeman's accessibility combine to create a delivery that neither could achieve alone.

---

### VII.C. Gesture and Bearing

The rabbi teaches: The physical presence of the sage is part of his message. The body speaks even when the mouth is silent. The student who masters the content of his teaching but neglects his bearing has prepared the feast but forgotten to set the table.

The posture of teaching and learning in the bet midrash is itself a form of communication. The sage sits; the students sit at his feet:

> <span dir="rtl">הוי מתאבק בעפר רגליהם</span>
>
> *Sit in the dust of their feet.* (Avot 1:4)[^363]

This physical arrangement -- the sage elevated, the students below -- communicates authority, respect, and the direction of transmission: Torah flows from above to below, from master to student. When the sage stands, it marks a special occasion: certain pronouncements are made standing, certain texts are read standing, certain blessings are recited standing. The transition from sitting to standing is itself a rhetorical signal -- it tells the audience that something of heightened significance is about to occur.

And when an elder enters the room:

> <span dir="rtl">מפני שיבה תקום, והדרת פני זקן</span>
>
> *You shall rise before the aged, and honor the face of the elder.* (Leviticus 19:32)[^364]

The act of rising is a gesture of honor -- but it is also a rhetorical act. When the entire assembly rises at the entrance of a great sage, they are communicating to one another the authority of the person who has arrived. The sage's subsequent words carry more weight because his entrance was marked with honor. Delivery begins before the first word is spoken; it begins with the physical response of the audience to the speaker's presence.

Dignity and restraint are essential qualities of the sage's bearing:

> <span dir="rtl">אין תלמיד חכם צועק כשהוא מדבר</span>
>
> *A scholar does not shout when he speaks.*[^365]

The sage speaks with authority, not with volume. He does not need to shout, because his words carry their own weight. The sage who shouts betrays a lack of confidence in his own arguments -- he is compensating with force of voice for what his reasoning lacks in force of logic. Conversely, the sage who speaks quietly but clearly commands attention precisely because his restraint communicates confidence.

Yet the sage must be heard. There is a balance between dignity and audibility, between restraint and projection. The meturgeman system was one solution to this tension: the sage speaks softly, preserving his dignity, and the meturgeman projects, ensuring audibility. But when the sage speaks without a meturgeman, he must find the balance himself -- speaking loudly enough to be heard by all, but without the shrillness or aggressiveness that diminishes his authority.

Excessive gesticulation is inappropriate for a sage. The use of the hands should be purposeful: pointing to a text, counting on fingers to enumerate items in a list, making a gesture of emphasis at a critical moment. Random or nervous movement of the hands distracts the audience and undermines the speaker's credibility. The sage's hands should be as disciplined as his words.

Dress and appearance are also part of delivery. It is taught:

> <span dir="rtl">כל תלמיד חכם שנמצא רבב על בגדו חייב מיתה, שנאמר: כל משנאי אהבו מות</span>
>
> *Any scholar upon whose garment a stain is found is deserving of death, as it is said: "All who hate me love death" (Proverbs 8:36).* (BT Shabbat 114a)[^366]

And further:

> <span dir="rtl">לא יצא תלמיד חכם במנעלים המטולאים</span>
>
> *A scholar should not go out in patched sandals.* (BT Shabbat 114a)

This may seem like a matter of vanity, but it is a matter of rhetoric. The sage's appearance communicates his self-respect and, by extension, his respect for the Torah he represents. A sage who appears in stained garments or broken sandals communicates carelessness, and the audience will wonder: if he is careless about his appearance, is he also careless about his learning? Appearance does not replace substance, but it prepares the audience to receive substance. The sage who is well-groomed, neatly dressed, and composed in his bearing has already begun to persuade his audience before he opens his mouth -- for his appearance says: "I take this seriously, and so should you."[^367]

The student should also mark the significance of the sage's facial expression. The face communicates warmth, severity, attentiveness, disapproval, pleasure, and pain -- all without a word being spoken. The sage who smiles at a student's question communicates encouragement; the sage who furrows his brow communicates challenge. The sage who maintains an impassive expression communicates nothing -- and the student is left to guess at the master's response, which breeds anxiety rather than learning.

The great sages were known for the expressiveness of their bearing. It is told of Rabbi Yohanan that his face shone like the brightness of the sun, so that his very presence communicated the radiance of Torah:

> <span dir="rtl">ר' יוחנן הוה רגיל דהוה יתיב אשערי דטבילה. אמר: כי סלקן בנות ישראל מטבילת מצוה, לסתכלן בי, דלהוו להו בנים שפירי כוותי</span>
>
> *Rabbi Yohanan used to sit at the gates of the mikveh. He said: When the daughters of Israel come up from their immersion, let them look at me, so that they may have children as beautiful as I.*

And it is told of Resh Lakish that his fierce countenance could intimidate even the wicked into repentance -- that when he looked at a person, the force of his gaze alone could shake the person's conscience. Each sage's bearing was an extension of his teaching: the gentle sage taught gently; the fierce sage taught fiercely; and the student learned not only from their words but from their entire physical presence in the bet midrash.

The student should also know the significance of *weeping* as a rhetorical gesture among the sages. The Talmud frequently records that a sage wept during a discourse -- upon reading a verse of Scripture that moved him, or upon contemplating a theological question that overwhelmed him, or upon hearing a teaching that touched the depths of his heart:

> <span dir="rtl">כי מטא רבי יוחנן להאי קרא בכה</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Yohanan reached this verse, he wept.*

These tears are not weakness; they are delivery. The sage who weeps at the appropriate moment communicates to his audience the gravity of the matter under discussion. His tears say what words cannot: this is not an abstract theological exercise; this touches the heart of our existence. The audience that sees its teacher weep is moved in a way that no argument, however brilliant, can achieve. But the student must beware: tears that are calculated rather than genuine will be perceived as manipulation, and the sage's credibility will suffer. The weeping of the sages was real -- it arose from genuine emotional engagement with the text -- and that is why it was effective. Manufactured emotion is the enemy of authentic delivery.

And there is a teaching about the eyes of the sage:

> <span dir="rtl">עינא דרב אשי</span>

The Talmud records that the gaze of certain sages carried such authority that a glance could silence an unruly student, confirm a correct answer, or signal disapproval without a word. The sage who has trained himself to communicate with his eyes has added an entire register of non-verbal delivery to his repertoire. The student should practice this: learn to communicate approval, inquiry, skepticism, and encouragement through expression alone, so that the words, when they come, are reinforced by the non-verbal signals that preceded them.

---

### VII.D. Reading Torah -- Cantillation as Delivery Notation

The rabbi explains: My son, there is a dimension of delivery in our tradition that surpasses anything the nations of the world have achieved. This is the system of *ta'amei ha-miqra* -- the cantillation marks, which we also call *trope* -- that are affixed to the text of Scripture.

These marks tell you where to pause, where to rise, where to fall, where to connect one word to the next, and where to separate one phrase from the next. They are, in effect, a complete delivery notation -- a set of instructions for the public reader that governs voice, phrasing, emphasis, and musical contour. Nothing comparable exists in the rhetorical tradition of the Greeks and Romans, who discussed the principles of delivery at length but never developed a written notation for it.[^368]

The cantillation marks fall into two categories: *disjunctive accents* (those that separate) and *conjunctive accents* (those that connect). Every word in the verse bears one accent, and every accent is either a pause (of greater or lesser strength) or a connector (binding the word to the one that follows).

The greatest disjunctive accent is the *sof pasuq* -- the sign at the end of every verse. This is the rhetorical equivalent of the period: it marks a complete thought, a full stop. The second greatest is the *etnachta* (or *atnach*), which divides the verse into two halves. The etnachta is the verse's rhetorical center -- the major pause that creates the bipartite structure of most biblical verses:

> <span dir="rtl">בְּרֵאשִׁ֖ית בָּרָ֣א אֱלֹהִ֑ים | אֵ֥ת הַשָּׁמַ֖יִם וְאֵ֥ת הָאָֽרֶץ</span>
>
> *In the beginning God created | the heavens and the earth.*

The etnachta falls on the word *Elohim* -- "God" -- dividing the verse into two halves: "In the beginning God created" (the act) and "the heavens and the earth" (the object). This division is not arbitrary; it reflects the rhetorical structure of the thought. The reader who pauses at the etnachta allows the listener to absorb the first half before proceeding to the second -- and the pause itself creates emphasis on the word that precedes it.[^369]

Below the etnachta and sof pasuq in the hierarchy of disjunctive accents are the *zaqef*, the *tipcha*, the *segol*, the *revia*, and others -- each marking a pause of decreasing strength. Together, these accents create a hierarchical phrasing system that organizes the verse into clauses, sub-clauses, and phrases, much as punctuation and typographical formatting organize a modern printed sentence.

The conjunctive accents -- the *munach*, the *mercha*, the *mahpakh*, the *darga*, and others -- bind words together into units that are spoken without pause. They tell the reader: these words belong together; do not separate them. The reader who ignores the conjunctive accents and pauses between words that should be connected produces a choppy, unnatural reading that obscures the verse's meaning.

But the cantillation marks are more than punctuation. They are also *music*. Each mark corresponds to a melodic phrase -- a brief musical motif that the reader sings when he encounters that mark. The entire reading of the Torah in the synagogue is therefore a musical performance, governed by a notation system that is at once linguistic (indicating phrasing and emphasis) and aesthetic (indicating melody and rhythm).[^370]

The musical dimension is not ornamental. It carries meaning. A rising melody at a certain point creates expectation; a falling melody at another point creates resolution. The melody of the cantillation guides the listener's emotional response to the text, just as the score of an opera guides the audience's emotional response to the libretto. A verse chanted with its proper melody communicates not only its intellectual content but its emotional register: solemnity, joy, urgency, grief, wonder.

Consider how different cantillation can change the rhetorical impact of a single verse. The verse:

> <span dir="rtl">שְׁמַ֖ע יִשְׂרָאֵ֑ל יְהוָ֥ה אֱלֹהֵ֖ינוּ יְהוָ֥ה ׀ אֶחָֽד</span>
>
> *Hear, O Israel: the Lord is our God, the Lord is one.* (Deuteronomy 6:4)[^371]

The cantillation divides this verse with an etnachta on the word *Yisra'el* -- "Israel" -- creating a dramatic pause after the opening call. The reader cries out "Hear, O Israel!" and then pauses, allowing the summons to reverberate before delivering the declaration: "The Lord is our God, the Lord is one." This pause is a rhetorical masterstroke: it creates anticipation, it lends weight to the declaration that follows, and it transforms a theological statement into a public proclamation.

If the verse were read without the pause -- as a single unbroken phrase -- it would lose much of its force. The cantillation notation ensures that every reader, in every synagogue, in every generation, delivers this verse with the same rhetorical structure: the summons, the pause, the declaration. This is a delivery notation that transcends the individual speaker's talent, encoding the optimal delivery into the text itself.[^372]

Consider another example. The verse describing the death of Aaron's two sons:

> <span dir="rtl">וַיִּקְח֣וּ בְנֵי־אַ֠הֲרֹ֠ן נָדָ֨ב וַאֲבִיה֜וּא אִ֣ישׁ מַחְתָּת֗וֹ וַיִּתְּנ֤וּ בָהֵן֙ אֵ֔שׁ וַיָּשִׂ֥ימוּ עָלֶ֖יהָ קְטֹ֑רֶת וַיַּקְרִ֜יבוּ לִפְנֵ֤י יְהוָה֙ אֵ֣שׁ זָרָ֔ה אֲשֶׁ֧ר לֹ֦א צִוָּ֖ה אֹתָֽם</span>
>
> *And the sons of Aaron, Nadab and Abihu, each took his censer and put fire in them and laid incense upon it, and offered before the Lord strange fire, which He had not commanded them.* (Leviticus 10:1)

The cantillation of this verse creates a relentless forward momentum -- a cascade of conjunctive and minor disjunctive accents that mirrors the reckless haste of Nadab and Abihu's action. The verse does not pause for reflection; it rushes headlong from the taking of the censer to the offering of the strange fire to the terrible climax. The cantillation enacts the narrative: the reader is carried forward, unable to stop, just as Nadab and Abihu were carried forward by their own impulsiveness. Only at the final phrase -- "which He had not commanded them" -- does the verse arrive at a full stop, and the silence that follows is devastating. The cantillation has made the listener experience the disaster, not merely hear about it.

The student should also know that different books of the Bible have different cantillation systems. The prose books (Torah and most of the Prophets and Writings) use one system; the three poetic books -- Psalms, Proverbs, and Job -- use a different, more elaborate system with its own set of accents and its own musical traditions. This distinction reflects the different rhetorical registers of prose and poetry: prose requires a delivery system that emphasizes meaning and phrasing; poetry requires a system that also emphasizes rhythm, parallelism, and emotional intensity. The existence of two separate cantillation systems within a single sacred corpus is itself a sophisticated recognition of the principle that different genres demand different modes of delivery.

And the student should know that the cantillation marks also serve a *hermeneutical* function -- they are not merely delivery instructions but interpretive guides. Where the accents place a disjunctive pause, they sometimes resolve an ambiguity in the text: by separating two words that might otherwise be read together, or by connecting two words that might otherwise be read apart, the accents determine the meaning of the verse. The sages were attentive to this hermeneutical dimension:

> <span dir="rtl">כל פסוק דלא פסקיה משה לא פסקינן ליה</span>
>
> *Any verse that Moses did not divide [with a pause], we do not divide.* (BT Megillah 22a)

This principle treats the cantillation as part of the Sinaitic revelation itself -- not a later addition to the text but an integral part of the Torah as given. If this view is taken seriously, then the cantillation marks are the most ancient delivery notation in human history, and their preservation through the millennia of oral and then written transmission is itself a miracle of memory.

The student should also observe the relationship between cantillation and interpretation. Where the accents place their pauses can determine the meaning of a verse. Consider the verse:

> <span dir="rtl">וַיַּעֲמֵ֣ד הָעָ֔ם מֵרָחֹ֑ק וּמֹשֶׁ֣ה נִגַּ֔שׁ אֶל־הָ֣עֲרָפֶ֔ל אֲשֶׁר־שָׁ֖ם הָאֱלֹהִֽים</span>
>
> *And the people stood at a distance, while Moses drew near to the thick darkness where God was.* (Exodus 20:18)

The etnachta falls on the word *merahoq* -- "at a distance" -- dividing the verse into two contrasting halves: the people stood far away; Moses came close. This division creates a rhetorical antithesis: distance and proximity, fear and courage, the crowd and the individual. The cantillation does not merely phrase the verse; it interprets it, highlighting the contrast between the people's retreat and Moses's approach. A different placement of the major pause would produce a different interpretation -- and this is why the cantillation marks carry interpretive authority.

The student who learns to read the cantillation with this level of attention will discover that the ta'amei ha-miqra are not merely a musical system but a hermeneutical one -- a layer of interpretive tradition embedded in the very notation of the text. Every time the student chants a verse with its proper cantillation, he is not merely performing a musical act; he is performing an interpretive act, transmitting not only the words of the verse but a particular understanding of their meaning and their rhetorical structure.

The exercise for the student: take a single verse and chant it three times, with three different placements of the major pause. Observe how each placement changes the meaning. Then chant it with the traditional cantillation and observe what interpretation the tradition has chosen. This exercise develops both the student's skill in delivery and his sensitivity to the hermeneutical dimensions of the text.

The student should know that the Greco-Roman rhetorical tradition discussed delivery at great length -- Quintilian devotes an entire chapter to voice, gesture, and bearing -- but never achieved a comparable notation system. The Greek orator was taught principles of delivery; he was never given a note-by-note score. The cantillation system is, in this respect, one of the most remarkable achievements of the rabbinic tradition: a fully developed delivery notation, embedded in the sacred text, that has preserved the rhetoric of biblical reading for over a millennium.[^372]

---

## PART VIII: ADVANCED EXERCISES

> <span dir="rtl">הפוך בה והפוך בה דכולא בה</span>
>
> *Turn it over and turn it over, for everything is in it.* (Avot 5:22)

The student who has mastered the preliminary exercises -- fable, narrative, chreia, thesis, commonplace, encomium, refutation, and confirmation -- and who has cultivated the five faculties of invention, arrangement, style, memory, and delivery, is now ready for the advanced exercises that will bring him to the threshold of full mastery. These exercises combine all that has come before. They require the student to deploy every tool in his arsenal simultaneously, as a master craftsman who has learned each individual skill now applies them all together in the construction of a finished work.

There are three advanced exercises: the *machloqet* (controversia -- arguing both sides of a legal dispute), the *eitzah* (suasoria -- composing a deliberative speech for a biblical or historical figure), and the *sugya* (the Talmudic dialectical unit, which combines all rhetorical skills into a single complex performance). These three exercises are arranged in ascending order of complexity: the machloqet requires the student to argue both sides; the eitzah requires him to inhabit a character and compose a speech that is appropriate to the situation; the sugya requires him to navigate a multi-voiced, multi-layered dialectical construction that draws on every technique he has learned.

The student should know: these advanced exercises are not separate from the work of the bet midrash. They *are* the work of the bet midrash. Every day, the sage engages in machloqet -- debating the law with his colleagues. Every Sabbath, the darshan performs a kind of suasoria -- composing a discourse that places the words of Torah in the context of the present moment. And every session of Talmud study is an encounter with the sugya -- the dialectical unit that is the bread and butter of rabbinic intellectual life. The exercises we describe here are not artificial constructions invented for the sake of training; they are formalizations of the practices that the sage performs every day.

---

### VIII.A. Controversiae (Machloqet)

The rabbi explains: "In the bet midrash, you must be able to argue that the sheretz is pure, then turn and argue that it is impure."

This is the *machloqet* as rhetorical exercise: the student is presented with a legal case and must argue both sides with equal force and equal conviction. He must marshal proofs from Scripture, from logic, from precedent -- first for one conclusion, then for the opposite -- and he must make both arguments so persuasive that the listener cannot easily determine which represents the student's own view.

There is a story about a student of one of the great academies who was asked to argue that a certain vessel was impure. He argued brilliantly, marshaling twelve proofs. Then he was asked to argue that the same vessel was pure. He argued even more brilliantly, marshaling fourteen proofs. His teacher said to him: "You have argued well -- but I notice that your arguments for purity were stronger than your arguments for impurity. This tells me that in your heart you believe the vessel is pure, and you allowed your belief to strengthen one argument at the expense of the other. Go back and make the argument for impurity *stronger* than the argument for purity. Only when you can make the weaker position seem stronger have you truly mastered the exercise."

This story -- whether it is historical or illustrative -- captures the essence of the machloqet exercise. The student is training not merely to argue but to *control* his arguments -- to deploy equal force in both directions regardless of his personal inclination. This is an extraordinarily difficult discipline, and it takes years to develop. But it is the discipline that produces true sages -- people who can see the whole truth, not merely the half that happens to align with their predispositions.

This exercise has its roots in the very nature of rabbinic discourse. Consider what is taught of Rabbi Meir:

> <span dir="rtl">שהוא אומר על טמא טהור ומראה לו פנים, על טהור טמא ומראה לו פנים</span>
>
> *He would declare the impure to be pure and show justification for it, and the pure to be impure and show justification for it.* (BT Eruvin 13b)[^373]

And not merely with superficial reasoning, but with the full weight of rigorous argument:

> <span dir="rtl">מאה וחמשים טעמים טמא ומאה וחמשים טעמים טהור</span>
>
> *One hundred and fifty reasons to declare impure, and one hundred and fifty reasons to declare pure.*

This is not deception or sophistry. It is the deepest form of legal understanding. The sage who can argue only one side of a case understands that side; the sage who can argue both sides understands the *case*. He grasps not merely the conclusion but the structure of the problem, the forces that pull in each direction, the considerations that must be weighed. And the tradition validates this exercise at the highest level:

> <span dir="rtl">אלו ואלו דברי אלהים חיים</span>
>
> *Both these and those are the words of the living God.* (BT Eruvin 13b)[^374]

Both positions -- even the one that is ultimately rejected -- partake of divine truth. The rejected position is not false; it is simply not the law for this time and this place. In another time, under other circumstances, it may become the law. This is why the Mishnah preserves both the majority and minority opinions: the minority opinion is not an error to be forgotten but a truth to be held in reserve.

The machloqet as structured exercise proceeds in four stages:

**First, state the case.** Present the facts clearly and without prejudice. The student must learn to describe the dispute in terms that neither side would reject. If he states the case in a way that already favors one conclusion, he has failed -- for the exercise requires that the case be presented as genuinely open.

**Second, argue for one side.** Using Scripture, logic, and precedent, construct the strongest possible case for position A. The student should not hold anything back; he should make this argument as if his life depended on it. Every proof he can find, every inference he can draw, every analogy he can construct -- all should be deployed in the service of position A.

**Third, argue for the other side.** Using the same tools, construct the strongest possible case for position B. Here the discipline becomes most demanding, for the student has just spent his energy on position A and may feel drained or biased. He must summon equal energy, equal conviction, equal rigor for position B. If his argument for B is noticeably weaker than his argument for A, he has not yet mastered the exercise.

**Fourth, the resolution.** In some cases, the student arrives at a resolution: one position is shown to be stronger than the other, and the law is determined accordingly. In other cases, no resolution is possible -- both positions stand in genuine tension, and the student must acknowledge this honestly. The ability to leave a question unresolved, to hold two competing truths in suspension, is itself a sign of maturity. As the sages taught, there are questions that will be resolved only when Elijah comes.[^375]

Consider examples from the tradition:

**Bet Shammai vs. Bet Hillel on the order of blessings** (Mishnah Berakhot 8): The two houses dispute the order in which blessings are recited at the conclusion of Shabbat. Each house brings its reasoning, and each reasoning is internally consistent. The student should practice arguing first as a member of Bet Shammai, then as a member of Bet Hillel, marshaling the proofs and logic of each position as if it were self-evidently correct.[^376]

**The Oven of Akhnai** (BT Bava Metzia 59a-b): This is perhaps the most famous dispute in all of rabbinic literature. Rabbi Eliezer declares an oven pure; the sages declare it impure. The facts of the case: an oven was cut into segments and sand was placed between them. Is this still an "oven" (and therefore susceptible to impurity), or has it become a collection of separate pieces (and therefore not susceptible)?

Rabbi Eliezer argued for purity and brought every argument at his disposal. When logical arguments failed to persuade, he invoked miraculous signs: "If the halakhah is as I say, let this carob tree prove it!" -- and the carob tree was uprooted from its place. "If the halakhah is as I say, let the aqueduct prove it!" -- and the water flowed backward. "If the halakhah is as I say, let the walls of the bet midrash prove it!" -- and the walls began to collapse. Finally: "If the halakhah is as I say, let Heaven prove it!" -- and a heavenly voice declared: "What have you against Rabbi Eliezer? The halakhah is always as he says!"

And Rabbi Yehoshua stood on his feet and declared:

> <span dir="rtl">לא בשמים היא</span>
>
> *It is not in heaven!* (Deuteronomy 30:12)

The Torah was already given at Sinai; we do not attend to heavenly voices. The majority rules. The oven is impure.

The student who argues this case as a controversia must contend not only with the legal question (is the oven pure or impure?) but with the meta-legal question: who has the authority to decide? What is the role of miracles in halakhic discourse? What is the relationship between human reasoning and divine will? Can a heavenly voice override the majority of the sages? And what of Rabbi Eliezer himself -- the greatest memory of his generation, the "plastered cistern that does not lose a drop" -- who was excommunicated for refusing to accept the majority's ruling? The student must argue *for* the excommunication (the integrity of the legal system requires that all submit to majority rule) and *against* it (the sage who is right should not be punished for being right). Both arguments are powerful; both have genuine merit; and the tension between them has never been fully resolved.[^377]

**Debates about capital punishment** (Mishnah Makkot 1:10): Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Akiva declare: "If we had been in the Sanhedrin, no person would ever have been executed." Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel responds: "Then they would have multiplied the shedders of blood in Israel." The student should practice arguing each position: the abolitionist position (the sanctity of life demands that we find every possible reason for acquittal) and the retributive position (the failure to punish emboldens criminals and leads to more bloodshed). Both positions appeal to the value of human life, but they draw opposite conclusions.[^378]

This exercise is identical in structure to the Roman *controversia*: a legal case, real or hypothetical, argued on both sides by students of rhetoric. The Roman students argued cases of inheritance, tyrannicide, adultery, and treason; the rabbinic students argued cases of purity, property, marriage, and ritual. The form is the same; the content reflects the different legal systems.

But there is a crucial difference. In the Roman *controversia*, the student argues to *win* -- the exercise culminates in a verdict, and the student's success is measured by the persuasiveness of his argument. In the rabbinic *machloqet*, the student argues to *understand* -- and both sides of the argument are valued as "the words of the living God." The Roman exercise trains advocates; the rabbinic exercise trains sages. The advocate needs to win; the sage needs to see the whole truth. This difference in purpose shapes the entire exercise: the rabbinic student who argues one side with devastating effectiveness and then fails to argue the other side with equal force has not succeeded but failed, for he has shown that he understands only half the case.

The student should also know that the machloqet exercise produces a distinctive form of intellectual character. The sage who has trained himself to argue both sides develops a quality that the tradition calls *yirat shamayim* (fear of heaven) -- not in the sense of timidity but in the sense of intellectual humility. The sage who knows that the opposing position has one hundred and fifty valid arguments is less likely to be dogmatic about his own position. He holds his views with conviction but without arrogance, knowing that the truth he has grasped is real but partial, and that the truth his opponent has grasped is equally real and equally partial. This combination of conviction and humility is the hallmark of the sage who has mastered the machloqet -- and it is, perhaps, the most important character trait that rhetorical training can produce.[^379]

The exercise for the student: "Here is a case. A man finds an object in the public domain. The previous owner has not yet despaired of recovering it. First, argue that the finder must return it. Then, argue that the finder may keep it. Make both arguments equally strong, using Scripture, reason, and precedent."

A second exercise: "Two neighbors share a wall. One wishes to build higher; the other objects, claiming it will block his light. First argue for the builder's right to build on his own property. Then argue for the neighbor's right to his light. Use the principles of *hezek re'iyah* (damage by sight) and *hezek she-eino nikkar* (intangible damage), and marshal precedents from both Bava Batra and the Jerusalem Talmud."

A third exercise, more advanced: "A community has always followed a particular custom (*minhag*). A new rabbi arrives and rules that the custom is in error and must be changed. First argue for the authority of established custom. Then argue for the authority of the rabbi's ruling. Consider: what is the relationship between custom and law? Can custom override law? Can law override custom? What happens when the community and the rabbi disagree?"

The student who masters these exercises will find that his understanding of the law has deepened immeasurably. For the sage who has never argued the opposing side understands only his own position; the sage who has argued both sides understands the entire structure of the problem. And this understanding is not merely intellectual -- it is also pastoral, for the sage who can see both sides of a dispute can mediate between the disputants with fairness and wisdom, knowing the legitimate concerns that motivate each party.

---

### VIII.B. Suasoriae (Eitzah)

The rabbi explains: The second advanced exercise is the *suasoria* -- the deliberative speech placed in the mouth of a historical or biblical figure. In this exercise, the student imagines himself standing at a critical moment in sacred history and composes the speech that a particular figure might have given.

"Imagine yourself as Moses at the sea. The Egyptians are behind you. The sea is before you. The people are weeping. What do you say to them? Now imagine yourself as Pharaoh pursuing. Your army is behind you. The sea has parted before the Israelites. What do you say to your commanders?"[^380]

This exercise combines two skills: *ethopoeia* (speech-in-character, which the student practiced in the intermediate exercises) and *deliberative rhetoric* (the speech that urges a course of action). The student must inhabit the character -- understanding his motivations, his fears, his knowledge, his ignorance -- and from within that character, compose a speech that is both persuasive and appropriate to the situation.

Consider the following exercises:

**What counsel would you give Abraham before the binding of Isaac?** The student must imagine a conversation between Abraham and a trusted advisor (or between Abraham and his own conscience). The advisor knows what has been commanded -- "Take your son, your only one, whom you love, Isaac, and go to the land of Moriah, and offer him there as a burnt offering" (Genesis 22:2). What can the advisor say? He cannot overrule the divine command. But he might urge Abraham to seek clarification, to request a substitute, to plead for mercy as Abraham pleaded for Sodom. The student who composes this speech must grapple with the deepest questions of faith and obedience -- and he must do so not abstractly but dramatically, in the voice of a character who is facing the crisis in real time.

**Compose the speech Moses might have given to the officers before entering the Land.** Moses knows that he himself will not cross the Jordan. He must prepare his officers -- Joshua, the tribal leaders, the judges -- for a transition of leadership. What does he emphasize? What does he warn against? What does he promise? The student who composes this speech draws on Deuteronomy, on the narrative of Numbers, on the traditions about Moses's final days -- and from all of this material, he constructs a speech that is both historically plausible and rhetorically effective.[^381]

**What did Jeremiah say when urged to flee Jerusalem?** The tradition holds that Jeremiah remained in Jerusalem during the Babylonian siege, enduring persecution and imprisonment rather than abandoning the city and its people. The student imagines the scene: Jeremiah's friends urge him to escape while there is still time. Jeremiah refuses. What does he say? The student must channel Jeremiah's prophetic conviction, his love for the people, his grief over the coming destruction, and his refusal to abandon his post -- all in the form of a speech that could persuade his friends to understand, if not to agree.

**The speech Rabbi Yohanan ben Zakkai might have given to the zealots.** Before his escape from Jerusalem in a coffin (BT Gittin 56a-b), Rabbi Yohanan attempted to persuade the zealots to negotiate with Rome. He failed. But the student is asked: compose the speech he *might* have given. What arguments could he have used? He might have appealed to precedent (Jeremiah counseled submission to Babylonia). He might have appealed to pragmatism (the Roman legions are invincible). He might have appealed to the sanctity of life (is it better to fight and die, or to surrender and live to study Torah?). He might have appealed to the lesson of the fox and the fish -- that Torah is our element, and survival is the prerequisite for Torah.[^382]

The historical suasoria has roots in the practice of the rabbinic academies themselves. The Talmud records occasions when the sages debated what they would have done in historical situations: "Had you been in the Sanhedrin that condemned..." This is a suasoria in all but name: the sage places himself in the position of a historical figure and composes the argument he would have made.[^383]

The student should note that midrashic literature is, in large measure, a collection of suasoriae. When the midrash puts words in the mouths of biblical figures -- when it has Abraham argue with God before the destruction of Sodom, when it has Moses plead with God to forgive Israel after the sin of the Golden Calf, when it has the angels protest the giving of the Torah to mortals -- it is performing exactly the exercise of the suasoria: composing deliberative speeches for historical (or in this case, sacred) figures at moments of crisis. The student who studies these midrashic speeches is studying model suasoriae, and the student who composes his own is practicing the same art that produced some of the most memorable passages in rabbinic literature.

Consider the famous passage in which Moses ascends to heaven to receive the Torah and is challenged by the angels:

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו מלאכי השרת לפני הקדוש ברוך הוא: רבונו של עולם, מה לילוד אשה בינינו? אמר להם: לקבל תורה בא. אמרו לפניו: חמדה גנוזה... אתה מבקש ליתנה לבשר ודם?</span>
>
> *The ministering angels said before the Holy One, blessed be He: Master of the Universe, what is one born of woman doing among us? He said to them: He has come to receive the Torah. They said before Him: The precious treasure that has been hidden... You wish to give it to flesh and blood?* (BT Shabbat 88b)[^384]

God then instructs Moses to respond to the angels himself. And what does Moses say? He composes a deliberative speech -- a suasoria -- arguing that the Torah is relevant only to earthly creatures: "Is there jealousy among you? Is there an evil inclination among you? Do you engage in commerce? Do you have parents to honor?" Each argument is drawn from the content of the Torah itself, and each demolishes the angels' claim. This is a model suasoria: a speech composed for a specific figure (Moses) at a specific moment (the giving of the Torah) that employs the tools of deliberative rhetoric (argument from relevance, argument from consequence) to persuade a hostile audience (the angels) to accept a particular course of action (the giving of the Torah to Israel).

The student who can compose such speeches -- inhabiting the character, understanding the stakes, marshaling the arguments, and delivering the speech with the urgency that the moment demands -- has mastered the art of the suasoria.

The exercise for the student: "Compose the speech Joshua might have given to the people at Shechem (Joshua 24), using all the techniques you have learned: scriptural proof, historical narrative, parable, argument from consequence, appeal to emotion, and clear arrangement. Let the speech be no longer than one can deliver in the time it takes to recite the Amidah."

A second exercise, drawn from the narrative of the Scroll of Esther: "Compose the speech Esther might have given to Mordecai when she first received his message to approach the king uninvited (Esther 4). She knows that approaching the king without being summoned is punishable by death. Compose two speeches: first, the speech of refusal -- why she should not go. Then, the speech of resolve -- why she must go, despite the danger. Conclude with the speech she actually gives to the people: 'Go, gather all the Jews... and fast for me.'"

A third exercise: "Imagine that you are Rabbi Akiva, and it is the day of your execution by the Romans. Your students surround you. You know this is the end. Compose the final teaching you would give -- not a lament, but a teaching about the meaning of the Shema, the meaning of loving God 'with all your soul,' and the meaning of a life spent in Torah. Draw on the tradition recorded in BT Berakhot 61b, but expand it into a full speech."

---

### VIII.C. The Sugya as Advanced Declamation

The rabbi explains: My son, we come now to the final exercise -- the one that combines everything you have learned. This is the *sugya*, the Talmudic dialectical unit, and it is the crowning achievement of rabbinic rhetoric.

The sugya is not merely a legal discussion. It is a dramatic performance, a multi-voiced dialogue, a dialectical engine that tests every proposition from every angle until it has been either confirmed or destroyed. It combines narrative, argument, refutation, confirmation, figure, trope, memory, and delivery. The student who can navigate a sugya has mastered every rhetorical skill we have taught.

> <span dir="rtl">הסוגיא -- זו היא כל התורה כולה</span>

The anatomy of a sugya follows a pattern that the student must internalize:

**First: Citation of a teaching.** The sugya opens with a quotation from the Mishnah (*matnitin*) or from a baraita. This is the thesis -- the proposition that will be examined, tested, and refined. For example, the opening of the Babylonian Talmud:

> <span dir="rtl">מאימתי קורין את שמע בערבית? משעה שהכהנים נכנסים לאכול בתרומתן</span>
>
> *From what time may one recite the Shema in the evening? From the time that the priests enter to eat their terumah.* (Mishnah Berakhot 1:1)[^385]

**Second: Questioning.** The Talmud asks: *Mai ta'ama?* -- "What is the reason?" Or: *Mena hani milli?* -- "From where do we derive this?" Or simply: *Mai?* -- "What [does this mean]?" The question activates the dialectic. The teaching has been stated; now it must be explained, justified, and defended.

**Third: Proof and counter-proof.** A proof is offered -- from Scripture, from logic, from another teaching. Then a counter-proof is offered: "But it was taught..." (*ve-ha tanya...*) or "But it says..." (*ve-ha ketiv...*). The dialectic begins to move: thesis, antithesis, synthesis.

**Fourth: Objection and resolution (*kushya u-terutz*).** An objection is raised: *Meitivei* -- "They objected!" A contradiction is found between the teaching under discussion and another teaching. The contradiction must be resolved. The resolution may involve distinguishing between the two cases (*ha be-X, ha be-Y* -- "this refers to case X, that to case Y"), or reinterpreting one of the teachings, or emending the text, or identifying a dispute between tannaitic authorities.

**Fifth: Tangential exploration and return.** The sugya often departs on a tangent -- an aggadic narrative, a biographical anecdote, a theological reflection -- before returning to the main thread. These tangents are not digressions in the pejorative sense. They are explorations of the human, ethical, or spiritual dimensions of the legal question. They ensure that the legal analysis does not become arid -- that the student sees the law not as an abstraction but as a living reality with human stakes. Consider: in the middle of a discussion about the laws of damages, the Talmud may pause to tell the story of a sage who was wronged and how he responded. The story is not "off-topic" -- it illuminates the human reality that the abstract law is designed to govern. The student who has learned to read these tangents as integral parts of the sugya, rather than as interruptions, has understood something profound about the nature of rabbinic discourse: that law and narrative, halakhah and aggadah, are not separate genres but intertwined dimensions of a single reality.

**Sixth: Final resolution or standing disagreement.** The sugya concludes either with a resolution (*u-she-ma'ata hi* -- "and this is the law") or with a standing disagreement (*teiku* -- the matter stands unresolved until Elijah comes). The ability to reach resolution is the mark of a strong sugya; the willingness to leave a question unresolved is the mark of an honest one.[^386]

This structure is more complex than anything in the Greco-Roman declamation tradition. The Roman *controversia* is dyadic: two sides argue. The *suasoria* is monadic: one speaker urges. But the sugya is polyphonic: multiple voices speak across multiple generations, the living debating the dead, the Babylonian sages arguing with the Palestinian, the Amoraic masters questioning the Tannaitic, and all of them held together by the editorial voice of the *stam* -- the anonymous redactorial layer that shapes the discussion into a coherent whole.

The sugya is dialectical, multi-voiced, and open-ended. It does not merely argue; it dramatizes the process of argument itself. The student who reads a sugya does not merely learn the conclusion; he experiences the struggle by which the conclusion was reached -- the false starts, the brilliant intuitions, the crushing objections, the elegant resolutions. This is rhetoric as pedagogy: the sugya teaches not by telling but by enacting, not by presenting conclusions but by reliving the process of deliberation.

Consider, as an example, the opening sugya of BT Berakhot 2a. The Mishnah states:

> <span dir="rtl">מאימתי קורין את שמע בערבית? משעה שהכהנים נכנסין לאכול בתרומתן</span>
>
> *From what time may one recite the Shema in the evening? From the time that the priests enter to eat their terumah.*

The Talmud immediately asks: Why does the Mishnah begin with the evening rather than the morning? This is a question about *arrangement* -- why this order and not another? The Gemara answers by citing the scriptural verse: "And you shall speak of them... when you lie down and when you rise up" (Deuteronomy 6:7). Since "lying down" (evening) precedes "rising up" (morning) in the verse, the Mishnah follows the scriptural order. This answer demonstrates *invention* -- the argument is discovered by examining the source verse.

But the Talmud is not satisfied. It presses further: if the Mishnah follows the order of the verse, should the tanna not have taught the morning Shema first and then the evening, following the common principle that one begins with the more frequent obligation? This is an *objection* (*kushya*), and it demands a *resolution* (*terutz*). The resolution returns to the scriptural verse and demonstrates that the Torah's own arrangement takes precedence over the logical principle.

And then another question: why does the Mishnah define the evening time by reference to the priests eating their terumah? Why not simply say "from nightfall"? This question opens a new avenue of inquiry -- into the relationship between the priestly schedule and the layperson's obligation, into the nature of sunset and twilight, into the halakhic definition of the boundary between day and night. Each question generates subsidiary questions, each answer opens new lines of investigation, and the entire sugya expands outward from its simple starting point like ripples from a stone dropped in water.

The student should observe how every rhetorical skill is employed in this single sugya: *invention* (finding scriptural proofs, constructing logical arguments), *arrangement* (ordering the questions in a sequence that builds understanding), *style* (the shift between the terse formulations of the Mishnah and the expansive discourse of the Gemara, between halakhic precision and aggadic elaboration), *memory* (the citations that leap across tractates and across centuries, connecting Berakhot to Leviticus to Deuteronomy to Mishnah Negaim), and *delivery* (the dramatic rhythm of question and answer, objection and resolution, that gives the sugya its propulsive energy and its intellectual suspense).

The student who can analyze a sugya in this way -- identifying each rhetorical move, naming the technique it employs, and understanding why that technique is deployed at that particular moment -- has achieved a level of rhetorical consciousness that will transform his own capacity for discourse. He will begin to see the sugya not as a chaotic jumble of questions and answers but as a carefully constructed rhetorical performance, as artful in its way as the finest Greco-Roman oration -- and, in its polyphonic complexity, surpassing anything that the classical tradition produced.

And the exercise for the student is this: take a short sugya -- no more than half a folio page -- and analyze every rhetorical move. Mark each citation, each question, each objection, each resolution. Identify the invention (where do the proofs come from?), the arrangement (why are the questions in this order?), the style (how does the language shift between the different voices?), the memory (what texts are cited, and from how far away in the corpus?), and the delivery (how would this passage sound when recited aloud, with the rhythm of question and answer?). Then attempt to construct a sugya of your own on a different legal question, following the same pattern: citation of the mishnah, question, proof, objection, resolution, tangent, and final determination. This is the most advanced exercise in the entire curriculum, and the student who can perform it creditably has truly earned his place among the students of the sages.[^387]

There is one further dimension of the sugya that the student should appreciate: its *editorial voice*. The sugya is not merely a transcript of discussions that actually took place. It is a literary construction, shaped by the anonymous editors (*stammaim*) of the Talmud, who selected, arranged, and connected the individual traditions into a coherent dialectical unit. The editorial voice -- the *stam* -- is itself a rhetorical achievement of the highest order. It is the voice that asks the questions ("What is the reason?"), that introduces the objections ("But it was taught..."), that proposes the resolutions ("No, it refers to a different case"), and that sometimes steps back to offer a meta-commentary on the discussion itself ("And they disagree about the following principle...").

The stam is, in effect, the master teacher of the Talmud. He does not merely report what the Amoraim said; he stages their words in a dramatic sequence that maximizes their educational impact. He knows which objection to raise first, which resolution will create the most productive sequence of further questions, and when to introduce an aggadic tangent that will illuminate the human dimensions of the legal problem. The student who reads the sugya attentively will notice the stam's hand everywhere -- shaping the discussion, guiding the reader, building toward a conclusion that feels inevitable even though it was, in fact, carefully constructed.

This editorial art is itself a lesson in rhetoric. The student who understands how the stam constructs a sugya has learned something about the art of arrangement that no abstract principle can teach: he has seen, in practice, how a master arranger takes raw materials (individual traditions, isolated teachings, fragmented discussions) and weaves them into a coherent, progressive, intellectually satisfying whole. The sugya is thus not only the culmination of rhetorical training for the student; it is also the greatest example of rhetorical artistry in the entire rabbinic corpus.

The rabbi concludes: "The student who has mastered all these exercises -- from the simplest fable to the most complex sugya -- is ready to take his place among the sages. He has learned to speak with precision and with force. He has learned to argue both sides and to hold competing truths in balance. He has learned to tell a story that teaches, to compose a parable that illuminates, to deliver a speech that moves. He has trained his memory to hold the tradition and his voice to transmit it."

> <span dir="rtl">יהי רצון שתהא תורה שפה על פיך ועל פי זרעך ועל פי זרע זרעך מעתה ועד עולם</span>
>
> *May it be [God's] will that Torah be fluent on your lips, and on the lips of your children, and on the lips of your children's children, from now until eternity.* (Based on BT Berakhot 35b)[^388]

The chain continues. What Moses received at Sinai, he transmitted to Joshua; Joshua to the elders; the elders to the prophets; the prophets to the men of the Great Assembly; and they to us, and we to our students, and our students to theirs. The art of speech in the bet midrash is not an end in itself; it is the mechanism by which this chain is maintained. Every sage who masters the art of rhetoric -- of ordered speech, of compelling argument, of vivid teaching, of faithful transmission -- is a link in the chain. And every student who sits at his feet, who absorbs his teaching, who practices the exercises, who develops his faculties, and who one day rises to teach in his own right -- that student becomes the next link.

Go, my son. Take your place in the bet midrash. Open your mouth, and let the Torah speak through you.

And know this: the exercises we have taught in this manual are not completed when the manual is closed. They are completed when they are practiced -- daily, weekly, over years of disciplined training. The fable is mastered not when the student can define it but when he can compose one that makes his audience weep or laugh. The narrative is mastered not when the student can identify its six elements but when he can tell a story that establishes a precedent and changes the law. The chreia is mastered not when the student can classify its types but when he can compress a lifetime of wisdom into a single memorable utterance. The machloqet is mastered not when the student can state both sides but when he can argue both sides so persuasively that even his teacher cannot tell which is his own position. The sugya is mastered not when the student can follow its dialectic but when he can construct one himself -- weaving together texts, arguments, objections, and resolutions into a coherent whole that illuminates the law and enriches the tradition.

The sage Hillel taught:

> <span dir="rtl">אם לא עכשיו, אימתי?</span>
>
> *If not now, when?* (Avot 1:14)

Begin now. Take the first exercise -- the mashal -- and practice it. Tomorrow, take the second. In a week, return to the first and practice it again, more skillfully. In a month, combine the first and second. In a year, you will have passed through all the exercises and will be ready to begin them again at a higher level. For the art of speech, like the study of Torah itself, is infinite: one can always go deeper, always reach higher, always refine further.

And let the student remember: the purpose of all this training is not virtuosity for its own sake. It is the service of Heaven. Every technique we have taught -- every tool of invention, every principle of arrangement, every device of style, every method of memory, every art of delivery -- exists for one purpose: to transmit Torah from generation to generation, to make the word of God heard in the world, to build and sustain the community of Israel through the power of ordered, truthful, compelling speech.

> <span dir="rtl">והיו הדברים האלה אשר אנכי מצוך היום על לבבך, ושננתם לבניך ודברת בם</span>
>
> *And these words which I command you this day shall be upon your heart, and you shall teach them diligently to your children, and you shall speak of them.* (Deuteronomy 6:6-7)

*Speak of them.* This is the commandment that encompasses all others. And the sage who speaks well -- who speaks with precision, with passion, with clarity, with beauty, with faithfulness to the tradition and sensitivity to the listener -- that sage has fulfilled the deepest obligation of the Torah: to make its words live in the mouths and hearts of every generation.

---

## BACK MATTER

### Glossary of Rhetorical Terms

The following glossary matches Hebrew and Aramaic terms used in the bet midrash to their Greek and Latin equivalents in the rhetorical tradition. The correspondences are not always exact -- the cultural contexts differ, and no translation captures every nuance -- but the structural parallels are genuine and illuminating. The glossary is organized to follow the structure of the manual: foundational terms appear first, followed by terms related to the preliminary exercises, the five faculties, and the advanced exercises.

The student who consults this glossary will find that many rabbinic concepts have close Greek or Latin equivalents, while others have only approximate parallels, and a few have no parallel at all. Where the rabbinic tradition has a concept without a Greek equivalent (such as the *meturgeman* or the *teiku*), this indicates a distinctive feature of rabbinic rhetoric. Where the Greek tradition has a concept without a rabbinic equivalent, this may indicate a gap in our surviving sources rather than a genuine absence from rabbinic practice. The glossary is offered as a tool for comparison, not as a claim of identity.

| Hebrew/Aramaic Term | Transliteration | Greek/Latin Equivalent |
|---|---|---|
| <span dir="rtl">חכמת הלשון הסדורה</span> | Hokhmat ha-lashon ha-sedurah | Rhetorike (rhetoric) |
| <span dir="rtl">המצאה</span> | Hamtza'ah | Heuresis / Inventio (invention) |
| <span dir="rtl">סדר</span> | Seder | Taxis / Dispositio (arrangement) |
| <span dir="rtl">לשון</span> | Lashon | Lexis / Elocutio (style) |
| <span dir="rtl">זכרון</span> | Zikaron | Mneme / Memoria (memory) |
| <span dir="rtl">נעימה</span> | Ne'imah | Hypokrisis / Pronuntiatio (delivery) |
| <span dir="rtl">משל</span> | Mashal | Mythos / Fabula (fable); Parabole (parable) |
| <span dir="rtl">נמשל</span> | Nimshal | Epimythion (moral/application of fable) |
| <span dir="rtl">מעשה</span> | Ma'aseh | Diegema / Narratio (narrative) |
| <span dir="rtl">מאמר חכם</span> | Ma'amar hakham | Chreia (anecdote of a sage) |
| <span dir="rtl">פתגם</span> | Pitgam | Gnome / Sententia (maxim) |
| <span dir="rtl">הספד</span> | Hesped | Enkomion / Laudatio funebris (eulogy/encomium) |
| <span dir="rtl">שבח</span> | Shevah | Epainos / Laus (praise) |
| <span dir="rtl">גנאי</span> | Genai | Psogos / Vituperatio (blame/invective) |
| <span dir="rtl">דין</span> | Din | Dikanikon genos / Genus iudiciale (judicial rhetoric) |
| <span dir="rtl">עצה</span> | Eitzah | Symbouleutikon genos / Genus deliberativum (deliberative rhetoric) |
| <span dir="rtl">דרשה</span> | Derashah | Epideiktikon genos / Genus demonstrativum (epideictic rhetoric) |
| <span dir="rtl">מדות</span> | Middot | Topoi / Loci (topics; places of argument) |
| <span dir="rtl">קל וחומר</span> | Qal va-homer | A fortiori (argument from lesser to greater) |
| <span dir="rtl">גזירה שוה</span> | Gezerah shavah | Analogia / Verbal analogy |
| <span dir="rtl">בנין אב</span> | Binyan av | Paradigma / Exemplum (prototype argument) |
| <span dir="rtl">כלל ופרט</span> | Kelal u-ferat | Genus et species (general and particular) |
| <span dir="rtl">סתירה</span> | Setirah | Antilogia / Contradictio (contradiction) |
| <span dir="rtl">קושיא</span> | Kushya | Aporema / Obiectio (objection/difficulty) |
| <span dir="rtl">תירוץ</span> | Terutz | Lysis / Solutio (resolution/answer) |
| <span dir="rtl">מחלוקת</span> | Machloqet | Controversia (legal dispute argued on both sides) |
| <span dir="rtl">עצה</span> | Eitzah (as exercise) | Suasoria (deliberative speech exercise) |
| <span dir="rtl">סוגיא</span> | Sugya | Declamatio / Dialectical disputation |
| <span dir="rtl">פתיחתא</span> | Petihta | Prooimion / Exordium (proem/introduction) |
| <span dir="rtl">תוכחה</span> | Tokhahah | Psogos / Vituperatio (rebuke/invective) |
| <span dir="rtl">מליצה</span> | Melitzah | Deinotes / Grandiloquentia (elevated language) |
| <span dir="rtl">לשון נקיה</span> | Lashon neqiyah | Eufemismos / Euphemismus (euphemism) |
| <span dir="rtl">דרך קצרה</span> | Derekh qetzarah | Brevitas / Syntomia (conciseness) |
| <span dir="rtl">סימן</span> | Siman | Mnemonikon / Nota (mnemonic sign) |
| <span dir="rtl">תנא</span> | Tanna (reciter) | Mnemon (professional memorizer) |
| <span dir="rtl">אמורא</span> | Amora | Exegetes / Interpres (interpreter/lecturer) |
| <span dir="rtl">מתורגמן</span> | Meturgeman | Hermeneutes / Interpres (translator/amplifier) |
| <span dir="rtl">דרשן</span> | Darshan | Rhetor / Orator (preacher/public speaker) |
| <span dir="rtl">טעמי המקרא</span> | Ta'amei ha-miqra | Prosoidia / Accentus (cantillation/delivery notation) |
| <span dir="rtl">שינון</span> | Shinnun | Melete / Exercitatio (repetitive practice) |
| <span dir="rtl">גרסא</span> | Girsa | Anagnosis / Lectio (recitation/reading aloud) |
| <span dir="rtl">שימוש</span> | Shimush | Askesis / Tirocinium (apprenticeship) |
| <span dir="rtl">חבר</span> | Haver | Synasketes / Condiscipulus (study partner) |
| <span dir="rtl">בית מדרש</span> | Bet midrash | Scholē / Ludus (school of rhetoric/academy) |
| <span dir="rtl">שלשלת הקבלה</span> | Shalshelet ha-qabbalah | Diadoche / Successio (chain of tradition/succession) |
| <span dir="rtl">בית דין</span> | Bet din | Dikasterion / Tribunal (court of law) |
| <span dir="rtl">עדות</span> | Edut | Martyria / Testimonium (testimony) |
| <span dir="rtl">בדיקה וחקירה</span> | Bediqah va-haqirah | Exetasis / Quaestio (examination of witnesses) |
| <span dir="rtl">הסברה</span> | Hasbara | Exegesis / Explanatio (explanation) |
| <span dir="rtl">אסמכתא</span> | Asmakhta | Kataskeuē / Confirmatio (confirmation/proof-text) |
| <span dir="rtl">פירכא</span> | Pirka | Anaskeuē / Refutatio (refutation) |
| <span dir="rtl">הלכה ואגדה</span> | Halakhah ve-aggadah | Logos / Mythos (law and narrative; reason and story) |
| <span dir="rtl">תיקו</span> | Teiku | Aporia (unresolved question) |
| <span dir="rtl">הפוך בה</span> | Hafokh bah | In utramque partem (arguing both sides) |
| <span dir="rtl">הרגלות קודמות</span> | Hargilot qodmot | Progymnasmata (preliminary exercises) |
| <span dir="rtl">חזרה</span> | Hazarah | Repetitio / Anaphora (review/repetition) |
| <span dir="rtl">ירחי כלה</span> | Yarhe kallah | Scholae (months of assembly for review) |
| <span dir="rtl">נוטריקון</span> | Notarikon | Notarikon / Acrostichis (acronymic mnemonic) |
| <span dir="rtl">רמז</span> | Remez | Allegoria / Hint (allusive interpretation) |
| <span dir="rtl">סתם</span> | Stam | Redactor / Editor (anonymous editorial voice) |
| <span dir="rtl">ברייתא</span> | Baraita | Extra-canonical teaching (tannaitic tradition outside the Mishnah) |
| <span dir="rtl">מתני׳</span> | Matnitin | Thesis / Propositum (the Mishnah citation that opens a sugya) |
| <span dir="rtl">מאי טעמא</span> | Mai ta'ama | Quaestio / Aitia (the question "What is the reason?") |
| <span dir="rtl">תא שמע</span> | Ta shma | Audi / Proof-text formula ("Come and hear") |
| <span dir="rtl">הוה אמינא</span> | Haveh amina | Prima facie / Initial assumption |
| <span dir="rtl">אתיא</span> | Atya | Derivation / Deduction (a legal derivation from Scripture) |

---

### Bibliography

The following bibliography lists the primary and secondary sources cited in this reconstruction. Primary sources are divided into rabbinic and rhetorical categories. Secondary scholarship is listed alphabetically by author. Full bibliographic details are provided at first citation; subsequent references use short titles. For rabbinic texts, the standard editions used throughout are indicated; the reader is referred to critical editions for philological precision. For rhetorical texts, both the original language editions and the English translations used are indicated.

#### Primary Rabbinic Sources

- **Mishnah.** Kaufmann MS (Budapest, Library of the Hungarian Academy of Sciences, MS A 50); H. Albeck, ed., *Shishah Sidrei Mishnah*, 6 vols. (Jerusalem: Bialik Institute and Dvir, 1952-1959). All tractates cited in this work are referenced according to the Albeck edition with variants noted from the Kaufmann MS.

- **Tosefta.** S. Lieberman, ed., *The Tosefta: According to Codex Vienna, with Variants from Codex Erfurt, Genizah Manuscripts, and Editio Princeps (Venice 1521)*, 4 vols. (New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1955-1988). For tractates not covered by Lieberman, the Zuckermandel edition (Pasewalk, 1881; repr. Jerusalem, 1963) is used.

- **Avot de-Rabbi Natan.** S. Schechter, ed., *Aboth de Rabbi Nathan* (Vienna: Ch. D. Lippe, 1887; repr. New York: Feldheim, 1967). Both Version A and Version B are cited; version is indicated at each reference.

- **Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael.** H. S. Horovitz and I. A. Rabin, eds., *Mechilta d'Rabbi Ismael* (Frankfurt am Main: J. Kauffmann, 1931; repr. Jerusalem: Wahrmann, 1970). English translation: J. Z. Lauterbach, *Mekhilta de-Rabbi Ishmael*, 3 vols. (Philadelphia: Jewish Publication Society, 1933-1935; repr. 2004).

- **Sifra (Torat Kohanim).** I. H. Weiss, ed., *Sifra de-vei Rav* (Vienna: Schlossberg, 1862; repr. New York, 1946). The Baraita de-Rabbi Ishmael, which opens the Sifra, is cited frequently in connection with the hermeneutical middot.

- **Sifrei on Numbers.** H. S. Horovitz, ed., *Siphre ad Numeros adjecto Siphre zutta* (Leipzig: Gustav Fock, 1917; repr. Jerusalem: Wahrmann, 1966).

- **Sifrei on Deuteronomy.** L. Finkelstein, ed., *Siphre ad Deuteronomium* (Berlin: Jüdischer Kulturbund, 1939; repr. New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1969).

- **Babylonian Talmud (Talmud Bavli).** Vilna edition (Romm, 1880-1886), the standard printed edition. Reference is also made to A. Steinsaltz, ed., *Talmud Bavli* (Jerusalem: Israel Institute for Talmudic Publications, 1967-2010), for its Hebrew elucidation; and to the Artscroll/Schottenstein edition (Brooklyn: Mesorah, 1990-2004), for its English translation and commentary. Critical editions of individual tractates by the Institute for the Complete Israeli Talmud (Yad ha-Rav Herzog) are consulted where available.

- **Jerusalem Talmud (Talmud Yerushalmi).** Venice edition (Daniel Bomberg, 1523-1524); Leiden MS (Or. 4720, Universiteitsbibliotheek Leiden), the only complete manuscript of the Yerushalmi. Reference is also made to the Synopse edition of P. Schäfer and H.-J. Becker (Tübingen: Mohr Siebeck, 1991-2001).

- **Genesis Rabbah (Bereshit Rabbah).** J. Theodor and C. Albeck, eds., *Midrash Bereshit Rabba: Critical Edition with Notes and Commentary*, 3 vols. (Berlin: Akademie-Verlag, 1903-1936; repr. Jerusalem: Wahrmann, 1965).

- **Leviticus Rabbah (Vayiqra Rabbah).** M. Margulies, ed., *Midrash Wayyikra Rabbah: A Critical Edition Based on Manuscripts and Genizah Fragments*, 5 vols. (Jerusalem: American Academy for Jewish Research, 1953-1960).

- **Lamentations Rabbah (Eikhah Rabbah).** S. Buber, ed., *Midrasch Echa Rabbati* (Vilna: Romm, 1899; repr. Hildesheim: Georg Olms, 1967).

- **Pesiqta de-Rav Kahana.** B. Mandelbaum, ed., *Pesikta de Rav Kahana: According to an Oxford Manuscript*, 2 vols. (New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1962; 2nd ed. 1987).

- **Tanhuma / Yelamdenu.** S. Buber, ed., *Midrasch Tanchuma* (Vilna: Romm, 1885; repr. Jerusalem, 1964). The "standard" (non-Buber) edition is also consulted for variants.

#### Primary Rhetorical Sources

- **Aristotle.** *Rhetoric.* Trans. George A. Kennedy, *On Rhetoric: A Theory of Civic Discourse*, 2nd ed. (New York: Oxford University Press, 2007). Greek text: W. D. Ross, ed., *Aristotelis Ars Rhetorica* (Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1959).

- **Cicero, Marcus Tullius.** *De Inventione.* Trans. H. M. Hubbell, Loeb Classical Library 386 (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1949). *De Oratore.* Trans. E. W. Sutton and H. Rackham, Loeb Classical Library 348-349, 2 vols. (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1942). *Orator.* Trans. H. M. Hubbell, Loeb Classical Library 342 (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1939).

- **Rhetorica ad Herennium.** Trans. H. Caplan, Loeb Classical Library 403 (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1954). Formerly attributed to Cicero; now considered anonymous, composed ca. 86-82 BCE.

- **Quintilian (Marcus Fabius Quintilianus).** *Institutio Oratoria.* Trans. D. A. Russell, Loeb Classical Library, 5 vols. (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 2001). The standard work on Roman rhetorical education; Books 1-2 on the education of the orator, Book 11 on memory and delivery.

- **Seneca the Elder (Lucius Annaeus Seneca).** *Controversiae* and *Suasoriae.* Trans. M. Winterbottom, Loeb Classical Library 463-464, 2 vols. (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1974). The primary source for Roman declamation exercises.

- **Theon of Alexandria (Aelius Theon).** *Progymnasmata.* Greek text with English translation in R. F. Hock and E. N. O'Neil, *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric, Vol. I: The Progymnasmata*, Society of Biblical Literature Texts and Translations 27, Graeco-Roman Religion Series 9 (Atlanta: Scholars Press, 1986). The earliest surviving progymnasmata handbook (first century CE).

- **Hermogenes of Tarsus.** *Progymnasmata.* Greek text and translation in C. S. Baldwin, *Medieval Rhetoric and Poetic* (New York: Macmillan, 1928), and in Kennedy (below). Hermogenes' handbook (second century CE) is more concise than Theon's and became the standard school text in the later Roman Empire.

- **Aphthonius of Antioch.** *Progymnasmata.* Greek text and translation in R. F. Hock and E. N. O'Neil, *The Chreia and Ancient Rhetoric: Classroom Exercises*, Society of Biblical Literature Writings from the Greco-Roman World 2 (Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 2002). Aphthonius' handbook (fourth century CE) includes model compositions for each exercise and became the most widely used progymnasmata text in the Byzantine and Renaissance periods.

- **Nicolaus of Myra.** *Progymnasmata.* Greek text in J. Felten, ed., *Nicolai Progymnasmata*, Rhetores Graeci 11 (Leipzig: Teubner, 1913). Translation in G. A. Kennedy, *Progymnasmata: Greek Textbooks of Prose Composition and Rhetoric* (Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 2003). The most detailed surviving progymnasmata handbook (fifth century CE).

- **Kennedy, George A.** *Progymnasmata: Greek Textbooks of Prose Composition and Rhetoric.* Writings from the Greco-Roman World 10. Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 2003. Contains English translations of all four major progymnasmata handbooks (Theon, Hermogenes, Aphthonius, Nicolaus).

#### Additional Reference Works

- **Hock, Ronald F., and Edward N. O'Neil.** *The Chreia in Ancient Rhetoric, Vol. I: The Progymnasmata.* Society of Biblical Literature Texts and Translations 27. Graeco-Roman Religion Series 9. Atlanta: Scholars Press, 1986. Contains the Greek text and English translation of Theon's *Progymnasmata* with extensive commentary. Indispensable for any comparative study of rabbinic and Greco-Roman pedagogical forms.

- **Hock, Ronald F., and Edward N. O'Neil.** *The Chreia and Ancient Rhetoric: Classroom Exercises.* Society of Biblical Literature Writings from the Greco-Roman World 2. Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 2002. Contains the progymnasmata of Aphthonius with model compositions, as well as additional exercises and commentary.

- **Webb, Ruth.** *Ekphrasis, Imagination, and Persuasion in Ancient Rhetorical Theory and Practice.* Farnham: Ashgate, 2009. On the rhetorical exercise of vivid description, relevant to the discussion of narrative and parable composition in Parts II-III.

- **Morgan, Teresa.** *Literate Education in the Hellenistic and Roman Worlds.* Cambridge Classical Studies. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1998. On the educational practices of the Greco-Roman world, providing context for the comparative analysis in this reconstruction.

- **Marrou, H. I.** *A History of Education in Antiquity.* Translated by George Lamb. Madison: University of Wisconsin Press, 1956. The classic survey of ancient educational institutions, from the Greek *paideia* to the Roman *ludus*, providing essential context for understanding the rhetorical school system that the progymnasmata served.

#### Secondary Scholarship

- **Alexander, Elizabeth Shanks.** "The Orality of Rabbinic Writing." In *The Cambridge Companion to the Talmud and Rabbinic Literature*, edited by Charlotte Elisheva Fonrobert and Martin S. Jaffee, 38-57. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2007.

- **Alexander, Philip S.** "Orality in Pharisaic-Rabbinic Judaism at the Turn of the Eras." In *Jesus and the Oral Gospel Tradition*, edited by Henry Wansbrough, 159-184. Journal for the Study of the New Testament Supplement Series 64. Sheffield: JSOT Press, 1990.

- **Bonner, Stanley F.** *Roman Declamation in the Late Republic and Early Empire.* Liverpool: University Press of Liverpool, 1949. Repr. Berkeley: University of California Press, 1969. The standard study of Roman declamation as educational practice, essential for understanding the *controversia* and *suasoria* as parallels to rabbinic dialectical training.

- **Boyarin, Daniel.** *Intertextuality and the Reading of Midrash.* Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1990. On the hermeneutical assumptions underlying midrashic discourse.

- **Daube, David.** "Rabbinic Methods of Interpretation and Hellenistic Rhetoric." *Hebrew Union College Annual* 22 (1949): 239-264. A foundational study arguing for direct influence of Hellenistic rhetorical method on rabbinic hermeneutics.

- **Fischel, Henry A.** "Studies in Cynicism and the Ancient Near East: The Transformation of a Chria." In *Religions in Antiquity: Essays in Memory of Erwin Ramsdell Goodenough*, edited by Jacob Neusner, 372-411. Studies in the History of Religions (Supplements to *Numen*) 14. Leiden: Brill, 1968. The pioneering study of the chreia in rabbinic literature.

- **Fischel, Henry A.** *Rabbinic Literature and Greco-Roman Philosophy: A Study of Epicurea and Rhetorica in Early Midrashic Writings.* Studia Post-Biblica 21. Leiden: Brill, 1973. Extends the analysis to include other rhetorical forms.

- **Fraade, Steven D.** "Literary Composition and Oral Performance in Early Midrashim." *Oral Tradition* 14.1 (1999): 33-51. On the interplay of written and oral dimensions in midrashic literature.

- **Haïk-Vantoura, Suzanne.** *The Music of the Bible Revealed: The Deciphering of a Millenary Notation.* Translated by Dennis Weber. Berkeley: BIBAL Press, 1991. A reconstruction of the musical significance of the cantillation accents.

- **Heinemann, Joseph.** "The Proem in the Aggadic Midrashim: A Form-Critical Study." In *Studies in Aggadah and Folk-Literature*, edited by Joseph Heinemann and Dov Noy, 100-122. Scripta Hierosolymitana 22. Jerusalem: Magnes Press, 1971. The foundational study of the *petihta* as a literary and rhetorical form.

- **Hezser, Catherine.** *The Social Structure of the Rabbinic Movement in Roman Palestine.* Texte und Studien zum antiken Judentum 66. Tübingen: Mohr Siebeck, 1997. On the institutional and social settings of rabbinic discourse.

- **Hidary, Richard.** *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric: Sophistic Education and Oratory in the Talmud and Midrash.* Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2018. The most comprehensive treatment of the intersection between rabbinic pedagogy and Greco-Roman rhetorical education. Essential for the structural parallels analyzed throughout this reconstruction.

- **Jacobson, Joshua R.** *Chanting the Hebrew Bible: The Art of Cantillation.* Philadelphia: Jewish Publication Society, 2002. The standard modern treatment of the cantillation system as a musical and rhetorical notation.

- **Jaffee, Martin S.** *Torah in the Mouth: Writing and Oral Tradition in Palestinian Judaism, 200 BCE-400 CE.* New York: Oxford University Press, 2001. On the relationship between oral and written transmission in rabbinic culture, with particular attention to mnemonic techniques.

- **Kennedy, George A.** *A New History of Classical Rhetoric.* Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1994. The standard survey of Greco-Roman rhetorical theory and practice.

- **Lieberman, Saul.** *Greek in Jewish Palestine: Studies in the Life and Manners of Jewish Palestine in the II-IV Centuries C.E.* New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1942. 2nd ed., 1965.

- **Lieberman, Saul.** *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine: Studies in the Literary Transmission, Beliefs, and Manners of Palestine in the I Century B.C.E.-IV Century C.E.* New York: Jewish Theological Seminary, 1950. 2nd ed., 1962. The foundational study of Greco-Roman cultural influence on rabbinic Palestine, including discussions of rhetoric, education, and literary forms.

- **Mack, Burton L., and Vernon K. Robbins.** *Patterns of Persuasion in the Gospels.* Sonoma, CA: Polebridge Press, 1989. Applies progymnasmata analysis to New Testament texts; useful for comparative method.

- **Moeser, Marion C.** *The Anecdote in Mark, the Classical World, and the Rabbis: A Study of Brief Stories in the Demonax, the Mishnah, and Mark 8:27-10:45.* Journal for the Study of the New Testament Supplement Series 227. Sheffield: Sheffield Academic Press, 2002. A detailed comparative study of the anecdote/chreia form across Greek, rabbinic, and early Christian literature.

- **Rubenstein, Jeffrey L.** *Talmudic Stories: Narrative Art, Composition, and Culture.* Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1999. On the literary artistry of Talmudic narratives.

- **Rubenstein, Jeffrey L.** *The Culture of the Babylonian Talmud.* Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 2003. On the social and cultural world reflected in the Bavli's narratives and redactional methods.

- **Stern, David.** *Parables in Midrash: Narrative and Exegesis in Rabbinic Literature.* Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1991. The standard study of the rabbinic *mashal*, with attention to its rhetorical functions and literary forms.

- **Yates, Frances A.** *The Art of Memory.* London: Routledge and Kegan Paul, 1966. Repr. Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 2001. The classic study of the Greco-Roman and medieval art of memory, including the method of loci and the architectural mnemonic traditions. Essential background for understanding rabbinic mnemonic techniques in comparative perspective.

- **Yeivin, Israel.** *Introduction to the Tiberian Masorah.* Translated and edited by E. J. Revell. Masoretic Studies 5. Missoula, MT: Scholars Press, 1980. The standard introduction to the Masoretic system of accents and vocalization, including the cantillation marks discussed in Part VII.

- **Epstein, J. N.** *Mevo'ot le-Sifrut ha-Tanna'im: Mishnah, Tosefta, and Halakhic Midrashim* (in Hebrew). Jerusalem: Magnes Press, 1957. The foundational study of the literary history and redaction of the Mishnah and related tannaitic compilations.

- **Albeck, Chanoch.** *Mavo la-Mishnah* (in Hebrew). Jerusalem: Bialik Institute, 1959. The standard introduction to the Mishnah, including discussions of its arrangement, language, and editorial principles.

- **Goodblatt, David.** *Rabbinic Instruction in Sasanian Babylonia.* Studia Judaica 9. Leiden: Brill, 1975. On the institutions of rabbinic education in Babylonia, including the role of the *tanna* (professional reciter) and the structure of the academies.

- **Halbertal, Moshe.** *People of the Book: Canon, Meaning, and Authority.* Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1997. On the relationship between textual interpretation and authority in rabbinic culture, with particular attention to the hermeneutical methods discussed in Part I.
