- **Saldarini, Anthony J.** *Scholastic Rabbinism: A Literary Study of the Fathers According to Rabbi Nathan.* Brown Judaic Studies 14. Chico, CA: Scholars Press, 1982. On Avot de-Rabbi Natan as an expansion and commentary on Pirkei Avot, relevant to the discussion of chreia exercises in Part II.

- **Kister, Menahem.** *Studies in Avot de-Rabbi Nathan: Text, Redaction, and Interpretation* (in Hebrew). Jerusalem: Yad Izhak Ben Zvi, 1998. The most detailed study of the textual history of Avot de-Rabbi Natan.

- **Boyarin, Daniel.** *Dying for God: Martyrdom and the Making of Christianity and Judaism.* Stanford: Stanford University Press, 1999. On the martyrdom narratives of the rabbis, including Rabbi Akiva's death as a chreia and as a model of speech-in-character.

- **Kelber, Werner H.** *The Oral and the Written Gospel: The Hermeneutics of Speaking and Writing in the Synoptic Tradition, Mark, Paul, and Q.* Philadelphia: Fortress Press, 1983. On oral tradition and its literary deposit, with comparative insights relevant to rabbinic oral pedagogy.

- **Saenger, Paul.** *Space Between Words: The Origins of Silent Reading.* Stanford: Stanford University Press, 1997. On the ancient practice of reading aloud and the gradual transition to silent reading, relevant to the discussion of *girsa* (audible recitation) in Part VI.

- **Neusner, Jacob.** *The Memorized Torah: The Mnemonic System of the Mishnah.* Brown Judaic Studies 96. Chico, CA: Scholars Press, 1985. An analysis of the Mishnah's mnemonic structures, relevant to Part VI.C on the order of the Mishnah as memory architecture.

- **Hauptman, Judith.** *Rereading the Mishnah: A New Approach to Ancient Jewish Texts.* Texte und Studien zum antiken Judentum 109. Tübingen: Mohr Siebeck, 2005. On the relationship between Mishnah and Tosefta, with attention to the use of *ma'aseh* narratives as legal precedent.

- **Green, William Scott.** "Palestinian Holy Men: Charismatic Leadership and Rabbinic Tradition." In *Aufstieg und Niedergang der römischen Welt* II.19.2, 619-647. Berlin: de Gruyter, 1979. On the *action chreia* tradition in Palestinian Judaism.

---

[^325]: On the relationship between memory and oral Torah transmission, see Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 1-22, 100-126. The concept of memory as the medium of revelation is discussed in Alexander, "Orality in Pharisaic-Rabbinic Judaism," 159-184. Compare *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.16.28: "The treasury of inventions, the custodian of all the parts of rhetoric, is memory" (*Thesaurus inventorum custos omnium partium rhetoricae memoria est*).

[^326]: Mishnah Avot 2:8. The image of the plastered cistern as metaphor for retentive memory has been compared to the wax-tablet metaphor used by Plato (*Theaetetus* 191c-d) and by the author of the *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.17.30, who compares memory to wax that receives and retains impressions. See Yates, *The Art of Memory*, 6-8.

[^327]: The distinction between natural and artificial memory is foundational to the Greco-Roman art of memory. *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.16.28-29 distinguishes *memoria naturalis* (natural memory) from *memoria artificiosa* (artificial memory) and declares that art can strengthen both. Cicero, *De Oratore* 2.86.351-354, attributes the discovery of the art of memory to the poet Simonides. Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.2.1-3, discusses the same distinction. The rabbinic parallel is the distinction between the naturally gifted student (the "plastered cistern") and the student who achieves retention through method (the student who "reviews four times").

[^328]: On the *tanna* as professional reciter, see Goodblatt, *Rabbinic Instruction in Sasanian Babylonia* (Leiden: Brill, 1975), 127-168; and Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, ch. 4. The tanna's function in the Babylonian academies is discussed in Rubenstein, *The Culture of the Babylonian Talmud*, 39-53. The formula *tanna tani qameih de-Rav* appears frequently throughout the Bavli; see, e.g., BT Shabbat 3a, BT Berakhot 36b, BT Eruvin 11a.

[^329]: BT Megillah 28b. The passage lists qualities that the sage attributes to his long life, including meticulous faithfulness in transmitting received teachings. Compare the emphasis on exact transmission in the chain of tradition (Avot 1:1) and Jaffee's analysis of "the oral-performative ideal" in *Torah in the Mouth*, ch. 5.

[^330]: The division of labor between *tanna* (reciter) and *amora* (interpreter) has structural parallels in the Greco-Roman rhetorical tradition, where the *anagnostes* (reader/reciter) was distinguished from the *rhetor* (speaker/interpreter). See Hezser, *Social Structure*, 204-218. On the relationship between memorization and interpretation in rabbinic pedagogy, see Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 100-126; and Alexander, "Orality in Pharisaic-Rabbinic Judaism," 165-178.

[^331]: BT Pesahim 68b. Rav Sheshet's blindness is noted in several Talmudic passages (e.g., BT Berakhot 58a). His thirty-day review cycle is discussed in Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 118-120, as evidence for the rigor of mnemonic discipline in the Babylonian academies. The joy he expressed upon completing his review is itself a rhetorical lesson: the sage who takes pleasure in his memory work will sustain the discipline more readily than the sage who treats it as mere drudgery.

[^332]: The imperative "make signs for the Torah" (*aseh simanim la-Torah*) is derived from BT Eruvin 21b and Shabbat 104a. On mnemonic devices in rabbinic literature, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 83-99, where he demonstrates that rabbinic mnemonic techniques parallel those described in Greco-Roman rhetorical handbooks.

[^333]: BT Pesahim 103a. The *Yaknehaz* mnemonic is one of the most widely known simanim in rabbinic literature. On the relationship between rabbinic simanim and the Greco-Roman use of mnemonic keywords (*notae*), see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, ch. 5; and Yates, *The Art of Memory*, 16-18, on the use of abbreviations and initial letters as memory aids in antiquity.

[^334]: Mishnah Avot 1:2 (Shimon ha-Tzaddik). The tripartite structure of this teaching is itself a mnemonic: the number "three" frames the content, and the three items form a memorable triad.

[^335]: Mishnah Avot 5:12. The fourfold permutation (quick/slow to learn crossed with quick/slow to forget) is a systematic mnemonic pattern that generates all possible combinations from two binary variables. This taxonomic structure is similar to the fourfold classification schemes found in Aristotle's logical works and in Quintilian's discussions of student types (*Inst. Or.* 1.3.1-5).

[^336]: On numerical lists as mnemonic architecture in Avot and in the broader Mishnaic corpus, see Alexander, "Orality in Pharisaic-Rabbinic Judaism," 170-174. The parallel to Greek and Roman use of numerical lists for pedagogical purposes is discussed in Bonner, *Roman Declamation*, 43-48, and Kennedy, *A New History of Classical Rhetoric*, 76-82.

[^337]: On catchword association (*siman*) as an organizing principle in the Mishnah and Talmud, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 83-99. The use of catchword chains in oral literature is a well-documented phenomenon across cultures; see W. Kelber, *The Oral and the Written Gospel* (Philadelphia: Fortress, 1983), 58-65, for a comparative perspective.

[^338]: On verse-based mnemonics in rabbinic literature, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 87-94. The technique of using a scriptural verse as a mnemonic framework has parallels in the Greco-Roman use of well-known poetic lines as memory hooks; see *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.21.34, on the use of familiar images and locations as memory aids.

[^339]: BT Horayot 12a. The declaration *simanah milta hi* -- that a mnemonic sign is substantive, not trivial -- validates the entire rabbinic practice of constructing mnemonic devices. On the theoretical significance of this principle, see Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 92-94.

[^340]: The six orders of the Mishnah are enumerated in BT Shabbat 31a and elsewhere. On the arrangement of the orders as a systematic pedagogical structure, see Epstein, *Mevo'ot le-Sifrut ha-Tanna'im*, 979-985; and Albeck's introductions to his edition of the Mishnah.

[^341]: On the arrangement of tractates within each order (primarily by size, with exceptions), see Epstein, *Mevo'ot le-Sifrut ha-Tanna'im*, 979-985. The principle of placing the largest tractate first is explicitly noted in the Talmud at BT Shabbat 31a and BT Bava Qamma 102a.

[^342]: The comparison between the Mishnah's six orders and the rhetorical "memory palace" (method of loci) is developed here as a heuristic parallel, not as a claim of direct borrowing. On the method of loci, see *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.17-20 (the fullest ancient description); Cicero, *De Oratore* 2.86.351-354 (the story of Simonides); Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.2.17-22 (a practical discussion of the method). On the structural parallel to the Mishnah's arrangement, see Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 110-118, who discusses the Mishnah as an "oral document" designed for mnemonic navigation.

[^343]: BT Sanhedrin 86a. This attribution of anonymous sections to specific sages demonstrates the editorial intentionality behind the rabbinic compilations -- each sage's arrangement reflects his particular pedagogical and hermeneutical principles.

[^344]: The principle of constant review is expressed in various forms: BT Berakhot 38b, BT Sanhedrin 99a, and BT Avodah Zarah 19a. The passage cited here follows the version in BT Sanhedrin 99b. Compare Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.2.40-41, on the necessity of regular review to maintain what has been committed to memory.

[^345]: BT Eruvin 54b. This passage is the locus classicus for rabbinic pedagogical method and the fourfold repetition system. The parallel to the graduated repetition recommended in *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.16-24 is noted in Lieberman, *Hellenism in Jewish Palestine*, 86-88. See also Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, ch. 4-5, for a detailed analysis of this passage and its implications for understanding rabbinic oral pedagogy.

[^346]: BT Avodah Zarah 19a. The emphasis on recitation even without comprehension reflects the priority of textual preservation over immediate understanding -- the student first secures the text in memory, then works to understand it. This two-stage process (memorization followed by analysis) parallels the Roman practice of memorizing speeches before analyzing their rhetorical techniques; see Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 2.5.1-12.

[^347]: BT Eruvin 54a. The derivation of the obligation of audible recitation from Joshua 1:8. On the cognitive benefits of oral versus silent reading, see Alexander, "Orality in Pharisaic-Rabbinic Judaism," 163-168. The ancient world's preference for reading aloud is well documented; see P. Saenger, *Space Between Words: The Origins of Silent Reading* (Stanford: Stanford University Press, 1997).

[^348]: Mishnah Avot 3:8 (or 3:10 in some editions). The severity of this teaching reflects the existential stakes of memory in an oral-transmission culture. Compare the emphasis on the dangers of forgetting in Deuteronomy 4:9, 6:12, 8:11-14, which form the scriptural foundation for the rabbinic anxiety about memory loss.

[^349]: The distinction between "rote memory" (serial recall, dependent on sequential access) and "structural memory" (organized recall, permitting random access) corresponds to the distinction in cognitive science between serial and associative memory. The rabbinic exercise of reciting in reverse order or in permuted order is designed to build structural memory. Compare *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 3.17.30-31, which emphasizes that the art of memory must permit the orator to begin at any point in his speech, not only at the beginning.

[^350]: BT Avodah Zarah 19b; cf. BT Qiddushin 30a. The tripartite division of study time is discussed in Jaffee, *Torah in the Mouth*, 68-72, and is compared to the Roman rhetorical curriculum's division among reading, composition, and declamation in Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 10.1-7.

[^351]: BT Megillah 28b; BT Niddah 73a. The hermeneutical wordplay (*al tiqrei*) -- reading *halikhot* (ways) as *halakhot* (laws) -- is itself a rhetorical device: the sage transforms a prophetic verse into a pedagogical directive through a minimal phonetic alteration. On the *al tiqrei* technique as a form of rhetorical invention, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 128-132.

[^352]: BT Ta'anit 7a; BT Makkot 10a. The principle that the teacher learns most from his students is attributed to Rabbi Hanina and reflects the rabbinic understanding of teaching as a dialogical, not monological, activity. Compare Seneca the Younger, *Epistulae Morales* 7.8: *Homines dum docent discunt* -- "People learn while they teach."

[^353]: On the public *derashah* as a form of oratory comparable to Greco-Roman epideictic speech, see Heinemann, "The Proem in the Aggadic Midrashim," 100-122; and Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 73-82. The darshan's adaptation to diverse audiences parallels Quintilian's discussion of adapting delivery to audience in *Inst. Or.* 11.1.1-46.

[^354]: BT Megillah 32a. Compare Quintilian's emphasis on the musical qualities of delivery: "For it is by the raising, lowering, and modulating of the voice that the orator stirs the emotions of his hearers" (*Inst. Or.* 11.3.14-18). The rabbinic insistence on melody in Torah reading goes beyond the Greco-Roman emphasis on vocal modulation, however, in that it prescribes a specific musical notation (the cantillation system) rather than merely general principles.

[^355]: BT Pesahim 3b. See also BT Hullin 63b. The principle of conciseness (*derekh qetzarah*) is the rabbinic equivalent of the rhetorical virtue of *brevitas*; see Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 4.2.43-44, and *Rhetorica ad Herennium* 1.9.14.

[^356]: BT Sanhedrin 38b (some editions cite this differently; the tradition about Rabbi Meir's tripartite discourse is widely attested). The three-part structure -- halakhah, aggadah, parables -- corresponds roughly to the tripartite structure of classical rhetoric's appeal to logos, pathos, and ethos. See Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 76-80.

[^357]: The principle of addressing intellect, emotion, and imagination simultaneously is discussed in Aristotle, *Rhetoric* 1.2 (the three means of persuasion: logos, pathos, ethos), and in Cicero, *De Oratore* 2.27.115: the orator must "teach, delight, and move" (*docere, delectare, movere*). Compare Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.5.2.

[^358]: On the *meturgeman* institution, see M. Weissenberg, "The Meturgeman in Rabbinic Literature," *Hebrew Union College Annual* 37 (1966): 97-132; and Fraade, "Literary Composition and Oral Performance in Early Midrashim," *Oral Tradition* 14.1 (1999): 33-51. The meturgeman's role in the public reading of Torah is discussed in Mishnah Megillah 4:4-10.

[^359]: Mishnah Megillah 4:4. The rules governing the pace of Torah reading vis-a-vis the meturgeman are designed to ensure accurate translation and effective communication. The one-verse-at-a-time rule for the Torah (vs. three verses for the Prophets) reflects the higher precision required for Pentateuchal translation.

[^360]: BT Yoma 20b; see also BT Ketubot 8b and numerous other passages where the sage addresses his meturgeman. On the sage-meturgeman partnership as a distinctive feature of rabbinic public discourse, see Fraade, "Literary Composition and Oral Performance," 40-46.

[^361]: Mishnah Megillah 4:10. The list of passages not to be publicly translated includes sexually explicit and theologically sensitive material. This principle of rhetorical discretion in public performance parallels the classical rhetorical concept of *decorum* -- the principle that certain subjects are inappropriate for certain audiences; see Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.1.1-8.

[^362]: The closest Greco-Roman analogy to the meturgeman is perhaps the rhetor-student relationship in declamation exercises, where the master assigns a theme and the student elaborates it. But the meturgeman system is distinctive in its liturgical function, its bilingual dimension, and its institutional formality. See Fraade, "Literary Composition and Oral Performance," 44-48.

[^363]: Mishnah Avot 1:4 (Yose ben Yo'ezer of Tzeredah). The physical arrangement of the bet midrash -- sage seated, students at his feet -- communicates hierarchy and transmission. Compare the description of the rhetorical school in Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 1.2.9-16, where the arrangement of the classroom is itself pedagogically significant.

[^364]: Leviticus 19:32. The obligation to rise before an elder is discussed at length in BT Qiddushin 32b-33b, where the parameters of this obligation are defined. The physical act of rising is itself a form of rhetorical communication -- it signals honor and authority.

[^365]: Based on BT Berakhot 24a and related passages on the dignified manner of the scholar's speech. Compare Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.3.45-46, on the importance of vocal control: the orator should avoid both shouting and whispering.

[^366]: BT Shabbat 114a. The hyperbolic formulation ("deserving of death") reflects the extreme importance the sages attached to the scholar's appearance as a component of his public persona. Compare Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 11.3.137-149, on the orator's dress and grooming.

[^367]: Compare Cicero, *De Oratore* 3.59.220-222, on the orator's physical bearing as a component of persuasion. Quintilian devotes *Inst. Or.* 11.3.65-136 to a detailed discussion of gesture, posture, and physical bearing in oratory.

[^368]: On the ta'amei ha-miqra as a delivery notation system, see Jacobson, *Chanting the Hebrew Bible*, 3-28. The absence of a comparable written notation in the Greco-Roman tradition is noted by Kennedy, *A New History of Classical Rhetoric*, 108-110: the Greeks and Romans discussed delivery principles extensively but never developed a score. See also Yeivin, *Introduction to the Tiberian Masorah*, 157-274, for a detailed technical analysis of the accent system.

[^369]: On the ethnachta as the verse's primary disjunctive accent and its role in creating rhetorical structure, see Jacobson, *Chanting the Hebrew Bible*, 47-68. Yeivin, *Introduction to the Tiberian Masorah*, 168-175, discusses the hierarchical system of disjunctive and conjunctive accents.

[^370]: On the musical dimension of the cantillation system, see Jacobson, *Chanting the Hebrew Bible*, 73-120, and Haïk-Vantoura, *The Music of the Bible Revealed*, passim. The musicological analysis remains debated, but the existence of a melodic system encoded in the accents is universally acknowledged.

[^371]: Deuteronomy 6:4. The cantillation of the Shema is discussed in Jacobson, *Chanting the Hebrew Bible*, 421-428. The ethnachta on "Israel" creates the dramatic bipartite structure of the verse.

[^372]: The cantillation marks as a standardized delivery system that transcends individual performance is discussed in Yeivin, *Introduction to the Tiberian Masorah*, 157-160. Compare Quintilian's frustrated acknowledgment that delivery is "the most difficult of all things to teach" (*Inst. Or.* 11.3.2) -- the rabbinic tradition solved this difficulty by encoding delivery in the text itself.

[^373]: BT Eruvin 13b. See note 6 above. On the ability to argue both sides (*in utramque partem*) as the central skill of both rabbinic and Greco-Roman rhetorical training, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, ch. 5; Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 88-92; Bonner, *Roman Declamation*, 35-46.

[^374]: BT Eruvin 13b. This principle -- that both sides of a halakhic dispute are "the words of the living God" -- has no exact Greco-Roman parallel, though the Roman practice of arguing *in utramque partem* (on both sides) reflects a similar pedagogical commitment to the value of opposing arguments. See Cicero, *De Oratore* 3.21.80; Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 2.10.1-15 and 7.1.1-8.

[^375]: On the formula *teiku* (the matter remains unresolved) as a formal acknowledgment of aporetic outcomes in rabbinic dialectic, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 198-204. The willingness to leave questions unresolved distinguishes rabbinic dialectic from the Greco-Roman controversia, which typically required a decision.

[^376]: Mishnah Berakhot 8:1-8. The disputes between Bet Shammai and Bet Hillel are the archetypal *machloqet* -- structured disagreements with clear positions, stated reasoning, and (eventually) a resolution in favor of Bet Hillel (BT Eruvin 13b).

[^377]: BT Bava Metzia 59a-b. The "Oven of Akhnai" narrative is analyzed in Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 34-63; and in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 205-220, as a case study in the limits of rhetorical authority.

[^378]: Mishnah Makkot 1:10. The debate about capital punishment as a controversia exercise is discussed in Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, ch. 5. Compare the Roman controversiae on capital cases in Seneca the Elder, *Controversiae* 1.3, 3.8, 7.1.

[^379]: On the structural identity between the rabbinic *machloqet* exercise and the Roman *controversia*, see Bonner, *Roman Declamation*, 1-26; Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, ch. 5; Seneca the Elder, *Controversiae*, preface. The comparison is also developed in Fischel, *Rabbinic Literature*, 100-118.

[^380]: The exercise of composing speeches for biblical figures parallels the *suasoria* of the Roman rhetorical schools, in which students composed speeches for mythological or historical figures at moments of crisis. See Seneca the Elder, *Suasoriae* (esp. Suas. 1: "Deliberat Alexander an Oceanum naviget"; Suas. 3: "Agamemnon deliberat an Iphigeniam immolet"). On the parallel to rabbinic speech-in-character exercises, see Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 186-198; and Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.8.1-70.

[^381]: The speeches attributed to Moses in Deuteronomy itself -- the great farewell addresses that constitute the bulk of the book -- are the biblical prototype of the suasoria. They are deliberative speeches placed in the mouth of a historical figure at a critical juncture. The midrashic tradition expanded these speeches extensively; see, e.g., the Mosaic discourses in Sifrei Deuteronomy and in Deuteronomy Rabbah.

[^382]: BT Gittin 56a-b; Avot de-Rabbi Natan A, ch. 4. The escape of Rabbi Yohanan ben Zakkai from Jerusalem is one of the most extensively analyzed narratives in rabbinic literature; see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 139-175. The composition of the speech he "might have given" to the zealots is a suasoria exercise that draws on this rich narrative tradition.

[^383]: The rabbinic practice of "imagining oneself in a historical situation" is attested in passages like BT Sanhedrin 17a (the qualifications of a member of the Sanhedrin include the ability to "show that a sheretz is clean") and in the various "what if" exercises scattered throughout the Talmud. On the connection to the Greco-Roman suasoria, see Seneca the Elder, *Suasoriae*, preface; and Quintilian, *Inst. Or.* 3.8.49-54.

[^384]: BT Shabbat 88b. The narrative of Moses' encounter with the angels is one of the great suasoriae of rabbinic literature. Moses composes a deliberative speech before a hostile audience (the angels), arguing from the content of the Torah that it is relevant only to earthly creatures. On this passage as a model of rabbinic deliberative rhetoric, see Rubenstein, *Talmudic Stories*, 85-92; and Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, 193-196.

[^385]: Mishnah Berakhot 1:1. The opening of the Mishnah and the Talmud. The sugya that develops from this opening mishnah (BT Berakhot 2a-3a) is one of the most frequently studied units in the Talmud and exemplifies all the rhetorical features of the sugya form.

[^386]: On the structure of the Talmudic sugya as a dialectical form, see Rubenstein, *The Culture of the Babylonian Talmud*, 15-38; and Hidary, *Rabbis and Classical Rhetoric*, ch. 6-7. The sugya's multi-voiced, dialectical character distinguishes it from the monological forms of Greco-Roman declamation and connects it more closely to the Socratic dialogue, though the sugya is more complex than any single Platonic dialogue in its number of participants and temporal span.

[^387]: The opening sugya of BT Berakhot is analyzed in detail in Rubenstein, *The Culture of the Babylonian Talmud*, 17-25, and in numerous traditional commentaries. Its use of every rhetorical skill discussed in this manual -- invention (scriptural proofs), arrangement (sequential questioning), style (the shift between halakhic and aggadic registers), memory (citations from across the rabbinic corpus), and delivery (the dramatic rhythm of question and answer) -- makes it an ideal demonstration text for the culmination of rhetorical training.

[^388]: Based on the prayer formula in BT Berakhot 35b and parallels. The wish that Torah be "fluent on the lips" (*shegura be-fikha*) captures the essential aspiration of all rabbinic rhetorical training: that the student's mastery become so complete that Torah flows from him as naturally as speech itself.
